
==================== SCENE 1 ====================
The squall arrived sideways, gray and insistent, driving salt into Mara’s eyes as if the wind meant to scour them clean. She hunched into it, one forearm over her brow, the other hand locked around the strap of the duffel banging her hip, and took the library steps two at a time. The town green across the street was a roiling sheet; the flag whipped so hard on its pole that the halyards were a constant metal scream. Every fifth gust threw mist from the harbor all the way up the block, so she could taste it—cold, a little like pennies.

The library rose from the granite like a moored ship. Wide steps, brass rails, stone lions going green with years of salt, and a door whose beveled glass quivered in its frame with each slap of the wind. The porch overhang was useless in the sideways rain. By the time Mara reached the landing, water was stringing off her elbows and running down her sleeves to her wrists, pooling in her watchband.

"Very welcoming," she told the door, breath fogging. "You look warm and lit and full of functioning outlets."

She set the duffel down, rummaged one-handed, found the laminated card with the alarm code and emergency contact numbers that Nora had texted her a photo of last week. A flip of the card revealed a smear of old coffee. "Classy," Mara murmured, and freed the keyring dangling from her belt loop.

Only, the key to the library wasn’t there.

She stared at the empty split ring as rain stuttered the surface of the step. The wind leaned on her. She checked again—keys to the pump house, the maintenance shed on the bluff, a collection of old brass beauties whose bites belonged to locks she hadn’t met yet. Not this door.

"Okay," she said. "Okay. Maybe you’re in the envelope. Maybe we love surprises."

She dug deeper in the duffel, past the rolls of painter’s tape, the UV flashlight she’d brought without quite a reason, the Ziploc of Sharpies. No envelope.

She lifted her face to the brass plaque—Essex Memorial Library—and squinted through the rain at the carved molding, the result of some benefactor’s money and pride. The transom window sat narrow above the door like a blind eye. The lock was an old mortise with a newer deadbolt nested above it, stainless steel now stippled with salt crystals.

"A bad day to be the person who thought she had a key," Mara said. She leaned one shoulder to the glass. Inside, the entryway bulbs glowed half-heartedly. The ceiling fixture wavered with the storm. The foyer beyond was shadowed, but she knew the shape of it from memory; two chairs, a rack of tourist brochures nobody would take because of COVID protocols three crises ago, a clock that ticked too loud when the building was empty.

She tried the handle.

It was not merely locked; it was resigned. The deadbolt had that sense some locks have of being turned with the full conviction of the person who did it—some last-thing-of-the-day and click. Then, as she breathed there, feeling the door cold against her cheek, a flicker passed through the foyer: the ceiling fixture brightened, dimmed, brightened, then steadied at a weak, amber tremble.

"Power’s a rumor," she said. She looked down the steps. The street was empty except for a smeared silhouette of a sedan nosed against the curb, wipers slashing. Gulls hugged low to the rooftops. The harbor was a moving slate wall.

She made herself stop, breathe, count. 1… 2… 3. She could feel time burning, but panic always wasted more of it. "Alternate access points," she said out loud, and shouldered the duffel again.

Around the side, the wind found her with new angles and flung the rain under her collar. The library’s clapboard addition—the children’s room, all cheerful windows in good weather—shuddered visibly with each gust. Somebody had taped an X across the glass of the seaside windows with painter’s tape. The tape had started to peel. Mara pressed the seam back with the heel of her hand as she moved, as if the gesture itself could hold back air pressure and fate.

The staff door on the side was under a shallow overhang, a mercy. The keypad there wore the smear of wrists and elbows, the patina of long use. She stepped close enough to keep the rain off it and wiped it with the hem of her shirt. The laminate card came free from her pocket, wrinkled with damp. She keyed in the numbers with a knuckle, felt for the slight give and spring. A polite chirp—then a longer, resentful buzz as the strike plate released.

"Thank you," she told it, not bothering to be under her breath, and yanked the door open. The alarm panel inside woke up in a startled way, a small green LED blinking uncertainty. She hit the sequence again, and the blink steadied into a constant light like a held breath.

Inside smelled like books and dust and ocean-basement. A fan somewhere ticked with a blade that hit its guard every revolution. The overhead fluorescents labored and went quiet, a lower hum still in the walls that made the hair on her forearms prickle.

"Any minute now," she said to the building, and kicked her boots against the threshold until clots of road grit fell loose. She closed the door with her hip, turned the deadbolt, and threw the latch. The sound of the storm stepped back like a body removed from her shoulders. She stood in the sudden comparative quiet and listened to the library creak.

She could feel the generator not running. It was an absence more than a thing. The bookshelves were blue-gray in the strange light. A set of whale postcards fanned on the children’s desk like beached fish. The emergency exit sign over the hallway door glowed a weak cherry red.

"Lights before water," she said. "Power before leaks. People before paper. But there are no people. So power."

She thunked the duffel down and unzipped it again. Headlamp, clip-on work light, heavy orange extension cord, cheap surge strip, a hand-crank NOAA radio she kept out of habit. She slung on the headlamp but didn’t click it yet. She could see enough. She wanted her hands.

The breakers were in the basement—she could feel it in her knees, that remembered cold. She was not eager to go down there if the harbor had come visit. "We’ll check upstairs first," she told the air, as if it were a sensible partner. "We’ll find the duplicate keys. We’ll make a station. We’ll get our systems."

She moved with an economy learned in a dozen other places where the weather was an adversary and a clock. Behind the circulation desk: keys hung on a board by string, labels in faded marker, little puffy stickers applied by someone’s sense of humor. CHILDREN’S ROOM, S. DOOR, CLEANING CLOSET. There was a nasty little lockbox screwed under the counter which you could miss if you didn’t know to kneel. She knew to kneel. She thumbed the combination Nora had texted, and the box let go with a same resentful buzz as the side door.

"Hi stranger," she said to the ring of keys inside. Nora had tied them with blue ribbon. Duplicate labeled in neat block printing: Nora’s keys. The feel of them was heavy and earnest. They clanked in her palm. She clipped them to her belt where the gap on her own ring had been waiting. Her breath left her in relief she didn’t have time to savor.

A flicker ran through the fluorescents again, like someone exhaled into the tubes. The printer on the desk did a little noise as if thinking of waking, then thought the better of it. The wall clock agreed to continue ticking.

"Makes me nervous to plug a surge strip into that," she said, and reached behind the desk for the big, red-handled switch she knew killed the few nonessential outlets on the public side. She threw it with the feel of pushing through baked-on sugar.

She pulled a laminated FLOOD PLAN from the corkboard. Milk-crate diagrams. Pencil notes beside a photocopied FEMA map. A series of instructions in three hands: Amps; Generator; Alarms. The generator key had a photograph of itself taped next to it in case someone moved it. It lived—according to the lamination—behind the returns bin. She tipped the bin, found the key exactly where the lamination said it would be, and held it up between two fingers. "Good Nora," she said.

The generator lived in a little cinderblock lean-to at the back, vented to the outside. She did not love the idea of stepping back out into that weather, but neither did she love the idea of the library’s sump pump forgoing its job. "Two minutes," she told the building. "Don’t do anything while I’m gone."

She cut through the back hallway, checked—without deciding to check—the bathroom for a leak under the window. There was a scatter of droplets on the tile but not a stream. The back door had a push bar. She palmed it and leaned.

The storm took the door, startled and mean, and would have slammed it wide if she hadn’t set her hip. She got herself in the opening, then out through it with the door pressed against her, and stood under the eave where the generator shed sat like a bunker.

The padlock accepted its key with a grit feel that made her wince. The door lifted on a checkered floor blackened by oil and footprints. The generator crouched, orange and self-satisfied. Someone had kept it neat; the little maintenance tag hung from a zip tie, initials and dates inked through spring.

"Let's be friends," she said, and ran through the startup in her head. Fuel valve. Choke. Battery indicator showing smug green. She pulled the cover off the external outlet, looped the extension cord through in case the wind wanted to make a meal of it, and slotted it in with a firm push. She checked for leaks she could smell. Diesel and old rain. She opened the fuel valve and felt the line fill under her fingers, a thread of colder even than the air. She pulled the starter. The generator coughed, considered life, and took it. A sound like a conversation at a different party came online, steady and indifferent to the weather’s dramas.

"Yes," she said simply, and tied the shed door back with twine because she trusted wind less than her own shoulders. She shouldered the library door open again with the extension cord in her hand, snaked it in along the baseboard, and kicked the door shut behind her. The sound of the generator moved from presence to background. The library felt like a lung that had learned to breathe again.

Back at the desk she plugged the surge strip into the extension, and her head went light as the UPS under the desk beeped its gratitude. The printer trilled. The router’s idiot lights blinked in a logical little marching band.

"We are going to prioritize," Mara said to the top of the desk. "We are going to make love to the essentials. We are not going to charge everybody’s phones. Not even my own."

She clicked on the headlamp at last as clouds squared their shoulders and made the room gloomier. She took the elastic bands and cards from the duffel and began to turn the end of the long old table by the windows into a station. She laid down trash bags as a tablecloth because it wasn’t her table and everything she owned was damp. She set out the tags and Sharpies and the waterproof notebook, the roll of orange flagging tape. She unpacked the portable scanner Nora had sent by courier and set its little feet on the table like a crab.

Her movements slowed to a deliberate tempo as the external pressures—the shriek of wind, the thud of loose shutters—became constants instead of questions. She unrolled a duct tape run along the table edge and wrote BASE in block letters with the Sharpie. The duct tape took the ink greedily. She wrote SUMP, GEN, DOORS on the left margin of her notebook and left space for check times.

"Basement," she said, and stood still for a beat to listen. The tick was still there. The fan’s blade still hit. No new noise of water that wasn’t a part of the building’s older symphony.

Nevertheless.

She took a mop and a bucket with her like talismans and opened the basement door. The air that came up was a colder wet, a quiet rot. The first two steps were dry. The third had a skin of moisture like the top of pudding. Her headlamp beam picked out the gray of concrete walls quilted with someone’s attempt at insulation. Halfway down, she paused to listen, the mop handle braced in front of her like a staff.

There was water, but it was behaving. A shallow sheet across the far side, gathered around the sump pit, licking the corners of ancient boxes nobody valued enough to carry upstairs into the light. The sump pump whispered to itself and clicked on like a cat thinking about a bird; it pulled water for ten seconds and tucked off again. A trickle came under the back wall, probably from the eave spouts overwhelmed by the rain. The headlamp washed over the breaker panel—old, but not ancient.

"You are going to resist insult," she told it softly, and popped the panel. None of the switches had thrown. She left it open to make it easier when they inevitably did.

Back upstairs, she drew the basement as a rectangle in her notebook and added the name of the book boxes closest to the line of water: Town Reports 1976–1989. She wrote check again in twenty. She made a little tick mark for the sump’s sound at the minute she’d heard it, knowing it was a habit and that she was the sort of person who comforted herself with data she couldn’t possibly all use.

The wind used the face of the building like a drum. The fluorescents flickered and threatened to be done. The generator’s hum stayed smooth, the extension cord lay docile along the baseboard like an obedient dog. The emergency light over the hallway was a cherry ember.

"Now we nest," she told the empty room. "Now we text people before the line goes in and out."

Her phone had two bars if she held it near the window, one if she breathed too hard. She propped it against a dictionary and hunched to shield it from the sight of whatever gods ran cell towers in a storm.

"In," she texted. "Library open from side. Gen running. Sump fine. Keys secured. Setting station." She sent it to Nora, to Rourke, to Owen, to the board’s general group chat whose name she resisted reading. Deliveries hit in a sputter. Delivered. Not delivered. Delivered. The board chat lit her screen with a bubble—Is it safe?—which she did not answer with words. She walked to the back door and put her shoulder to it and felt the logic of the latch. She set a chair under the handle on the staff side as nothing more than a message to herself: we are set.

She pulled a rolling whiteboard into the zone near the table and wrote on it in long strokes.

- ENTRY: SIDE STAFF DOOR ONLY (CODE 7723#)
- DRY ZONE: START HERE
- WET ZONE: BASEMENT HALF, CHILD. RM WINDOWS WATCH
- POWER: GEN RUNNING 1600; SUMP OK 1605
- KEYS: AT DESK (NORA DUP IN USE)

She printed it so big it would be legible through two layers of bad news. She set the NOAA radio on the corner of the table and wound its crank just to feel the weight of doing it. A man with a copper voice described the gale in all its measurements and none of its feeling. She turned the volume down. Her heartbeat was a metronome unhelped by barometric pressure.

"Alright," she said to the room, to the desk, to the old portraits in their oval frames watching from the walls. "Base of operations. Minimum comfort. Maximum access."

She took the plastic bin she’d brought for categorizing and labeled it INTAKE with the tape. A second she labeled TO DRY. A third she labeled REFERENCE. She set the little book stand at the end of the table with a handwritten sign that said DROP. She found a percolator in the staff kitchen so old it was cool and filled it with water that stuttered from the tap. The generator took the draw without complaint. Coffee happened like a blessing.

She shrugged off the wet outer layer and hung it on the back of the chair she’d just wedged against the door, because needs aligned. She rolled her sleeves to the elbow and felt her body find a different temperature, a different relationship to the world.

The fluorescents made their last valiant flicker and surrendered. The emergency lights colored the hallway. Her headlamp threw a clean, white disc across the table that made a small island of daylight inside the storm.

"Better," she said into it, and took a breath that smelled like coffee and damp paper and old wood. The clock on the wall ticked.

Her phone buzzed an arrival. From Nora: Knew you’d do it. Third drawer down left of the desk: tags and zip ties. Also there’s an extra pair of socks in the bottom of the returns bin because I am a genius.

Mara found the socks exactly where they said they’d be, tucked in a zip bag in a corner with a note on the bag’s exterior in Nora’s neat hand: YOU’RE WELCOME, PAST ME TO FUTURE US.

She laughed once, short and abrupt, a sound that broke the seal on her sternness. "Thank you, past you," she said, and sat on the desk long enough to strip her boots off, wring her socks into the mop bucket, and pull on the dry pair. The relief traveled up her spine like a fuse going the right direction.

She arranged the tags and zip ties in a little Army of Useful Things along the tape line’s edge. She checked the generator through the back door window—not going out, just looking—and noted the regularity of its small shiver. She made another set of tick marks in the notebook, and another beside those that said doors latched.

The library had a feeling when it was ready. Not safe. Not finished. Just—set. The knowledge that if someone came in out of that weather, she could look at them and say: here. Do this, put that there, start with this. It was a specific peace and it settled into her like body heat.

The wind hit the windows again and the tape on the children’s room glass lifted a little. She walked there and pressed it down with the heel of her hand. The sea was a flat pane of violent pewter beyond the wetlands, like another sky that had fallen.

"Hold," she said to the tape, to the glass, to the building. "Hold while we make a map."

She went back to the table and drew the library on the first page of the waterproof notebook. She labeled the rooms. She wrote her own name next to Base with a time. She circled the side door and wrote access above it and underlined it twice.

When the next text came—Rourke: Copy. On my way. Twenty?—she looked at the clock, marked the time in the margin, and replied without leaving her station. "Come in side. Watch the last step. Everything important is inside."

She sipped her coffee. She breathed. She listened, and the building answered in what counted, then and there, as a steady voice.


==================== SCENE 2 ====================
The librarian’s office kept its own weather. The rest of the building rattled and sighed with each gust, but in here the noise turned to a deep, muffled thud, like someone dropping luggage in the next room over and over. The fluorescents hummed. Tide marks of Scotch tape on the filing cabinets showed where signs had lived and died. The floor had that adhesive drag of old carpet.

Mara shut the door with a hip and set her notebook, tags, and zip ties in a line along the desk blotter. She pulled the chair back with a squeak and slid open the top drawer. Paper clips, spare library cards, a handful of wrapped butterscotch, a blue inhaler with a rubber band around it. Third drawer: tags and zip ties—those she’d already taken. Bottom drawer: nothing of interest but a tangle of charging cords and a stapler that had the heft of a small mallet.

“The box,” she said to the quiet. She crouched to look under the desk. Nothing but a tangle of power strips and a pair of flats with a pebble stuck in one sole. She straightened, scanned the room: framed photo of a kid breaking a ribbon at a Fun Run, the spine of a policy binder with READINESS in block letters, a corkboard with a faded schedule grid.

The lockbox was behind the corkboard. Five screws held the board; someone had hung it as a disguise, not thinking about urgency. She pushed on the bottom pin, jerked the cork free, and the board swung down with a burr of Velcro. A steel box the size of a lunch pail sat bolted to the wall, paint scuffed at the corners, a narrow combination dial like a toy safe.

The door behind her clicked open an inch.

“Hey—hey.” A voice somewhere between an apology and a warning. “We’re closed.”

Mara didn’t turn. “Good evening to you too.”

A pause, one boottread on the threshold. “Ma’am, office is staff only.”

“Agreed.” She looked over her shoulder. The custodian in the doorway had his hood up so tight only a crescent of face showed: tired eyes, gray whiskers, the dark gleam of rain on his jacket. He was a big man gone careful in the knees. The key ring at his belt would have dragged her jeans down.

“I’m Gus,” he said, as if it might help. “Custodial. I just came to latch the back des—what are you doing with Nora’s wall?”

“Nora’s out,” Mara said. “She texted me to retrieve her duplicates. Combination should be six-one-three-two. Do you know it?”

Gus stepped in, shut the door, and leaned his shoulder to it like he expected the wind to argue. “Even if I did, I can’t just—hey. You can’t just. There’s a process.”

“There is.” She set her notebook flat on the desk where he could see. The corners were already dark with wet. “I’m initiating it.”

“Who are you?”

“Mara. I’m here to stabilize and inventory.” She didn’t add the rest—how many shelters, how many nights, how many times she’d said those exact words with other names attached. “Nora asked.”

“Uh-huh.” He crossed his arms. “Phone calls ask a lot until the lawyer shows up.”

“Rourke is on his way,” she said. “You can wait with me if you want. We can stare at the safe together. Or we can open it and I can document everything while your eyes are on the page.”

Gus’s brows went up under the hood and then down again. The wind pushed once at the window and it answered with a single groan. He looked at the corkboard hanging like a swung door and sighed through his nose.

He moved closer, the tang of wet wool and Pine-Sol coming with him. He looked at the combi dial like it might bite. “Six-one-three-two?”

“That’s what Nora sent.”

“What if it isn’t.”

“Then we close the board and pretend we just checked the tape on the window.”

He grunted. “Cute.”

“I don’t do cute,” she said. “I do signatures.”

He huffed something that might have been a laugh and jammed his hands into his jacket pockets. He rocked on his heels, decided, and pulled one hand out again. His keys chimed against the steel of the box as he tapped the face, not to open it, but like he was introducing them. “I’m not authorizing anything. You hear me?”

“Clear as the generator.” She lifted her phone so he could see the text: Nora’s name, the combination, the instruction. The little blue bubble laid it out with a librarian’s economy.

Gus took in the screen, the name. His mouth pulled sideways. “That her. With the apostrophe in the right place.”

“Do I have your eyeballs?”

“You got ’em.”

Mara spun the dial: right 6, left past 6 to 1, right to 3, left to 2. The mechanism inside was the cheap kind that complained as it moved. She pulled down on the little lever and felt the grudging give.

Inside: a foam insert with hollows cut for rings. Two rings lay in their beds: one with Denver Broncos lanyard for the circulation desk and returns; one with a plain blue webbing and metal tags stamped with numbers. A third cavity was empty with a label on masking tape that read HAZMAT CABINET—ARCHIVES in Nora’s hand. On the inside of the door, a card sleeve, and in it a one-page protocol sheet with shaky plastic lamination. Retrieval requires: 1) signature of librarian or acting authority, 2) signature of witness, 3) notation of time, date, purpose.

Mara held the box open without touching anything else. “Okay,” she said, for both of them. She snapped a photo with her phone: the open box, the rings, the protocol. Another of the card sleeve. Another close on the tags.

“You’re doing that for who,” Gus said, not quite skeptical.

“For whoever asks later.”

“Later’s always a crowd.”

“We’ll get through them.” She set the phone down and took out her pen. “I need your full name.”

“Gus.” He stared at the blue webbing ring like it might leap. “Gus Vale.”

“Augustus?”

He rolled his eyes but said, resigned, “Augustine. Augustine Vale. No one says it unless they’re mad.”

She wrote. “Augustine Vale, witness, time nineteen-thirteen.” She checked the wall clock—quartz, dim tick behind the hum. “Purpose: to secure duplicate master access for storm response and site inventory with custodial witness. Authority: text authorization from Nora L., librarian-in-charge.” She put Nora’s number next to the name. “Rourke ETA in twenty.”

Gus rocked again, once. His hand lifted, hovered over the ring, fell. “You touch it, you keep eyes on it,” he said. “No setting down somewhere and wandering off because you had to go scotch a door.”

“I don’t wander.”

“Everyone wanders,” he said, matter-of-fact. “They just call it something clean.”

Mara slid on a pair of nitrile gloves from her back pocket. She took the blue webbing ring between two fingers and lifted it free. It had weight. The tags were stamped with numbers and cryptic letter pairs: 1A, 2C, LB MECH, BOILER, ELEC, S, and one ugly brass oval hammered with MASTER in a font no one loved. The small one that caught her finger without asking was the safe key for the petty cash tin, she recognized the cut of it; she left it on the foam. She photographed the ring front and back, then threaded a zip tie through the webbing and a manila tag. On the tag, she wrote the date and her name, purpose, witness: A. Vale.

“You always make it this… fussy?” Gus asked. Not unkind.

“I make it so that when someone points and says why, there’s paper.”

“How about the pen runs out.”

She took a second pen from behind her ear and held it up. “Spare.”

He shook his head, but the edge in his mouth softened. He put his finger on the protocol sheet inside the door. “Sign that too,” he said, and then, reluctant but resigned: “Here.”

He leaned it toward her, bracing it with a forearm, and she scribbled her name on the line for acting authority. There were other names already there, battered by years—Nora’s, two of the old directors she knew by gossip only, a blank line with a tear through it where some pen had punched too hard.

Gus took the sheet when she finished and signed in the witness box in block letters that leaned right. He dated it and added a time, paused, listened to the wind count three slow breaths, and added a minute. “So it’s honest,” he said.

“Appreciated,” Mara said.

She closed the box with an audible click and lifted the corkboard back onto its Velcro. It hung crooked. She fixed it with two fingers so the Fun Run kid’s ribbon wasn’t skewed. Then she ran the zip tie’s tail through and snugged it to seal the ring to the tag, the small rasp loud in the quiet room. She tucked the tail under itself so it wouldn’t whip.

Gus’s eyes tracked every movement. “You know what all those go to?”

“Not all.” She turned the brass oval in her palm. “Enough to get through the building without breaking anything. The rest we’ll test and list.”

He made a sound in his throat, not agreement, not disagreement. “People get fond of rules when they need in.”

“I get fond of the rules that work.” She placed the ring where he could see it as she opened the desk drawer and took out a clear zip bag from Nora’s stash. On the bag’s label in marker, she wrote KEYS–DUP MASTER–REC’D BY M. [last name], 19:15, WITNESS A. VALE. She slid the ring inside and zipped it closed, then snapped another photo with the bag seal visible, the label legible.

“What’s the ‘S’?” Gus asked, nodding at the tag initials inside the bag.

“Stair,” she said. “Or storage, depending on who stamped it. We’ll find out. You’ll be there.”

“Will I.”

“You’re my witness,” she said, not smiling when she said it because that would be a kind of thing, and she didn’t do that kind of thing either. “If you’ve got something else, tell me now.”

Gus peeled his hood back at last, let damp hair steam in the office heat. He looked older all at once, like a man who’d done this building’s floors and arguments for a long time. He nodded at the protocol sheet again. “You send a copy to Nora.”

“She’ll get it,” Mara said. “I’ll text her a photo now. You want me to copy you too?”

He looked offended. “I don’t text.”

“Then you get the satisfaction of not having to look at your phone.” She shot a photo of the filled form, the label on the bag, the ring inside. She sent the first to Nora with a one-line note: Retrieved per your text. Vale witness. Tagging now. The second she attached to a message to Rourke: Keys secured. Documentation complete. Back to Base in one.

Gus cleared his throat. “You got a place for that where it’s not going to go walking.”

“Base,” she said, lifting the bag so the keys inside knocked politely against plastic. “In my sightline. Taped to the underside of the table when I’m not holding it.”

He blinked. “That’s a new one.”

“Nothing can slide off tape it wasn’t meant to slide off.” She tucked the bag into her jacket’s inner pocket and patted it. The weight against her ribs balanced something inside of her that had been leaning.

Gus looked toward the hall. The light in the office wavered for a breath, pulled back, steadied. The hum returned to its same note. “All right,” he said. “If anyone asks, I’ll say I watched you do it by the book.”

“Even if it wasn’t the book you wrote.”

“It’s the book I respect,” he said.

Mara slid her gloves off and folded them, then tucked them into a little trash sack in her pocket. She gathered her notebook, pens, the leftover butterscotch—no, not that; she set the candy back in the drawer where it had lived—and she put the chair back where she’d found it. She pressed the corkboard once, firm, and checked the window latch out of habit.

“You need me to haul anything?” Gus asked, lingering.

“You could walk me back,” she said. “That’ll be enough.”

They stepped into the hallway. The corridor smelled of dust warmed by forced air, the not-unfriendly musk of wet coats on old hooks. The emergency EXIT sign buzzed an insect note. From somewhere nearer the door, they could hear the generator’s steady throat. Mara marked the time at the top of a new page as they walked, and wrote KEYS SECURED, VALVE—no, VALE—WITNESS, in a hand that would be readable later by someone with a finger on a budget line.

Gus slowed at the threshold to the main room, listening. “You going to run me through what you’re doing, once we sit down?” he asked.

“I’ll run you through while we do it,” she said. “Less sitting. Little more string.”

He made a gratified grunt at that. “Good. Better than watching the wobble on the big fern for the thirty-third time.”

They reached Base—her table with its map, tape, the Army of Useful Things flanking the edges. Mara slid the key bag onto the table, took out the roll of painter’s tape, and laid two clean strips in a cross on the underside. She pressed the bag to the adhesive and felt it stick. She made a note in the margin: bag taped underside, 19:18. She underlined it once. She took a last photo, her hand in frame, pressing the bag down so there was no confusion about which table, which hand.

Gus watched her write as if the motion itself settled the building a fraction. “You’re a lot of work,” he said, but it landed as approval.

“It’s cheaper than do-overs,” she said, and capped her pen.

At the sound of wind on glass, both of them looked toward the children’s room, toward the strip of tape she’d reinforced earlier. It held. The hum held. The keys, stuck under the table within reach of her knee, were a new weight and a kind of answer. Mara marked the time in her notebook again and drew a small square around it for emphasis.

“Okay,” she said to Gus, to the office behind them, to the task in front of them. “Let’s make the list.”


==================== SCENE 3 ====================

The circulation desk wasn’t built for triage, but it had a counter with elbow room and a wall outlet that didn’t spark when you looked at it. The squall pressed its palm to the front windows; the glass pushed back with a small, muscular sound. Overhead, the EXIT sign’s insect buzz held steady. The generator’s throat never broke.

Mara slid the returns cart out of the well and parked it under the book drop to keep it from drifting. She wiped the counter down with alcohol, then wiped the bottle so she wouldn’t be the person who left it ringed and tacky. She pulled a roll of blue painter’s tape and made right angles: a square labeled Intake in block letters, an arrow to a rectangle she called Photograph, a second arrow to a tight square labeled Seal. She wrote the words big, like you would for a group you hadn’t met yet.

She set her notebook open to a fresh page and wrote at the top: Temporary Evidence Station—Library Main, Circulation—D1, 19:27. Under that, she drew a box for Chain of Custody Log and made columns with her pen, neat as a ledger: Item No., Description, Found by, Found at (loc.), Date/Time (found), Condition, Photographed (Y/N), Photo File Nos., Packaging Type, Seal ID, Bin No., Transfer to, Transfer by, Transfer date/time, Signatures. She underlined Signatures twice and put a star in the margin, a private joke about where things go to die.

“Okay,” she said, to the counter, to the sticky air. “Let’s build a spine.”

She unrolled the orange evidence seals and counted off ten, reading the microprinted numbers aloud for her phone’s mic to pick up: “ES-103441 through ES-103450.” She filmed the strip briefly, panned the digits, then rolled it back and slid a rubber band around it so it wouldn’t telescope all over the floor the second she looked away. A second roll—red—got the same treatment. She made a tiny note to herself: ES-103441–450 in play; reserve red logged.

She laid out tags: manila with wire ties, matte cardstock with a hole. The manila ones got Item No. preprinted in sharpie in the corner; the white ones were for description overflow and cross-referencing to photo numbers. She stacked pencils in a cup, black pens in another, and wrote: “Pencil on book paper only” on an index card and taped it low where a hand would find it.

The camera came out of its dry bag without a fight. She checked battery—eighty-one percent—slotted in a fresh card, and spun the dial to AV. She shook out the folding lightbox she carried for scenes like this: a fabric cube that popped into shape with a little violence and lined with white. She taped the seam where it had blown once, straightened the diffuser, and laid in the Photo ID card she’d already printed: LIB-MAIN-D1. A two-tone scale and a small color checker went next to it. She photographed her own hand holding the scale so that anyone later could calibrate skin tone under these particular fluorescents.

“White balance, tungsten,” she told herself as she clicked. “Still a little green. Note it.”

She taped an index card to the counter: Photo Template—Include scale, color card, ID, tag legible. Distance: twenty inches. Angles: overhead, 45°, macro detail. No hands unless gloved.

She opened a fresh box of nitrile gloves, sizes small to large, set them in three stacks, and wrote CLEAN HANDS and DIRTY HANDS on two more cards. CLEAN was the zone around the lightbox; DIRTY was the intake square on the counter. A small bottle of baby powder went behind the CLEAN sign—a trick for making two pair of gloves go on without sticking. She set down a box of cotton swabs and a handful of sterile file cards in case a smear needed lifting. She didn’t expect dusting here, but expecting not to expect was its own religion.

The bins—polymer, latchable, rubber gasket lids—she pulled from under the desk and numbered with a black grease pencil: BIN-01 through BIN-06. Each bin got a strip of blue tape on its lid for the day and a scannable code she made with her pocket printer. She wrote a second set of numbers on laminated tags and zip-tied them to the bin handles. A small indulgence: the tags clacked; it sounded like readiness.

“Weight limit twenty-five pounds,” she said, setting the spring scale next to the bins. “We’re not migrating elephants.”

She set her old labeler to spit out numbers for the tags: 001 through 020 in a dumb, pleasing progression. As it chirped and fed adhesive, she peeled and pressed each onto a manila tag, rubbed them down with her thumb so the corners wouldn’t peel up later and betray her with a curl.

Her phone, propped against the desk in a shallow cup, recorded everything. “Initial station setup,” she narrated, because later she would forget the easy parts and the recording would give her unfancy truth. “Primary operator: Mara Vale. No second. Weather: storm, generator on. Lighting: fluorescents, cool. Intake—photograph—seal zones designated with tape. Supplies staged: gloves, tags 001–020, seals ES-103441–450 orange in play, red reserved. Bins—six—numbered. Chain-of-custody log initialized.”

She stopped recording long enough to cut a clean stack of chain-of-custody forms from her pad and hole-punch them with the old desk punch she found in a drawer, its metal puck reassuringly heavy. She clipped the stack to a board, labeled it with a strip of tape, and wrote START HERE in boxy letters.

A short, rolling chair squeaked as she nudged it aside. In the well of the desk, she found a Ziplock stuffed with rubber bands and a coil of twine. She took the twine, left the bands. She measured out lengths for tag ties and taped the ends so they wouldn’t fray into oblivion.

Outside, the wind pressed and released, pressed and released. Somewhere, a ladder creaked once like a door forgetting itself.

“Test run,” she said, and looked around for something harmless. The butterscotch in the drawer lived here, and she refused to move it. She checked under the desk and found a single loose screw with a little square of paint still stuck to the threads, the pale green that lived on these walls. She set the screw deliberately in the DIRTY square.

“Item Zero-Zero-One,” she said into the room, pleased by how the number held its own weight. The phone’s red dot watched her steadily. “Description: loose screw, flathead, approximately—” She grabbed the measuring tape. “—one inch. Paint on threads, light green. Found at: Library main floor, circulation desk well. Found by: Mara Vale. Date/time: D1, 19:41. Condition: dusty. Note: likely desk hardware, no immediate relevance.”

She wrote it down in neat blocks, tagged the manila with 001, tied the tag through the screw’s slot so it didn’t slip off, then put on CLEAN gloves and transferred the screw from DIRTY to PHOTO. Overhead shot. Forty-five degree. Macro on the paint fleck. The color checker. The scale.

She paused, checked the images, nodded. “Photo files,” she said, reading the numbers off the screen. “IMG_4132–4135.” She wrote them on the log next to the line, then set a small Mylar evidence envelope on the SEAL square, slid the screw in, the tag string out, the tag itself on the outside with a bit of painter’s tape to keep it married but not in the glue.

She peeled an orange seal and laid it across the envelope flap. “Seal ES-103441,” she said. She printed her initials across the seam with the date. It looked official because it was.

She set the sealed envelope in BIN-01. She weighed the bin—three ounces, including the envelope, ridiculous—and wrote the bin number on the line. Then she took the chain-of-custody form and made the first entry in the transfer box: From: Mara Vale. To: Mara Vale. Date/time: D1, 19:47. Reason: intake to storage. She signed both boxes because there was no one else to witness the obvious. Sometimes you wrote down your own shadow.

Her stomach reminded her she’d promised it something not out of a machine, and she told it, “After labels,” like a truce.

She taped a workflow onto the back edge of the counter for anyone who’d come behind her:
1. Gloves on (DIRTY).
2. Place item in Intake. Do not clean.
3. Describe in log. Assign Item No. Tag item with Item No.
4. Transfer to Photograph (CLEAN gloves).
5. Photograph: overhead, 45°, macro. Include scale, color card, photo ID.
6. Package item. Seal. Initial/date sealing. Record Seal ID.
7. Stage in bin. Record Bin No., weight.
8. Chain-of-custody transfer entries. Signatures.
9. Gloves off. Wash hands.

She looked at it from the vantage point of someone new to her, someone tired. It read like a person had thought of them in advance. Good. Small orders were kindness.

She made a tight kit at the far end of the desk: swabs, envelopes, a few empty pill vials with childproof lids she repurposed as microcontainers, each labeled with a blank for item numbers. She taped a note to the vials: DO NOT PUT FOOD IN THESE, which struck her as obvious until she pictured the way long days blurred edges. She set a small trash can at the SEAL square and tied a fresh liner, weighed down with two magazines so the first gust through the doors wouldn’t send it off sailing.

Thunder stepped on the roof and walked across slowly. The fan over the returns bin did its best impression of a person with clear lungs.

She made a second test, so that the screw wouldn’t be the lone dummy: a felted chess pawn from the jar that lived near the check-out, marked OUT OF ORDER the same week the kids had decided the board didn’t need to match the pieces. She thought better of borrowing it, then put it back. She took instead a paperclip from her own notebook pocket and gave it the process. Item Zero-Zero-Two: green-coated paperclip, standard size, bent slightly at the small loop. Found at: Base table. Found by: Mara Vale. Condition: used. Photographs, seal, bin. It shone momentarily in the lightbox like a keepsake.

She printed a small sign and stuck it at the entrance to the desk: INVENTORY IN PROGRESS. ASK BEFORE ENTERING. PLEASE DO NOT MOVE ITEMS.

Her phone buzzed once with a weather alert. She flicked it down, recorded a quick note: “Noise level acceptable. Possible brief outages. Ensure paper logs mirror digital. No reliance on single medium.” She put an extra pen in her breast pocket, because pens had a way of going elsewhere without malice.

She drafted a simple intake form for other hands, if she needed to deputize: Finder’s name. Contact. Where found (be specific). When found. Was item moved (Y/N). If yes, from where. Any known context. She left three copies on the counter with a pen tethered by twine to a tape loop that said LEAVE ME HERE PLEASE. The tape loop made her smile. It might work; it might make someone want to break the rule; both were real possibilities and the sign was half the point.

She pulled a chair from children’s and set it just outside the desk, marking it OBSERVER with tape, an informal seat for anyone she needed to talk through the process. She put a second index card under the lightbox: No Social Photos—Legal Hold. She paired the camera with her old laptop and opened a folder: LIB-MAIN-D1. Subfolders: RAW, EXPORT, LOGS. She typed the image numbers into a file named Photo Index so that she wouldn’t later have to squint at EXIF data through exhaustion. She put a spare battery on charge from a USB brick and set two headlamps beside it.

An old brass date stamp sat by the barcode scanner. She inked it and found the date wheel still obedient. She stamped the corner of the chain-of-custody forms with a little rectangle of authority and felt a low, nonspecific satisfaction that could have been a meal.

“Station live,” she said, and clicked the recorder off and on again purely to fix the moment.

She made one last pass with her eyes. Intake square empty and unsticky. Photo box set. Seal zone stocked. Bins labeled and closed. Keys taped under the table where her knee could find them. The corkboard in the office would keep its own counsel. The fern’s wobble across the room was not her department.

She wrote the time in the notebook: 19:58. She boxed it with two strokes and underlined STARTS NOW.

She placed her palm lightly on the counter and felt the way a library held a collective breath at night—the weight of pages, the idea of order waiting to be invited. She took a breath and gave it back to the room.

“First thing that comes in,” she told the empty, useful air, “goes through by the book.”


==================== SCENE 4 ====================
The reading room had turned into a narrow campsite: a cot folded between the long catalog table and the radiator, a crate for a nightstand, the geometry of emergency living grafted onto the hush of shelves. The desk lamps were off; only the exit light washed the room in weak green, and beyond the mullioned windows the sky flickered with the storm’s intermittent throat-clearing. The old rugs hung on to a gritted damp, and the air smelled of paper starch, cloth glue, and the mineral breath of the sea leaking up through the foundation.

“Vivian?” Mara kept her voice low without a clear reason. She had the recorder on her collar and felt the button with her thumb to check the click. Headlamp on, beam tight. She stayed at the threshold of the rug’s edge and checked the floor for obvious prints and drips. Nothing fresh. Dust filmed along the baseboard where the cot skirt didn’t reach.

Vivian lay on her side on the cot, knees tucked, a thermal blanket smoothed to her waist. The cot was the kind with canvas that hugged the hips, untenable after two hours for anyone with a back. Her hair had been gathered in a loose knot that had defeated itself against the pillow. Her face turned toward the stacks at an angle that asked to be adjusted. Mara made herself look squarely, not skirting angles: the jaw slack, lips parted, a minor bluing at the angle of the nose. No condensation on the headlamp’s beam at the mouth. Hands under the blanket, one shoulder pulled a little higher like she’d hiked it against a draft at some point and then never let it go.

Mara said, to the recorder, “Reading room. Cot present. One occupant. No observed purposeful breathing. Initiating primary check.” Her own voice sounded too professional in her ear; she could hear the thin string of her pulse in it. She pivoted the beam away and took three establishing shots with the handheld: doorway to cot, cot to exit, cot to stacks, wide and then tighter.

She moved in on a line that used the edges of the rug and the legs of the catalog table to keep her feet where others’ had been. Gloves on. She crouched, careful to plant her knee where the weave already showed a dip. “Vivian?” She didn’t touch yet. At the pillow edge, a halo of salt crust like dried sweat. The pillowcase had been folded over once with an old-house tidiness that had outlasted a lot.

She pulled the blanket back an inch at the shoulder and put two fingers to the carotid. Cold first, then the deep stillness under cold. No pulse. The skin had that mild, resistant drag of paper left in the sun. She noted the ear lobe lightly—no warmth. The jaw did not fall further when she let go. She didn’t try to open the eye; the eyelid, half-down, had already clouded enough to answer the question she didn’t want to ask with her hand.

“Time of assessment: 20:11,” she said. “No pulse, no chest rise. No resuscitative attempt initiated due to unknown downtime and scene integrity.” She hated the sentence as she said it and kept it anyway. She lifted the blanket back over Vivian’s shoulder exactly to where it had been.

On the crate beside the cot: three amber prescription bottles, one white OTC ibuprofen, a ceramic cup with a lipstick print wrong for this room’s usual inhabitants, and a paper pharmacy tray with days of the week printed in cheerful font—Monday empty, Tuesday half gone, the plastic lid clipped back. The tray label said VIVIAN ALBRIGHT in municipal pharmacy font, initialed by a tech and time-stamped 06:45. The date stamp on the side was that morning’s date. The Tuesday blister bubbles had two punctures left ragged, not clean-pill pop domes—pressed from the backing, messy. The Monday blister was peeled. The Wednesday row was pristine.

The amber bottles were lined up with the labels facing out, but the one for atenolol was empty, the cap unscrewed and set upside down like a coin, and the atenolol bottle wore a cap printed for a different pharmacy with a different color band. The label on the second, sertraline, had a refill sticker that didn’t match the circled “pickup” date—it had been hand-corrected with blue ink and initialed JH. The third bottle, for clonazepam, rattled with way more than a week’s supply.

Mara photographed from above, then closed in on labels, lot numbers, the address, the little orange triangle with the do-not-crush warning. She angled the light to get the microprint on the pill faces. She didn’t touch. The ibuprofen’s seal lay peeled and folded under the bottle like a tiny umbrella in a drink, sticky with glue. Next to it, a library pencil, chewed end, rolled and stopped against the crate’s lip.

“Note: mismatched caps—atenolol bottle cap from Bayview Pharmacy, label from Harbor Light Dispensary. Refill date on sertraline sticker February twentieth, pickup initialed JH, corrected to March second.” She looked at the blister tray again. “Tuesday punctures jagged, Tuesday card shows two missing doses, no cut edges. Tray time-stamped 06:45.”

She went back to Vivian, made herself look at hands. The right hand had an ink smear along the side of the little finger, small, the kind you get from writing against a soft cover. The left hand had a crescent of something opaque under the thumbnail—soap, maybe, or adhesive. She photographed both. There was no bracelet for allergies, no medic alert. A library checkout slip had been used for a bookmark in a paperback on the floor, spined open under the cot, and the slip had a date from three weeks back. The title was something about coastlines; the pages near the middle had a faint corrugation from damp.

Wind slapped the windows, hard enough to flex the leaded panes, and the exit light skittered a greener shade. She resisted the reflex to pick up the ceramic cup and smell it. Instead she crouched to photograph the meniscus line, the edge of a comma where someone had set the cup down after a sip, and the two faint lipstick ghosts—notched, then smoothed.

Footsteps in the hall, and then the scrape of a shoulder against the door frame, someone stopping, recalculating traffic lines the way people do when something in a room is wrong.

Julian said, “Don’t touch anything.” His voice had its thickened courthouse timber, pitched low but big enough to fill a gym.

“I haven’t.” Mara stood and angled her body so that she blocked the cot with her shoulder but kept him in frame between the stacks. She lifted the camera, shot him at the threshold. “Please stay at the rug edge.”

He obeyed the line without crediting that he was obeying anything. His hair was damp and combed to keep damp from winning. His jacket had the sort of waterproofing that didn’t show until it mattered. He looked not at Vivian but at Mara, then at the pill cluster with the same kind of glance you give a chessboard before you know the rules.

“Is she—” He didn’t finish the word. He swallowed. His jaw set in that composed way that read easily as control if you wanted control to be a visible thing.

“Yes,” Mara said. “Do you have the number for Rourke?”

“He’s already on his way,” Julian said. “Dispatch rang him. I was told to secure the area and to ensure we don’t violate process.”

“Great.” Mara took two steps back, put her back against the catalog table, and breathed once, just to get her breathing and her voice back on the same page. “Then we need to photograph and log what’s immediately perishable and not move anything. I’m going to finish the set around the bed and the meds. I’ll notate with flags, not cards.”

“You’ll do nothing until he arrives,” Julian said. “For liability.”

“I’m not moving anything,” she said. “I’m recording what’s in plain view. That preserves your liability.”

His eyes flicked to the recorder on her collar and then to the headlamp. He stood very still. People who were trying not to contaminate scenes tended to perform stillness; people who didn’t care did the opposite. He had pivoted so that the wet edge of his coat didn’t drip onto the rug, which put him two degrees in the positive column whether he liked it or not.

“Fine,” he said. “Photographs only. No bagging. No repositioning.”

“Copy,” she said. “Can you stand where you are and do me a favor?”

He lifted his chin.

“Call the power company and ask them to hold any planned shutoffs within a quarter mile of here for the next two hours. Tell them we have a death scene with no backup generator and we need stable lighting.”

“Is the lighting not sufficient?” He looked up as if evaluating the heavens.

“It’s sufficient,” she said. “I want it to stay that way.”

He pulled his phone, stepped an inch backward, then realized the inch took him off the rug and back forwarded the inch again. “Don’t instruct me,” he said, and dialed anyway.

Mara set out three small evidence flags—she refused the urge to use the labeled index cards she’d made because paper fibers shed—yellow plastic triangles with numbers that were not chronological with the last set she’d used because she never made the numbers mean sequence. She put Flag 12 by the atenolol bottle and cap, Flag 27 at the blister tray, and Flag 31 by the ceramic cup.

She panned the camera across Vivian’s face, down the blanket, once steady, once for detail. She said, for the record, “Livelihood appearances consistent with a window exceeding…” She swallowed the guess. “…with a window we’ll establish.” She took a photo of the clock over the circulation desk, which read 20:16 and ran five minutes ahead of her phone.

The nightstand crate had a library accession stamp on the inside lip as if it had once been a display crate for pumpkins or copies of a mystery novel. In the crate’s shadow, a stack of four index cards rubber-banded. She checked herself. Left them. Photographed them. The top card had a list in narrow, disciplined writing: 22:00—check doors, 22:30—boiler gauge, 23:00—call Nora (if awake), 23:30—meds. The bottom of the card had a box with M and E and V next to it like a rota. There was no check in the 23:30 box. The second card had a column of pharmacy phone numbers that matched the labels on the bottles and one that didn’t. The ink color on that extra number was different.

Julian made his efficient voice at the power company. “—not asking for a new line, just requesting you postpone any maintenance cuts. Yes, an incident. Public building. Thank you. Yes, I’ll hold.” He lowered the phone from his ear and looked at Vivian finally, the look landing and sticking.

He said, quiet, “She’d hate this cot.”

“She would,” Mara said, because it was both the safe thing to say and the true one.

He glanced at Mara’s hands. “Gloves,” he said, as if approving, as if he’d caught her out and needed to let her know he’d caught her out. Then, “Don’t take offense, but if you get the sense you’re about to move something in the course of being… thorough, stop.”

“I’ll stop,” she said. She took two more shots of the blister pack, one with the headlamp dimmed to bring out the stress fractures in the plastic around the puncture sites. “Julian, do you know who initialed JH on this bottle?”

He didn’t come closer to look. “No,” he said. “I’m not handling pharmacist initials. Rourke will.”

“Not asking you to handle them. Asking if you know the person. Local. If they’re reachable tonight.”

“I said I don’t know,” he said. “And even if I did, I’m not exposing anyone to a… to a raft of… inquiries.”

“Understood,” she said, and wrote JH? on her internal list of later calls. She photographed the label with the initial again, making sure the pharmacy phone number was legible.

On the floor by the wall, tucked under the cot, a narrow plastic water bottle lay on its side with the cap on, dented. She pointed, angled the camera, fired. The dent was on the side opposite the label, a clean thumb press or a knee. The fill line sat half. The label’s ink had bled a little against the condensation.

“Anything in your training about moving a blanket to check for… additional evidence?” Julian asked, watching her, not the scene.

“I won’t lift more than I did,” she said. “We’ve got the face, the hands, the meds, the immediate surroundings. I’ll take the room. Then I’m done until Rourke gets here.”

He nodded, unhappy, and lifted the phone again. “Yes. Thank you. Two hours.” He hung up. “They’ll hold,” he said. “They wanted a case number. I told them I didn’t have one yet.”

“Good,” she said. She moved to the periphery. The reading room’s wall of biographies and county histories looked at the cot the way a crowd looks at a street accident. On the chair nearest the cot, a wool cardigan, Vivian-sized, folded over the back with a neatness that spoke of the person putting it there standing up and doing it deliberately. The cardigan sleeve had a snag that had been looped back through the knit with a bobby pin. The bobby pin was black. She photographed the snag. No hair on the pin.

She crouched to level the camera with the radiator fins; dust had gathered in stripes, and there, faintly, were two parallel smudges, about a finger-width apart, at the edge where a hand might have reached when pulling the blanket up. On the cot leg was a smear—dull gray—shoe rubber. She noted it, shot it, did not touch the cot leg or lace the scene into a narrative. She wasn’t here to be a novelist right now. She was here to nail the corners down.

“Who authorized this cot?” Julian asked.

“Nora,” Mara said. “She told me last week they were making a sleeping station here for overflow when the north wing was too cold.”

“And now we’ll be on the front page for it,” Julian said, almost amiable, as if reheating an old argument kept him from the thing on the cot.

“It’s a cot in a library,” Mara said. “That’s not a scandal.”

“Everything is, depending on who profits,” he said. He put his hands in his pockets, then took them out and rested them against the sides of his pants instead, the way a man does after he remembers he’s not supposed to put his hands in his pockets at a service.

From the office beyond, the little brass date stamp on the intake station caught her headlamp and threw a neat flash. It made her think of stamping some correctness onto the room. She didn’t go to the desk. “Julian, do you see a phone, a landline, in here? If Nora calls, I want you to take it. I don’t want this room to hold that call.”

He looked at the wall phone by the door, a beige plastic piece half as old as the building. “I’ll step into the hall for any calls,” he said. “I’m not going to relay anything to you that’s not appropriate.”

“That’s fine,” she said. “Take them anyway.”

She finished the room with a slow pivot: ceiling cracks, the spiderweb in the corner with one fly husk, the track of water down the window trim from an old leak, the slippers under the cot—soft, a brand with memory foam, heels pressed slightly out, one on its side. She didn’t straighten them. She photographed the shadows in the hollows of the slippers as if the shadows had evidence to give.

She shot the floor from the door to the cot, ten percent overlap, her usual grid. The camera clicked, clicked. It was the sound of doing what could be done.

Julian cleared his throat. “When he gets here,” he said, “I’m going to need you to give him your notes and then step back. There will be forms.”

“There always are,” she said. She took the last shot of the cot, straight down, centered. “I’ll give him the card, the file names, and the index so he can match numbers to views. I’ll keep a duplicate. That’s not negotiable.”

“It won’t be negotiable until it is,” he said.

She ignored that. She took out a fine-tip marker and, without placing it on any surface, wrote on a plastic flag still in her hand: anomaly: mismatched cap; sticker correction; tray stamp 06:45; Tues two puncture types. She set the flag not touching any object, the triangle pointing. She wrote a second: water bottle dent; half fill; cap on. She wrote a third: index cards present; 23:30 meds unchecked.

“Stop labeling,” Julian said.

“I’m not moving anything,” she said. “He’ll want to know what to re-shoot. This saves time.”

He looked at the flags, at the neat, controlled writing, and said nothing. Over his shoulder, lightning blue-washed the hallway, and the building took the light and didn’t object. The fern’s fronds on the reference desk trembled in the breeze off the window’s draft.

Mara checked the recorder battery and the camera’s storage. She felt the tug in her chest that would be crying later if she let it be that now, but she had learned that there were two versions of grief and one of them was process. Process kept your hands above the waterline.

She stood at the cot one last time, not photographing, not inventorying, and said, soft enough that the recorder might miss it, “I’m sorry for the cot.”

She walked back to the intake station without letting herself put her palms on the counter again. She sat, typed the image numbers into the index, wrote: Reading Room/Cot Area—Scene set complete. Flags: 12, 27, 31. Observed anomalies: bottle/cap mismatch; corrected refill sticker; blister tray Tue puncture pattern mismatches uniform; cup with two lip impressions; water bottle dent. Clock discrepancy: wall 5 min fast vs phone. Hand smears—ink right hand, residue left thumb. No movement of objects.

Julian stood at the doorway, a figure carved by the exit sign, making a point of not entering further. He had taken his shoes off and set them neatly on the bare wood outside the rug, socks gray. It made him look like a boy at a museum told not to make noise. He did not say anything else. The hallway smelled like wool and wet leaves and the metallic jaw of weather.

Mara saved the file twice. She clicked the camera off, then on again, then off, fixing the moment in a way that had meaning only for her. She slid the chain-of-custody forms forward to the corner of the desk where her knee could find them without looking. She did not put a date stamp on anything.

In the reading room, the three yellow flags shone like small, stubborn flowers in winter grass. She let them do their small work while the night kept on with its work, and footsteps elsewhere announced what the next hour would be. She wrote the time: 20:29. She boxed it: SCENE PHOTOGRAPHED. Under that, she wrote two words: anomalies flagged.


==================== SCENE 5 ====================
The foyer of the library was a long rectangle of shadow and marble where the storm’s breath came and went under the doors. The lights there were on a sensor and had clicked up to half when Mara and Julian stepped out of the reading room, washing the wood-paneled walls in a pale winery glow. The coat tree curved like a question mark above a heap of wet jackets. The scent was old paper, damp wool, lemon oil, and rain with a line of copper through it—wires humming somewhere unseen.

Julian kept his shoes in his hand like a token. He didn’t look at her. He walked to the carved table under the framed donation plaque and set his shoes parallel, toes pointed to the door, then aligned them again until the right sock just kissed the baseboard. The brass plaque read, in tiny serif letters: This foyer dedicated by the Friends of the Essex Library, 1987. The Friends had given them a clock, too—thin black hands, a solemn tick.

“Let’s do this here,” Julian said. He sounded like he was taking attendance.

Mara slid her intake notebook on the table and placed her pen atop it. The pen wasn’t special, but she set it straight, because he was setting things straight, and people mirror what they need to deflect. The recorder on her collar blinked its silent red dot at the foyer’s shadowed ceiling. She did not touch it.

He took a breath and put on a face that had been ironed earlier in his life and kept folded in a drawer for moments like this. “Effective immediately,” he said, “we are in process.”

“Define it,” Mara said.

“It means movement is restricted, communications are limited to designated channels, and no one touches anything that could be material.”

“We’re at a public library in a storm with a dead woman inside,” Mara said. “Material is the air.”

“That’s why there’s a process,” Julian said. His hands were folded behind him like he ran drills at a summer camp. The gray socks made him less of an officer and more of a cousin who handled family money. “I’m designating this foyer as the staging area. No one enters any room beyond it without clearance. Devices are to be surrendered until custodial control is established. No calls out. No texts. No photography, no recording.”

Mara glanced at the double doors back to the reading room, where her yellow flags were doing their small work. The storm fanned the doormat’s bristles. The foyer clock ticked once, loud, like it wanted attention.

“You’re late to that last part,” she said. “The room’s photographed. I’ve logged and flagged. I haven’t moved anything.”

“You’ll surrender the camera and the recorder,” Julian said.

She tapped the blank line at the end of the intake page. “Chain of custody?”

“I’ll be taking legal custody on behalf of the estate.”

“You don’t get custody by saying the word,” she said. “You get it by signing things and sealing things and following an order of operations we both already know.”

His face did not change, but his left shoulder moved a fraction, like a shrug that had been cut off at birth. “Mara.”

“I’ll surrender them,” she said, “if we seal them and sign the seal and you don’t breathe on the SD card until we can hand it to Rourke.”

“Rourke is not here.”

“The card will still be sealed when he is. You get a duplicate of the index and the file list and my notes. But I’m not handing you this like a coat check.”

“That’s not how this works.”

“It is literally how this works,” she said. She slid the roll of evidence tape out of her bag and set it next to the camera, then uncapped a Sharpie. “We can do a split: one seal with your initials across the tape as temporary custodian, one with mine as the origin. If you break it, we both log it.” She looked at his socks. “Or you can tell me you want a suppression motion to have something to do while we wait.”

He looked toward the dark glass over the doors. Somewhere in that glass lightning rolled by like a fish just under the surface. “There will be no calls,” he said, returning to his script.

“Fine,” Mara said. She opened her palm and rotated her phone so he could see the battery percentage. “I’ll power it down and leave it in your sight. But I’m keeping the recorder on until we seal it, and I’m keeping my notes on me, because you can’t hold a person’s brain in escrow.”

“That’s not—”

“Process includes human memory,” she said. “You can’t restrict that. You can ask me not to talk. I won’t talk. You can’t ask me not to think, and you can’t ask me not to write or you’re just playing.” She tapped the notebook with the capped Sharpie. “This is contemporaneous documentation. That’s not a luxury; that’s the point.”

The foyer had a runner, red with a pattern of diamonds, faded permanganate where shoes had dried their salt. He stepped off it to the wood to put himself square with her. “You will remain within sight,” he said. “Here.”

“Sight of what?” she said. “Your socks?”

“Sarcasm doesn’t help.”

“It’s better than hysteria,” she said. “Here’s what I need in order not to be unhelpful: permission to bag the cup and two bottles I already flagged. They’re in view of the door. I can reach, bag, and return to the foyer in under a minute per item. You can watch from the threshold. Then I seal the bags and add them to the table. Nothing else. You can even hold the tape.”

He didn’t answer. The clock ticked once more.

She didn’t fill the silence. She set a stack of baggies beside the tape and Sharpie. She wrote, neat block letters across the first piece of tape: Card SD-01, Reading Room, 20:29, and left a little space for his initials. She wrote the same twice more for the backup SD. She slid the camera out and thumbed the hatch to show the card’s presence without touching it, the way you show a room you’ve cleared with your hands up. Julian’s eyes flicked to the card like it might bite.

He took the tape, tore it with a delicate rip. “If we do this,” he said, “we do it properly.” He wrote JH in his lawyer’s initials, exacting, no flourish. He affixed the strip across the hatch and smoothed it with the flat of his nail.

“Thank you,” Mara said. She wrote her initials, MK, across his on the overlap. The tape lay like a neat wound.

He held out his hand for the recorder.

“It needs to be running until the bag’s sealed if you want continuity,” she said. “Otherwise we have a gap.”

He didn’t like that. He also didn’t like being the reason for a gap. “Fine,” he said.

She undid the clip, dropped the recorder into a bag, zipped the seal, wrote the line: Recorder Primary, Foyer Capture to Bag, 20:33. She held it up. He took it like he was accepting a kitten for temporary petting and then put it down very gently as if it might go off.

“You’ll power off your phone,” he said again.

She held the power button. The screen sighed to black. She slid it into an empty evidence bag and wrote: Phone Off, 20:34, no communications permitted. She initialed and turned the bag so he could initial across the seam.

“You will not leave the foyer unaccompanied,” he said.

“Then accompany me to the threshold,” she said. “One minute per item.”

“Two items,” he said.

“Three,” she said. “Cup, water bottle, two pill containers. That’s three. I’ll leave the tray.”

“The tray stays,” he said. “No one touches the tray. Or the clock. Or the flags.”

“I wasn’t going to touch the flags,” she said. “But I appreciate the thought experiment.”

He put the rolled tape into his pocket like it was a weapon he had decided to holster. “Two minutes total,” he said. “And you narrate each motion.”

“I always narrate,” she said, and didn’t look at the recorder bag to show she wasn’t doing this for it. “We’ll go now.”

They walked to the threshold. The reading room exhaled that mix of dust and ghostly book glue. The cot sat quietly in its rectangle of lamp light. The three yellow flags made a slant of color against the carpet. Mara stopped with her toes on the worn line in the rug where people had stopped for a century.

“Entering reading room threshold,” she said, low. “No footfall past the line without visual confirmation.” She raised her palms and showed him empty fingers.

“Not in a courtroom,” he said.

“No,” she said. “In a library. They’re stricter.”

She stepped over the line like a track runner hitting a mark. The second her soles touched the other side, she felt her breath change, not because the rules shifted, but because the room had that quiet that wraps your tongue. She went to the side table, pointed at the cup, kept her other hand visible. “Item one: ceramic cup, white, lipstick double impression, right rim. No condensation. Possible partial on handle.”

“Do not editorialize,” Julian said.

“Describing,” she said. “Bagging now.”

She slipped the cup into the bag, careful to touch only the outside, zipped, sealed, Sharpied: Cup, reading room side table, 20:36. She walked it back to the threshold and held it until his fingers took the weight. He put it on the foyer table like a chess piece.

“Item two,” she said, and pointed: the water bottle, dented near the middle, label with a mountain on it. “Same. Bagging.”

She bagged. She sealed. She brought it back. His initials went on the tape with the seriousness of a notary. She didn’t smile. The clock ticked again; time kept registering itself as if it needed to justify the sound.

“Item three,” he said, grudging.

“Two containers,” she said. “Pill bottle with corrected refill sticker, cap mismatch. Blister pack tray stays; I’m not touching it.” She went to the little collection on the shelf where she’d flagged them, and pointed with a pencil, not her finger. “Bottle one: amber, white cap, cap possibly swapped, label overlabel, residue at lip. Bottle two: generic pharmacy, label peeled and re-adhered. Both bagged separately.”

He didn’t talk while she did it. He watched with the attentiveness of a chaperone at a dance, all eyes and rules, and it would have been funny if Vivian wasn’t dead. She sealed the last bag, set it on the foyer table, wrote under the list in her notebook: Cup, water bottle, pill bottles—bagged, 20:38, in foyer custody (temp). Initials MK/JH. Recorder sealed 20:33, camera card sealed 20:31. Flags remain in place.

He picked up the notebook to look and then set it down exactly as it had been. “You’ll stay here,” he said. “No more reentries.”

“I was done,” she said. She touched the corner of the intake sheet and didn’t lift it. “You have my camera. You have the recorder. You have my phone. You have my attention. What you don’t have is my compliance with imaginary rules that jeopardize evidence. You want to call off what I just did, you’ll need to have that fight with yourself.”

“Mara.”

“Julian.”

They stood in the foyer with their first names like hats they held in front of them. Rain tap-danced on the window above the plaque in an unambitious rhythm. Somewhere in the stacks a dehumidifier kicked and coughed.

“All communications go through me,” he said. “If you need to speak to anyone, you speak to me, and I’ll decide whether and how that communication occurs.”

“Need to speak to anyone,” she repeated. “What about labels? What about the seals that require two names?”

“I’m here,” he said. “You have names.”

“What about the city?” she said. “You don’t get to decide if the city is told there is a deceased person on public property.”

“The city has already been told,” he said, and that was all the confirming he did. “You are not going to call Sera, you are not going to call Owen, you are not going to call Cass. You’re not going to post, you’re not going to send files to the cloud, you’re not going to anything.”

“I didn’t take you for someone who says ‘the cloud’ with a straight face.”

“You’re not going to test me,” he said, and for the first time something moved under the lawyer’s starch that might have been fatigue. “Sit. Write. If you remember anything material, tell me.”

Mara sat because sitting was still a choice. The foyer chair had been carved with a forget-me-not someone had painted too blue sometime in the nineties. She wrote: Foyer, process invoked by JH, movement restricted, comms controlled. Items secured under seal. Awaiting next steps. She wrote it in a square, then drew a thin line under it.

Julian stood over the table with the bagged objects like he could will them into a different category. He took out his phone, glanced, put it back. He chewed the inside of his cheek and then did not.

“Do you want me to read you my notes?” Mara asked. “We can do story time. I do voices.”

“You can spare me voices,” he said.

“Then let me make you a list,” she said. “Not of suspects, not of theories—of logistics. We will need: ice packs for the HVAC if power goes, a dry space to stage, and someone to sit with the door. We will need trash bags that are not green. We will need a second roll of evidence tape because you’ll pretend you haven’t used any and you will be out.”

“I’ll get someone for the door,” he said. He didn’t argue about the tape. “This is a library.”

“It is. That means people want to enter because they think it belongs to them,” she said. “They’re not wrong. Your job is to tell them it’s closed. My job is to describe it so that when you open it again, it still has the same shape.”

He blew out a breath he might have meant to keep hidden. “You always have a line,” he said.

“You always like to pretend you don’t,” she said.

He went to the door, checked the bolt, slid the chain into the track, then checked it again. “One of the trustees is on their way.”

Mara nodded and didn’t ask which. She wrote: Door chained by JH, 20:42. She capped the pen.

“Do you want water?” he asked, and it was ordinary in a way that made the foyer feel more like a hallway and less like a gate.

“Not from the reading room,” she said.

“From the staff sink,” he said. “I can get it.”

“Glass cups back there are all contaminated by being near, so paper,” she said. “And two, please. In case my hands decide to shake later.”

He looked at her hands as if that might be visible in the metacarpals, then nodded and went toward the staff door, unlocked it with a key he must have had in his sock, and left it open in a narrow, polite angle so she could see the slice of light like a painter’s sliver. He returned with two paper cones nested and a third for himself that he did not drink.

“Thank you,” she said. She set one cone beside the notebook, one between the tape and the phone bag, like placeholders.

He didn’t sit. He didn’t quite stand still, either, his weight shifting like a metronome listening to a faster song. “You understand why I had to do this,” he said.

“I understand that you did it,” she said. “Understanding motive is for later.”

“Everything is later with you,” he said. He rubbed the bridge of his nose. “Stay in the foyer.”

“I’ll stay in the foyer,” she said.

“You’ll wait,” he said.

“I’m waiting,” she said.

He nodded like they had agreed to something more binding than the obvious. He folded his arms and looked at the bagged cup and bottles and then past them to the reading room through the crack in the doors, where the flags stood at attention and didn’t waver. The clock ticked again, and again, and the building made its old, expansive sound of settling into weather, and they held the line they each had drawn because for the next hour that was what there was to do.


==================== SCENE 6 ====================
The librarian’s office was smaller than Mara remembered from earlier in the year, or maybe the storm pressing on the windowpane made every room feel like a chest someone had laced tight. The desk had a half-moon bite taken out of its veneer where a blotter had once been; there were penciled call numbers on the wall calendar and a rubber plant drooping like a tired green hand. Julian stood in the doorway, one foot inside, one foot out, so his body could be counted as both witness and barricade.

He reached up and pulled the chain for the overhead. The light hummed, then settled into itself. “All right,” he said. “No one enters except us. You narrate. I hold the phone.”

“Your phone,” she said.

“Mine,” he said, already opening the camera app. “Timestamp visible. Save to the shared drive when I’m back at my desk.”

“Announce the time,” she said.

He did, aloud, adding the date and his initials like the words themselves were a stamped seal. She unfolded her notebook and wrote the same, pen hooked in two fingers so the nib would find the next line fast.

The safe had been built into an alcove behind the file cabinets, a rectangular mouth with its lips of painted trim. Someone had tacked up a postcard of a Tintoretto beside it, the paint flaking from the edges of the cartouche like dandruff. A box fan sat dead near the baseboard with a sooty thumbprint on the switch—old from summer.

“Nora keeps a desk key set,” Mara said, “and a second for lockouts.” She placed the zippered bank pouch next to the safe, unzipped it, and poured the ring into her palm. Each key had a tag in Nora’s narrow writing—TELE RM, MAIL BX, EQ CLOSE, ADMX—then the larger brass cut Nora had labeled just: BIG.

“You read the tags into the recording,” Julian said, his voice pitched not to carry beyond the threshold. He angled the phone toward her hands. “Hold still.”
