Mara read them off. She hesitated with the large one, felt the old habit to test it against the lock, then put it on the blotter instead and photographed it face up, face down, with a ruler from the desk for scale. “No prints on the tags that I can see,” she said.

“You see prints with your eyes now?” Julian said. It wasn’t sarcasm. It was the kind of question he’d learned to put between them like a folded towel.

“With my eyes, I don’t smear anything,” she said.

He nodded. “Proceed.”

The safe’s dial had hairline numbers that forced you to lean in to read. She didn’t touch it yet. “Exterior appears intact,” she narrated. “No scrape marks, no fresh paint over the hinges. Dust pattern consistent.” She angled her notebook under the lip and snapped three pictures so the phone would capture the same angle. She slid a business card along the seam to test for drag. “Seals look fine.”

“The combination,” Julian said, and opened his palm like he expected it to float there.

Mara opened the sealed envelope labeled EMERGENCY— ADMIN SAFE that Nora had given her before dinner and held it flat so the number sequences could be filmed before she looked. “Witnessed by JH,” she said. She cut it with her penknife so cleanly it could have been resealed, then didn’t. The numbers were in Nora’s hand, the tilt leftward.

She read them aloud, twice, once as numbers, once as the sequence of left-right turns. Julian repeated them back to her exactly, and she corrected a single “thirty-five” that he’d turned into “thirty-six,” because that was the kind of correction that would keep later arguments small.

Her ear to the metal, fingers on the dial, she felt the bite at the gates. The clicks were quiet and assured. Third turn, the dial stuck slightly. She stopped, took a picture of her hand placement, then continued. The handle yielded with a sound like a shoulder freeing itself from a shirt too tight across the chest. She didn’t swing it wide immediately.

“Open slowly,” Julian said, like she might forget.

“I want the dust fall,” Mara said. She made the first inch of space count, angling the phone flashlight in so the camera saw the drift and the way it didn’t. “No negative shadow. That means it’s been opened in the last week,” she said for the record.

“By whom?” Julian asked, automatic, procedural.

“You,” she said, because he had told her he’d checked the contents after the summer fundraiser. He didn’t argue. He didn’t agree into the recording, either. That was his own procedure.

She swung it open. Inside, the top shelf held two red string envelopes with the library’s crest, a cigar tin, a hardbound ledger with the spine broken neatly at the six-month increments, and a shallow archival box with a cotton tie. The bottom shelf held a lockbox with a keyed tumbler and a rolled map bound with blue tape printed CATALOG SURVEY.

Julian shifted his weight, his shadow cutting across the lower hinge. “Do not remove,” he said, as if the safe’s depth were a ledge and her knees might bend by instinct.

“Agreed,” she said. “We document in situ.”

He stepped closer to film while she called out each item. Her voice stayed even; his breath was the only thing that moved on the audio. She measured the distance from the ledger’s edge to the safe wall with the ruler, photographed the dust crescents around the tin. One of the paper envelopes had heat rippling the surface where the varnish had tightened and cooled again sometime long before. She leaned in until the camera could see the ridges.

“Are you smelling it?” Julian asked.

“Old varnish has a smell,” she said.

He didn’t say anything about it. He kept filming. The phone’s screen reflected in the glossy strip of the ledger’s cover, a tiny looping repeat of their movements. The cigar tin was standard grocery-store cheap from the sixties, with a corner dent. She didn’t touch it. She touched the air above it and stopped to take two more shots.

The lockbox at the bottom had a tag taped to it: DEPOSIT—ARCHIVE. The handwriting tilted differently. “Not Nora’s hand,” Mara said.

“Read it to camera again,” Julian said.

She did. The rolled survey’s tape had a thumbprint pressed into it, a crescent of grit along the edge. She put the ruler next to it without contacting the paper and zoomed in until the phone processed the grain.

“Is this thoroughness or indulgence?” Julian asked. He kept the question plain.

“If I don’t catalog the state now, the state changes under our feet,” she said.

“Cataloging the state is not your job,” he said.

“Then fire me later,” she said. It came out mild because it was true in a way that wasn’t meant as a fight.

He took in a breath and let it go. “No,” he said. “No, I won’t.”

The top shelf’s archival box drew both their attention the way a wrapped present calls a hand. Mara checked the cotton tie. It had been tied with a left-hand preference, the bow looped backward. She filmed her finger pointing without making contact. “Left-handed,” she said.

“You’re narrating assumptions,” Julian said, but she saw the small acknowledgment in the tilt of his head. “State the object.”

“Shallow archival box, board construction, off-white, cotton tie, unlabelled.” She paused. “I want to open it.”

“Not without the trustee,” he said.

“You chained the front door,” she said. “We have limited time while everyone’s nerves are still aligned.”

His mouth quirked. “Is that a measurable condition?”

“You can measure anything if you commit to the lie of the instrument,” she said.

“Mara.”

She looked at the cotton tie again. “I’ll open it inside the safe without moving it. You’ll film. No removal. We refold the tie exactly as found.”

He thought about it, eyes hooding in the way he had when he counted against himself. “No leaning your weight on the shelf,” he said.

She pulled the chair over and knelt instead, so her center was below the shelf line and nothing of her forearm would shift the dust. She took a still—top of the knot—then brought the lens down and looped the tie open with the dull end of a pencil. The bow loosened with a dry whisper.

She lifted the lid just enough for the camera to see the contents. Inside were paper shapes nested like fragile bones: folded documents, one with a singed, crimped edge, flakes of black like fish food clinging to the board. The top page had been folded once, then refolded smaller, the crease uneven from being done after the fact. The first line was a formal preamble in a typist’s font; the bottom edge of that page was missing, a cut with the jagged imprecision of someone using a letter opener out of patience.

Julian inhaled. It wasn’t surprise; it was the air one takes when a thing demands more air.

“Do not touch,” he said.

“I’m not,” she said. She released even the pencil back to the desk so the video would catch her open hands. “Photographing corners. Top right: tear pattern acute. Bottom left: intact.”

She took the shots: the char bloom on the edge, the curve where ink thinned against heat, the way the paper fibers flashed under the LED in a fine, white pilling. She filmed the thickness of the stack, measuring it against the pencil like a schoolchild’s trick. She noted each heading visible without disturbing the order, each watermark in the paper where the light found it: HADDAM & Co., 1987, curled faintly like a thread.

“Content?” Julian asked. He kept his voice flat. He did not lean.

“Later,” she said. She lifted her eyes to him so that the phone recorded the exchange. “We don’t read aloud. We record condition. We seal. We tag. We log.”

He nodded; the slow kind. “Agreed.”

She closed the lid over the box and retied the cotton bow the way she’d found it—left loop crossing above—and photographed the knot when she was done so that later it would be clear she respected the original hand. She shot the safe again in full, then panned slowly from shelf to shelf.

“You’ll send me the files within the hour,” he said. “And the stills.”

“Yes,” she said. She sealed the EMERGENCY—ADMIN SAFE envelope inside a new clear bag and labeled it with the time and his initials and hers, writing through the plastic carefully so the ink didn’t bead. She labeled a second bag for the archival box—empty for now—and wrote: Location identified, contents not yet removed. She placed the blank bag on the desk next to the phone and the ruler so it would make it into the room-wide shot.

“We call Rourke,” Julian said.

“We text him first,” she said. “He won’t pick up while he’s talking to the trustee in your foyer.”

“You don’t know which trustee,” he said.

“I know how they talk when they think closed doors are walls,” she said. She tapped the edge of her notebook twice, and the sound of the cardboard on wood made a neat period.

He came further into the room, enough that his shoulder touched the bookcase. He didn’t touch anything else. “You don’t take anything out,” he said.

“I don’t,” she said. “I’ve seen what I needed to see to keep it from shifting. That’s all for now.”

He nodded. He held the phone on the safe, on the face of the door, as if its plain gray paint held the narrative whole. “State for the video,” he said. “State the outcome.”

Mara looked at the safe, then at him so her words entered his lens as well as the room. “The library safe has been opened using Nora C.’s key and combination,” she said. “Video and photo documentation collected. Contents recorded without removal. Condition preserved.”

“—under my supervision,” he said, shaping the syllables without adding weight.

“—under Julian Hale’s supervision,” she said.

He stopped the recording, checked the video, hit play, listened for the clean catch of their voices and the absence of scuff. He watched the first ten seconds, then the last ten, then did it again. He nodded when he was satisfied and saved the file to a folder he named exactly—DAY1_SAFE— with the time nested in the title so nothing could be confused later.

The room held itself a beat, orderly as a shelf full of spines. Outside the office, a distant door hinge sang its small complaint against the frame. The rain went about its work against the glass. Mara labeled the page in her notebook and capped her pen.

“All right,” Julian said. He stood up away from the bookcase as if it had asked nicely. “We’re finished here for now.”

“For now,” she said, echo neutral.

They left the office together, and he closed the door without locking it, then turned the key anyway because turning the key was part of how he breathed. She watched the way his hand had learned the gesture and wrote: Door relocked by JH, 21:13. Then she put the notebook away and pulled out her phone to send the text to Rourke with the attached photographs, the subject line as unadorned as the safe itself: SAFE OPENED. DOCUMENTED. READY.


==================== SCENE 7 ====================

The rain had thinned to a fine hiss by the time Mara and Julian came back. The house held the hour like a held breath; the library’s lamps cast clean pools on the green leather, and the office beyond waited with its square of safe in the corner like a blank lifted from a floor plan.

Rourke was already there, his coat hung on the back of the chair, a plastic case open on the desk with blue seals and numbered tags fitted in their compartments. He had taken over the lamp nearest the safe and angled the shade to keep glare off metal. He looked up only long enough to count them with his eyes—one, two—and then tapped his clipboard. “Time in,” he said. “Twenty-one fifty-eight.” His pen clicked. “State who’s in the room.”

“Mara Levin,” Mara said, and she set her backpack on the blotter with the same gentle care she used for a good book. “Consultant.”

“Julian Hale,” Julian said. “Legal counsel.” He shot a brief look at the safe, as if it could be persuaded to revert to merely decorative storage, then at Rourke’s case. “What changed?”

“Command decided we’re bagging the document tonight,” Rourke said. He kept his voice even and empty of discussion. “Storm risk. Power irregularities.” He lifted a small, vacuum-sealed packet. “And because singed paper doesn’t like humidity.”

“I said as much earlier,” Mara said, pulling on nitrile gloves. “We’re at dew point inside this house.”

Julian put a hand against the bookcase as if it were a person he could steady. “Under my supervision,” he said.

“Under mine,” Rourke said. “With yours recorded.” He extended the pen to Julian, and Julian signed the chain-of-custody line with the crisp flourish of a man who did not send emails without subject lines.

Mara unzipped her backpack and laid out her kit: a cutting mat with a grid, a Mylar envelope, a sandwich of acid-free boards, soft brushes in two sizes, a microspatula, a pair of thin magnifying lenses that clipped to her glasses, a cloth tape measure, a small photo scale. She set the camera on its articulated arm and adjusted the gooseneck LED so the light could be moved without heat. She spoke as she worked, steady dictation to the camera’s tiny red eye.

“Day one,” she said. “Twenty-one fifty-nine. Office inside library. Safe already opened, documented, no removal in prior session. Returning to extract fragile fragment for preservation and evidence bagging under Sergeant Rourke’s custody.”

Rourke nodded at the safe. “Proceed.”

Mara tested the edge of the door with her thumb—the paint cool as a damp stone—and swung it open. Inside, the shelves looked as they had: the ledger, the tin box, the paper‑wrapped parcel. The scorched corner of the upper shelf still held that thin dusting of black like a smear of soot on a dented pie plate. She had watched this space earlier without touching it; now she let herself breathe closer.

“Document appears torn,” she said. “Heat exposure and charring at margins. Ash flake present on upper surface. No airflow.”

Julian set his phone on its stand and hit record. “State who has hands on,” he said, because systems soothed him and it cost nothing to indulge.

“Mara only,” Rourke said. “For now.”

She took the softest brush and coaxed visible ash into a folded evidence paper, coaxed, not swept; you didn’t encourage the document to leave itself behind. Ash lifted like a shadow refusing to take part in the shape. She slid the photo scale beside the fragment and shot four angles, then a detail on the charred edge where letters still clung.

“Do you see a seal?” Julian asked, standing just behind the line taped on the floor that Rourke had put down as a courtesy to everyone's blood pressure.

“Not yet,” Mara said. “Text at top suggests a title. The upper right corner is gone.” She leaned in with the magnifiers. “Looks like—‘Last Will—’ then—‘—and Testament.’” The words were crisp where the flame had missed them, the old typewriter ink dark and unwavering.

Julian exhaled like a man who’d been holding a heavy suitcase below the elbow. “So we’re not guessing.”

“We’re documenting,” Rourke said.

Mara slid the microspatula under the largest piece, felt how it gave and how it didn’t. Heat had stiffened the paper, then humidity had relaxed it again; it moved like a leaf that wanted to remember being green. The tear line looked like a coastline on a map. She noted that, then kept her own breath shallow.

“Support board,” she said. Rourke passed her the first cut of acid‑free card without touching her glove. She eased the fragment onto it, letting gravity do most of the carry. Small particles whispered down. She paused, counted five, let them settle.

“Hold.” Rourke’s voice didn’t rise, but it was the kind of word you put a hand under. He bent closer. “What’s that on the underside?”

Mara glanced. A fleck of wax or a varnish-like bead sat in the middle of the soot like a dropped pearl. She took another photo. “Drip of sealing wax, possibly,” she said. “Color difficult to determine under soot and charring.”

“Photograph taken,” Julian said, a bit stiff because the air in here rewarded stiffness.

Mara lifted the Mylar sleeve—polyester film, archivally inert, the sort of material you could trust with old breath. She opened its mouth with the microspatula and slid the board and the document in together like a patient onto a stretcher. There was a crackle, delicate and hay-dry. She stopped, let the sound finish itself, then continued.

“Fragment A,” she said, as the sleeve swallowed the last of the torn paper. “Dimensions to be recorded post‑stabilization.”

“You have another piece,” Julian said, peering into the safe. He was right: a triangle of paper had lodged in the shadow where metal met back panel. The tip had gone glossy from heat.

Mara nodded. “Fragment B.” She moved the light gently until the shadow bled out. With the magnifier down, she could see the faint ghost of a signature stroke—pressure marks without ink, as if someone had pressed too hard with a ballpoint and left the groove. She photographed the groove as if it were text, because sometimes evidence was more like music notation—implied vibration rather than sound.

“Going to use the tape,” she said.

Rourke lifted an eyebrow. “Adhesive?”

“Not on the document,” she said. “The painter’s tape on the spatula to give it grip. No transfer.” He made a small approving sound that registered as loud as applause in the room.

She wrapped the dull side of the microspatula with a cuff of low‑tack tape, touched the very edge of the triangle, and felt it accept the idea of moving. She brought it to the second board, paused as a hairline crack appeared, then set it down and let the crack be. Mylar again; breath again shallow.

“Two fragments,” Julian said, his voice more even now, as if having numbers steadied him like having a railing on a stepladder.

“Two fragments,” Mara echoed. She pulled a small, squat bottle from her kit. “Silica gel sachet,” she said. “To regulate humidity in the temporary sleeve.”

“Log the addition,” Rourke said.

“Logged,” Mara said, and she made the note with her pen on the form taped to the board: FRAG A + SILICA, FRAG B + SILICA, TIME 22:07. She dated it, initialed it, and then took the next set of images, the sleeves themselves on the mat with the scale, the labels visible and in frame.

Julian shifted his weight. “Can we confirm it’s a will beyond the title at some point?” he asked, words careful, hands anchored in polite sight.

“At some point,” Rourke said. “Not in the safe.” He looked at Mara. “Anything else in there?”

“Charred dust and a paper clip that’s gone soft,” she said. “No other attached fragments.” She glanced at the underside of the shelf, felt for heat warp, saw none. “We should inspect the tin box next session, but not tonight. This is all the document will tolerate.”

He nodded. “Chain-of-custody tag numbers?”

Mara held up the first blue tag. “Nine-eight-seven-two-three-A for Fragment A. Nine-eight-seven-two-three-B for Fragment B.” She threaded the cable ties through the Mylar sleeves’ punched holes and clicked them tight, then smoothed the numbered stickers onto the edges so the digits read left to right in certain light and not in others—an old habit of hers, for the camera’s sake as much as her own.

Rourke filled the lines with his slow block letters. “Transferred to Sergeant Rourke, 22:10,” he said as he wrote it. He put the clipboard down and opened the hard plastic evidence bag with both hands, not touching the inside. “You’re ready?”

“Almost,” Mara said. She took one last photo of the safe interior with the fragments gone, because absence was part of the condition now. She stepped back to center herself in the frame for the video. “Statement: removed two paper fragments consistent with a formal document titled ‘Last Will and Testament’ from library safe. Both fragments are heat‑affected, edges torn, soot present. No other contents disturbed.”

Julian lifted his chin. “Under my supervision,” he said.

“Under Hale’s and mine,” Rourke said, with the dry ghost of a smile for the semantics. He held the bag open, and Mara slid the sleeves inside. The bag swallowed the careful rectangle of Mylar and board with the sound of clean plastic. Rourke ran his fingers along the seal, making sure the red lines met red lines, no fold, no bubble. He pressed the tamper strip until it clouded white under his thumb.

Mara peeled off the duplicate barcodes and stuck one in her notebook. She wrote the metadata out like a ritual: location, date, time, personnel present, document condition, environmental notes, camera IDs, light temperature, lens used, tags, seals, chain-of-custody reference. She printed her letters, steady as if she’d measured the strokes.

“Anything else you need on video?” she asked.

“State the storage instructions,” Rourke said.

“Evidence bag to be housed flat, no weight on top,” she said. “No exposure to high heat sources, no direct light. Recommend temporary placement in climate-stable cabinet pending lab transfer.”

“Good,” Rourke said. He lifted the bag and looked at it the way one looks at a filled canteen in a dry place. He slid it into his larger case and engaged the locks—a calm, symmetrical sound.

Julian edged a little closer to the safe, and then stopped himself at the tape line. He looked at the empty shelf, now just a clean, vulnerable plane. “What about the ash?” he asked, voice low, as if he was asking after a person who had left the room.

“In the folded evidence packet,” Mara said, and she showed him the small white rectangle with its own tag. “It’s part of the story. Ash preserves shape in the negative.”

He nodded, a small concession to the idea that even loss had to be accounted for. “All right,” he said. He smoothed his tie with a thumb. “You’ll send me the metadata copy.”

“I’ll send the PDF,” she said. “Filename includes tag numbers.”

Rourke closed his case and hefted it. The house gave a tiny groan, normal wood’s work, and the rain ticked a little louder as if remembering itself.

“Let’s wrap this room,” he said.

Mara switched off the gooseneck LED, let the darkness warm the space back to itself, then turned the office lamp half a notch down. She pulled up the checklist on her phone and read down the points.

“Safe closed, but not locked,” she said.

Julian stepped forward and turned the dial to neutral, then the key because the key was part of how he breathed. He spoke into his own phone, logging his piece. “Door relocked by JH, 22:18,” he said, and he let himself be exact.

Mara glanced at the edge of the desk, where soot had made a faint moon crescent next to her cutting mat. She took a dry microfiber cloth from her bag, hesitated, then looked at Rourke.

“Leave it,” he said. “We’ll do the surface swabs in the morning. No cross‑contamination tonight.”

She nodded and returned the cloth to the bag. “Metadata sent,” she said as her phone pinged its confirmation. “Rourke cc’d. PDF symbols legible.”

“Received,” Rourke said without checking—he trusted the habit more than the signal. He pocketed his pen. “Good work.”

Julian said nothing for a breath, then: “Good,” as if the word had been in quarantine and released with a clean bill.

They stepped out into the library together. The air out there smelled like pasted paper and linen binding. The lamps made coins of light on the spines. They paused while Mara slid the tape line into her pack. She looked back once at the door to the office to make sure it was what it was: closed, ordinary, unremarkable.

“Next session,” Rourke said, already halfway to the hall.

“Schedule pending weather,” Mara said, and she put her hand flat against her notebook to feel the idea of the fragments traveling ahead of them, safe in plastic, tagged, signed, and lodged in the system that turned objects into information and kept them from dissolving into afterthought. Then she put the notebook away, and they went down the corridor, their footsteps measuring the night in pairs.


==================== SCENE 8 ====================

The foyer desk had been repurposed with ruthless neatness. The silver bowl that usually caught keys now held nitrile gloves. A brass letter opener sat blade-taped and retired beside a stack of tamper strips. Someone—Mara—had turned the old hall table into a flat, clean island: blotter covered in disposable kraft paper, a handheld barcode scanner asleep like a small animal, the label printer warming with a periodic tick, and three clear bins nested at her feet. The longcase clock across the entry murmured its seconds. Rain nudged the windows and hissed under the door with a seam of cold.

Rourke came up from the corridor, case under his arm. He set it on the desk without ceremony and popped the latches. “All right,” he said. The word was permission, not indulgence. “Let’s put a spine in it.”

Mara already had the fragments staged on a clean sheet inside a shallow tray. The biggest piece showed a section of the signature block, the singed edge like a coastline. She had numbered the fragments with tiny dots of painter’s tape, each dot annotated with pencil on her grid card.

“Visuals complete?” Rourke asked, pulling out a stack of pre-printed chain-of-custody forms and a carbon book of property receipts.

“Library office cameras, stills on my body cam, and the phone overhead,” she said. “Each fragment photographed recto/verso with scale. Metadata sent. You’re cc’d.”

He slid a form to the blotter and wrote in capital letters, pen angled sharp. Case number. Location of collection. “Source,” he said, glancing up.

“Subject: Library safe, lower compartment, recovered under key provided by Nora Greene,” Mara said, not looking away from the tray. She’d put on fresh gloves and braced her wrists on the desk edge. “Condition: torn, heat exposure, singe marks, particulate soot. Quantity: twelve fragments.”

Rourke wrote as she spoke. He checked his watch. “Time recovered?”

“Twenty-one fifty-nine to twenty-two thirteen,” she said. “I have the interval broken out by fragment sequence if you care.”

“I care in court,” he said, and put the range. “Collector: Quinn,” he added, then on the next line, “Witness: Rourke.”

He pulled a labeled evidence bag from his case—heavy Mylar with a white write-on panel and a red zip channel. He set it by her hand. The bag already had a barcode. He peeled a matching label from the sheet and stuck it to the chain-of-custody form so the numbers lined up. “This bag is yours to pack,” he said. “Custody comes to me at seal.”

Mara nodded. “Clarity,” she said. “We like clarity.”

He cocked an eyebrow, reading the subtext without inviting it. “You’re contracted to the board. I’m sworn to the county. The paper doesn’t care who pays the light bill.”

“Julian cares who can walk into his house with a warrant,” she said. She picked up the tray and tipped the fragments into the bag along the crease she’d made with her knuckle, guided them with a sterile spatula so nothing caught the seam. “And he cares whose name is on the log when he files for injunctive relief.”

Rourke shrugged, not unsympathetic, and slid the property receipt nearer to her. “He can care. The coroner’s phone is in my pocket. If this is a will, it intersects probate. If it’s burned, it intersects tampering. That’s us.” He nodded at the bag. “Flatten the air out.”

She did, easing the sheet so the fragments didn’t ride on edge. The bag made a faint sound as the ribs of the channel closed under her thumb. She looked at him. “You ready?”

“Seal when you’re ready,” he said. He pulled the tamper strip halfway from its liner and held it like a small flag.

Mara pressed the red zip, top to bottom, then took the strip and laid it across the zipper track so the VOID pattern would register if anyone tried to peel. She pressed with the heel of her hand. “Initials across the seal,” she said, and scrawled MQ with the date and time. She handed the pen to Rourke. He added his initials across the opposite angle so the lines crossed.

He set the bag on the small postal scale. “Weight,” he said, calling the number aloud. “One hundred forty-two grams, bag included.” He wrote it on the form, then made a quick note: “Contents: paper fragments consistent w/ testamentary document; heat/smoke odor present.”

The label printer ticked and fed a narrow strip. Mara caught it and stuck it to the back of the bag, low right, so the ad hoc inventory number she’d assigned in the library stayed married to the official barcode. “Cross-indexed,” she said.

Rourke took his phone out, snapped the bag face and back, zoomed on the seal, then on both sets of initials. He held the screen for her to see. “Good?” he asked.

“Legible,” she said. Her voice stayed even. The rhythm of it calmed the skin between her shoulder blades.

He fitted the bag into a hard-sided evidence box with foam rails. The box was pre-numbered. He peeled a duplicate barcode and bridged the break between lid and body with a tamper sticker, then wrote across it: Case 23-1172, Item 01. “All right,” he said again, more to the process than to her. “Transfer.”

He turned the chain-of-custody form so it faced her and pointed with the pen. “From,” he said.

“From: Mara Quinn,” she read as she wrote, “collector, independent investigator, employed by Essex Island Preservation Board. To: Detective Peter Rourke, county sheriff’s office. Date and time.” She checked the longcase clock and then her phone to reconcile the minute. “Twenty-two twenty-three.” She signed. The carbon paper sucked up the ink and made a ghost of it on the sheets beneath.

Rourke signed under her name. He printed his ID number. He pressed down hard enough to give the copy a bruise. He tore off the white original and slid it into a clear sleeve taped inside the box lid. He left the yellow attached to his book and handed her the pink.

“Receipt for your records,” he said.

She folded the pink copy once and tucked it into the back of her notebook where the other receipts lived like a small wallet. “I want to be on the next logistics email,” she said. “When it hits property, when it goes to the lab, when it’s released. I don’t need to touch it; I need to see the timestamps.”

“You’ll get the entries,” he said. He wasn’t being generous. He was being precise.

Her eyes went to the will through the bag, the faint curve of the singe, the printed words that meant everything and nothing until a judge said which. “One more label,” she said. She printed a caution and stuck it to the box beside the barcode: FRAGILE—DO NOT FLEX. “And I want it standing, not flat.”

“Standing,” he said, and rotated the box so the corrugations ran vertical in the satchel. He slid the box into his padded carrier, then click-clacked the latches. The carrier looked like it might survive a fall off a truck. The rain made a point of itself again against the transom.

Mara pulled off her gloves, turned them inside out, and dropped them into the burn bag. She wiped the blotter paper with a fresh disinfectant wipe and peeled the paper up, rolling it on itself so the dust and char flecks stayed contained. She taped the roll closed and sharpied it as waste from the evidence station.

“Jurisdiction, clarity,” she said, not quite a joke this time. “To be explicit: who’s running point on this item once it crosses your threshold? Property sergeant or you direct?”

“Property logs it,” Rourke said. “I check it in. It stays in the cold room until the lab courier. If probate asks, they go through me. If Julian’s counsel asks, they go through the county attorney. You can look at it again in the lab clean room by appointment with me present. No copies, no extra photographs, nothing unbagged without a lab tech and a bench protocol. That’s not a shot at you. That’s how we don’t get beaten to death later.”

“I’m not asking to freehand,” she said. “I’m asking not to be told after the fact that some deputy opened it with a pocketknife.”

He gave a short snort that conceded history. “I’ll walk it in myself,” he said.

They both went quiet for a moment. The clock gave them three seconds. The foyer smelled faintly of wool and rain and furniture polish, and beneath that the academic odor of singed paper, as if a lecture had ended badly.

“Anything else here?” Rourke asked, closing his case.

“Just your signature on the site log,” she said, and flipped a page on her clipboard to the column that recorded who had crossed the line of blue tape and when. She handed him the pen.

He wrote his name, arrival, departure tick marks. “Foyer desk, turned evidence station,” he read aloud as he signed the location line. “This is a good idea.”

“It’s a flat surface on the way to the door,” she said. “It keeps people from improvising.” She looked at the hallway and the dark cut of the front door. “And it’s visible.”

“Visible’s good,” he said. “Visible makes it hard to invent alternate realities.”

She nodded. “That’s why I tape the pens to the clipboard,” she said, to break the last of the tension, and he glanced down to confirm that she had, in fact, taped the pen so it wouldn’t walk. He smiled with only one side of his mouth and signed the last line.

He slid the carrier strap across his shoulder and took the weight of the box against his hip. “Call if you find more paper,” he said. “Or anything that looks like it was meant to be missing.”

“Call if you need a clean table,” she said. “This one’s open until midnight.”

He inclined his head. “We’re good.” He lifted the carrier a fraction in a gesture that said: this, specifically, is now my problem. He tucked the chain-of-custody forms back into his case and latched it. “I’ll text the property log number once it’s in.”

“Text and email,” she said. “Pink copy says you owe me a line.”

“I owe you a line,” he said, not begrudging, and set his hand on the case top like a seal. “We’re done.”

Mara stood where she was and watched as he turned toward the door, the carrier snug, the case balanced, every part of it made official by paper and signatures and the boring, beautiful spine of procedure. The rain took him when he opened the door. The foyer drew a breath of cold and closed again.

She checked the desk: no loose tags, no wandering pens, no open bags. She folded the blue tape back on itself at the edge of the rug to make a neat tab and wrote one more line on her own log—Evidence sealed, logged, transferred under Rourke custody; receipts in file—then capped her pen and clicked off the label printer. The clock marked another minute and found nothing out of place.


==================== SCENE 9 ====================

The library basement woke like a throat clearing—pipes ticking, a faint burble behind walls, the thin breath of the air handler warming itself. Mara held the flashlight under her chin to leave both hands free and eased the access hatch open with a flat bar. Cold dust lifted, smelling like old paperbacks and coins. Beyond the panel, the maintenance passage ran the length of the library’s foundation: a shallow, rectangular crawl with stone on one side and studded wall on the other, perforated by a line of porcelain standoffs where the old call-bell wire rode like a taught clothesline.

She clicked on her headlamp. The beam flattened everything: pipe clamps, the chalky runs of efflorescence, lint that had knitted into gauze over a century. Her breath fogged and then vanished as the room remembered her again.

She crouched and slid one knee in, guarding her clipboard. On the first page she’d taped a half-sheet of graph paper, gridded two feet to a square, sketched the library in quick blocks, and labeled the wall cavities by the old carpenter’s pencil lines she’d spotted yesterday. She ran her finger along the wire. Cloth insulation, black-and-tan, brittle if you pinched too hard. Someone had wiped a finger along its dust; the smudge mark was recent, the oil still dark. She noted it.

“Tell me you didn’t go in there yet.”

She turned. Owen filled the bottom of the stairs, a dark silhouette against the slice of light. He had a compact ladder balanced on his shoulder and a contractor’s pail dangling from his fingertips. He set both down and ducked through the low lintel, moving carefully, not to knock his head on the fire main.

“It’s not for people,” he said. “That’s the idea of it. Passage for wires and excuses, not bodies.”

“I’m not a person,” she said. “I’m a clipboard.”

He took in the clip and the notches of blue painter’s tape she’d already stuck to the wall at shoulder height with arrows pointing at every penetration. He unsnapped the pail lid and revealed a nest of tools: non-contact tester, multimeter, tone generator, a stubby flathead, nut drivers, a tiny folding hex set, chalk, zip ties, vinyl labels.

“You raided the cart,” she said.

“You said early,” he said, passing her the tester. “It’s early.”

She waved the tester’s beak near the wire. The LED stayed dark. The call-bell system ran on low voltage; the non-contact wouldn’t pick it up. She dug out the toner and clipped the red alligator to the cloth-wrapped conductor, the black to a nearby pipe for ground, and tapped the tone to steady beeping. The crawlspace took the sound and absorbed it, as if listening too.

“Hold the ladder,” she said.

“No,” he said. “I hold the ladder somewhere sane.”

She angled her head toward the hatch. “The sane part is upstairs, three rooms away from here. The wire leaves the bay at the study column. We’ll miss the first junction if I don’t start here.”

He looked at the gap. “You’ll get stuck.”

She glanced down the cleared path. “I’m smaller than failure.”

“That better not be a slogan,” he said, but he crouched and used the toe of his boot to nudge aside a stack of roofing shingles leaned against the wall—leftovers from some previous patch—making her a bit more room to slide.

She worked one shoulder in, then the other, and inched forward on forearms. The studs ran at odd intervals, old house logic, and the wire wove between them, the porcelain cleats shining like teeth. Every three feet, she stopped and labeled, “CB-1, CB-2,” with the vinyl gun and stuck the tags to the cloth insulation with bands of painter’s tape so she wouldn’t abrade the original.

“Talk,” Owen said behind her, voice patient. “So I know you didn’t go quiet because you took a nail to the chest.”

“CB-3,” she said. “No splices. Porcelain standoff; rusted screw but stable. Wire’s intact.”

“Copy.”

Her shoulders brushed both walls. Sweat ran along her spine despite the cold. She angled her light and found the first junction where she expected—small wooden block, shellacked, with two screws and short pigtails bridging. Beyond it, the wire should have turned right to the kitchen annunciator board line, if the house obeyed its own drawings.

It didn’t.

She held still. The wire did something inelegant: it climbed a new galvanized staple, bent up into a metal junction box that had no business in a run this old, then split: one branch traveling on the original cloth toward the kitchen; one wrapped in gray low-voltage jacket that disappeared toward the library wall.

She put the tone wand to the cloth. The beep was present and weak, as expected. On the gray jacket, the tone was brighter, cleaner, as if the conductor were newer copper with less loss.

“Owen,” she said. “Unexpected box. Four-by-four, not bonded. Newer cable off it.”

“Color?”

“Gray. Two-conductor. Jacket looks five to ten years old, not last century.”

He knelt at the hatch, and his arm came in up to the elbow, handing her a stubby driver. “Can you crack the cover?”

She considered the angle. The box was anchored on the stud with two lag screws and the cover held with a single rusted flat. “If I pivot, yes. If I pivot, I wedge.”

“I can pull you back by your ankles,” he said, not kidding.

“I hate being a wheelbarrow,” she said.

“Safety hates urgency,” he said.

“Urgency hates lost signal,” she said, and shifted her ribs against the studs until she could wedge the flathead into the slot and lean gently. The screw moved by degrees. She caught it in her palm before it could fall into the mud of the sill.

She lifted the cover and set it against the joist. Inside: a wirenut meant for AC work, not low-voltage, crammed around spliced cloth to new copper. The gray jacket had been stripped too far back; the bare ran a half-inch before insulation resumed. Amateur work. Or hurried.

She took two quick photos with her phone and said, “Improper connector. Conversion to new cable. No junction label. No bonding path.”

“Document it,” Owen said, defaulting to her language without sarcasm.

“Documented,” she said. She set the tone wand on the gray conductor. The beep sang. She followed the cable a foot, two, then lost it as it vanished behind a plaster lath in the wall that bordered the library proper. At ankle level, there was a small punched hole, neatly bored, then patched with painted tin set over it like a scab and two screws.

She pressed the tin with one finger. It flexed and clicked, the sound transmitting up the stud. Air drew through around the edges, faint and cold. She held the end of the tone wand near the tin and heard the beep clearly. Beyond that patch was either a chase or a void.

“Owen?”

“I’m here.”

“Tin patch in the library wall at floor level. Air movement. Tone good. Something’s pulling.”

“Vent,” he said. “There’s a dead vent behind the stacks. We cap it to keep the mildew from winning.”

“Stack location?”

“Low shelf under the local history. Left of the study door.”

She bit her lip, thinking in squares. On her sketch she drew a small rectangle, labeled V-1, and an arrow. “We need the ladder upstairs. But first I want to see which way the branch goes on the kitchen line.”

“You going to keep scraping the ribs off yourself or can you reverse out?”

She backed by inches, headlamp scraping, telephone vibrating with the tone against her chest. A sliver of wood snagged her collar and she swore softly. When the hatch framed her again, Owen caught her elbow and eased her clear. He saw the dust on her front and the fine line a nail had drawn along her forearm.

“That is why,” he said.

“I saw it happen,” she said. “It was fast.”

He wiped the scratch with an alcohol pad from the pail and passed her a bandage. “You’ll ruin your paper.”

She covered the cut. “I’m not bleeding on the sketch.”

“Good. Because the sketch is how things become real instead of superstition.” He hefted the ladder and nodded toward the stairs. “Show me the shelf.”

They moved up as if the building could be woken, each footfall a courtesy. The library’s early light was gray, the cloud cover pressed to the leaded panes. He set the ladder inside the blue tape boundary she’d already marked at the stacks. She found the shelf he meant: local history low-run, books with names like A Gazetteer of Essex and Mills of the North Coast. At the baseboard, behind the last row of spines, a brass grille had been painted to match the wood, slotted, louvered closed.

He pulled the shelf forward two inches with both palms and said, “Don’t kill me. They’ll notice.”

“They notice everything,” she said. She crouched and set her ear to the grille. The tone came through, steady as a metronome. She slid the flathead into the tiny screws and loosened them, then lifted the brass free and set it on a labeled sheet of paper she’d spread on the floor for just that purpose. A draft cool as the basement touched her forehead. Beyond the grille, a square shaft, wood-built and black with age, climbed. Its walls bore staples at irregular intervals, some shiny, most old. A gray cable hugged the corner and ran up and out of sight.

“Vent chase,” Owen said over her shoulder. “That dead riser serves the old desk nook and the second-floor landing. We cut its throat when we put in the handler.”

She threaded the tip of the tone wand in. The beep amplified and echoed. She nodded, took a photo, then shot a short video with the phone to record the cable’s path relative to the corner and the staple pattern.

“Ladder,” she said.

“Safety,” he said, but he set the ladder feet firm on the runner and put his hand on the rail as she ascended, one sneaker and then the other, gloves grippy on the rung. At the desk nook, a second grille, same brass, same paint. She removed it, labeled the screws in a snack bag, and leaned in. The gray cable ran past without a break, stapled once to the far stud, then vanished toward the ceiling.

He steadied the ladder with his hip. “What exactly are you hoping to prove with this this morning?”

“I’m not proving,” she said. “I’m mapping initial path.”

“And that’s helpful because?”

“Because when people say they heard a bell, I want to know which wall nodded when they did,” she said. She reached into the chase with a hand mirror, angled her headlamp, and tried to persuade light around corners light didn’t want to go. The mirror fogged and she wiped it on her sleeve. “I need the flex scope.”

He pulled the pail lid, found the coil of a cheap borescope, and passed it up, trailing its cable to her phone. She snaked the head into the chase and watched the screen. The image stuttered and then steadied: wood grain close, a bright oval where the lamp hit, a staple, then gray jacket heading north.

“No junction here,” she said. “No breaks. Vertical run.”

He looked up. The baluster shadowed the landing overhead. “You going to pop upstairs?”

“In a minute. I want the kitchen branch.”

“You want everything,” he said.

“I want enough,” she said. She climbed down and they returned to the basement. She slid back into the crawl but stopped short of the box and followed the original cloth branch the other way, toward the kitchen line. Ten feet later, the wire dipped under a copper pipe, turned left at a sill plate, and then—again—found an interruption: a small, plastic inline connector with spring clamps. Newer still.

She tested tone. Weak through the cloth, absent beyond the plastic. “Break,” she said. “Kitchen path interrupted. Possibly a disconnect.”

“Deliberate?” Owen said.

“Not my job,” she said, and wrote: CB-K branch: dead beyond clip; verify upstairs. She took photos. She backed out slowly, her breathing echoing close.

At the hatch, she paused and looked at the floor plan again, solving her line work. “We’ve got a new gray rising in the vent chase by local history and an old cloth to the kitchen cut with plastic. So the active bell pull ran into the vent path, not the kitchen board. Which means when someone pulled the cord on the second floor—”

Owen lifted a hand. “I’m not hearing conclusions. I’m hearing ‘we mapped initial path into wall and vent chase.’”

She looked at him and smiled a little. “That.”

He handed her a capped wax pencil. “Mark the baseboards you touched. We have people with opinions.”

She drew tiny Xs where the brass had been and where the shelf had shifted, then photographed the marks next to the time on her watch face and her clipboard sheet number. She relabeled her sketch: V-1: rises to desk nook; gray two-conductor; new staple pattern; tones present. CB-K: dead past clip.

“Ladder to the landing?” Owen asked, almost resigned.

“In five,” she said. “First, I need to bag the tin patch screws. I don’t want to drop them again.”

He blinked. “Again?”

“Almost,” she said. “I caught it.”

He shook his head and smiled with only one side of his mouth, then sobered. “Look at me.”

She looked.

“No more full-body entries,” he said. “Not today. You want to send the scope, send the scope. The crawl’s tight and I’d prefer not to explain a fractured clavicle to Rourke and his forms.”

“Safety hates urgency,” she repeated, putting the flathead back in the pail.

“Safety likes ladders placed correctly and humans remembering they’re not ferrets,” he said, and shouldered the ladder again. He paused at the stair. “Coffee’s on the table in the boiler room. You will accept it because your hands are shaking.”

She looked down. They were, a little. She hadn’t noticed. “That’s adrenaline,” she said.

“That’s your body complaining,” he said.

She wrote one last line on the sketch with steadier letters than she felt: Initial trace complete. Vent chase V-1 verified. Kitchen branch interrupted. She capped the pen and taped it to the clipboard spine out of habit.

“Five minutes,” she said, and he nodded, turned, and went up with the ladder like it weighed nothing.

She stood a moment longer in the basement. The tone beeped on behind the wall, indifferent. The air tasted of galvanized dust and old glue. She switched the tone off, and the silence felt heavier. Then she grabbed the pail, the scope coiled inside, and followed him.


==================== SCENE 10 ====================

The vent hatch above Local History was a square of dull tin the size of a record sleeve, paint-gummed, set into the plaster just under the crown molding. From the top of the ladder, Mara set the flat of her palm against it, felt the tack and give of old caulk, and worked a thin putty knife around the seam. The metal sighed free, bringing a thread of parched dust and the cold breath of the duct.

Below, the stacks ran in blue rows, aisle lights off, ambient daylight from the clerestory throwing a pale stripe across spines. Somewhere, a cart wheel thocked against the gap where a tile had chipped, then went quiet. The library had a way of holding noises in its throat.

“Radio check,” Owen’s voice came from the handset on her shoulder.

“Up,” she said, setting the hatch aside with both hands. “I’ll keep the face outside and send the scope. No full-body entries. Copy.”

“Copy. Coffee is at the head of the stairs if you remember to be a person.”

She smiled without looking down. “Noted.” She stuck her head just to the lip, rolled her shoulders to keep out of the opening, and fed the inspection scope into the dark.

The camera’s LED threw a ring of cold light up a sheet-metal throat. It was a return chase, rectangular, soft with lint and the accumulated fluff of a building’s years. The light picked out seams smeared with long-decayed duct mastic and, more telling, a new band of matte black against galvanized. She nudged the scope. The black resolved into a zip-tie, tight around something thin and round that ran with the corner of the duct.

She pulled the scope back a hand’s breadth, slid in a rag-wrapped finger, and felt along the corner. Smooth jacket, not cloth. Two conductors? No, more. She got the magnet grabber onto it and tugged slightly. It didn’t move. Zip-ties every foot, the cheap sort that leave a sharp clipped edge. She took out the phone and shot from the threshold: the zip-tie base, the cable, the corner seam, and a folded paper ruler stuck to the lath at 10 cm for scale. Then she reached for the wax pencil and drew a dot and a date on the hatch flange, low and to the left, where it could be found again.

“Describe,” Owen said.

“Non-utility conductors zip-tied in return air. Black jacket. Current staple pattern consistent with work done after the last paint cycle.” She leaned, listening. The duct gave a small sympathetic tick as the building settled. No air moving; the returns were passive in this wing.

She unspooled a slender fish tape with the GoPro on its end, ran it alongside the scope so she could get a wider shot, and shoved both to the bend. Past the first elbow the cables multiplied: a ribbon of flat black the width of two fingers, three round cables with printed legends scuffed to near illegibility, and a thinner gray line with a soft twist—two-conductor bell wire.

“Talk to me,” Owen said.

“Bell wire joins a bundle in the return. Zip-ties are black poly, clipped, head oriented facing out—someone wanted easy snip access with right hand. The flat ribbon looks like passive HDMI. Round jacketed—one’s RG-59. One’s Cat5e with a braided sleeve over it to kill the shit-blue look.”

“Language.”

“Cat five,” she amended. “Covered. Purpose is concealment, not just neat runs.”

There was a single printed number on the heat-shrink of one of the round cables. She angled the scope to it. 03-B. The ink had smeared when applied to hot jacket, the edges feathering. She photographed the number, the bundle, her wristwatch face to time-stamp.

She traced the path another two feet and found the tie-off point: a little plastic adhesive mount stuck to raw metal where insulation had been peeled back by hand, the tell-tale fur of fiberglass torn and jammed aside. Her throat tightened at the itch; she resisted the urge to rub her wrist where the cuff met skin.

She slid the fish tape past. The return jogged into a larger void where two duct paths met to form a cavalier T, and there, sitting in the lee of a seam, was a breadbox-sized rectangle the color of dead chalk, its face smooth, no screws visible.

“Describe,” Owen said again, voice neutral.

“Concealed hub, surface box with a magnetic face or silicone bead. No visible fasteners. The bundle terminates left side. There’s a short harness of pigtails into the box. I have—I have runtime links.”

She pushed closer until the GoPro lens almost kissed the thing. On its underside, a row of keystone jacks were set into a thin removable strip. Two were populated with Cat5e ends, one with RG-59 coax, one with a 3.5 mm barrel jack. Someone had used a Sharpie to draw a dot on one Cat5e tab and an X on the other. The bell wire fed the 3.5 mm jack via a tiny inline transformer—a balun. There was a glued-on block at the back of the box with “POE INJ” embossed.

“AV hub in a return?” Owen said.

“Not standard library-grade,” she said. “We’ve got an injector, at least one passive HDMI balun, coax coupler, and a mini-amp. It’s not network-grade; this is homebrew, meant to push video and audio with minimal footprint.”

There was a line of fuzz pressed into the edge of the box—a gasket that kept the face from rattling. The magnet-latch hypothesis looked more and more right. She tried the grabber’s hook on the bottom lip, got a bite, and tugged. The face didn’t move.

“Don’t open,” Owen said, hearing the shift in her breath.

“I’m not,” she lied, and stopped.

She took pictures of the face from three angles, each with scale, then a short video rotating around the keystones, making sure to catch the POE INJ block, the balun, the way the fibrous insulation puckered around the plastic mounts. She could hear the world below: a page turning, a whisper, a heel, all filtered through the duct’s throat. She felt very exposed with her hands at the edge of a hole and her face just inches from it.

She pulled back, re-angled the scope down the duct branch that ran north. The bundle split there: two cables went forward along the branch, two draped down into a smaller chute—narrow, older tin, with a smear of old painter’s putty across one seam where a register grille had once been. She paused to sketch on her clipboard balanced on one knee, the drawing vibrating on the ladder rung with each heartbeat: V-2 rises above 300s—tie at 10’—hub at T—Kansas box type; magnet face; jacks underside. Split: forward along north return; drop to east register.

“Cass is in the reading room,” Owen said. “If you need a pair of hands—”

“I’m good. I need quiet,” she said. The whisper felt sacrilegious in the duct.

Through the smaller chute, the camera caught the back side of a carved grille that looked into the long-closed reference alcove—with the polished brass slotted mask that was for show more than function. The cables had been painted the same gray as the duct with an indifferent brush; the brush marks ran onto the tie heads. She pictured arms up in a tight space, paint drips on a sleeve, someone careful enough to dull the sheen of plastic for the next pair of eyes.

She fished back and took the other path. The forward run skimmed along the top flange, then dove again near the corner above the stacks’ endcap. She followed as far as the scope cable let her and caught it dropping inside the wall void where the Local History map case stood. On the inside face of the sheetrock, a saw-cut rectangle had been patched and painted; the joint tape showed hairline ridges if you knew how to look. Her camera light grazed it obliquely, and the fault shadowed up.

“Okay,” she said to herself, letting the breath leave slow. “Okay.”

She backed the scope and fish tape out in careful inches, every limb aware of the four meters to the carpet. She slid the faceplate back over the opening without sealing it, set two stainless screws into two of the old pilot holes just enough to hold, and stuck the phone in her back pocket.

She climbed down one rung, then another, and then the ladder boomed slightly as it found a wobble and she stopped to lean her forearm against the wall and breathe until the shake left her muscles. She dropped the last meter, flexed knees, and the hush of the stacks rose up to meet her.

On the back of the nearest rolling shelf, she spread the clipboard and mapped routes in pencil with her body still angled toward the ladder, as if someone might be staged below a stack and waiting for her to turn. V-2 hub above Local History T. Cat5e A to North reference alcove. RG-59 to Map Case cutout. Ribbon HDMI to—unknown, drops further east. Bell wire to 3.5 bay—likely inject oscillator to sound. Heat-shrink number 03-B on coax. Zip-tie heads outward; right-handed install, crown to room side.

She took her wax pencil and marked the baseboard below the patched area with a minute X next to an equally small dot, then photographed the X with a fingernail in frame for scale, the time on her watch, and her clipboard page number. Because she was already kneeling, she laid her cheek to the cool wood and looked along the baseboard to catch light slivers where the patch had shrunk; there were two.

“Status?” Owen said.

“Hub located,” she said. “Cabling run in return air to avoid detection. Branches into walls behind Local History and Reference. No opening of hub. Photographs and initial diagram complete.”

“Copy,” he said. “Do you want me to put eyes on the outside of those drops?”

“I’ve got visual on the patch at the map case and the old grille,” she said. She hesitated, listening to the growing hum as the library woke further. “Let’s not move anything. I don’t want to improve anyone’s work for them.”

There was a snort in her ear that might have been a laugh. “No improving.”

She moved to the Reference alcove, where the carved brass grille wore a thin veil of dust. She took a photo through it at an angle to catch the false side and the painted cable loop. The shot had to be done carefully to avoid her reflection in the polished faces between the slots. Next to the grille, tucked under the lip of the soffit, there was a single matte-gray circle the size of a coin, flush to the paint. She touched it with the magnet on her chain. The magnet sang to it softly and then slid a millimeter against the paint skin.

“Tag,” she said quietly. “Mount. Whatever they needed to make the face sit. They masked the sound with the gasket.”

She took separate photos of the coin, then the coin with a ruler, then one of the soffit’s run with a red thread pulled taut to show straightness. She wrote on the sketch in tight letters: Coin magnet flush. Gasket to suppress rattle. Face not fastened, but held.

Her fingers had dust in the creases and a faint drag on the skin where the fiberglass itch was blooming. She wiped them on a rag, then put the rag in a bag and labeled it with time, place, and short content: “lint/plaster from V-2 edge.” It was habit to keep contamination from seating in her own pocket and building a second skin that went home with her.

On the far side of the bay, at the endcap—above eye level on the inner face of the cornice—someone had brushed a muddy rectangle to blend an access plate into the trim. She climbed two steps on the ladder, reached, felt the line where paint met plate with a fingernail. The plate clicked microscopically under her probe. She took a photo and a note: East cornice access—painted; probable cable drop.

Her radio hissed softly. Owen again. “Did you say ‘bell wire’?”

“Yes.”

“Active?”

“Not at the moment. But it’s patched into the hub with a balun to an audio jack. They can put signal on it. If someone pulls a cord upstairs, the hub can take that and do something with sound inside the return. Or reverse: push tone back so it seems like it came from wherever those grills and chases open.”

“Which is Local History, Reference, the works.”

“Which is Local History, Reference, the works,” she repeated, and it felt sour. She drew a final route line and circled the hub with a box and three Xs: “Do not open.”

She set the pencil down and looked at the stacks: the hundred quiet spines, the gap in the middle where a book had been reshelved lazy, the way the dust lifted and found the light when someone walked by in the aisle two over. She put the phone up and took two more pictures, out of habit: one as a blank establishing shot of the bay, and one with an index card that read V-2 in block letters held up in the lower left of the frame. Then she took one of her hand with the marker ink still under the nail beds, the ladder behind, and the vent hatch like a tacked-on square. It felt like evidence, even if it wasn’t decisive. It felt like proof of the shape of something she couldn’t open yet.

“Anything else?” Owen said.

“Photos taken,” she said. “Cable routes sketched. I’ll draw clean copies and log them. Leave the hub intact. We’ve got enough to tie the AV path through vents for our purposes.”

“Good. Come down to the boiler and drink like a mammal. You’ve got fiber in your face.”

She looked at the gray of her knuckles and the faint glitter where glass had stuck. “I can feel it,” she said, and dropped her voice. “We keep this quiet.”

“No one wants to have this conversation in the noon group,” Owen said. “I’ll keep them off the floor.”

She clipped the radio to her collar, slid the clipboard under her arm, and took one last look at the coin magnet, the painted cable, the old brass smile of the grille. Then she climbed down, heel by heel, letting the ladder talk softly to the floor with each step.


==================== SCENE 11 ====================
The storage closet behind Periodicals breathed like a fridge. Cold air pooled along the tile and climbed her sleeves; the overloaded metal shelf in front of her glittered with a film of lint on shrink-wrapped bound journals—Town & Country, Harpers, a run of Yankee in a collapsing banker’s box. The overhead pull-string light trembled in the draft from the duct chuffing somewhere behind the wall. Someone had jammed a book truck into the aisle and left it, half‑full of government pamphlets in milky binders. The smell was dust, hand sanitizer, and the sharp electrical note of something with a transformer that never slept.

She eased the cart an inch with her hip, ducked, and shouldered into the back corner, where the wall jogged around an old flue. The corner was wrong: a rectangle of pegboard leaned there, cut to fit, its holes smeared with paint. Behind it, she could see a shadow of cabling that didn’t match the rest of the building’s ancient conduit.

She set the clipboard on a crate, took a photo of the pegboard, then slid her fingers along the edge. The board wasn’t fixed; friction and a single magnet held it. It came away with a breath and a soft scratch that sounded too loud in the cold.

Behind it was a steel plate mounted to the studs, with a small perforated aluminum box stuck to it on foam tape and three cable drops entering through grommets. A coil of red‑and‑white zip wire, a coax run with a screw-on terminator, and a heavier sleeved bundle that ducked into the wall. The aluminum box had a slot cut in the lid for an SD card and a peel‑and‑stick label from a mail-order house: auric stage relay v2.1. Under that, someone had printed by hand with a laundry marker: STACKS/REF-LOCAL.

“No,” she said, a flat thing, and then, “Okay.”

She turned her phone’s flashlight on and got in close. Two small terminal blocks, two relays with transparent covers and red LEDs, a palm-sized board with a cheap OLED that showed a frozen pixelated clock. There was a stubby antenna hot-glued at a stupid angle. A four‑gang DIP switch labeled MODE. A pigtail to a power wart plugged into a multi‑tap that fed a dehumidifier and nothing else.

She brushed the tip of her probe along the zip wire. Crisp, new insulation. She traced it to a beige box roughly and recently screwed to the wall above the aluminum lid—old school, with a hinged plastic door and a bell icon. Inside, a tarnished bell transformer hummed; a set of screw terminals were labeled CALL, RETURN, AUX. A second hand label read PULL CORDS—3RD.

Her radio clicked twice in her collar. Owen: “You still under Periodicals?”

“Yeah. Closet behind it,” she said. “Everything the fire code dreams about. I’ve got a hub and a staged relay. It’s labeled for Stacks, Reference, Local History.”

“I can be in Reference, thirty seconds.”

“Do it,” she said. “Bring your ears.”

She set the radio on the crate, clipped low so it wouldn’t scrape. She took three slow photos: the whole plate; the relays with the little red LEDs; the SD card slot. She crouched and angled the phone under the aluminum box, found a micro‑USB, and the ghost‑green blink of a barrel connector. The OLED flashed alive, briefly, then rolled through a menu she didn’t touch. It paused on LOG: 42 ENTRIES, then TIME: 10:42:13. She took the shot before it rolled.

The beige box had a tiny test button inside, a membrane dot covered in dust that said BELL TEST. She breathed out, wiped it with her sleeve, and pressed once.

From the duct behind her shoulder came a thin, intimate chime that didn’t belong to any system the building had a right to have: two high notes, a breath, then the softened clack of something mechanical. Not from the closet, not from the beige box—the sound came out of the duct opening, the way an old vent will push a radio across a house.

A startled scrape came from the far side of the stacks—somebody out there had flinched. Then Owen, a calm notch lower: “Reference, west end. I heard it. Sounded like it was over my left shoulder. Where did you press?”

“Closet test. It pushes tone into the return. Listen,” she said, and pressed again.

He whistled, one silent note of disbelief caught in before it turned into cold work voice. “Same angle, different note. No reverb. That’s a direct injection.”

“The relays are labeled for three zones,” she said. “I’m going to switch.”

“Say when.”

She slid her probe’s tip under the first relay’s clip to read the stamped ID. The tiny print glowed in her photo: CH2-REF L. Ten bucks of plastic with a number that would ride well in a table. She took the picture, zoomed to make sure it held, then looked at the DIP switches. They were set SCHED/REM=ON, MANUAL=OFF. An engraved bit of panel next to them had a white paint dot on a rocker labeled ZONE. She thumbed it. The relay’s red LED jumped on a twin to the right. On the OLED, the text shifted to CH3-LOCAL: READY.

“Now Local,” she said, and pressed the test.

Owen came in, disembodied and precise: “That one’s right above the county histories. Clearer. It’s like a gloved finger on glass.”

“Again.”

She pressed. He was silent a beat.

“It’s not the building,” he said, finally.

“No,” she said.

She backed up and looked at the heavy sleeved cable. It ran down into the wall, a neat chisel cut into plaster patched badly and painted to match. She put the camera into the hole and took a blind shot. Reviewing, she saw the cable fermata in an old tin chase—someone had opened the past and jammed the present into it.

She went back to the plate. The OLED held on that log line. She pressed the side button and it clicked into a screen that listed lines of time-stamped events, the way cheap embedded software likes to do: 09:58:17 TRIG: CH2 REM; 10:03:04 SCHED PLAY SEQ 1; 10:11:56 TRIG: CH3 REM; 10:23:43 KEEPALIVE; and so on, each with a device ID at the end, a string of hex she could feel in her fingers as a fact.

She took a picture of the screen. The glow moved; she took another. She held the phone closer. The entries went back days. She didn’t scroll. She didn’t need to. The alphabet soup of the device identifiers—RFID: 34:A2:9C; TX: 87-14—was exactly the kind of stupid signature that got left behind when someone thinks hiding is pulling a pegboard into place.

“I want one more thing,” she said. “Stay.”

“I’m leaning on the atlas stand,” Owen said. “World’s oldest bouncer.”

She opened the beige box all the way and leaned her shoulder to hold it. The CALL terminals had two bell wires on them—one red, one white—twisted, then split to go upward. Next to it, under AUX, a ribbon cable went to the aluminum box. On the bottom of the beige housing, under the transformer, a paper tag had half a serial hanging off: FHC-9 3 2. She wrote that down and took a photo, then set the pen against the CALLOUT contacts and bypassed the button with a touch.

The duct went soft and human for a second—someone’s recorded throat clear, then the faintest audible whisper, not words, just the caress of mouth shape. It ran through the metal above Owen’s head and poured out on a tick, not a ghost’s idea of an entrance but an audio file in a queue.

“Don’t like that,” Owen said.

“It’s on the box,” she said. “Preloaded sequences. The log shows scheduled plays.”

“Staged,” he said.

“Staged,” she said, and took the third picture she always took, the one with her index card in frame: V‑3, C‑3, the time she thought it was, so she didn’t have to untie it later.

She washed the pad of her finger over the DIP switches, feeling the burr. She didn’t change them. She pressed through the OLED menu until she hit a screen that said DEVICES and showed a list. Each line had an RF tag and a role: RX-REM/UPPER, RX-REM/3RD, TX-VENT/2, TX-VENT/REF. She photographed each in turn and wrote the grouping by hand, block letters: UPPER/3RD remote receivers, vent transmitters at Reference and Local. There was also a line for TIMER/SCHED with an icon she recognized from a hundred $14 smart plugs. She pressed until it showed the schedule grid: had every day at 12:12, 17:30, 20:05, and a loose block labeled RANDOM: LATE AM. The date range boxed yesterday, today, tomorrow.

“Do you want Cass to hear it?” Owen asked.

“No,” she said. “Not yet. I want the IDs.”

She popped the SD card, looked at it, slid it halfway back, took a photo of it half-abducted so she could prove it belonged in the slot, then pushed it back in so the menu beeped. The OLED puked a curt message—CARD PRESENT—and she took that photo too.

She pressed BELL TEST once more for her own record. The duct whispered. The first relay tripped, the second didn’t. She didn’t need it to. She set the pegboard back, angling it like a book at the back of a shelf.

She keyed the radio. “I have a log of device IDs and schedules,” she said. “Remote receivers tagged UPPER and 3RD; vent transmitters for Reference and Local. There’s a schedule. There’s a manual trigger. The test pushes audio on demand.”

Owen’s voice came back subdued into something dense. “I can stand under it and it’ll happen if you push.”

“That’s right,” she said.

“Copy.”

She took a clean copy on the clipboard, drawing a little rectangle where the aluminum box sat, two arrows for the relays, the coil of wire like a candy cane, the ugly cut in the wall. In the margin, she wrote: Closet hub—auric v2.1—SD present—IDs captured—timers logged. Underlined. Everything she’d needed out of here was in the phone already and now in ink. There was no angle left where this was an accident of old construction or some friendly quirk of a creaking house. This was wires and cheap ham kit and someone’s decision to press a membrane button and hear a sound bloom from wherever they wanted it to.

She turned the light off. The closet went back to cold. The pegboard was just a blank panel again. The duct, close to her ear, held nothing but the steady draw of the building’s lungs.

When she opened the closet door, the library’s light hit her and her breath came out old and visible. Owen stepped into her line between Periodicals and Reference, shoulders up, eyes on her collar where the radio sat, as if the little black box could tell him what the other one couldn’t.

“You get it?” he said.

She held up the clipboard. “We have it.”

He nodded once. His gaze tracked up past her shoulder and back. People were reading. No one was running. The building made its regular noises, small and uninterested.

She picked up the cart that blocked the aisle, rolled it a foot, and let it bump back into place like nothing had ever moved. Then she tucked the phone into her vest, set her hand on the closet door to feel the steady little hum through the hollow core, and let go.


==================== SCENE 12 ====================
Harbor Light had a way of smelling clean and marine at the same time, bleach threaded with tide. The side conference room had no view, only a slit of frosted glass where gull shadows crossed. Someone had set a carafe of water that tasted like the plastic lid and two glasses that sweated onto a blotter with the nonprofit’s logo in navy. The HVAC hummed as if determined to shush actual voices. On the wall, a framed poster reminded everyone of core values in a typeface so soft it could barely stand up: dignity, stewardship, candor.

Mara took the chair that didn’t face the door. Habit. Her notebook sat open. The recorder on the table was turned but dark, a deliberate display: reachable, idle.

The board member came in without anyone at reception announcing him. A navy jacket, salt at the cuffs, hair clipped to anonymity. He checked the hall, touched the room’s door like it had a pulse, closed it until the latch kissed quiet.

“Three minutes, maybe five,” he said, eyes not meeting hers. “They run out of HR at two-fifteen. That gives me a gap.”

“Understood,” Mara said.

“Nothing on the record.”

“Off,” she said. “Deep background. You don’t exist to this.”

He nodded once like that hurt a tooth. He didn’t sit. He stood behind the second chair, palms on its back, as if ready to push it into her.

“You asked me at the dedication who signed Alistair’s incident report,” he said. “You didn’t know there isn’t one.”

Her pen hovered. “Walk me.”

“You know his last name.”

“I do.”

“He was a short-stay detox.” He said it like a line item. “Volunteer intake—Sue—logged him at 20:36 on the second. Vital signs within acceptable ranges. He was placed on the east corridor, bed five. We had two techs on that strip. Owen floated.” He squinted, corrected himself without looking at her. “No. Owen was on maintenance. Night techs were Rhea and Theo. Nurse Iris had meds. Nora signed the evening rounds at twenty-two hundred. He was seen at twenty-three-forty-two, sleeping. At zero-one-oh-four, he was found unresponsive in the shower.”

The room’s hum swelled and dropped, as if it wanted to be in the conversation.

“Shower?” Mara said. “At one a.m.?”

“That’s what the paperwork says,” he said. “What we said.”

She waited. He watched a corner where paint met frame, anger like the pinch of a stapler at his mouth.

“There was water,” he added. “There was a slip. That’s what I was told in the first call. I live six blocks away; I walked. The night manager had already called 911 and the on-call physician. We had a dead man in a towel and a barefoot tech crying into her hands. The paramedics checked as if it would change. It didn’t.”

He rubbed his jaw. His knuckles stayed white. “The on-call said we wait on the medical examiner and we do nothing else. Then Julian arrived.”

“Julian,” Mara said, as if she didn’t know the surname was a legal envelope and a smile that never showed teeth.

“He’s very good with his voice.” The board member said it without admiration or contempt. “He told us we had protocols, and the protocols told us where this would live. In ‘adverse incident’ under quality improvement, not where it could be discovered in a negligent death claim. He called it a ‘nonwillful mishap under care’ and he gave me a script to read to the board by nine the next morning. He took the night logs and the med admin record and said he would compile.”

“Compile,” Mara echoed.

“By eight-thirty we had a notice drafted to family care partners, signed by the director, expressing sorrow for a ‘night-time fall.’” He shifted his weight off his palms and put it back, grounding again. “By nine-ten, he had me gavel a ‘special closed session.’ He said we had a duty to preserve program integrity. He said we would review the policies in camera. He said the words ‘peer review privilege’ three times so that if anyone repeated them out loud later, they’d be burned into the minutes.”

She turned a page. “Timeline after the discovery. Zero-one-oh-four found. Paramedics pronounce?”

“Zero-one-eighteen.” He shut his eyes like he was checking an internal clock. “We called Rourke—the police liaison—at zero-one-twenty-five. He came. He looked. He asked how often we checked beds. He took notes. He stood under the vent and asked why it was warm.”

A gull scraped across the frosted glass. The board member didn’t look.

“We handed them our logs,” he said. “We kept our notes.”

“And the shower,” she said.

“We photographed the tile,” he said. “Julian insisted. He made a file called ‘Tiles.’ He told me it was important to have detail in case ‘items developed.’ Later, he told us that the medical examiner accepted a determination consistent with ‘fall-related blunt force with contributory intoxication’ because there were no hemorrhages inconsistent with a short fall. He said it’s what any reasonable person would reach.”

He made two fists where she could watch, then flattened them into the chair back again. “He said we would not ‘invite fishing expeditions’ by volunteering internal deliberations. He said we had a duty to our clients not to make our house look unsafe.”

Mara wrote dates, times, quotation marks around the words that mattered. “You’re saying the fatality was smoothed over.”

He studied the core values poster until his gaze reflected back at him as a pale oval. “Yes.”

“By Julian.”

“By all of us,” he said, sharper than he meant to. “By doing what he said. By letting the minutes say we met to ‘review routine matters.’ By letting our public statement say nothing. We are not villains in long coats, Ms. Vance. We are a nonprofit with a waiting list. We did not want empty beds. We told ourselves we were protecting the program.”

“Names for the east corridor that night,” she said, businesslike. “Rhea. Theo. Iris. Nora. Night manager?”

“Maureen,” he said, quick. “And the director, Vivian, arrived at zero-one-fifty. She stood by the door and watched and did not speak until the sheet was over him.”

“And Alistair’s family?” Mara said.

“Brother,” he said. “Left a message at three. Called back at eight. He was told there had been a fall. He asked if someone had waited with him. We said everything had been done. He sent flowers to the staff.”

The humming dropped another register. In the hall, a copier spit a page and stopped.

“What do you need?” Mara asked.

“For this to be a purge,” he said, too honest, then shook his head. “No. Not here. For this to be presented as something more than rumor so that if we correct the record, it isn’t a self-mutilation.”

“You keep mentioning minutes,” she said. “You said ‘we kept our notes.’ Where.”

He breathed out. “We use BoardEffect for packets, but Julian asked that the special close stay off the cloud. He uses a local drive. It lives on a Synology box in his office, behind the diplomas, in a cabinet with a perforated panel. It hums. He calls it ‘the toaster.’ He labels things in a way that looks like nonsense if you don’t know his system.”

“Which is.”

“He names folders after ships,” the board member said. “Not even famous ones. Ketches, icebreakers, light tenders, anything with a registry photo he likes. The special session is under HL-Side/USCGC Iris.
