There’s an ‘in camera’ memo dated the third, one-thirty-seven a.m., and a supplemental where he outlines the ‘integrity narrative.’ He refers to the shower three different ways so that each of them could be defended as ‘colloquial.’” His mouth twitched. “You’ll have to sit and copy. He doesn’t let drives leave.”

He finally sat, as if now that he’d said it, his knees were allowed to bend.

“You can get me in,” Mara said. Not a question.

“I can get you an appointment,” he said. “Your pretext is that you’re From Away and you need to ask for a statement about… the drone incident, which he loves to talk about because it lets him say ‘trespass’ and ‘prank.’ He’ll take you into his office because he wants you to feel represented. He will not imagine you know what a Synology is.”

“And passwords.”

He tapped his wedding ring once against the table and stopped. “Admin account on the box is harborlight-admin. He’s never changed it because he thinks the firewall is a fairy ring that protects him from wolves. The password is what you expect.”

“Password?”

He looked at the poster on the wall. “Dignity2020.”

“That’s not what I expect,” she said.

He shrugged. “He cycles the values with election years. It’s easier for him to remember.”

She slid her pen up the margin and back. “I need copies of the memos. Overexposure is a risk. You want distance? You’re anonymous here. That only holds if I don’t leave footprints.”

“You won’t.” He looked at the recorder, then at her hand near it. “And if you quote me, you’ll destroy what we have left. You know that.”

“I won’t quote you,” she said. “You have my word.”

He exhaled, one shoulder dropping a fraction. He glanced at the door again, then back. “One more thing. You asked for corroboration you can carry without naming me.”

“Yes.”

“Cass,” he said. “He was here the morning after. He was shooting for his channel, some piece about the storm windows—they had him on a ladder; Vivian indulges him when he stays on the porch. He left his mic open. He recorded Theo saying ‘we moved him’ to Rhea.”

Mara stilled.

“I told him to delete it,” the board member went on. “He didn’t. He showed it to me on his phone like it was a card trick and then he pulled the phone away. He thinks it’s his insurance against us telling him not to film. He thinks half the world is his audience.”

“How do I get it,” Mara said.

“Ask him like a journalist and he’ll posture,” he said. “Ask him like a big sister and he’ll hand it over. He wants to be told he has something real.” A bare half-smile flicked over his face and disappeared, more like a muscle stretch than an expression. “Say you heard the word ‘moved’ and watch him light up. He’ll play it for you in exchange for telling him he’s first to air when you break something that lets him say he was on the scene.”

“I don’t trade publication order,” Mara said.

“You could promise him exactly nothing and he would still show you,” he said. “He is hungry to be taken seriously.”

The handle ticked as someone out in the hall leaned on it. A laugh, a shrug, footsteps away.

The board member stood like he’d been pulled up by a string. “You have what you need. You don’t have me.”

Mara closed her notebook. “I don’t have you.”

He took a card from his wallet and set it face down. Not his name. A phone number with no area code, just seven digits local. It looked like a lottery. He covered it with his palm for a beat, then lifted his hand.

“If you call that, it rings the phone in the downstairs coat closet,” he said. “No one answers. If you let it ring to six, hang up, and call again in a minute, I’ll come out to the sidewalk. If you ring it at any other pattern, it’s the plumber.”

She slid the card under the blotter where the logo’s navy printed through to it. “Understood.”

He reached for the carafe, poured water into one of the warm glasses, didn’t drink it. “You’ll need someone to stand at his door.”

“Who.” Now a question.

“Ask Maureen to take a phone call for him at her desk,” he said. “She likes to see him flustered. She will keep him talking until she’s bored.” He set the glass down with care. “Don’t get theatrical. He notices theatrics. He doesn’t notice ordinary.”

“Thank you,” she said.

He gave the smallest nod. “You write things,” he said.

“I do.”

“Write this like we loved him,” he said, and in that sentence there was nothing covert at all.

“I’ll write it like it happened,” she said.

The door moved air when he opened it. He went out without looking back, jacket shoulders drawing square as he met footsteps in the hall. His voice changed shape into something ordinary as he said hello to someone she didn’t see.

Mara took the card back out and took a photo of it, then put it away again. She turned the recorder on, let it capture the room tone for five seconds, then clicked it off. On her notebook page, under the times, she wrote:

— HL-Side/USCGC Iris
— In camera, 01:37, integrity narrative
— Synology “toaster” behind diplomas
— harborlight-admin / Dignity2020
— Cass has mic clip: Theo to Rhea: “we moved him”
— Maureen stall

She slid the chair back under the table so its legs made no sound. Outside the frosted glass slit, a gull’s shadow went by once and was gone.


==================== SCENE 13 ====================
The Harbor Light loading bay breathed brine and bleach. A gull hunched on the dented handrail over the ramp and eyed the orange cones like they might scuttle. Plastic strip curtains slapped and sighed in the door to the laundry, letting out a warm damp smell when people pushed through with carts of folded towels, then the colder air from the outside reclaimed the concrete.

Mara stood in the yellow hash of paint by the dock plate and watched the reflection of the bay through a puddle. The water in it shivered with every truck that went past on the service road. She kept her profile turned so the fisheye camera under the overhang saw a staffer waiting for a delivery, not someone taking notes. In her pocket, the recorder warmed in her hand.

Cass came out of the laundry swinging a mesh bag of cloth napkins like a schoolkid with gym clothes. She had a Harbor Light badge on a retractable cord, half buried by the frayed hood of her sweatshirt. The hood had a salt ring on it. Her eyes flicked once at the upper corner where the fisheye lens lurked, then at Mara, then down the ramp, performing that quick math and writing in the sum that said it was safe enough.

“I told Nora I’d take smoke breaks more often,” Cass said. “She said smoke breaks are for people who smoke. I said if the policy is about the average, I can be your outlier. That’s science.”

“You’re doing science," Mara said. "I’m pretending to wait for a crate that doesn’t exist.”

Cass set the mesh bag on the pallet where it wouldn’t get splashed. “In three seconds, the linen cart’s going to bang the door and you’re going to think your fillings are loose. Just to set the vibe.”

A second and a half later the door clapped as a metal cart clanged through. Cass didn’t look. She put both hands in her hoodie pocket like you do when you’re going to either steal or give something away and went with the second option. She pulled out a little USB microphone with a foam windscreen and a grubby silver binder clip snapped over its cable.

“I kept this off the Wi‑Fi on purpose,” she said. “No nervous little backup to some ‘secure’ cloud that’s not. I have the quote. You’re going to ask me to say it myself. I know.”

“I’m going to ask if you’ll say it in your own words,” Mara said. “And only if you want it recorded. We can let it be off the record if that’s safer.”

“Safe is a relative term here,” Cass said. “Like ‘clean’ is a relative term. Like ‘stable.’ Like ‘we’re doing everything we can.’ Like ‘bad weather.’ I can’t listen to anyone say ‘bad weather’ without wanting to bite them.”

Her hands were shaking in a controlled way, like she had told them they could do it but only inside her sleeves. She glanced over Mara’s shoulder, catalogued passing staff: a tall man in gray slacks with a logo vest; two CNAs laughing, pushing a rack of gowns; a woman in green Crocs picking tape off her fingers.

Mara took the mic, looked at it, then held it up for Cass to see the input levels bounce through the tiny LEDs. “We’ll slate,” she said. “No names, no clock, no date. You set the conditions.”

“I have a list,” Cass said. She said it with a little smile like it was a joke about being that person with a list, and then she was that person with a list. “One: you don’t use my name. You don’t use my job title. You describe me as ‘a Harbor Light staffer with access to scheduling and pharmacy handoff’ if you have to describe. Two: you shift my voice. Not cartoon. Just so if my mother hears it, she doesn’t know. Three: you don’t time‑stamp it. No shifts, no days of the week. Four: if they ask you how you got it, you say ‘a person offered it.’ Five: you don’t conceal it from Rourke if he asks. He’s baseline decent.”

Mara nodded. “Agreed. Will you say those conditions on the recording?”

“Yes. That’s Six: make my conditions part of the thing. So if they chop it up, the head and tail keep it honest.”

“Okay.”

Mara pulled the recorder out, clipped the little mic cable to its input, and let it hang between them. Through the open bay, wind carried up from the water in a salt rasp, and somewhere down the corridor a steamer tripped and reset with a thunk. She waited for the linen cart to bang through again—then it didn’t. The loading bay’s particular silence returned, that emptier hollow between machines.

Cass rubbed her palm on her jeans. “Maureen’s got him on hold,” she said. “She told him she doesn’t know the difference between a summons and a subpoena and is waiting for the next person to walk by to tell her. She is not waiting for the next person to walk by. He’s explaining into a tunnel. You’ve got seven minutes.”

Mara hit Record. Three seconds of room tone. The loading bay beeped faintly as a forklift reversed somewhere on the other side of the freight elevator. Cass watched the LEDs like you watch a stove, not because it’s interesting but because you need to be sure it stays on.

“For the record,” Mara said, her voice even, “this is Mara Kline. It is the afternoon of Day Two. I’m speaking with a Harbor Light staff member in the loading bay. Will you state your conditions for giving this statement?”

Cass pulled the hood down, smoothed her hair back once, squared her shoulders like she was hanging a coat on them. “My conditions: No name, no job title. If you have to describe me, say ‘a Harbor Light staffer with access to scheduling and pharmacy handoff.’ Alter my voice enough that my family can’t recognize me. Don’t include dates, shifts, or identifiable timestamps. If asked how you got this, you say ‘a person offered it.’ If Rourke asks to hear it, let him. If my conditions aren’t met, you don’t use it.”

“Do you consent to being recorded under those conditions?”

“Yes.” Her mouth trembled once. “Yes, I consent.”

“Tell me what you heard or were told.”

Cass looked past Mara at the sliver of harbor and the flat white chop going south. Three beats. Then she looked back, and when she spoke it came all in one breath.

“I was told to keep the numbers clean,” she said. “Those exact words. ‘Keep the numbers clean.’”

“Who told you that?” Mara asked.

“A person who signs my time sheet and likes to say ‘optics,’” Cass said. “You can’t use their name from me. You can maybe triangulate it from someone else. But I’m not the one who gives you that, not today.”

“What did ‘keep the numbers clean’ mean to you?”

“It meant don’t document things that make us look like we don’t have control when we’re supposed to. It meant if there’s a fall but the person gets back in bed and isn’t bleeding, you call it a near‑fall and you don’t file the IR. It meant if a pill goes missing you call it a waste and you sign it yourself. It meant if someone dies, you use the phrases that play better with the families. It meant you don’t put four bed alarms in a row under ‘faulty’ because then Procurement has to answer why they didn’t replace them when they said they did. It meant moving numbers between columns until they make a picture you can show.”

The strip curtain lifted with a gust and slapped, then settled again. Cass’s voice steadied. Saying it out loud seemed to lay it somewhere outside her.

“I printed a report,” Cass said, “that showed incident reports by shift and unit. It made a spike. The spike was real. We had three call bells that were down. We had a CNA out with COVID. We had three new agency people who didn’t know the hall. We had a family at the desk crying. The spike made sense. I brought it upstairs because that’s what you do. He glanced at it and he said—this is the quote—he said ‘we need to keep the numbers clean.’”

“Say the quote again,” Mara said.

“We need to keep the numbers clean,” Cass said. “And then: ‘No one’s going to run the narrative for us if we don’t.’ And then: ‘These people are scared enough without seeing a graph.’ And then: ‘Don’t put that spike in the monthly. Spread it.’”

Mara let two seconds pass. “Spread it how.”

“Copy the IRs from that week into the prior two and the following two. Take the call bell downtimes out of the main log and put them in the maintenance ticketing where nobody looks at them except to mark them closed. Change the wording on two of the falls to ‘assisted lowering’. Mark the pharmacy discrepancy as ‘end of blister’—which it wasn’t. When Alis—” She stopped herself, swallowed, started again. “When someone dies, you don’t use ‘fall’ and ‘aspiration’ in the same paragraph. You put ‘decline’ and ‘comfort measures.’ You keep them separate. You keep the numbers clean.”

Mara watched the way Cass’s mouth pulled like a stitch when she said the last part. “Did you do it?”

“Yes,” Cass said. “Not all of it. Not all the time. Enough times to feel like I’m standing under a shower that’s not water. Enough times that when I look at the board”—she tilted her chin toward the corridor where a dry‑erase map lived—“the magnet pins look like lies.”

“What risks do you feel giving this testimony?”

“Losing my job,” Cass said. “Not getting another one within forty miles. Getting labeled as having a problem with authority and with ‘communication.’ Getting my schedule changed until I can’t get my kid from aftercare and then ‘resigning.’ Or—” She cut it off. “Losing my job.”

“What do you hope will happen by giving it?”

“I want the picture to be the picture,” Cass said. “I want the families to understand that when they see ‘0 falls’ on the monthly, it’s not because we are miracle workers, it’s because we moved the dot. I want somebody with a badge and a printer to sit in a room and look at the paper and not talk about optics. I want less time spent making numbers and more time keeping the humans in one piece.”

A forklift beeped again in the deep of the building. The gull on the handrail opened and closed its beak at nothing. The bay smelled like the inside of a shell.

“You said there’s a specific quote,” Mara said. “You’ve just given it. Do you have it recorded?”

Cass glanced at the mic with the binder clip on it, then at Mara’s recorder. She nodded. “I have a clip on my badge,” she said. “Sometimes I use it to listen to music when I fold linens. Sometimes it’s just on. It caught that sentence.” She didn’t take the device out. “You’ll get it. But not now, not in the parking lot. I will send it from a library computer that prints receipts when you log off. I will use a burner login. You will delete your cache. I will never have known what that means and somehow I will have done it.”

“Okay,” Mara said. “For the moment we have you on tape, saying the words yourself, under your conditions. Is there anything you want to add?”

Cass thought. Her face smoothed out for a second like she’d ironed something flat. “We are not monsters,” she said. “We’re tired and some of us are being asked to act like this is PR. We’re nurses, and aides, and the woman who knows which screw goes where in the lift when the lift refuses. We are doing the best we can while a man who likes to say ‘optics’ talks about optics. I don’t want to burn this place down. I want to stop feeling like we’re standing on a rug that somebody keeps pulling and telling us it’s because the room needs rearranging.”

Mara let the recorder have the silence after that. It took it in—salt, gull, distant clatter, her own breath going in and out—then she said, “You can revoke this any time before it’s used. If you change your mind, you tell me and I hold it.”

“I know,” Cass said. She took the mic cord in her fingers, pinched the foam windscreen and let it go. “If I revoke it, it won’t be because it’s not true. It’ll be because I didn’t get brave enough. I’m trying to get brave enough.”

“Do you consent to the use of this recording under the conditions you stated?”

“I do,” Cass said. “Yes.”

Mara clicked Stop. The little red light canceled itself back to black. She tucked the recorder into the inner pocket of her jacket, felt it there against her ribs like a flat bone.

“You didn’t ask me about the other thing,” Cass said, then shook her head. “No. One thing at a time. I’ll send you the clip. The ‘clean numbers’ one. I’ll put it in a folder called Recipes so nobody clicks it. When you get it, call the coat closet. Six rings and hang up. I’ll walk past like I have somewhere ordinary to be.”

“Okay,” Mara said. “Thank you.”

Cass lifted the mesh bag of napkins and put it on her shoulder. The weight of it pushed her hoodie down and pulled the lanyard taut. She adjusted it like you adjust something you’ve been carrying a long time.

“Maureen’s going to get bored in about thirty seconds,” she said. “He’ll start looking for where the drafts come from. If you hold the door to the ramp open for me right now, we will just look like two people who know how to share a door.”

Mara stepped aside and pressed the metal bar. The strip curtain clattered. Cass walked past, her sneaker slipping for a second on the wet paint, then finding grip. She didn’t look back, which made it cleaner.

At the top of the ramp, someone laughed inside at a joke that had nothing to do with any of it. The gull lifted off the rail and went out over the dumpsters toward the shimmer of the harbor. Mara held the door until the hinge caught and the door eased closed on its own. Then she picked up an empty clipboard from the dolly and stood for a breath like a person who had always been where she was, and then like a person who was somewhere else already.


==================== SCENE 14 ====================
The front desk at Harbor Light had been designed to soothe: kiln‑white tile, curved counter in sea‑glass resin, a bubbling column of aerated water where a pink‑eyed fish drifted past on repeat, and a rack of pamphlets with gradients that went from mint to calmer mint. It was late enough that the day’s light had thinned to a milky shine through the windows, and the gulls on the parking‑lot lamps looked like claim tags.

Mara stepped off the elevator with the empty clipboard held at an angle that implied she was both occupied and harmless. Her jacket collar still smelled faintly of salt and fryer oil from the alley. The recorder’s rectangular weight rested against her ribs where she had slipped it after Cass’s “I do.”

At the desk, the Harbor Light Administrator looked up, smiled the smile of someone managing an entire building with politeness, and took in Mara the way nurses took in a fall risk: shoes first, hands, lanyard, eyes. Mara had no lanyard. She had the clipboard.

“Hi,” the Administrator said. The ID placard read L. VIERA. “Can I help you?”

“Yes,” Mara said, using the voice you use to approach counters—neither apologetic nor too sure. “I just need to sign out. I was up with Cass from dining. She had me pick up napkins for the back room. Sorry—I should’ve stopped here on the way in.”

L. Viera’s smile didn’t move. One hand hovered over the log book as if it were sacred text. “Of course. Whom did you check in with?”

“The volunteer coordinator,” Mara said after a beat that suggested a long day. She set the clipboard down. On it was an old housekeeping checklist from the library—Mop closet/Lock windows/Return keys—that lent comfortingly normalized bullet points to her hands. She didn’t slide the sheet forward. She let it sit in her orbit.

“We don’t have volunteers working without badges,” Viera said. She had a milk‑glass nail polish that made her fingers seem antique in a deliberate way. “May I see your badge?”

Mara tapped her jacket as if she had one and found a pocketful of regret. “It must still be… I just left it upstairs when I washed my hands.” She made a small face. “I should not have done that. I can go back up.”

“Where did you wash your hands?” Viera asked without any cruelty. She improved her posture. A small stack of pink slips was aligned at her elbow, the kind you fill out when someone’s family wants a dietary change. Behind her the wall clock ticked with the slow vanity of a spa.

“The staff galley on two,” Mara said, naming a thing she had seen when Owen had walked her past the vending machine the day before. “Right by the coat closet.”

Viera’s smile thinned to regulation. “We’ll just pause. Can I ask your name?”

“Mara Pierce.” She said the last name like it was spelled correctly on a file somewhere.

Viera’s eyes did a tiny travel that said she’d heard it before. She lowered the hand toward the phone. “Ms. Pierce. I think Mr. Julian asked that we escort you when you’re on the premises. Would you mind waiting here a moment while I page security?”

Mara breathed in and let the air sit in the architecture of her chest. Her voice stayed mild. “I’m already on my way out. If you need to page them, go ahead, but it’ll be a waste of their time.”

“I prefer not to let people move through the building without a badge,” Viera said. “Please wait.” She pressed the intercom. “Security to the front desk.”

The sound of amplified hallway seeped back through the speaker: cart wheels going over the seam between tile and carpet, someone’s laugh, a door’s hydraulic hiss. The pink‑eyed fish did another loop and learned nothing.

“Understood,” Mara said. She turned the clipboard so it faced herself again, adding the tiny grain of refusal that read as habit rather than defiance. “Do you want me to sign out anyway?”

“We’ll do that when—” Viera began, and then a radio sputtered behind her angled shoulder and two security staff stepped off the elevator like a thought that had arrived.

They wore navy polo shirts with the lighthouse logo stitched so that the beam went straight through their hearts. One was rangy and old enough to have seen a few too many late nights; the other was compact, hair shaved to discipline. The taller one scanned Mara’s hands first, then her pockets, and relaxed a notch when he found no visible shapes beyond the clipboard and a pen loop with no pen in it.

“Afternoon,” he said. His name tag read ED. The other’s read HERNANDEZ. “Administrator Viera?”

“She says she’s leaving,” Viera said. “No badge.”

“Ms. Pierce,” Ed said, in a voice that stayed awfully kind until it didn’t. “We’re gonna walk you out.”

“Excellent,” Mara said. “That’s what I’m here for.” She took one minuscule step left so she wouldn’t have to walk around them and get funneled. She aimed that step toward the frosted panel door that led to the automatic vestibule.

“Before we go,” Hernandez said. “Do you have any recording devices on you? There’s signage you passed.”

“I passed it,” Mara said. “I also read it.” She touched the outer pocket where her phone would have been if she’d been careless. “No recording device in use on premises.” She did not touch her inner pocket.

Viera’s smile was gone now; she was pure policy and a selected haircut. “We’ll need you to open your bag.”

“I don’t have a bag,” Mara said, turning so the absence of a bag was clear.

Ed made a small tsk with his tongue and mouth. “Look, we can do this nice or we can do this weird. You’re leaving anyway. Let us check your jacket and we’re square.”

“Weird how?” Mara asked, and that deflated it enough to reform the air.

Hernandez went up on his toes and landed again, a boxer’s bounce. “Just open your jacket, ma’am.”

“It’s not a jacket,” Mara said, hooking her forefinger into the zipper pull, showing three inches of interior like a person showing that they are not carrying a knife. “It’s weather.”

“You work for who again?” Viera asked, as if the delay would produce a badge out of possibility. “Because Mr. Julian left instructions regarding—”

“I’m independent,” Mara said. “You can put ‘reporter’ if you need a noun. If Mr. Julian has concerns, he can write them down.”

“Mr. Julian will do that,” Viera said, pleasant as a tide table. “And when he does—”

“We really are walking you out,” Ed said.

“Good,” Mara said. She kept the clipboard in her left hand and her right hand relaxed by the zipper that was not going to unzip any further. As a unit, they moved toward the vestibule. There was a thin delay as the inner doors sensed bodies and hummed open, releasing a thread of air that smelled like lemon cleanser and last Tuesday’s nettle tea.

On the second beat, Hernandez put one arm out—not touching, the kind of barrier that would look fine in a camera. “Hold up. Before the door. Administrator, do you want to note the time?”

Viera slid the pink slip forward and wrote as if she were engraving. “Sixteen fifty‑two,” she said. Her face softened for a moment, the human leak through the administrative shell. “Ms. Pierce, if you want to come onto this property again, you’ll coordinate with me. No more drop‑ins.”

“Understood,” Mara said. “I prefer to ask permission. It just isn’t always granted.”

“Let’s go,” Ed said.

They stepped into the vestibule. The automatic door to the parking lot had a tented sign in front of it that read Please wipe your wheels. Next to it was a woven basket with microfiber cloths, as if wheel hygiene were a spiritual practice. Through the glass, the late light made a road out of the parking stripes and turned the low shrubs into black lace.

Mara took the moment where both doors were open to tilt the clipboard against her chest and use it as a desk. She slid the housekeeping checklist forward a centimeter and wrote a phone number along the top margin with the stub of golf pencil tucked into the metal clip. She folded the corner over it with her left thumb—the motion hidden by the movement of stepping and the attention directed at the door’s slow motor. Then she palmed the tiny triangle off the page and into the cuff of her sleeve. If anyone looked directly at her hands, they’d think she was smoothing a hangnail.

“Ma’am,” Hernandez said, catching the smallness of the shifting and not the content. He didn’t finish the admonition.

“Hands where you like them,” Mara said. She held the clipboard flat and kept her forearms measurable.

Outside now, the air came in with its own vocabulary. A slant of wind pushed smell under the overhang. She could hear a delivery truck idling, the hollowness of the harbor farther off, the gull that always sounded surprised by weather. The recorder was a cool bone against her ribs.

“We’re gonna wait here till you get in your car,” Ed said, like a courtesy.

“I don’t have a car,” Mara said. “I’m not staying.” The lie was true enough in the way most exits are true. She looked down along the loading zone where the door to the ramp sat half‑latched. The strip curtain combed the afternoon. No Cass in sight. Good.

Hernandez shaded his eyes, scanning for an imaginary accomplice. “How’d you get here?”

“Like everybody,” Mara said. “Feet.”

“No,” Ed said. “Not like everybody.”

“What do you want me to do?” Mara asked. “Read you my plans?”

“Just keep moving,” Hernandez said.

She did, but not toward the waiting bench with the decorative anchor on it. She angled toward the cigarette can where three butts bloomed like insults. She reached the brass tube and then, as if interrupted by ethics, turned back.

“Before I go,” she said to Viera, who had followed them to the threshold to supervise the leaving, the exactness of it, “you never answered me. Do you want me to sign out or not? There’s a line on your form.”

Viera’s politeness snapped back on like a lamp. “Fine,” she said. “Bring it here.” She held out her hand.

Mara offered the board, holding it so the top left corner faced Viera and the bottom edge still faced her. The penned phone number triangle had already gone to sleeve. All that remained was an old list of mop closet duties that looked innocuous enough. Viera’s nails clicked against the clip.

“I’ll sign,” Mara said, and wrote her own name in the wrong line anyway, diagonally, larger than the space allowed. It made bureaucracy twitch and satisfied something.

“Date,” Viera said. “Time.”

Mara wrote both. The pencil left a soft, erasable trail, useful to no one. She handed the board up a second time, and as they traded it back and forth, Mara let her right wrist roll in the way that dropped the folded triangle into her own palm. She transferred it to her pocket with the same movement you use to tuck hair behind your ear.

“You doing card tricks now?” Hernandez asked, but he was smiling despite himself.

“I charge extra,” Mara said. Then to Viera, with simple sincerity, “Thank you. For the calm.”

Viera did not answer, but the corner of her mouth made a decision, then unmade it. “Good evening,” she said. “Please be aware that any further contact with residents requires advance clearance.”

“Copy that,” Mara said, and took three steps back into the wind.

Ed and Hernandez stayed just under the overhang, as if the edge of the building were a leash. Ed radioed something that sounded like the word escorted, and Mara cataloged the syllables because that’s what she did: gathered, kept, understood. The radio chirped back, uninterested.

She walked the length of the front row of parking, pausing once as if to choose between cars that were none of hers, then kept going past the blue‑painted curb and the crushed gravel to the public sidewalk where the Harbor Light tastefully pretended to end. Every window on the façade reflected the same sky in slightly different temperaments. A kid on a scooter went by, slow for her, tongue out, dragging the toe of one sneaker to make noise.

At the corner, Mara turned left. The building hid itself behind a willow and the disused bus stop that had been converted into a smoking shelter with a nailed‑on poster about resilience. Her feet found the depressed path that went behind the hedge to the side street. No one followed. If they had, they would have seen a woman rehearse a text in her head and decide not to send it; count to six, out of habit, and not call; catalog the names she had acquired with their adjacent verbs and the one clean number written down, now creasing her pocket into honesty.

She stopped beside a utility box painted to look like a lighthouse. The paint had chipped, salt bite showing in jagged moons. She pulled the little triangle from her pocket and smoothed it against her thigh, reading what she had written. She folded it once more, smaller, and slid it inside the edge binding of her notebook’s back cover, careful, certain.

Then she set off down the street, the recorder warm now from her body, the afternoon yawning toward evening at a manageable pace. She didn’t look back. She didn’t change her speed. She didn’t have to. The door behind her had already sealed. The fish, probably, had found its loop again. The lighthouse on the utility box kept shining in paint the way paint knows how.


==================== SCENE 15 ====================
The door to the boiler room had a sign in block letters: STAFF ONLY. Someone had drawn a smiley face on the O with a grease pencil. Owen ignored it and put his shoulder into the handle. The door stuck on swollen wood, then gave with a grunt. Warm, damp air pressed out and slipped along the corridor into the smell of books.

“Watch the lip,” he said.

Mara stepped over a heaved seam of linoleum and followed him down a flight of iron treads. The rails were cold. The air was a complicated stew—diesel, salt, hot rubber, a sweet hint of antifreeze—and underneath all of it the iron smell of old water.

The room opened in two directions: to the left, the boilers shouldered against one another, painted a color that had probably been optimistic when the cans were opened; to the right, the pump array squatted on its drip pans, valves like articulated elbows at parade rest. A wall fan turned slowly and did nothing but move the heat into different corners.

“Why are the lights in here always theatrical?” Mara said. Fluorescents flickered, making the room blink.

“Because the front of house gets bulbs,” Owen said. He didn’t look like anyone’s idea of a maintenance tech until he laid a hand on metal; then he fit the room. He had a scuffed canvas jacket, a faded cap, and prints of grease across his knuckles that had their own map. “Back here gets whatever feudalism can spare.”

He took her past a rolling cart with two dead pump seals laid out on a rag like coins. The boilers ticked to themselves like cooling beetles.

“Start with circulation,” he said, and slapped the side of a panel box. The cover wobbled. Inside, a row of fuses watched them like cataracted eyes. There was a piece of blue painter’s tape over an alarm LED; someone had written later on it in Sharpie.

“Who wrote ‘later’?” Mara asked.

“I did,” Owen said. “As a joke, at first. Then it turned out to be policy.”

He flipped a switch. A pump down the line made a hard throat‑clearing sound and shivered. The glass eye in the sight fitting showed a blur of water and air going to war.

“That,” Owen said over the noise, “is cavitation. We’ve got microbubbles in the line from the expansion tank and the filters haven’t been changed since the derby days. Hear the high‑pitched scrape? That’s the impeller eating itself. You can run it, but you’re shaving life off with every minute.”

Mara took out her notebook and held her phone over the sight glass. “Seventeen forty‑two,” she said, and hit the shutter. “Loop me in on operating temp, pressure in and out.”

He handed her a temp gun. “Skin temp’s the lie you can afford,” he said, and pointed to the gauge. The needle quivered a little under seventy psi. The face of the dial was fogged from hairline cracks around the bezel.

“Seventeen forty‑two,” Mara repeated. “Sixty‑eight psi on discharge. Inlet?”

“Inlet’s flirting with negative when it shouldn’t.” He traced the line with his finger to a valve where someone had tied the handle in the open position with a nylon zip‑tie. The tie’s excess tail had been trimmed neat. “Whoever did this didn’t want anyone throttling back.”

“‘Whoever’ being?” Mara flipped a page with the side of her thumb.

“We’re all whoever around here,” Owen said. “If I tell Julian I need a new actuator, it goes on a spreadsheet called ‘Behavioral.’ He tells me we can’t ‘incentivize failure’ by rewarding breakage. Then two weeks later we’re in here with a pocket knife making ‘behavioral’ choices because heat doesn’t wait for returns to clear the ethics committee.”

Mara logged: zip‑tied open. “And if we tried to close it now?”

“It’d scream, spit air, the board would call it unprofessional, and we’d be back where we started with a louder problem.” The pump changed tone, less metallic for a moment, and then settled back into its grind. Owen flicked the switch off and the noise subsided to a wet, disappointed sigh. “Next station.”

They passed a panel where an alarm horn had its two wires twisted together and taped—bypassed so it wouldn’t wail. The tape had oxidized to the color of old nicotine.

Mara crouched and photographed the connection. “Seventeen forty‑five. Horn disabled. Label reads LOW SUMP/SPRINKLER.”

Owen’s jaw worked. “It trips at random when the power dips. The building eats power dips like popcorn. Callouts went to the night staff three nights in a row and Julian said it was ‘demoralizing.’ So I made it quiet. Because if you make enough noise around here, you’re not a professional, you’re a problem.”

He walked them to the day tank. It sat on a stand like a glum pig, painted OSHA yellow. There was a sight tube on the side with two bubble lines in it. A piece of masking tape marked FULL in a handwriting that looped its Fs like waves. The actual level was a long way below the tape.

“Fuel,” Owen said. “That’s the number you came for.”

“How bad,” Mara said, already writing.

“Day tank at, call it, nineteen percent,” Owen said. “Bulk tank? We can go look. It’s worse. Delivery’s net ‑ fourteen days because someone wanted a thirty‑day discount on a vendor we stiffed last quarter.”

Mara leaned in and scraped a thumb over the grime on the sight tube to clear a stripe. “Seventeen forty‑seven,” she said. “Nineteen percent. Who’s on the account?”

“Officially? Rourke’s procurement office,” Owen said. “Which means, practically, nobody with a signature. We burned through last week on test runs when the grid hiccuped. But why run test cycles, Owen, when it upsets donors to hear the generator? Because the alternative is it choking when you need it, but what do I know, I only live here.”

Mara glanced at him. “You live on site.”

“I live in the mews behind the kitchen with a rat who eats better than I do,” he said, and smiled without humor. “Let me show you the sumps.”

He led her along a low passage lined with insulated pipes. Condensate dripped with indifferent rhythm into trays someone had improvised from old baking sheets. At the end, three sump pits wore metal lids with cutout handles. The lids were cold and left crescents in Mara’s palms when she lifted one with him.

The light inside the pit was greenish. Water sloshed like a shallow thought. Two pumps sat on bricks, painted flaking blue. One had a float switch whose cord was tied in a knot around the discharge pipe.

Mara pointed. “That’s not what a manual would recommend.”

“It’s what you do when the float sticks low but the water keeps coming,” Owen said. He took a small magnet from his pocket and tapped it against the side of a housing. “And before you ask, yes, that’s a magnet on the reed switch for the high‑water alarm. If we left it alone, it would call the night watch every twenty minutes when the tide breathes and the switch gets tired. So now it doesn’t.”

She tested the magnet and felt it tug. “Seventeen fifty‑one. Float tied up. High‑water alarm held off with magnet.”

“Write ‘I’m the asshole’ under that if you want,” Owen said. “I won’t argue. But if you want ten more buckets in the Rare Books before breakfast, my way is merciful.”

Mara stuck the magnet back where it had been. “I don’t write names next to the word merciful.”

“Try explaining that to a board packet,” he said, and let the lid fall. It chattered like teeth.

They stood, and in the quiet the building made its own tiny sounds: a turn in a duct, a shiver in a pipe, the long mammal‑like exhale of a boiler settling into its story. The library above them—the polished oak, the catalog drawers, the backlog of request slips clipped to a string—felt far away and made of different priorities.

“What about sprinklers?” Mara said.

“Standpipe pressure’s okay if you don’t look too hard at okay,” Owen said, and moved to a red‑painted globe valve with its handwheel chained open. A padlock held the chain in a formal loop. “Top floor branching is the problem. Old iron. We cleaned the heads last winter, but there’s a run above the Essex Room that eats pressure like a boxer. If the jockey pump kicks on, you hear it punching air for a good ten seconds before it finds itself.”

“Jockey pump,” Mara said, writing. “That’s what, two horsepower?”

“Two if you’re generous and you like rounding up,” he said. He put his hand on a small motor housing. It was warm. There was a coffee ring on the top of it where he’d set a cup and forgotten. “And it’s on a breaker someone oversized because the original popped whenever a heater cycled.”

“You oversized the breaker,” Mara said.

“No,” he said, and looked at her in a way that was either approval or disappointment. “Someone brought me a breaker out of a closet because the label said the right number, and it wasn’t. We use what we have. Or we wait. Waiting is how they like it upstairs. They think clean reports are adult. I think reality is adult.”

“Upstairs,” Mara said. “Names.”

“You have them,” Owen said, and gave the motor casing a pat that made it hum a question. “Julian thinks pipes are behavior. Maureen thinks pipes are a past that owes her something. Nora thinks pipes are a liability. Cass thinks pipes are content unless they’re not. Viera thinks pipes should never men­tion themselves in public. Rourke thinks pipes exist to demonstrate chain of custody. Alistair thinks pipes should’ve been copper because copper is a good story. Meanwhile, it’s always water. It doesn’t care.”

Mara wrote the list in her head, not the notebook. “Theo?” she said.

Owen snorted. “Theo thinks air is a runway. Come on.”

He took her through a door with a ruined EXIT sign and into a narrower room with a low window set at ground level. Rust had drawn a map around its frame. Above the window, a vent grille buzzed with a nervous fly. On the workbench, there were two drone props, a set of little carbon‑fiber arms, and a bin of watch batteries.

“We found this junk in the gutter last week after his ‘survey,’” Owen said. “He flies close. Lost one into the intake louvers because the wind shears off the south wall, and then I had to pull the screen to get it out, and then the screen didn’t go back right because the frame rotted while we were waiting for money that’s always on the other side of a sentence. See this?”

He tapped the intake screen. It had been bent and then hammered flat again. The mesh had a bright rectangle where it was newer.

“Open that at the wrong time, you get salt fog straight down the line,” he said. “Salt fog and electrics make a marriage.”

“A bad one,” Mara said.

“I didn’t say that. Some marriages like to eat money,” he said. “He also likes to plug his battery chargers anywhere he finds an outlet. I’ve pulled him off the generator transfer panel three times. ‘It’s just a trickle, man.’ Yeah? So is rain.”

“Seventeen fifty‑eight,” Mara said, and took a photo of the intake screen and the drone bits. “I need to talk to him about access. For now, keep him off anything with a lock.”

“Locks,” Owen said. “He brought me a bump key set. Says it’s for his YouTube. He calls it education. I told him education is knowing when someone’s going to be mad.”

Mara tucked that away. “Fuel bulk tank,” she said.

“Through here.” He kicked at a piece of plywood propped against a doorway. It slid down and showed the tank’s end: a dull cylinder with gauges like old car speedometers. The glass was cloudy and had the rainbow of looked‑at for too long. There was a dipstick with a notch filed into it.

“I don’t trust the gauge,” Owen said. “I trust the stick. But the stick gives you an honesty nobody upstairs wants to have to hear.”

He dragged the stick out with both hands and laid it flat across the bench, then checked his sleeve for the chalk line he’d made earlier. “At this morning’s read, we were at here.” He tapped a mark. “After running boiler three for four hours today, we’re at—” He shoved the stick back in and drew it out again. The wet line stopped below the earlier chalk. “—here.”

Mara bent, sniffed the stick, and wiped the tip on a rag to get the edge cleaner. She set her phone next to it and took a picture with the timestamp on screen. “Eighteen oh‑three. Bulk tank dipped one notch since ten hundred.” She drew a line on the stick with his chalk. “Percentage?”

“Gauges say thirty,” Owen said, “stick says twenty‑two if you’re generous and I am not.” He thumped the side of the tank with the heel of his hand. It answered in a tone that wasn’t confidence. “Delivery is scheduled for a week from Tuesday if nobody gets their feelings ahead of a phone call.”

“Is there a way to lock the day tank feed to non‑essential?” Mara asked.

“Define essential,” Owen said. “Heat is essential. Sprinklers are essential if you don’t like fire being a personality trait. The generator is essential when the grid shrugs. The elevator is essential if you don’t want to carry a resident with a broken hip down stairs. The kitchen tells me a hot line is essential because donors like soup.”

“The library climate control,” Mara said.

Owen made a face. “You mean the dehumidification that keeps the books from turning into saltines? That’s essential until someone with a title wants to open a window ‘for air.’ Then it’s a luxury.”

She wrote: climate essential; prioritize. “Safeties,” she said. “Which ones are bypassed right now that you’re aware of.”

He didn’t flinch. “Low fuel alarm on the day tank is taped. Jockey pump breaker oversized. Sump high‑water is magneted. Two relief valves are safety‑wired open because they chatter and waking up at three a.m. to a howler is considered dramatic. The fire pump’s pressure transducer is old enough to vote and lies by three to five psi. The boiler low‑water cutoffs are good because I’m superstitious about those. The flue monitor is probably honest, but the stack cap blew off in the last blow and Theo used the ladder I had up to film a cloud at sunset and then left the ladder standing, and Viera walked by and told me the ladder was unsightly.”

“Eighteen oh‑seven,” Mara said, and kept her hand steady while she took down the list. “You’re saying we’ve taken systems designed to holler and asked them to whisper.”

“I’m saying we turned off the scream because the scream made someone feel criticized,” Owen said. “I’d rather be criticized than flooded. But I don’t run the meeting where pictures get taken.”

He walked to a whiteboard with a chart he’d made by hand: columns for pump hours, fuel, filter status, alarms. The markers were dry. He’d used pencil in some rows where the pen didn’t take. Under today’s date he’d written: grid 96% stable, gen test 40 min, boiler 3 on, boiler 2 delayed, filters 3/6.

“Cascade risks,” Mara said. “Let’s name them. For the file.”

“File,” he said, tasting the word. “Okay. If we run out of fuel, heat goes cold and the boiler lockouts are honest enough to stay that way. No heat, humidity climbs, the books sweat, the drywall sweats, mold says hello. If a pump eats itself—and one’s nibbling—circulation stops and we get hot spots and air pockets, which means more cavitation, which means noise and complaints and shut‑offs, which means thermal stress and cracked fittings. If the sumps fall behind, Essex Room floods first because it sits over the old coal trench. If the jockey fails or the fire pump doesn’t get the signal because the pressure transducer lies, the sprinklers don’t move until someone’s here to push, and that’s a bad day with dry pipes.”

“If the day tank runs dry,” Mara said.

“We get air in the line, the generator can’t prime, and you don’t have lights to find the flashlight drawer with the flashlight in it,” he said. “If the intake screen lets in fog because someone left it open to fish a drone, the transfer switch cabinet doesn’t love salt and you get ghost trips. If Theo keeps plugging into the wrong circuits, he’ll toast a breaker that should be saved for a call that matters.”

“And if you keep bypassing safeties,” Mara said.

“I keep us not getting screamed at at dinner,” he said. “That’s not a defense. That’s a description.”

Mara tapped the face of one gauge and watched the needle bounce. She took a pen and, on blue painter’s tape, wrote the time and stuck the tape to the gauge glass: Eighteen twelve. She did the same on the fuel sight, the bulk tank stick, the sump lid, the intake screen, the jerry‑rigged horn.

“What’s that for,” Owen asked, “aside from my sense of being audited.”

“Memory is biddable,” Mara said. “This is a timestamp you don’t need a password to see. If someone comes in and moves a valve or peels tape off a light, we’ll know when the before was.”

He watched her do the last one. “You really think someone’s going to peel tape off a light because it makes a better picture.”

“I think the picture is already made,” she said. “I think I don’t like arguing with the past. This helps.”

He leaned his hip against the bench and rubbed a thumb under his jaw where stubble had found a second wind. “You know what the house is,” he said, quieter.

“It’s expensive,” she said.

“It’s leverage,” he said. “You can make a lot of things happen if you touch the right valve at the right time. Water, heat, fans, doors. That’s why I lock what I can. That’s why I sleep like a dog with one ear open.”

Mara nodded, not writing that down, just letting it hang. “We need a lockout list for Theo,” she said. “We need delivery confirmation for fuel. We need alarms that are allowed to call for help without worrying about Julian’s feelings.”

“Good,” Owen said. “Put it in a sentence with your name on it so when somebody gets mad they don’t use mine.”

“Copy,” she said. She checked the phone’s clock. Eighteen fifteen. The fluorescents flickered again and settled. The room seemed to hold its breath.

In the silence, above them, a cart rolled over a seam in the library floor and clicked. A page turned. Air moved in a duct in a long, calm draw.

“Is that it,” Owen asked, “or you want to see how the sewer ejector makes poetry.”

“Poetry later,” Mara said. “For now, we’re documenting, not revising.”

He laughed once. “Careful. They’ll quote you.”

“They can,” she said. She stuck the last piece of tape to the chain on the sprinkler valve and wrote: Eighteen sixteen. Her hand was steady and her letters small.

“What does that say,” he asked, leaning.

“Now,” she said. “It says now.”


==================== SCENE 16 ====================
They climbed the service stairs two flights, past a fan cage that hummed in small, insective complaint, past a coil of heavy rope looped like a sleeping snake on a landing, up to the last door with the brass hasp. Owen palmed the padlock, keyed it, and shouldered through. Cold evening air poured down the stairwell, smelling of salt and tar.

The roof hatch was a squared‑off bite through old felt and new EPDM. At the lip, where the flashing met the aluminum frame, Owen crouched. “Don’t step on the marks,” he said. “If you can help it.”

“What marks,” Mara said, and then saw them. A pale crescent of abraded tar, two thumbnail dings on the aluminum, and a faint powdering of grit where something had touched and pivoted. Fresh enough that the grit still sat where the prop wash would have blown it if anyone had done much else up here.

Owen tapped with a knuckle. “Landing legs. The rubber feet leave a kind of clean. See the twin? Same spacing. Four legs, but only two load when you flare to set down. Somebody learned it here, not on grass. The paint on the hatch makes a nice target.”

Mara bent, eyes level with the plane of the roof, and measured the distance with her fingers, then with a tape she took out of her pocket. “Thirteen inches center to center,” she said.

“Common pattern,” he said. “Mavic class, maybe an Autel. Theo’s got both.”

He pointed to another arc where the topcoat had been lightly kissed, a half fingerprint of silver raw under oxidized gray. “Prop,” he said. “He let one idle too close. You get lazy when you think you own the sky.”

“Who owns the sky,” Mara asked, standing. The horizon line cut the sea low and steady, a dull aluminum sheet under the cloud. Gulls rode it. The evening was all edges.

“People who can afford batteries,” he said. He gestured. “Come on.”

He led her to the widow’s‑walk rail. The spindles had fresh divots—a sequence of tiny nicks on the top rail where hard plastic had taken bites. A stair of marks, one at each post.

“Hooked a lanyard?” Mara asked.

“Zip‑tie. Tied his tablet sling to it so he could set both hands left‑right on the sticks and not worry about dropping the screen.” Owen’s face had a tightness to it, not anger, not yet, just the habit of somebody taking in angles. “Also here.” He crouched at a windward corner where the flashing lifted and revealed a thumb of wood. “Hot shoe for a booster antenna. He used the magnet mount; it pulled this up the first time he took it off. That scrape right there? That’s from the steel foot.”

Mara took a photo, then another, with a clean card and a second phone. “What were his hours.”

“Let me show you.” Owen pulled a cracked‑cornered tablet from his jacket, the case tattooed with a map of oil and thumb salts. He thumbed through. “He thinks he deletes, but the controllers cache. Also, he saved his flight tracks to brag.” A page opened with a neat list of dates and times. He scrolled with a rashed thumb. “Day zero, before you got here: eighteen‑twenty, nineteen‑ten, twenty‑two‑oh‑four quick up‑down. Yesterday,” he flicked, “zero‑six‑fifty‑eight for three minutes, then noon, then sixteen thirty‑two. Today?” He coughed a dry laugh. “He gave himself a triple medal. Nine‑ten, nine‑forty‑four, and again at fourteen hundred and… forty‑eight.”

Mara watched the thin red spaghetti of tracks draw themselves over the property. Each line annotated: altitude, distance, battery percentage. Tail numbers—FAA reg IDs—sat in the corner of each log with little stars and the names Theo had given them. “N72TF,” she read. “And NR‑X0, which isn’t even a number, but all right.”

“He put vanity plates on them,” Owen said. “The real reg is in the app info. Here.” He opened a nested menu with the resigned skill of someone who’d been practicing for court. “Airframe one: N7245T, registered to a shell address in Peabody. Airframe two, the orange one—Autel—no FAA sticker at first; he added one after I told him. Reads ‘N19TEO,’ which is nothing, because he made it on a labeler. But the serial is here.” He slid the screen closer. “Take it down.”

Mara took it down. She wrote, on the back of a folded envelope, two tails and two serials, and under that the flight windows like a grocery list for a house that had nothing edible in it. 06:58–07:01. 12:03–12:22. 16:32–17:08. 09:10–09:16. 09:44–10:02. 14:48–15:11. “He flies when the light’s raking,” she said. “Edges.”

Owen squinted at the far corner of the roof. The greenhouse lay below like a lung. The library’s high windows were black squares. The grounds fell away to clipped yew and the gravel ellipse where deliveries turned. He held the tablet up. “This line,” he said, touching the red thread, “is the pass over the greenhouse ridge and the cut to the bell tower. See the dip? He dropped under the power run to keep line‑of‑sight free of those sycamores. He steps up here to clear the widow’s‑walk finials and then loiters above the east gutter for twenty seconds.”

“Why loiter,” Mara asked, not looking at the line but at the way wind made a skin of the bay pucker. Her hair was in her mouth; she pulled it back without thinking.

“Because there’s a slit in the eave above Nora’s workroom. Vent screen slumped. See?” He pointed down. From above, the gap was visible as a dark, tidy wound. “He’s not just taking property porn. He’s peeking.”

“Curiosity,” Mara said, flat. “And an ability to distribute curiosity later, at scale.”

He angled the tablet again. “Here’s a ground pass. He ran a low transect along the service lane yesterday, waist height, as if he were tasting. There’s a little wobble over the sump vent. He got ground effect wrong and kissed the grate. Which is why your painter’s tape’s going to tell us if he reaches in there. He’ll try to scrub the scuff.”

They moved along the rail. Mara put her palm against the old wood and felt it vibrate with the little world’s wind. Owen pointed out a string horizontally across the interior of the railing at belly height. It was thin, a fishing leader, anchored with electrical tape and sunk screw eyes.

“What,” she said.

“Sight line,” he said. “Tensioned enough to hold a laser pointer so he could level his frame before dark. He was teaching himself to make pretty, but also to come back to the same frame again. He does loops. He matches them. That’s how you make a before and after without leaving footprints.”

Mara traced it with her index finger. “He ran it here and here,” she said. “That’s two positions. Opposite corners.”

“Yeah,” Owen said. “So watch. From here you get the greenhouse, the houseface, and the staff cottage. From there,” he nodded to the northwest corner, “you get the cliff path, the funicular run, and the boathouse roof.”

“Privacy was invented for people who can afford it,” Mara said. “Everyone else stalls for it until they’re alone in the bathroom.”

“You’re not wrong,” Owen said. Down on the lawn a sprinkler head clicked as if remembering its childhood. The day heaved and let go. He handed her the tablet. “You drive.”

She frowned at him, then pinched to zoom. The map centered. The last track traced a clean figure eight over the front court, then a straight shot out to the boundary fence and back. “He teased the neighbors,” she said.

“He learned where the watchers are,” Owen answered. “Someone on the abutter’s deck thinks this is a toy and waves; someone else writes a letter. He wants both.”

“What’s he doing with the video,” Mara asked.

“Compiling. Talking to himself. Listening to his own movie.” Owen’s mouth held a line that tasted of salt. “I told him to bring me his tail numbers—real ones. He laughed. He said, ‘It’s not like I’m flying over airports.’”

Mara set the tablet down on the hatch frame and looked along the lines the roof made. She opened her notebook. “We’re mapping line‑of‑sight,” she said. “Start with the obvious. Roof to greenhouse ridge. Roof to east gutter. Roof to bell tower. Roof to staff cottage back stair. Roof to laundry vents. Roof to funicular track head.”

“And to the pantry skylight,” Owen said. “And the cistern hatch when it’s open. And the compost heap because he likes a gross thing moving.”

“You know him,” she said. It wasn’t a compliment, but it wasn’t nothing.

“I’m paid to know what breaks,” he said. He put out his arm and sighted down it like an old yard surveyor. “There. That phone pole is your constraint. He either flies over it, or he cuts under and risks a branch. He’ll go over. Look at the wind drift; he’ll crab left and correct on the drop. His line brings him parallel to the long axis of the library windows. He does it because a diagonal reads more flattering. He’s not stupid. He’s just excited.”

“And excitement doesn’t ask,” Mara said. She tore a sheet and began sketching. The roof a rectangle, the greenhouse another, the track lines like veins. She labeled them with the times, with arrows: 06:58 low sun east. 12:03 top light—flat, but windows mirror. 16:32 golden hour striping boards. “Tail numbers,” she said, and wrote: N7245T—DJI. Autel—serial: AU‑OE‑… She left the end blank until Owen read it off.

He read it off. She filled it in.

“On the ground too,” he said, and led her down the hatch and around the side of the house to the lawn. It was the kind of clipped green that made shoes whisper. He took her to the gravel where a shallow fan of tiny scratches showed a repeated set‑down by something the weight of a lunchbox. “Pads,” he said. “He used a landing pad sometimes; other times he didn’t. When he didn’t, he made this. Rotor wash flings the fines outward and leaves a crescent. Count the crescents.”

Mara kneeled. There were four, offset, as if he’d tried once, adjusted, set again, then gotten impatient and launched without it. “Where’s line‑of‑sight here,” she asked.

He pointed between two yews trimmed to bells. They made a channel that framed the kitchen doors and the service porch. “He flies through those bells at four feet. You can hear it under your ribs, that note, when he clips a leaf.”

She stood, brushed grit off her jeans, and turned in a slow circle. The boathouse roof was a blind slate. The gazebo’s copper cap flared green. Out at the edge, beyond the herb knot, a stake with a little reflective tag at eye level showed itself only when the light hit it. “What’s that.”

“A makeshift VLOS marker,” Owen said. “He stuck it where he could see it from the roof. It catches sunset and the drone’s belly light. If you’re bored, look at the bark on that willow. He shaved it once. Prop rash. Look low.”

She walked to the willow and saw, at shin height, a pale scrape where the blade had kissed the living skin. She ran a finger over it and felt the fibers lifted. “It’s not curiosity,” she said. “It’s acquisition. He approached the house like a toy.”

“And the house let him,” Owen said. He held out his hand for the notebook and she gave it to him. He wrote with a carpenter’s pencil and the letters were angry by their nature. “Tail: N7245T—06:58, 12:03, 16:32. Autel serial ending …H72—09:10, 09:44, 14:48. Common paths: Roof hatch to greenhouse ridge; roof northeast corner to pantry skylight; lawn pad to service porch; east steps to cliff path. LOS constraints: pole at library northeast, sycamores mid‑lawn, gazebo cap at 278 degrees. Comfort winds: light southerly. He doesn’t fly north wind.”

Mara took it back and underlined lines of sight. “We’ll stand in the windows and find the angles the camera likes,” she said. “If he can see, anyone can. If the camera can settle, anyone can settle.”

“Yeah,” Owen said. He looked tired, as if a new thing had been added to a stack just when the stack had looked stable. “I want a lock on this hatch that tells me when it’s opened and by who. I want a place to put the batteries that isn’t Theo’s backpack. I want a fence, but not the kind you can see.”

“Start with the list,” Mara said. “Tail numbers, flight windows, line‑of‑sight paths. We’re not arguing with the past.”

He looked at the scuffs on the aluminum again, measuring with his eyes. “I told him to fly over water if he had to fly,” he said. “He said there’s more to look at here.”

“And there is,” Mara answered. She tore another page, stuck it to the inside of the hatch with a piece of her own blue tape from her pocket, and wrote without flourish: N7245T; AU‑…H72. 06:58–07:01. 09:10–09:16. 09:44–10:02. 12:03–12:22. 14:48–15:11. 16:32–17:08. LOS: roof→greenhouse; roof→east gutter; roof→bell tower; roof→pantry; lawn pad→service porch; east steps→cliff path. “So when someone asks,” she said, “we don’t have to ask our memory.”

Owen nodded. He put his hand flat on the tape like a promise, then took it off again. The wind lifted the corner, thought about it, and let it be. They stood leaning on the rail of a house that had learned to listen, the tablet ticking down battery on its own private clock, the sky taking its time getting dark.


==================== SCENE 17 ====================
The reading room had the last of the daylight banked in its glass — a flat, exhausted blue beyond the windows — and the lamps made pools on the long table where Mara had spread the floor plans. The vellum sheets were old enough to curl at the edges, the newer copies crisp and bright with a printer’s scowl. A paperweight shaped like a lead whale held down the library wing. A chipped mug weighted the service corridor. The house, laid open, looked like something that could be counted and therefore controlled.

Owen came in carrying the caddy he used when he didn’t trust a toolbox — a wire handle, an assortment of pliers and tags, a flashlight set to its lowest lumen. He set it on a corner, glanced at the plans, then went straight to the small rolling whiteboard Mara had dragged out and drew a square with an X, then another, then a row of circles that might be tanks.

He didn’t sit. “You want systems. Fine. Fuel first.”

She pushed a plan toward him with her knuckles. “Generator, boiler, emergency pump. Show me the path.”

He put the carpenter’s pencil down and placed his fingertip on the east corner of the basement plan where someone had once written MECH in block print. “Bulk tank’s under the carriage room. Three hundred seventy-five gallons nominal. Transfer pump is here. Inline filter, cracked bowl, hairline. I’ve been babying it. It feeds along this run,” he traced the line under the pantry, “to the day tank by the generator. That’s sixty gallons. Return line here. The boiler got its own day tank back when, but someone yoked it to the same feed with a T and a ball valve that sticks.”

Mara made a square of blue tape around the T. “Show me where a human hand can do something stupid.”

“Transfer switch on the pump is manual-remote. The remote is a lighted toggle by the carriage door. Anyone in the room can dump fuel uphill by holding down that switch. If the return valve’s closed or clogged, you pressurize the day tank. You also aerate the lines if you cycle it wrong. You can also flood the boiler tray if you want to be creative.” He pointed to a little rectangle Mara hadn’t labeled. “There’s a drip pan. It’s not big.”

“Stupid or malicious?” Mara asked.

“Either.” He shrugged. “You want clean words? Capability.”

She wrote CAP: REMOTE TRANSFER SWITCH and underlined it. “Mitigation?”

“Lockable cover on the toggle. Lock-open on the return. Filter bowl replaced, not patched. And we isolate the boiler feed. It’s on my list.”

“Tonight?”

“Bowl and cover tonight, lock-open is a parts run.”

Mara nodded and wrote R: COVER; BOWL; TEMP TAG RETURN OPEN. She moved to the next sheet — alarms, the old bronze of the annunciator diagram printed in a color the copy shop had called “sepia” like that made it gentle. “Alarms.”

Owen exhaled through his nose. “You found call bells and AV. That’s one limb. The house system — actual fire and intrusion — runs off two panels. Main in the reading room closet behind the map case. Secondary by the kitchen office. Some zones are still the old wire loop, some are wireless. The panic paddles by the beds tie into a loop that can be shorted with a paperclip. Not that anyone would ever do that.”

He reached behind the map case as if to demonstrate and found the recessed panel with his fingertips. He didn’t open it. “The siren staging is built dumb. There’s a relay. You trip one zone and you blow sound in four wings whether you need to or not. It’s attention theatre. Lots of sound, lots of light.”

“Device,” Mara said, almost to herself. “Sound to move people. Panic to shape the map.”

Owen nodded. “And it’s got a silence key on a little brass hook. Which is… missing.”

Mara stopped writing and looked up at him. “When?”

“Not today. I keep a duplicate, so functionally it doesn’t matter. But it is missing.”

She boxed the word MISSING and added FIND/REKEY. “What else on alarms.”

“You can mask zones in software if you know the codes. You can name a wing ‘sleep’ and it will ignore a breach in the middle of the night while still telling the panel, not the siren. That’s not hypothetical. That’s a feature the board liked for events so guests wouldn’t be disturbed if a door banged.”

She wrote CAP: MASK ZONES SILENT. “Mitigation?”

“Pull logs. Change codes. Kill remote programming. And we cold-label the paddles — numbered seal tape that breaks if you mess with it.”

She done with the panel, she tapped the greenhouse corner of the plan even though the glasshouse wasn’t technically on this floor. “Vents.”

“The house is a flute,” Owen said. He leaned over the plan and tapped the dotted squares. “Original wind-driven ventilation up here in the cornice. Operable louvers still work in three bays. The reading room has transom vents to the corridor. The service corridor has grilles to the vertical chase that runs from the laundry to the roof. That chase also carries cable, now, because someone liked how ready-made it was. The kitchen exhaust makes a negative pressure that drags air through all that when it’s on high.”

“If you wanted to move something through the house,” Mara said.

“Smell, smoke, sound, dust — yes. You drop a pellet in the grille here,” he tapped above the pantry door on the plan, “you get a trace in the reading room in under five minutes if the kitchen’s hood is drawing. You put anything airborne into the greenhouse fan intake or the service porch louver, you get it in the nursery wing if the transoms are open. We prop transoms in summer because the windows stick.”

“What about combustion.”

“You’re not going to get flashover with the materials we’ve got unless you try. You can get a lot of stupid with fog machines or bug bombs. You fog, the detectors see it as smoke. Alarms trip, doors open, people move. You can also starve the boiler of air and get soot, which sucks but is not exciting unless you want to cough.”

“Sprinklers?”

“Dry pipes in the hall ceilings from the 80s retrofits. Head coverage is spotty. The riser for this wing,” he pointed to a closet on the plan by the main stairs, “is in a not-locked closet with a wheel valve you could spin. Someone spun it last season to stop a drip and didn’t tell me. We did a flow test and found it dead.”

Mara circled the riser. “Mitigation.”

“Chain and tag the wheel valves. Tamper switches wired to local buzzers so you can’t turn them without someone hearing it.”

She wrote: CHAIN/TAG RISERS; LOCAL TAMPER BUZZERS. Then, “Pumps.”

Owen rolled his shoulders like the word was a knot he recognized. “Sump pumps — two in the basement, tied to the French drain. One at the greenhouse pit to keep the beds happy. One transfer pump from the cistern to the kitchen for grey use, because someone liked the romance of ‘cistern water’ and then we remembered bacteria exist. The greenhouse pump’s on the same circuit as the grow lights, which is… not great. You can trip that breaker and kill both. The basement sumps are on separate circuits but share a check valve that backflows if grit gets in it.”

“And the house can be…”

“Flooded. Or just… softened. You stall the sumps, you invite water, you keep people in certain rooms because the corridor’s wet. Again, not theoretical. It’s a capability.”

“Mitigation.”

“Separate the greenhouse pump circuit tonight. We can run a temporary cord from the workshop panel and label it. Swap the check valve this week. Install a second float switch as a high-water alarm in the basement with a screaming buzzer. No tie to the main panel — local loud.”

Mara wrote: TEMP CIRCUIT GREENHOUSE PUMP; NEW CHECK; HI-WATER BUZZER LOCAL. She kept going. “What am I missing.”

“Gas,” Owen said. He half-spread his hands and then put them down. “Propane bottles for the deck heaters are in the carriage room. There’s a cage. The cage is welded. The lock is not bad. But a fool with time can take a bottle, put it under a window, open the valve. You don’t get an explosion — that’s not how it works with ventilation like this — but you get smell, confusion, calls.”

“Mitigation.”

“Move the bottles farther from the envelope. Put chain through the handles and use a better lock. Quick add: the lawn shed has jerrycans. Red. Marked. Half full. They move.”

“Log them,” Mara said. “Count them morning and night. Take a picture. Put a piece of tape on each with today’s date and my initials. If it walks, it glares.”

He allowed the corner of his mouth to move. “Tape glare. Okay.”

She nudged the plan toward the servants’ corridor. “Weird little things.”

“Dumbwaiter,” he said immediately. “It’s bricked at the kitchen level but the shaft still exists, and the counterweight box is in a closet behind the scullery. You can drop anything down it from the second floor and it will hit that box with a slap. If you want a noise when someone’s in the kitchen, you can make a noise. The counterweight still rides in its runner, so you can also jam it, which is fun when you want to pin a door.”

“Mitigation.”

“Board it at the second floor opening tonight. Screw, not tape. And we wedge the counterweight with a shim that can’t back out unless you know where to pull.”

“Call bells,” Mara said, because she wanted it on the table in plain words.

“Buzzer wire runs in the same conduit as the nurse-call that got installed in the seventies.” He straightened a pencil on the plan. “You want to be a child, you can tie in a battery and make it ring whenever you feel like it. The annunciator board here is pretty, which is to say it’s dumb. No logs. You see a lamp, you go. That’s it.”

“Mitigation.”

“Kill power to the old annunciator. Leave the board lit for show if you like, but feed it from a dead source. We make the path out of service without telegraphing it.”

Mara was writing more than she was speaking now. The list had a muscle. It wanted to be long. She forced it to be clear. She wrote: ANNUNCIATOR DEPOWERED; BOARD ON ISOLATED SUPPLY; PADLOCK REMOTE TRANSFER; RETURN VALVE LOCK-OPEN; REPLACE FILTER BOWL; ISOLATE BOILER FEED; CHANGE ALARM CODES; PULL LOGS 30 DAYS; DISABLE REMOTE PROGRAMMING; SEAL TAPE PANIC PADDLES; CHAIN RISER VALVES; ADD LOCAL TAMPER BUZZERS; TEMP CIRCUIT GH PUMP; REPLACE SUMPS CHECK; ADD HI-WATER BUZZER; PROPANE CAGE RELOCATION/CHAIN; JERRYCANS INVENTORY AM/PM; DUMBWAITER 2F BOARD/COUNTERWEIGHT WEDGE; CALL BELL ANNUNCIATOR ISOLATION.

“Keep going,” she said softly.

He studied the plan as if it might flip on its back and show him a simpler side. “Windows,” he said at last. “You can open them from the outside if the stops are loose. The little brass pins — half the pins are missing. If you know the window that sticks and you know the sash you can lift with a knife, you can walk into the reading room at night while everyone’s listening to the storm and you can be a story.”

“Mitigation.”

“Replace pins. Tonight we push furniture against the ones with missing hardware. It’s ugly but it speaks.”

“Vents again.” Mara put her finger on the corridor transoms. “We can’t seal the house.”

“No. But we can close transoms and put blue tape in an X across the ones that don’t latch. Not a seal. A tell. If an X breaks, we know it. We can also fit cheap filters behind the transom grilles in the corridor — magnetic ones from the hardware store — not to stop malice, but to slow anything airborne long enough to notice a smell before it’s in the room.”

She wrote: TRANSOMS CLOSED/TAPED X; MAG FILTERS CORRIDOR GRILLES. “Kitchen hood,” she said. “Negative pressure.”

“Set it to low as default. Lock the high speed behind a switch in the kitchen office. You want high, you pass a person to get it. And we cut power to the greenhouse intake fan after hours. It’s wired constant because someone thought plants are people.”

“Spray systems?” she asked.

He pointed to a scrawl in the greenhouse margin. “Misting manifold is manual. Hose bib in the service yard has a backflow preventer that’s old. If it fails, someone can draw from a bucket if they remember how pressure works. Replace the preventer. Put caps on the hose ends. People laugh at caps until they don’t.”

Mara wrote: REPLACE BACKFLOW PREVENTER; HOSE CAPS. She flipped to an elevation, the reading room side, the big windows and the fluted radiators. “Heat.”

“Steam,” he said. “Single pipe. You can hammer a radiator to scare a person into a wrong decision. That’s not a system threat, that’s a heart rate trick. The boiler pressuretrol is set conservative. You crank it, you get a little more heat and noise. Someone who wants to perform can use heat to make a room unbearable.”

“Mitigation.”

“Guard on the pressuretrol. Wire the sight glass light so you can see level without opening the door. Put a tamper seal on the fuel screw. None of this is permanent — we’re making it so you have to make a decision, not an impulse.”

She checked those off, too.

He touched the edge of the east wing plan with one finger. “Service porch door,” he said. “Closer’s weak. If the latch doesn’t catch, the wind will do the rest. You want movement, you prop a door. Suddenly you’ve got a corridor that breathes.”

“Mitigation.”

“Adjust the striker tonight. Stick a broom handle through the push bar bracket and zip-tie it at night. It’s ugly. It works.”

They were quiet for a moment, the house tick-ticking in the walls where hot met cool. The lamps hummed at a frequency that felt like held breath.

“You know what I hate,” Owen said.

“What.”

“That this reads like an instruction manual.”

“It reads like a rehearsal,” Mara said. She didn’t smile, and he didn’t either.

He put two dots on the plan with the pencil. “Fuel and siren,” he said. “If you want ‘urgent’ as a category, it’s those. Fuel line’s fragile. Siren key’s missing. If you can move fuel and you can move people with sound, you can do a lot with very little.”

Mara drew a square around FUEL LINE INTEGRITY and SIREN/SILENCE CONTROL and, next to each, wrote URGENT in block print. She pulled a red pen from her breast pocket and, after a half second of visible dislike, used it. Two red boxes. She added a third around MASKABLE ZONES, and a fourth around GREENHOUSE PUMP CIRCUIT / BASEMENT CHECK VALVE.

“Why that one,” he asked, touching the greenhouse note.

“Because water hides intent,” she said. “And because we can fix that on a table with two cords.”

He thought about that and nodded once. “Add this,” he said. “Label the remote transfer switch at the carriage room in words a stranger would understand. Not the electrician’s poetry. Big letters: DO NOT HOLD DOWN.”

“People ignore ‘Do not,’” she said, writing it anyway.

“Then add: ‘Causes spill.’”

She underlined causes.

He crouched and opened his caddy, came up with two lengths of chain, a small brass hasp, a handful of numbered tags, a fake leather pouch that held a lockout hasp like a spider. “We can do four of these now,” he said. “Riser chain, transfer switch cover, pressuretrol guard, and the porch bar zip-tie. The rest is a morning. The filters are a hardware run.”

Mara slid the plans toward the edge until the lead whale almost tipped and then thought better of turning the house into a floor event. She started a second sheet titled: Night Mitigations. She listed: RISER CHAIN/TAG; TRANSFER TOGGLE COVER INSTALLED; PRESSURETROL GUARD; SERVICE PORCH BAR ZIP-TIE; TRANSOMS CLOSED/TAPED X; KITCHEN HOOD DEFAULT LOW/LOCK HIGH; GREENHOUSE INTAKE OFF; ANNUNCIATOR BOARD ISOLATED; DUMBWAITER 2F BOARD; COUNTERWEIGHT WEDGE; PROPANE CAGE TEMP MOVE/CHAIN; JERRYCAN COUNT/PHOTO; WINDOW PINS TEMP BLOCK/FURNITURE; PANIC PADDLES SEAL TAPE.

She tapped her pen against URGENT twice. “Fuel. Siren. Masks. Pumps.”

Owen looked at the board behind the map case again and then at the map case and then back. “You want me to open it,” he asked.

“Not yet. I want a witness, and I want a photo with the tag numbers visible.”

He tapped the numbered tags. “You.”

“Me,” she said. “If you’re the only set of eyes, you’re the only mouth, and I don’t like that on anything that touches a switch.”

He squinted at the plans, then at the window where the blue had gone to a heavier shade. “I’ve got enough chain,” he said.

“Good.” She stood and stacked the plans by urgency. “Walk me through fuel in person after we chain the riser.”

“We’re doing the porch first,” he said. “No point in speaking if the house can breathe through its lungs while we talk.”

“Porch,” she agreed. She tore the Night Mitigations list halfway down, folded it, and slid half into her pocket. The other half she affixed to the corner of the table with the blue tape she preferred over the house’s sad, yellowing roll. On the bottom, she wrote, in a smaller hand, FURTHER: CHANGE CODES; LOGS; BACKFLOW; FILTERS; VALVE LOCK-OPEN; REPLACE FILTER BOWL; LABELS IN PLAIN LANGUAGE, then drew a thin line beneath it and a box around the word START.

Owen took up the chain and the hasp and the numbered tags and slung the caddy in the crook of his arm. “You really think tape glares,” he said, as if trying the words on again.

“It does if you put it where people look,” she said.
