They left the reading room with the lamps still burning and the plans set like a blueprint for intention. In the doorway, she turned back and, with the red pen still in her hand, circled the two red URGENT boxes again until they were unmistakable from across the room. Then she capped the pen and put it away.


==================== SCENE 18 ====================
The Essex Historical Room had that soft, constant page‑smell that seems to rise from boxes themselves: a breath of starch and old paste, a thin sweetness of photo envelopes, the dry bark of cardboard. Morning made a pale rectangle on the long table, bright enough to show the rub marks where hands had worried the varnish into gloss. In the corner, the microfilm reader’s fan made a modest hum that you only noticed when someone switched it off. Maureen didn’t switch it off.

“Coffee’s in the thermos, cups are paper,” Maureen said, already behind the counter with the map case, keys laid out like a small invitation. Her cardigan was the same gray as the sky through the pocket windows, hair pinned back, reading glasses on a chain that tapped once against a drawer as she pulled it.

Mara set her messenger bag on the end of the table, produced her little flatbed scanner and the padded folder she used as a staging surface, and clicked the scanner’s cable into her tablet. “I’ll keep liquid on the far end.”

“You’d think you were on the floor of the state archives,” Maureen said, not unkindly. The key turned, oak slid, and she lifted out a flat gray Hollinger box with the neat spine: Selectmen: Actions 1978–1986. She set it down as if it were heavier than it was, habit rather than necessity. Then another: Litigation: Civil Actions, Outside Counsel. The second got both hands.

“That the quarry.” Mara had her notebook open to a fresh page, the date written and the time when she set her pen down. 08:13.

“That’s the quarry,” Maureen said. “You asked for parties and the docket, and anything municipal—counsel memos, board minutes, filings, decisions, note of settlement if any. I pulled the related selectmen’s minutes so you can see what made it into public record.” She slid the box toward Mara but did not let go. “And I’ll say this once because it applies to the whole shelf: everything here is public. If you’re treated otherwise, you tell me.”

“I will,” Mara said. “Chain of custody—on your end and mine.” She laid two evidence flags beside her notebook, more to satisfy herself than anyone else, and wrote Essex Historical Room, Box 2 of 4: Litigation: Civil Actions, Outside Counsel. Initials M.R. and M. K.—hers and Maureen’s. Maureen’s mouth curved slightly at the ritual.

Maureen let go. “Folder names are typed. If you see pink slips, those are contemporary pulls—someone had them within the last month. I try to note reasons.”

Mara lifted the lid. Inside, royal‑blue legal folders marched left to right. She found Quarry, Essex v. Maritime Aggregate & Stone and set it in the light. Foam wedge under the left flap, to keep strain off the binding. The staples at the top were the big office supply kind from a decade and a half ago, just starting to rust.

“Who was outside counsel?” Mara asked, peeling back the first document: a letter on letterhead.

“Dunham & Leeds out of Newburyport. Partner was Sowards.” Maureen slid a slip of paper across the table. “I put names and printer numbers on a reference card so you don’t have to go digging for the firm’s address line each time. It saves a scan.”

Mara wrote Parties: Town of Essex, by and through its Board of Selectmen; Maritime Aggregate & Stone LLC (formerly Maritime Aggregate Co.). Docket: Essex Superior Court, Civil No. 83-1142. Filing dates: Initial complaint 6/21/83; Amended complaint 9/13/83; Preliminary injunction hearing 10/04/83.

She paused. “Who filed the amended complaint. Same counsel.”

“Same. Added the blasting schedule and an estoppel argument,” Maureen said, already opening the second box of selectmen minutes. “Minutes from June through October are here. July 7 has the vote to authorize counsel to seek injunctive relief. Public session. There’s an executive session right after, under pending litigation, which is standard. The public record is the vote.”

Mara scanned the first page of the complaint. The scanner’s light made a clean sweep. The soft beep at the end was satisfying. She named the file as she always did: ESX_LIT_Quarry_1983_Complaint_pg1_2024-09-—she looked at the tablet. “Today’s the ninth?”

“Third day you’ve been here,” Maureen said. “But yes, ninth.”

“ESX_LIT_Quarry_1983_Complaint_pg1_2024-09-09.” She hit save. “Do you want copies of what I pull into your digital?”

“Send me the directory when you’re done and the citation list. I’ll load it into the town server and burn a disc for the reference desk. Saves someone elbowing the staples off the original.”

Mara smiled. “We approve of avoiding elbowing.”

Maureen set the July minutes on a stand beside her. “This is the public‑pressure portion, since you asked. If you read those, you’ll see the parade of letters from the quarry’s liaison, the Chamber president, a state rep’s office—two offices, actually—and three property owners on Powder House Lane. Everyone worried about jobs, or noise, or their view, depending which end of town they lived on. The rep’s aid called twice asking if the town could ‘slow down to allow for stakeholder input.’ It’s in the call log.”

“Names?” Mara asked.

“I’ve typed those on a card too. Politics comes in names, not titles.” Maureen’s tone was flat, informative. She adjusted her glasses and tapped a minute line with a pencil eraser. “Here: July 7, open session. Motion by Rector, seconded by Souza, unanimous to authorize counsel. There’s the paragraph that notes ‘considerable citizen comment,’ and the clerk did her job—she writes ‘remarks summarized,’ and then itemizes them by subject. No suppression. It was brisk because the room was full.”

Mara scanned the minute page, naming it ESX_SEL_Minutes_1983-07-07_pg3_2024-09-09. Then she read the list, pen ticking at each: dust on wash lines, blasting schedule not posted, truck brake noise, stream siltation, jobs at risk.

“Who’s Maritime’s principal at that time?” she asked without looking up.

“On their filing, Charles Albright. You’ll see a later tap‑dance when the LLC gets refiled after a sale, two years on.” Maureen pulled a second folder from the box and kept her hand on it. “Now here’s where I say what I said, again: public. The roster of owners was entered to establish standing at the time of the suit and again when they moved to modify the injunction. You can copy every page. If anyone tells you otherwise, send them to me.”

“Has anyone requested that roster lately?” Mara asked.

Maureen’s mouth did the smallest thing. She slid a pink slip from the fourth folder. “Two weeks ago. Requestor wrote ‘for genealogy and local history.’ We let folks have their reasons. They asked specifically for abutters on the east and north boundaries and for the baptism register at St. Columba’s for 1979 through 1982.”

Mara stopped. “Baptism register is here?”

“Microfilm copy. We host a duplicate reel on agreement with the parish for anything prior to 1985. It got pulled after. Pages are intact except for one leaf—misplaced long ago, if you believe the note on the reel index.” Maureen lifted a small white box, set it down like she was placing a stone. “Reel four. The missing leaf is marked ‘Page 62/63 missing—see staff log.’”

“Staff log,” Mara echoed.

Maureen pointed to the three‑ring binder at the end of the table with Staff Use printed friendly on the cover in blue marker. “I keep it. We don’t record what you read, but we record what the room is asked to cough up and whether pages are damaged or absent when returned. That’s for preservation, not policing.”

“And the log notes the missing baptism page.”

“It notes when the absence was first noticed, by whom, and in what context. It also notes who had the reel most recently prior to the note, because we track circulation. That’s transparency for the object’s sake.” She looked at Mara, eyebrows level. “No one gets to make a page disappear without at least an attempt at a record of when we noticed.”

Mara nodded, writing: Baptism register, St. Columba’s, Reel 4, Pg 62/63 missing—see staff log. She added a small box to the left and checked it. “I’ll scan the log note and the reel index. I’m not going to run the whole reel today.”

“No need. I pulled the index card on the particular inquiry from two weeks ago. Requestor initials are in the margin.” Maureen slid a second white card across. “Again, public. If someone comes in and says this is suddenly delicate, I’m going to stand where I stand.”

Mara didn’t glance up. “Has someone said that.”

“Someone suggested it.” Maureen’s voice stayed at desk volume. “They asked whether the requestor list was ‘sensitive in a family sense’ and whether we could remove sensitive items from the public index to ‘avoid confusion during an active period.’”

“Who asked.”

“A board member by telephone on a Saturday morning. If you need the name, it’s on the call log.” She shifted the selectmen minutes onto the second stand. “I told them what I always tell them: you’re too late. You can set policies in advance. You can’t redact your past because your present is uncomfortable.”

Mara looked up, met Maureen’s face for a steady second, then went back to scanning. The tablet made its small visits to green with each pass: ESX_LIT_Quarry_1983_PI_Notice_pg1; ESX_LIT_Quarry_1983_PI_Order_pg1; ESX_SEL_Minutes_1983-09-12_Extract.

“Preliminary injunction order reads,” she said aloud, “Blasting limited to two days a week, posting of schedule at town hall forty‑eight hours in advance, sediment control plan to Conservation by October 20, truck route revised to avoid residential streets between 7 a.m. and 9 a.m. Counsel to confer regarding bond.” She named it and saved it.

“That bond turned a few heads,” Maureen said. “Town wanted ten; the judge set three. You’ll see the letters about that—more noise than light.”

Mara scanned the letters anyway. She didn’t underline, she didn’t bracket, she didn’t write anything in the margins of the originals. Her notes took those impulses: Albright letter 9/15/83: ‘unnecessary hysteria’ phrase; Chamber letter 9/25/83: ‘regional impact’ phrase. She put each page on the glass and took it off the same way, clean.

“How did the case end,” she asked, pen waiting.

“It didn’t end. It sat with the injunction in place for a year while they negotiated. Then the new LLC owner came in and they moved to modify. That didn’t fly. They scaled back operations and sold the back forty to a land trust. You’ll see that land trust deed in the registry copy if you pull it, but I don’t keep the whole registry here.”

“I’ll note the book and page if it’s in the minutes.”

“It is.” Maureen flipped through and ran a finger down a page. “November 26, 1984: acknowledgment of receipt of Notice of Conservation Restriction Recorded; Essex South District Registry, Book 8237, Page 14. Clerk added ‘see map 21.’ We don’t have the map, but ConsComm does.”

Mara wrote the registry book and page in her list with a small star and bracketed it. Her scanner arm kept moving. She adjusted the foam wedge once and shifted her chair a quarter inch to keep the glare line off the signatures.

“Who is the requestor two weeks ago,” she asked, pen ready.

Maureen reached for the call log binder and opened it to the green sheet with the phone extensions at the top. “Initials on the white card are I.R. on the baptism reel and T.T. on the abutters roster. The form asks for a purpose; they wrote ‘family research’ and ‘historic property lines’ respectively. We don’t ask for proof of purpose.” She looked up. “But we do ask whether anyone made you uncomfortable about looking.”

“No one made me uncomfortable,” Mara said. It wasn’t exactly true; it wasn’t exactly untrue. “But I note pressure points.”

“They tend to stand on the carpet just inside the door and talk to the sign,” Maureen said. “As if the rules will soften themselves if they sound reasonable enough. This is a room, not a person. It doesn’t soothe.”

Mara glanced at the door. The sign did just sit there: Please place bags under table; gloves for photographs are in drawers left of microfilm reader; no pens on bound volumes; this is a public room. On the wood below it, someone had placed a bowl of dull pencils.

She pulled the staff log close, flipped to the bright yellow divider for Film and Audio, and scanned the printed header: Date, Item, Barcode/ID, Staff Initials, Notes, Return Condition. Halfway down the second page, there it was in Maureen’s handwriting: 08/24/24 – St. Columba’s Baptisms Reel 4 – SCB-4 – M.K. – Patron reports index lists pp. 62/63 missing; verified absence; checked storage box for loose leaf; no leaf present; crossref prior notes (2001); added missing flag to index; patron sought entries for winter 1980–81. She scanned that and named it ESX_StaffLog_FilmAudio_2024-08-24_StColumbas_Reel4_MissingLeaf_note.

“Thank you,” Mara said.

“That’s the job,” Maureen said, as if the bowl of pencils had said it. “If a page is gone, we write that down. If a call comes in suggesting we reconsider the existence of a page, we write that down too. We don’t mind being boring.”

“Boring is clarity.” Mara turned to the quarry injunction modification motion and scanned the clerk’s docket note: Denied 01/17/85. She could feel the morning steeping—the slow devout way rooms like this keep time, minute by minute with a pencil and a slip.

Maureen checked her watch and poured coffee into a paper cup without moving from behind the counter. “We open to general public at ten today,” she said. “You’ve got an hour before chatter.”

“I’ll be done with the essentials.” Mara put a small square beside her list and wrote CITATIONS TO LOG. “For your server: I’ll export as a single folder with subfolders for Minutes, Litigation, Staff Logs, and Microfilm Indices. I’ll include a document with file names and formal citations.”

“Include the docket numbers in the citation list,” Maureen said. “People forget those when the letterhead dazzles them.”

“I always include docket numbers.” She put it in the list: Cite docket no. ESX-83-1142; Order on PI 10/10/83; Minutes 7/7/83 and 9/12/83; Registry Book 8237/14 (Cons Restriction). She drew a tidy line beneath it.

“And one more thing,” Maureen said, taking a narrow manila envelope from the second box. “This is a copy of the selectmen’s correspondence sheet for October 1983. It shows the roster of who wrote in and on what side they leaned, so to speak. Green highlight is pro‑injunction; yellow is anti. I highlight after the fact. The original is unmarked.”

Mara held the sheet up to the light, saw the old dot‑matrix perforations still hanging off one edge. “That’s useful.” She scanned it, saved it as ESX_SEL_Correspondence_1983-10_Highlighted_Copy, and wrote: Original unmarked; highlight is reference.

“I’ll get the unhighlighted for your scan if you want it,” Maureen said.

“This is fine,” Mara said. “I’ll note the difference. The substance’s the same.” She typed quickly, then turned the screen so Maureen could see the directory tree filling. Minutes. Litigation. Staff Logs. Indices. Under Litigation, filenames lined up in her tidy style; under Indices, the reel cards went into place.

Maureen nodded once, satisfied. “You’ll leave your list at the desk when you go?”

“I’ll leave two copies.” Mara pulled a fresh sheet and began the boilerplate she used for rooms like this: Essex Historical Room, Morning, 09/09/24, pull list and scans: Quarry litigation (ESX-83-1142) complaint, PI motion, PI order; Selectmen minutes (7/7/83, 9/12/83, 11/26/84); Selectmen correspondence sheet (10/83); Staff log note re St. Columba’s Reel 4 missing pp. 62/63; Microfilm index entry for same; Registry book/page reference. She numbered them, boxed the total at the bottom, and wrote the time: 09:56.

“Any objections to me noting in my report that the room received an inquiry and a suggestion to treat this as ‘sensitive,’ and that you declined.”

“You may say exactly that. I keep a public notebook for those moments.” Maureen tapped a small black Moleskine at the corner of her desk. “You can quote me—‘We don’t redact our past because our present is uncomfortable.’”

“I wrote that down,” Mara said, tapping her notebook with the same pen. She stood and stacked the blue folders exactly as they had been, small pressure with the palm of her hand to settle them in. Lids went back on boxes; keys tapped once against the clasp.

Maureen rolled the map case drawer shut and locked it with a practiced half‑turn. “If you want the cons restriction map, I’ll call Conservation. They like giving tours of their map drawer.”

“I’ll call,” Mara said. “I’ll bring a wedge.”

“Bring two,” Maureen said. “Their maps are enthusiastic.”

Mara slid her scanner into the padded sleeve and zipped it. The tablet chirped with the final write to the archive folder. She ejected it, clicked the USB out, and coiled the cable into the tidy loop she always made, the sort that never remembers a kink.

“Copies for you,” she said, placing two stapled pages on the counter: her citation list and her pull log, signed and initialed. “I’ll email the directory link at noon.”

Maureen took them and flattened the staple with her thumb against the wood. “If anyone asks what you did in here today, tell them you did exactly this,” she said, tapping the two sheets. “And tell them that’s what anyone may do.”

“That’s what I’ll tell them.” Mara tucked her notebook away, slipped the pen into its loop, and lifted her bag. At the door, she paused to put one of the dull pencils back into the bowl point down.

“Next requests,” Maureen said as Mara reached for the latch, “make them specific as you’ve been. It saves us both the dance.”

“I like a room that doesn’t dance,” Mara said.

“You and me both,” Maureen said, and the microfilm reader hummed on, oblivious to every suggestion anyone, anywhere, might make.


==================== SCENE 19 ====================

The parish register shelf lived in its own climate: cooler by a few degrees, draft threading along the stacks, the faint sweet ache of old leather. The spines were hand-lettered in three generations of hands—some in slanted penmanship, some in tidy block, one in a looping purple ink that had bled into the calfskin like a bruise.

Maureen’s ring of keys brushed the wood as she moved a rolling stepstool into place. “St. Columba’s,” she said. “Bottom bay, right side. Baptisms and marriages. The sacristy sent most of the nineteenth-century volumes here after the radiators hiccuped.”

“That can’t have been a good day,” Mara said.

“It was an exciting day. Parish councils are very nimble when steam threatens children.” Maureen squatted without ceremony, slid two ledgers out with the flat of her hands, and heaved them onto the adjacent desk. The desk was a slab of varnished maple kept bare except for a foam cradle and a box of thin lead “snakes” that lay like tame eels in a nest.

Mara parked her bag against the leg of the desk. “May I?” she said, already reaching for the nitrile gloves.

“You may if it makes you happy,” Maureen said. “But the rule here is clean, dry hands. The leather likes skin oil better than it likes powder.”

Mara flexed her fingers, rubbed her palms against her jeans to lose any imagined moisture. “I’ll be clean and dry, then.”

Maureen levered the top ledger, a rust-brown book with a gilt cross darkened to graphite. The spine was labeled—BAPTISMS 1979–1986—in careful, slightly shaky letters. Maureen set it in the cradle, centered two snakes across the fore edge, then glanced at Mara. “You saw my note,” she said. “Reel 4, pages sixty-two and sixty-three.”

“I wrote it in the pull log,” Mara said. “And on my copy for you.”

“Good. So no one will ask where we got the idea.” Maureen’s mouth made a small line that was not unfriendly, merely practiced. She opened the ledger with fingertips, easing against the sewing so the binding sighed rather than snapped, and let the weight of the snakes do the rest.

The entries were neat at first: name of child, parents, sponsor, date of birth, date of baptism, priest’s signature. Lines ruled in pale blue were as steady as a cardiograph. Names ran through the familiar Irish-Massachusetts family trees: Sullivans seeding into O’Sheas, Barrys into O’Connors, then the late eighties started to diversify—Nguyen, Rivera, Da Silva in a careful hand.

“Where are our sixties?” Mara asked.

“Wrong book. This is post–Vatican II comfort. The one we want is older.” Maureen slid that ledger aside and pulled the one beneath. It came reluctantly, leather clinging to leather. This spine was a thicker block of calf with a black label, BAPTISMS 1968–1984, and the corners were frayed to the boards like knees through denim. She shifted the foam cradle closer, set the book down, and drew in one breath the way anyone does before lifting a stubborn thing that matters.

They opened to the front matter—title page hand-done by some faithful clerk in swashed pen: Parish of St. Columba, Register of Baptisms, Vol. II—then Maureen thumbed toward the middle, counting signatures under her breath. “Fifty-seven, fifty-eight…”

Mara leaned in, eyes taking the grid and the old ballpoint that had dug through three sheets on the thicker hands. A paperleaf stub stuck up like an incisor at page fifty-nine, where someone had torn a prior adhesive label.

“Sixty,” Maureen said, and stopped.

On the left, page sixty-one lay flat. On the right there was nothing. The sewing showed, bare and tidy, two kettle stitches like dimples where a folio would have nestled. The paper had been cut clean, not ripped—sliced near the inner margin so close you had to tilt the page to see the narrow fuzz of fiber where a blade had kissed it.

Mara slid a snake down to hold sixty-one, then pressed a knuckle lightly along the stub of the missing sheet. No brittle edge, no hangnail of tearing. A straight missing. “So it is sixty-two and sixty-three,” she said, low.

“Mmm,” Maureen said. She did not sound surprised so much as confirmed. She leaned in until almost nose to paper. “And done by someone who knew paper. Not a child, not an impatient person. Clean blades and a steady hand.”

“Could be a conservation cut?” Mara said. “Mold, something that spread?”

“Then you’d see ghosts,” Maureen said. She tapped the paper near the stitch. “Shadows of removal, staining, scraping, the hallmarks we all learn when a book has had surgery. This looks like a page that went for a walk and didn’t come back.”

“Priest,” Mara said, head tilted. “Clerk.” She studied the entries on sixty-one. “Aunt with scissors.”

Maureen made a small pleased sound. “I once had a grandmother remove all her children’s names from a grammar school Honor Roll book because the third-grade nun would not correct a misspelling. But she ripped. This is not a ripping woman.”

“Could be a perfectionist,” Mara said. “Could be…random.” She let the word sit between them. “A page slips, a knife is set somewhere wrong, a maintenance guy trims a hanging shred.”

“And that,” Maureen said, “is what I wanted to check against.” She reached for her Moleskine, flipped it open to a page flagged with a red plastic tab, and laid it beside the ledger. “Staff log.”

Mara stepped around so she could read upside down. Maureen’s handwriting was a precise print. The date column ran left; the notes made a neat paragraph. She read: Tues 10/18/83: morning—copier jammed; afternoon—visitor asked for staff log re: dorm schedules; St. C. Reel 4 marked ‘pp. 62/63 missing (Baptisms),’ first notation. Next: Wed 10/19/83: roofers; Fri 10/21/83: gas smell; Mon 10/24/83: paraments returned; Tues 11/01/83: ledger locked. A later line: Thurs 03/14/84: Nora notes, “Staff timecards incomplete in August 1983; check with rectory.” Arrow back to the first notation.

“You’re telling me the first time anyone wrote down that the page was missing lands in October eighty-three,” Mara said.

“Yes.”

“And someone else—Nora?—notes staff timecards incomplete in August eighty-three.”

“Mm-hmm. She used to write in pencil so she could erase herself when she felt fussy. But she didn’t erase that.” Maureen tapped the arrow. “It’s a neat little trio: missing page notice in October, timecard gap in August, my ledger lock notation in November because we had a teenager who considered everything a surface for ballpoint.”

Mara turned the book a little to catch the light. The sewing of the missing leaf gleamed like a fine tendon. “You think the timing isn’t random,” she said.

“I think patterns are like gossip,” Maureen said. “They want to be believed. I like to make them prove themselves.” She lifted her head toward the register. “Question is, do we have a pattern here, or did a blade knock at an angle when someone was trimming?”

“Do you have microfilm?” Mara asked. “You wrote ‘Reel 4’ in the log.”

“We have an index entry,” Maureen said. “The reel is off-site. Library Co-op storage. It’s not the parish’s film; it’s the diocese’s, and we keep a finding aid. The hard part is whether the filming happened before or after the haircut.”

“Microfilm is a tyrant,” Mara said. “If it exists, it exists exactly as it was.” She flattened her palm near the missing page so the draft didn’t tickle the edges.

Maureen fed her a smile. “Practical poetry.” She turned to the back of the ledger, where a clerk had tucked a strip of onionskin with a typed list of “burials cross-ref.” She ran a finger down signatures—Fr. C. McInerney most of the way through seventy-nine, Fr. P. Lahiff taking over eighty-one, and a block of signatures for August ’83 in an unfamiliar hand, brief, clipped. “Look at the hand for August,” she said. “Substitute priest. That month has a scribal character all its own.”

“Could someone have pulled the page to fix a misspelling? Send it back? Never returned?” Mara asked. Her voice had the caution one uses when the room’s air is full of the past.

“If they did, they did it with a blade, and they stitched nothing in,” Maureen said. “I’ve seen taped patches, I’ve seen slips tipped in with hideous Scotch tape, I’ve seen onionskin overlay with an apology. I don’t see a repair here. Do you?”

“No,” Mara said.

They both bent nearer, as if the blank could answer. The desk lamp made a little circle of butter on the page edges.

Maureen flipped two gatherings forward—page sixty-four sat there, minding its business, with a baby named Tobias baptized on September 10, 1983. The hand for that entry was the clipped one. “So the page before Tobias is missing,” she said. “Which could include August baptisms. The timecards note ‘incomplete’ for August.”

“And your first notation of the missing page is October,” Mara said. “After.”

“Yes.” Maureen’s finger hovered over Tobias without touching. “It could very well be nothing. Or it could be someone who wanted something not in here anymore.”

“Which brings us back to belief,” Mara said. “Randomness or pattern.”

“I won’t teach a conclusion to fetch,” Maureen said. “But I will put a leash on it while we walk it to the card catalog.” She set the snakes aside, eased the ledger closed, and stood. “We document what we see, and we ask for what we don’t have.”

Mara nodded. “Let me photograph the absence,” she said.

“You may, if you don’t blind the leather.” Maureen moved the lamp so the glare wouldn’t flare. “I have a little felt strip for the spine. Wait.” She fetched it from the drawer, laid it in the cradle’s dip to cradle the joint, then nodded. “Proceed.”

Mara pulled the slim scanner out again, but this time she used her phone: the camera was faster, and Maureen had already said yes. She shot the open spread—sixty-one on the left, the neat gutter stitches on the right where sixty-two and sixty-three should be. She took tight shots of the sewing, the stub’s edge, the ink density on the adjacent entries. She took a wide that showed the spine, the cradle, the snakes, the little felt strip. She shot Maureen’s Moleskine page with the red tab and the dates in frame, then her own notebook with the entry, her time, the Moleskine visible to tie them.

“I’ll put those in the directory,” she said. “Label each with page numbers and ‘absence.’”

“Make sure you include the hand for August,” Maureen said. “Even if we end up bored by it, bored is information.”

Mara flipped back to the signatures and took three more. Then she pivoted her phone so Maureen could see. “Good?”

“Good.” Maureen was already tugging a shallow drawer free on the adjacent desk—the one that held forms like a tidy card trick. She drew out a carbonless request sheet and a stamped, self-addressed inter-library envelope. “Pick the language,” she said. “We’ll ask for Reel 4, St. Columba’s, and I’ll put my print on it so no one plays keep-away.”

“Film date?” Mara asked.

Maureen thumbed the finding aid hanging from a brass rod at the end of the shelf. “Filmed June 1981–December 1984, in three passes. Reel 4 is August ’83 through January ’84. Which means our gap is within that reel’s scope.”

“If it was missing before the film, the film shows the absence,” Mara said. “If it was intact, the film laughs at us and tells us what was on the page.”

“And the laughter is what we want,” Maureen said. She filled the top line with St. Columba’s BAPTISMS, Reel 4, pages 060–066, request: confirm presence/absence of pp. 062/063; if present, provide scans of pp. 62/63. She signed it, then slid it across. “You add your line.”

Mara printed her name, date, and, under Notes: “Observed physical removal of pp. 62/63 in ledger. See attached images; staff log notes first missing-page notation 10/18/83; staff timecard gap 8/83.”

“That’s the correlation,” Maureen said. “We are not gossips; we are good neighbors who report that the porch light is out and the mail looked damp.”

Mara smiled. “I’ll put that on a t-shirt.”

“Do, and cut me in for five percent.” Maureen made a neat tear on the carbonless, tucked the pink copy into her Moleskine, and slid the white original into the envelope. She sealed it with a practiced thumb press, then stamped the corner with the room’s oval: ESSEX HISTORICAL ROOM REQUEST. “I’ll run this when I go for lunch.”

“Thank you,” Mara said. She set the ledger back in its place with Maureen, both women using the flat of an arm like a seatbelt. The book’s weight went pleasant in the shelf. The draft moved again and then stopped as if it, too, understood closure.

“Before you leave,” Maureen said, tapping the desk, “write your correlation on my desk copy.”

Mara took the pencil from the bowl—one of the dull ones, as always—turned it once in her fingers to find which face had been flattened by another hand, and set the lead to the lined pad. She wrote: 09/09/24—Observed Baptism Vol. II—pp. 62/63 missing; clean cut at inner margin; staff log first notation 10/18/83; staff timecards incomplete 8/83; Reel 4 request placed. She added her initials.

Maureen read it, eyes moving fast. “Acceptable,” she said. “Now we have an observed absence, a staff log correlation, and a request out. Not a conclusion. Not a theory. Just the kindly work of noticing.”

“Patterns want to be believed,” Mara said, capping the pencil by turning it upside down in the bowl.

“And we will be polite to them,” Maureen said, “until they show their teeth or wag their tails.” She slid the request envelope under a paperweight shaped like a small whale. “I’ll call the Co-op after I stamp mail, in case they’re in the mood to expedite.”

“Do they get in moods?” Mara asked.

“They are always in some mood,” Maureen said. “Our job is to send an invitation in the right font.” She turned back to the shelf, gave the ledger a last affectionate pat, and then, as if nothing large had just been nudged, picked up the black Moleskine and wrote in it the smallest line: Parish register—missing pp. 62/63 confirmed; Reel 4 requested.

Mara watched the ink dry. The room’s hum reasserted itself—the microfilm reader purring on the far table, the HVAC in the ceiling, a gull somewhere outside dragging a syllable across the air.

“It’s odd,” Mara said, not disagreeing with the air. “How much satisfaction there is in naming an absence.”

“It’s half our work,” Maureen said. “And the other half is not rushing to fill it with our wishes.” She closed the Moleskine, laid her hand on it. “We’ll see what the film says. You’ll get an email when it lands.”

“I’ll bring wedges,” Mara said, reaching for her bag.

“Bring two,” Maureen said, without looking up as she jotted the day’s outgoing requests in a thin column. “The Co-op reels can be enthusiastic.”


==================== SCENE 20 ====================
The copy station lived in the library’s utilitarian belly, a square room of beige counters and bright machines that never really stopped talking. Staplers clinked on their leashes. A wide-format printer clicked to itself in even boredom. The laminator exhaled heat and plastic-sweet air like a toaster obsessing over clear bread. Fluorescent ballast sang along the ceiling, a thin wire note that seemed to ask, Are you sure?

Mara slid the tote Maureen had given her onto the counter under the glass-fronted “COPY/SCAN” sign. Inside: the quarry lawsuit folder, Maureen’s bundle of folded clippings clipped with a rusted bulldog, a photocopy request form, and the thin pad with Mara’s handwriting from five minutes ago, the ink still ghost-pink at its edges. She spread everything into lanes. Noise to her left: the schoolkids making tri-fold posters about mollusks. Signal to her right: the depositions with margins like winter hedges, names tucked in them.

“Card or coins?” the clerk asked. He was wearing the library vest, a plastic name tag: DEV. He chewed a piece of gum that had no taste left by the look of it and gestured at the payment pad as if it were an altar.

“Card,” Mara said. “Do you have a flatbed that doesn’t squash old ledger spines?”

“‘Doesn’t squash’ is not a thing that exists,” Dev said. “But 3B’s lid has a broken hinge, which is functionally the dream.”

He slid her a strip of Post-its and a fresh yellow pencil with an eraser still square at its corners. The laminator breathed. Someone made a copy of a hand—pale splayed fingers fused in greyscale—and giggled.

Mara took the hinge-compromised flatbed and opened the quarry file. On top was the complaint: town residents v. Essex Granite Holdings LLC. Behind that, two motions to compel, three affidavits, the list of abutters. She pulled her notebook closer.

The names were a low local thrum—Seaver, Tynan, Rourke, Pappas, Donahue, Cassiani in its long version like a fishing net. She moved each into a column. “Abutters,” she said softly, tapping the pencil to each. “Counsel. Prior proceedings. Mineral rights chain.” Her own voice came back to her in the laminate heat as if the room wanted everything labeled plainly.

On the first pass, she scanned the abutters list, set the machine to 600 dpi, grayscale, no auto-contrast. She hovered the broken lid so the paper lay without pressure and pressed SCAN. The machine’s bar woke, slid its green eye down the page. A migraine of stripes ghosted and was gone. On her screen, the names arrived in a polite font, printable, sortable. She saved the file to a new folder she named LAW/APRT/QUARRY and timestamped it. Dev leaned one hip to the counter.

“If it coughs out black lines,” he said, “lift the paper and blow on it like a Nintendo cartridge. The machine believes in ritual.”

“Good to know,” Mara said. She stepped into the rhythm: clean, scan, save, annotate. She copied the abutters, then the list of “concerned citizens” who had signed the petition two summers ago when the blasting cracked the old stained glass at St. Brigid’s. She scanned the photograph of the crack—blue glass split like ice across sea—that someone had tucked in. She labeled the file GLASS_DAMAGE_07-18.

Noise rose and fell behind her. “Do you do color on cardstock?” a woman asked. “Is the 11×17 broken again?” someone else said. Mara ignored all of it and kept her pencil track steady.

She moved to the affidavits, using the highlighter Maureen had stuck into the folder. She ran it down the column where deponents named “property acquired by instrument,” drawing out book and page numbers. Deed Book 1032 p. 44. Deed Book 847 p. 12. The annotations began to look like little flags planted on a map.

From her bag she took the slip Maureen had made her write—Observed Baptism Vol. II—pp. 62/63 missing; clean cut at inner margin; staff log first notation 10/18/83; staff timecards incomplete 8/83; Reel 4 request placed—and set it above the scanner as a compass. The missing pages ran like a invisible creek through the rest of it. If the register lost two pages, which baptisms would that erase? Whose names would be missing from the public memory? The staff log notation date put a pin in October 18, 1983. She jotted the range: late summer–mid-October.

She opened the clippings bundle. The bulldog sneeze-squealed and let go. Out slid yellowed columns with headlines that rang like tiny bells even now: SELECTMEN TABLE QUARRY SUIT; TOWN COUNSEL DECLINES TO COMMENT; BAPTISM RECORDS TO BE MICROFILMED. A photograph of a man at a folding table, a hand raised to block the camera, the caption naming the man: Julian L. Grey, counsel for the estate. He looked younger, cheeks not yet bracketed, the practiced refusal already there. She scanned the photograph and the caption. File name: JULIAN_PUBLIC_MTNG_83.

“Do you want me to stamp anything confidential?” Dev asked, because he had to ask it of everyone. He held a stamp that said CONFIDENTIAL in red block. The temptation to stamp the entire room with it gave him a private smile.

“Not yet,” Mara said. “I’m still translating.”

He nodded like he knew what that meant. The laminator hauled another sheet of melted plastic forward and sighed.

Mara made a new stack: records that intersected the missing-page window. She pulled the local paper calendar of parish events for August to October 1983. Bazaars, bake sales, baptism Sundays noted in tiny cross symbols. She scanned the calendar and highlighted the Sundays with crosses. She wrote in the margin: probable baptism days: 8/7, 8/21, 9/4, 9/18, 10/2. She had a list from the lawsuit of family names with long local branches. She began to overlay them.

“Seaver,” she said, keeping her pencil near the paper as if the word might spook. She checked the clippings for any mention of a Seaver christening in ’83. Nothing open. She went to the genealogical index on the station’s public terminal, typed SEAVER + ST. BRIGID + 1983, got “See Parish Register Vol. II (missing).” She wrote: absence noted. She tried Tynan. She pulled up wedding announcements, obituaries, no baptisms at the right point. She added: absence? She tried Pappas; the index offered a baby shower at the VFW in September 1983, registries at Sears and The Fair. She wrote: probable birth late summer; check.

A child at the end machine had decided the copy button would click more satisfyingly if hit with a flat palm. The machine beeped its protest and then spat a jam notice in dot matrix. Dev glided down the row. “Hey, champ,” he said gently, “it’s not the elevator in Charlie and the Chocolate Factory. Two fingers. We respect the button.”

Mara lifted her own lid and realized she had been pressing the SCAN key like a metronome. She let her hand relax. The machine made a small happy chirp as if its feelings had been considered.

She spread the photocopied staff logs Maureen had let her take—thin carbon copies of schedules with initials and shift notes. She circled 8/83—comprehensive gaps. 9/83—evening desk staff initials that repeated, notes like “film vault locked” and “Baptism register to bindery—reshelve upon return,” then that familiar neat hand: 10/18/83—Note: Vol. II pp. 62/63 missing. She scanned this and labeled it STAFF_LOG_NOTE_10-18-83. She added a note to herself: bindery? call.

She pulled the affidavit of a woman named Margaret Donahue who said she remembered “the church asking for donations for binding the old registers” around the time the first blasting happened on the quarry’s new parcel. She highlighted that line, a thin glow that turned the sentence into a signal flare.

“Is that your cousin?” Dev asked, gesturing to a photo of a group in a Knights of Columbus hall, men in sashes with the exact pleased weariness of men who have lifted too many folding chairs.

“No,” Mara said, not looking up. “But he has folded a chair.”

“Everyone has, eventually,” Dev said.

She built stacks the way she liked, with edges aligned, the stacks each a concept. Stack A: Lawsuit timeline—filings, hearings, selectmen actions. Stack B: Parish register and events—calendar, the missing-page note, any binding references. Stack C: Families intersecting both—names with plausible baptisms in the missing window who also show up as complainants, abutters, signers, deponents. She took a binder clip for each and gave them little Post-it flags: A/TIME, B/REGISTER, C/CROSS.

She set the three stacks across the counter so she could see the scale of each. Stack C was the thinnest. That felt honest.

“Would you like to staple something unconscionably thick?” Dev asked. He was holding the long-arm stapler in both hands like a ceremonial sword.

“Later,” Mara said, and then, because she had hit that point where you have to make structure out of noise, she spoke her thoughts out loud, arranging them into a line that felt like walking on rocks in a stream, testing each for wobble.

“Okay. The quarry suit lit up 1983. The parish planned to microfilm the registers then. The register goes to binding; the pages go missing; staff logs note it October 18. Families active in the suit had children around then—probable baptisms in late summer into early fall—but the names aren’t in the public index because the pages are gone.” She touched stack C. “So, the people who might care about proving a connection—land rights, paternal lines, old promises—have holes where the community expects congruence.”

She took her pencil and made a clean page called “LEADS.” She numbered them.

1. Call bindery: confirm dates, work order for Vol. II; ask who paid, and if they documented missing sections on arrival.
2. Reel 4 ETA from Co-op (Maureen calling) — schedule return to scan baptisms around 8/7–10/2.
3. Cross families in abutters/affidavits with parish donation lists for binding appeal; look for overlap: Seaver, Donahue, Tynan, Pappas, Cassiani.
4. Find baptism witnesses for families in window via newspaper birth announcements; check if any appear in lawsuit affidavits as “concerned citizens.”
5. Pull notary logs from 1983 for mineral rights filings; see if same surnames appear as sponsors in parish events.

She wrote them quick and clean. The noise of the laminator faded, then returned, the cycle like breathing. The fluorescent whine had been there so long it was part of the room’s skin.

“Do you need the three-hole punch?” Dev asked, rolling up to her with the heavy one that made that satisfying confetti. “It’s not a project until you make waste.”

“I will,” Mara said. She collated the scans into a single “dossier” PDF, appended the clippings as images, linked the staff log scans. She wrote a one-page executive summary at the front, as if she were an unfussy future reader who had ten minutes and a headache. It said what it had to and stopped when it had done so.

On the way to the printer she passed the kids’ poster showing a moon snail eating a clam, arrows and labels pointing to “RADULA” in bubble letters. In the margin someone had drawn a small human saying, Rude. It made her laugh, quietly. She collected her pages, still warm and smelling of toner, and tapped them on the counter to align. The long-arm stapler came into service; it made a decisive chonk through the corner of the summary. The three-hole punch chomped its neat moons, and Dev lifted the little drawer to show her the confetti well, full to the brim with paper stars. He offered the bowl.

“Lucky,” he said. “Take two.”

Mara took a pinch and put the tiny circles in the pocket of her notebook. She slid the dossier into a simple black binder and labeled the spine in a clear hand: QUARRY / REGISTER / 83. On the inside she slid the LEADS sheet under the clear plastic of the front cover where it would glint at her whenever she thought of drifting.

She made one more list—priors. She drew a star next to 1 and 2. Binding dates and Reel 4 were the first doors; the rest could line up after. She boxed them.

“Anything to ship?” Dev asked, out of habit, as if her binder might want to go somewhere without her.

“Just carrying,” she said. “It knows where it’s going.”

She paid, put the binder into her tote, tucked the pencil behind her ear. The room’s noise lined up briefly, even the fluorescent hum climbing to a neat, agreeable pitch. It felt like catching a signal between AM stations, a crisp voice in the static.

Mara picked up the tote and gave Dev a two-finger salute that respected the button. The door opened on the soft hallway carpet, and the copy station’s machine-talk, secret and industrious, fell behind her as she moved into the library’s wider quiet with a working dossier and three good doors to knock on next.


==================== SCENE 21 ====================
The first blink was just that: a blink. The fluorescents took a breath the way they sometimes did when a compressor kicked, a collective dip like a school of fish changing direction, and then they were gone. The copy station’s hum collapsed into a neat silence. For a heartbeat the library heard itself—ductwork and faint rain and a printer thinking about a last job—and then every battery‑backed thing began to shriek in its own pitch.

“Okay,” Dev said into the sudden dark, voice higher than usual. Something clonked as he reached for a flashlight and found the stapler instead. “Okay, fine.”

The exit signs stayed ghostly, low‑red, not enough to shape anything. The windows were slabs of black. In the children’s corner a bright jellyfish lamp lit for a nervous two seconds on stored charge and sighed out like a match. Mara had already set the tote on her shoulder—weight of the binder sure and square—and slid her hand down to the headlamp at her belt.

She clicked it once to check it wasn’t in some obnoxious strobe mode, then lifted it to her forehead and snugged the stretchy band under the pencil. The LED came on in a hard white wedge that made the dust in the air look like it had mass.

“Genny?” Dev called into his desk phone as if it might still carry current. It made the same thin dying beep as the scanner. “Rina?” he tried, as if the night guard might answer from the other side of the dark. “I’m not walking to Circulation without light. I have a policy.”

“Stay put,” Mara said. She turned to sweep the beam. The main floor reverted to what it actually was under all the brightness—edges, angles, aisle‑rivers between stacks that read as darker shadow. The ceiling receded. The carpet drank the light.

From deeper in the building: a radio crackle.

“—Panel says transfer,” a man’s voice came, punctuated by a squawk. “But the emergency string is dead. If you can hear me, get the sticks.”

“On it,” a woman answered, closer. “Front desk has the tub.”

The front doors showed up as clean, reflective rectangles. Outside, the storm had turned the town to a low roar. The trees made their own sound. A gust shoved at the vestibule and made a line of brochures tilt.

Dev finally found his flashlight—he yelped triumphantly and then yelped again when it stuttered and gave a weak ring around his toes. “I might have a candle,” he said. “Which they definitely told us not to.”

“Don’t,” Mara and a voice from the entrance said at the same time.

The voice belonged to Rina, all black jacket and a reflective stripe, a belt full of keys. The light from Mara’s headlamp picked out her cheekbone, the metal of her radio. Beside her was Tony from custodial with a five‑gallon tub full of fat green glow sticks like overgrown celery.

“Who needs light?” Tony sang, grin too wide. He was a big shape with a mop handle under his arm like a pike. “We got rave for days.”

Rina took in the room with her own flashlight, a proper one, the beam clean and steady. “Generator kicked for HVAC, not for emergency. Of course,” she said. “That box is older than me.”

“Maybe older than all of us,” Tony said happily, cracking a stick with two fists. It went from dull plastic to a bright submarine green. He shook it, flicking drops of light onto his sleeve. “Ooh. Science.”

Mara moved the beam to the circulation desk, the black hump of the computer, the cord trailing down. A string of battery backups under there was beeping like a flock of small robotic birds. She didn’t like that the tones were slightly out of phase. It was one of those stupid dissonances that got between a person and their thoughts.

Rina peeled another glow stick and planted it upright in a plastic cup on the counter. “We’re placing these along the aisles,” she said. “It’s going to look like Tron had a baby with a municipal parcel map. Everyone keep to the main floor. If you think you can see, you can’t.”

“Copy,” Tony said. He shoved the mop through the drip ring and then thought better of pushing it anywhere. “Flood by Periodicals is still contained. If the sump goes—”

“It won’t,” Rina said without commitment. “If it does, we move the bins.”

Dev had his little ring of light on the long‑arm stapler, admiring it as if it had become a creature. “We just got youth chess out the door,” he said, rattled. “Lucky. Imagine telling twenty kids to castle in the dark.”

“Evidence station?” Mara asked.

Rina’s gaze snapped to her. “Your table by Reference?”

“Yes.” She swept the headlamp toward its location—between the Maine Collection and the glassed door to the Essex Historical Room. Sterile white bags in a shallow stack, a clipboard with chain‑of‑custody, the seal stickers lined like postage. She’d taped the rubberized mat to the table so it wouldn’t creep under elbows, the kind with a ruler edge and a packing list pocket.

“Go,” Rina said. “Bring what you need in here. If the panel’s lying, it’s going to lie again.”

“Got it.”

“Take a stick,” Tony said, holding one out with two hands like an offering. The green made his knuckles look otherworldly.

“I have a lamp,” Mara said. “You take care of aisles fifteen through twenty.”

“I’m a glowworm of duty,” Tony said, already cracking two more and tossing one underhand to Dev, who half‑caught, half‑batted it into a drawer. “Oh, come on,” Tony told him. “It’s sports.”

“I don’t do sports in the dark,” Dev said, retrieving the glowing baton with exaggerated dignity. “I only do forms.”

The beam from Mara’s forehead picked out the sign for Reference—white on black, Helvetica as if calm were a font. She crossed the carpet with the tote under her arm, her free hand brushing the top of chairs, the way you check furniture in unfamiliar rooms.

The library had changed with the power. It had gone from the public square version of itself to a private body. Her light showed her the little things you didn’t see in full illumination: the scuffs on the book cart wheels, a coin under a chair, the line where someone had stuck a piece of tape to mark “story time circle” and then peeled it, leaving a tongue of adhesive. Dust motes threw tiny shadows.

Back by her table, the Essex Room’s glass was a mirror. Her headlamp bounced herself back: pale oval of face, band a dark seam, pencil like a horn. Don’t laugh, she told the laugh trying to get out. She set the tote down and went inventory without preamble. The subconscious thing that had been keeping track counted quicker than her eyes. Binder: check. The torn will bagged and signed, inside the evidence bin: check. Flash drive from the call bell audit: check. The chain‑of‑custody clipboard had slid and knocked the pen sideways; it looked careless though nothing had happened. She straightened it and clipped the pen back on like pressing a badge to a lapel.

The emergency lights along the baseboards had tried, failed, and tried again. One flicker ran around the perimeter and went out after an audible little pop, a quarter of the string giving up like Christmas lights. Rina swore politely at the front.

“Mara?” she called. “You all set?”

“Almost,” Mara said. She pulled the deep drawer of evidence stickers and grabbed two rolls, one red stripe for sealed, one yellow for temporary. She found the tamper‑evident seals for the bins and counted out four. She pulled the zip‑tie spool out of her bag and fingered the ties one by one to make sure the ends weren’t bent.

A dull clunk came up through the floor, far away and heavy—the building sneezing. The rain changed tempo, a sudden drum on the west windows as the wind found a new angle. The door to the Essex Room creaked in its frame, just a little air pressure let.

She shut it. The rubber gasket made a soft self‑satisfied sound. In the headlamp’s glare, the smudges on the glass read like brushstrokes. Someone’s cuff had left a ghost of a print. She didn’t think about prints; this was not for that. She was keeping the station whole. She tightened the strap of her lamp again to push its circle more precisely where she wanted.

“Hey, the weird printer is singing,” Tony called from a stack over.

“The weird printer sings when it dies,” Dev said. “It’s like a sea cow.”

“Oh, good,” Rina said. “Haunt us later.”

Mara sealed. She pressed the red stripe stickers across the lid seam of the bin—one north-south, one east-west, one diagonal—and wrote the date with the pencil, firm enough to impress through the paper but gentle with the point. She added the time from her watch face; the watch caught her headlamp and threw a flash in her eyes. She blinked, blotted the afterimage on her tongue.

Back at the chain‑of‑custody, she initialed receipt to herself and moved the clipboard into the bin’s expandable pocket, zipped, clipped. She threaded a zip‑tie through the hasp and pulled it tight; it made a dry, satisfying rasp. She left the extra ties and stickers in the tote and clicked the tote top closed.

The copier nearby woke up for no reason, made a hopeful burp, and then went dead like someone had put a pillow over its face. The sound had that wrong intimacy that machines sometimes do, a little human in it. The alarms under the circulation desk changed patterns again—beep‑beep‑beep‑beep in a new configuration that meant nothing except that it wanted attention.

At the corner of her table she’d taped a laminated sheet: Rourke’s temporary protocol. SHEET IN, SHEET OUT in blocky font, the checkboxes like neat corrals. She ran her finger down it and drew a half‑smile at herself; it had survived the day without coffee on it. She added a tick mark for “sealed for transport” and one for “environmental anomaly: outage.” She put the pencil back behind her ear and felt the headlamp band tug at it in protest.

“Mara?” Rina’s voice again, nearer. “Need a hand?”

“Go ahead,” Mara said, not looking up yet. She was flipping the binder to check the sleeves: clippings sealed, LEADS sheet under cover, the collated scans labeled. She wanted everything doubled like a parachute.

Rina’s flashlight bar cut across her field for a moment. “We’re locking down the side entrances,” she said. “Alarms are dumb, but the bolts still work. Once that’s done, we’ll move people toward tables with light.”

“Who’s here?” Mara asked, not as small talk.

“Couple of folks on the computers got very offended at reality,” Rina said. “Maureen took her tea and went to find a window and make doomsday call lists. Tony’s happy. Dev is declaring maritime law at the copy station.”

“I can hear you,” Dev proclaimed from across the room. “And I’m seizing your glow sticks as contraband.”

“Please,” Tony said, clacking his sticks together like drum majors. “I’m deputized by the Department of Aisles.”

Mara slid the bin off the table. The weight was right; the handles didn’t flex. She set it on the floor next to her tote and threaded another zip‑tie through the tote’s D‑rings and clicked it, not for security so much as for dignity. The headlamp made the cable look surgical.

“Where are you staging?” she asked Rina.

“Main desk,” Rina said. “Behind the counter. We’ve got two lockable drawers and a chain. It’s not Fort Knox, but if someone wants it, they’d have to be stupider than I am. And I am pretty stupid right now.”

“You’re fine,” Mara said. She picked up the bin, then the tote, and adjusted. The plastic squeaked against her sleeve, a little friction bark.

They moved through the green corridor Tony had made with glow sticks. He’d wedged them into the little metal caps of the bottom shelf uprights so they stood like runway lights. The effect was unintentional theater: stacks lit from the ankle up, books given a base of eerie radiance, air left unlit above like a low sky. A child’s poster near the children’s room door glowed in the headlamp’s bloom—the moon snail with its radula, the little person in the corner still saying Rude in the margin. In the dark it looked like a threat and a joke at once.

The front desk was an island. Rina had thrown two flashlights onto the counter, both pointed down, to make a little pool. Tony had planted three glow sticks in a coffee mug, a pencil can, and the tip jar for the Friends of the Library; their labels read GIVE and GOT in a crooked script where someone had missed a letter. The beeping from the UPS units under the desk made the pool feel like a hospital for small robots. Dev had put the three‑hole punch on top of a stack of returns as if to keep them from floating away.

“Make way for the sacred bin,” Tony boomed softly. “All hail the sacred bin.”

“Don’t,” Rina said, but she was grinning. She moved the hanging chain aside for Mara. “Bottom drawer is empty. Chain runs through both anchors.”

Mara set the bin down and crouched. The drawer didn’t glide; it thunked along and then surrendered. She nested the bin inside, tested the fit, then passed the chain through the handles and the drawer eyelets. The padlock closed with a flat kiss. She wrote the padlock number on the top of her hand with a rollerball from the pen cup and made herself repeat it once, out loud, absurd and necessary.

“Fort‑ish,” Dev said, leaning on the counter and looking down into the glow. “Fort adjacent.”

“Fort enough,” Rina said. Her radio sputtered in her armpit. “Jorge, say again?”

A man, tinny: “North stairs emergency still dark. Panel’s arguing with me. I need someone to stand there and play human exit sign.”

“I’ll send Tony,” Rina said, as if she’d been craving the chance. “He radiates.”

“Finally,” Tony said, happy. “My time to shine. Literally.” He picked up two sticks like batons and twirled them with showy wrists. “If I don’t come back, tell my stories. Tell them I was mediocre but had heart.”

“Go,” Rina said, rolling her eyes. He went, the sticks leaving green afterimages in the corner of Mara’s vision.

Mara slotted her tote into the second drawer and repeated the chain‑padlock thing, not because anyone here was going to steal a tote but because the act was the thing. She ran the yellow temporary sticker across the drawer face and signed it, the slightly rough laminate grabbing the ink.

Outside, the storm pushed at the doors again. A book cart somewhere rolled a half inch and stopped like someone had tapped it with a knee. Maureen appeared out of the stacks with a thin flashlight that looked like a pen and a stack of index cards. She saw the glow sticks and stopped for a second, her face softening as if the ridiculousness were a kindness.

“Do you need bodies?” Maureen asked Rina. “To point elderly people and city councilors at chairs?”

“Always,” Rina said, grateful. “We’re grouping by light. Board books and gossip rags closest to the glow. Serious reading to the dark.”

“Then I belong in the dark,” Maureen said cheerfully, and disappeared again.

Dev peered at Mara’s band. “That is a very flattering look,” he said. “Miners‑of‑the‑Doom style. If you could please stay, we could use you to read spines. I can pay you in confetti.”

“I already got paid,” Mara said. She touched the pocket where she’d stowed the paper circles and found one had escaped into the seam. She pressed it back in with a forefinger as if it mattered. It did, a little.

Rina leaned on the counter and listened to her radio breathe. “Okay,” she said to no one and everyone. “Okay. We’ve got a path. Aisles fifteen to twenty are runway. East exit has a glow up. North stairs are being impersonated by Tony.”

“Look at him go,” Dev said, craning to watch Tony’s green fans bobbing into the distance.

“And we’re keeping this,” Rina added, knocking the locked drawer with two knuckles. “No one touches. If someone says their name is Authority, ask them to spell it.”

“Copy,” Dev said. He closed his little flashlight to preserve it and peered into the dim with earnest eyes. “We’re still open, technically,” he said, unsure whether that was good.

“We’re a room people are in,” Rina said. “That’s enough.”

Mara, done with the motions, felt a small unclench along the inside of her ribs, where things sat and worried. She tilted the headlamp down for the first time, at shoes, at the thin line of light it made across the rubber toe. The noise of the building had rearranged itself. You could read it like a new map.

“Hold there,” Rina said suddenly into her radio. “Do not open that panel yet. I need to be standing there if sparks come out.”

Jorge: “Copy.”

Dev: “Please no sparks.”

“Please yes light,” Tony’s voice bounced faintly from the stacks, gleeful, echoing.

Mara checked her watch again, recorded the time on the clipboard in the drawer, shut it, settled the chain with her palm like petting a restless animal. She looked up at the room: green cups, white cones of lamp and flashlight, aisles with little luminous bones. It was not orderly, not really. It was contained enough.

“Headlamp fashion is catching on,” Dev said, nodding at a father in the children’s area who had a camping light on his forehead and was reading a board book in boringly dramatic voice to a toddler, both of them lit up like saints at a rave.

“Be useful,” Rina told Dev, and smacked a glow stick against the counter until it flared a little brighter. “Stand there. When people come in from outside, tell them don’t. The door opens, the heat leaves.”

“Consider me a bouncer,” Dev said, squaring his shoulders in a way that made him no taller.

Mara reached up, clicked her headlamp off, then back on at a lower level. The room came in a notch. She liked how the lower beam cut the glare and gave back a little of the dark. She could see beyond it. She adjusted the strap one last time around the pencil. It held. She exhaled.

“Anything else?” Rina asked her quietly, making space for a yes if there was one.

“Might borrow a glow,” Mara said, nodding at the tub. “For the path back.”

Rina fished one out and smacked it into brightness, then handed it over. “Path,” she said, practical benediction.

Mara tucked the green stick into the outside pocket of her jacket where it made a small swamp of light and stepped away from the counter to sweep the path she’d taken, to see if she’d thrown off anything that mattered. She hadn’t. The station was closed. The drawer didn’t rattle when the building took a breath. The bin was part of the desk now, in the way of a thing you notice once and then stop seeing. The darkness still had teeth, but they were not pointed at her work.

Somewhere past Periodicals, Tony whooped because he’d made an elderly couple laugh, or because he’d startled himself by appearing in a reflection as a bipedal glow stick. The main floor absorbed the sound and gave it back muted, an animal choosing not to startle.

“Right,” Rina said, businesslike again. “We ride it.”

“We ride it,” Dev repeated, as if he’d just been given a motto.

Mara turned her lamp toward the aisles, toward the line where Tony hadn’t gotten yet, and walked. She knew where every edge was, and if she didn’t, she had light. The outage had taken order’s topcoat, that was all. Underneath, the grain of the place was still there. She could lay her hand on it and feel it run either smooth or rough, obedient or not. For now: not. She accepted that and kept going, the headlamp’s small star bobbing with her steps, the glow stick making a green halo at her hip as she moved to put her tools where they would still be tools when the lights pretended to wake again.


==================== SCENE 22 ====================
The stairwell had the smell of cold iron and old rope, even though there was no rope—only the suggestion of it, the twist of dust along the wall where a pull had once hung. The steps were steep and stingy, boards with a narrow bite where her boots landed. She kept one gloved hand on the rail for the lie of balance, light pinned low from her headlamp so it didn’t blind her in the corners. The glow stick rode her jacket pocket like a pocketed swamp, its green insistence leaking up and making the nails in the trim look wet. Wind pressed along the outside of the house and found the seam at the top of the well; the stairwell breathed, stale air in, colder air out.

She stopped twice to let the house settle, not that it did—only to give it a chance to say what it was going to say before she put her weight on the next riser. She didn’t want it making new noises because of her when there were already enough. The banister had an interrupt where someone’s palm had worn it satiny, then rough again. She touched both parts. A memory, then a patch. The place had had hands on it, lots of them.

At the turn, the wind turned too. The headlamp caught a ladder of damp on the plaster and the dry lacing of a mud dauber’s nest up under the stringer. A moth scrolled past her face and knocked a fat, soft body against the lens on her forehead, then lodged underneath the brim of her hood and buzzed like a tiny engine before it found a place to die on her collarbone. She thumbed it away gently, a light, stupid thing looking for the wrong kind of moon.

She took the last eight steps in one count. Up there—the last landing, the door to the widow’s-walk—had its own cold. It wasn’t cold like downstairs. It was the color of a struck nail. The door’s paint had once been marine gray and now had the sick cast of faded teeth. The knob was blacked brass, the plate dented and skinned at the screws.

She drew the beam up the face of it and then lowered it again to save it for her eyes. The low setting put a soft cone around the knob. The glow stick made a queasy halo along the jamb where sharecropped light pooled, enough to paint the dust.

Her breath left a slick on the inside of her mask and made her aware of each drag. She stood very still, arm dropped so the light wouldn’t wash the wood. She’d learned the trick with luminescence and skylight, with long corridors and surprise—give your eyes a minute and they’ll give you detail. She stepped back once, heel to heel with the riser, and counted quietly, a ten that became twelve because the house added two in the walls.

When she raised the headlamp again—just a sliver—the first hand bloomed.

It was a left, heel of palm just below the latch height, the fingers splayed out as if to find a better purchase and failing. There was a delay to it, a stupid magic trick: the grain of the paint, the microbeads of dust, then the faintest green coming up from nowhere, more in the whorls and the valleys of the print than the flats. Then another, offset to the right—a right hand—fingers not as spread, a cocked thumb. As the light grazed the area again, a third, higher, made itself known in negative for a second like a flash afterimage, then filled in: it hadn’t been there to her eyes and then it was, a slow surfacing like breath through gossamer.

It made her scalp pull tight. Not because she believed in prints from nowhere, but because the body believes what eyes tell it before the brain does its protest. She could hear the pockets of brine in the clapboards flexing and popping, taking small bites out of the night.

“Alright,” she said, to no one and to the door. Her voice made a soft box out of the space and then put the box back down.

She stepped forward until her body put the wall at her shoulder and the edge of the landing at her heel. She clicked the headlamp off and let the glow stick do the work. The green light was cheap and even and had its own short radius. The prints did not die. They coasted, then steadied. Not self-illuminated, which calmed one part of her. She lifted her hand and spread her fingers alive over the ghost print—didn’t touch—and measured the reach. Taller than a child. Shorter than Owen. Could be anyone, which was no use.

She took out the little camera from her chest pocket and turned the dial by feel. She didn’t want to wash the phenomenon with a flash, didn’t want to lie to the sensor about what was there. She propped the camera against the jamb to make a body out of the edge and braced the rest of it with her face, the ridiculous intimacy of forehead to cold paint. She took a long exposure, counted, took another, bracketed. The shutter ticked in three little polite clicks that felt louder than necessary.

“Don’t make up your mind,” she told herself, the same way she’d tell a witness in an intake room while their mouth kept trying to decide.

She let the headlamp come back up to a low slit, then swept the beam sideways across the door to test it, quick as a fish. The prints lifted brighter under the sweep, then tapered. Not a constant. Not a thing that came alive because she came near. Something charged, something that needed to be shown light and then, as if embarrassed, held onto it for a while.

She crouched to see if there were lower marks. There were. Smears at knee height—soft V’s of glow—not prints exactly, but the story of dirty hands drawn down the surface, dragging out their brightness until there wasn’t much brightness left. One smear led to the baseboard where it died in a chapped line. She leaned in and smelled the seam for cleansers, for cheap detergent, anything with optical brighteners. Just the mild sweetness of old latex and the mineral cold of the wall. Her nostrils stung and she pulled back.

She hinged her light down and kept it steady while she checked the knob and plate. Above and below the knob—two more. On the plate, a crescent where the thumb rides when it knows its way home. The absolute center of the knob had a little starburst where sweat or oil—something human—had made a better bed for the glow. A ring. That was interesting: a ring had scraped, left a darker cut through the paint where the glow broke. She held her own hand up to the knob, slid her ring finger against it by habit, then moved off, aware of the temptation to fit herself into other hands.

In her pocket, her glove rubbed over the small notebook and the pencil she’d wedged under her headlamp strap downstairs. She took them out and wrote, using the doorframe as a desk: Luminous transfer. Delayed. Residual. Left, right, high. Drag down to baseboard. No footwear glow. No knees.

She checked the tread of the last riser and the landing; if someone had stepped up with luminous on their hands, they might have had it elsewhere. Nothing obvious. A soft scrape like chalk along the edge of the riser that was only paint giving itself up.

The thought that sat in the stairwell with her wearing a cheaper, louder coat was the tall story: hands from nowhere, trying the lock from outside, from the widow’s-walk where the gale peeled at the shingles and a human would not linger. She let herself feel the shape of the story because someone else would, and her job tonight was not to banish it—just to see both. Her own breath made a fog bar in the beam.

She tested the surface with the back of her glove. Dry. She didn’t touch skin to paint. She kept the part of herself she could contaminate away from the part she could read. If it was paint—a glow medium—where had it come from? The library had no kindergarten glitters, no novelty jars. The workshop beneath had solvents, varnishes, stains, not this. She had seen this kind of thing on kids’ bedroom ceilings and the weird corners of skate parks, and in a forensic lab where a shoe print lit up like an aquarium when hit with a lamp. Her headlamp was not that lamp, but it was enough.

She turned the beam down to dark and then, after a count, brought it up quickly, just to test the bloom again. It behaved the same way, almost obligingly. Not spirit. Not a unique intelligence. A property.

On the hinge side of the door, toward the jamb, there was a blunt-ended print, an incomplete palm. It had a streak up through the index that made a comet of it. She put her own index finger in the air to match the angle. The streak told her the hand had been wet with the stuff, then someone had dragged a fingertip through it, either absentminded or because the drip annoyed them. Smear logic. She could hear, in her head, the sound fingers make when they worry at paint: the stick-lift, stick-lift, stick.

She photographed that one close, then closer, then with her phone so she had redundancy in a world where batteries were getting precious. She shut the phone off again immediately like a miser.

On the floor, something clicked and rolled toward the stair edge. She froze, then saw the penny by her boot, the old copper making its own dead glow in the green. She hadn’t had a penny. It must have fallen from the rail or from a pocket seam in the years of hands, a disc jarred by the house inhaling. She left it where it had found itself rather than inventing meaning.

She went to her knees and sighted along the line of the bottom of the door in case there was a draft big enough to show itself as motion. There was a black, uniform crack. The prints didn’t move. The wind knew about the house but the door didn’t know about the wind in any way that changed this.

She took a breath and spent it on the latch, just to feel. Locked; the bolt was thrown. She didn’t need to go out there to know that a gale across the boards would take her hat—maybe her—and toss both. She held the knob lightly while she counted the seconds of the next big push of weather, to feel whether the jointing shifted. It didn’t. The house took the shove in its bigger bones.

She stood and tried the trick the other way: killed the light entirely and let the glow stick be the only sun. In full dark the handprints were fainter. The green, which had seemed bright when it first bloomed, was a dusk of its own. The uppermost print went first, as if its pigment had less to give; the lowest smear hung on. Not a ghost that chose a different story; a chemistry running out.

She wrote: Decay curve. Higher less loaded? Touch order? She drew a quick outline of the three hands with their heights in pencil: 65", 59", 31" smear. She put a small mark by the right-hand print to show the thumb orientation—north-east. Left hand’s pinky had a chip of absence where it hadn’t picked up any glow at all, as if the glove—or skin—had a defect.

She bent to the hinge side again and measured the wheel-scar on the plate with her pencil, then wrote: Ring scrape 3 mm, crescent right of center. She ran the light along the jamb and found a ghost edge of the same stuff where someone had breathed hard and fog had condensed on the paint and made a tiny, mottled bloom. She leaned and breathed on the wood herself and watched the little green freckles intensify under the wet for a half second, then ebb. She could not help the small satisfaction of having it behave.

A draft from below tugged at the backs of her knees. The glow stick, in the pocket, knocked lightly against her thigh in a lazy metronome.

“Not a haunting,” she said, not convinced, not trying to be. “A residue.”

The prints were part blessing, part bravado, part sloppy. They were not placed ceremonially; they hunted around the latch with the impatience of someone who knew the door and had paint on their hands and needed it open anyway. The drag down to the baseboard made a little scroll of frustration. That was what she saw because that was what she knew to look for.

She took the pencil and made a dot low on the door where the smear ended, a soft graphite tick off in the margin where it would not draw an eye. Later, if there was a later, she could check whether the glow—whatever left of it—moved with time, with air, with mystery. For now it was a small, private datum. Not enough.

She stepped backward until her boot found the riser and then stepped down two steps so the door was something seen from below. From that angle, the prints lost some of their theatricality and became what marks always are when taken out of their frame: messy evidence of hands being hands.

She pocketed the camera. She glanced at the hand-lamp beam and, without changing the angle, nudged the brightness up and down to watch the bloom. She gave it one last sweep, quick and mean, and then she left the light down. Behind the wash, the afterimages ticked across her retina like tiny meteors. She blinked them away.

On the way down, the house made two more statements through the stringers. The first was the sudden bang of the funicular cable down slope letting go a fraction and catching again in its cradle. The second was the thin complaint of a picture wire snapping somewhere on a wall out of sight. She put those sounds where they belonged, in the ordinary. Her boots talked to the treads as she went, telling them exactly how much she weighed and exactly how long she intended to make use of them.

At the middle landing, where the mud dauber’s nest smoked out the corner like a fossil cloud, she stopped and wrote one more line: Note: smear in arcs not ovals. Pressure changes. Possible glove texture? She drew a close-up: parallel striations across the heel, not skin whorls. She could see them when she caught the angle just right—fine ribbing like the belly of a fish. She put the pencil back under the headlamp strap and pressed the eraser until it caught.

When she looked back once—only once—the prints were still present, but they’d become ordinary to the eye, as if she’d seen them enough to flatten them down into evidence. The glow stick dripped green onto the hall, and she took that down with her, a small moving swamp, while the upstairs kept its own odd astronomy humming without her.


==================== SCENE 23 ====================
The supply closet’s door stuck a quarter inch against swollen jamb and paint drips. Mara put her shoulder into it. The latch let go with a little sheet‑metal ring, and the dark inside exhaled cold, the kind that lives in bleach and damp wood. Her headlamp took the room in strips: mop heads like gray wigs, a row of gallon jugs shoulder to shoulder, coils of hose, a milk crate of rollers, and then the back wall, where the art bins lived—clear tubs with tape labels in three hands, Iris’s the neatest.

She closed the door most of the way, propped it on her heel so the hall glow wouldn’t throw in, and raised the lamp to a shallow angle. Dust shone in a thousand points. Shelf brackets threw hard little shadows downward. The hum of the generator was filtered here until it felt like a borderline headache.

She took out the recorder and thumbed it on without drama. “Essex House incident log. Day three, twenty‑two forty‑seven. Supply closet off north service hall. Inventory: art and maintenance.
