I’m looking for UV sources, luminous compounds, anything that explains the handprints on the widow’s‑walk door.”

The bins at eye level read: IRIS – PIGMENTS A‑D. IRIS – PIGMENTS E‑K. IRIS – TOOLS. IRIS – SOLVENTS/VARNISH. Below: HOUSE – PVA/GLUE. HOUSE – PLASTER. Someone had jammed a bundle of drop cloths between two shelves; a corner hung down like a tongue.

“Okay,” Mara said to the bins. “Let’s not be coy.”

She slid the IRIS – TOOLS bin forward with two fingers on the rim to keep her glove from brushing anything unnecessary. It was light—compartment boxes, matte knives, a coil of nichrome wire, a packet of nitrile gloves. A sharpie had bled a dotted name across the packet: Iris. The next bin had more heft; when she eased it onto the floor, something inside thumped, then rolled to a stop.

She popped the lid half off—talking to the recorder, to the lamp, to the quiet. “IRIS – SOLVENTS/VARNISH. Cap integrity check… lids look clean. I have D‑limonene, turp, two jars of shellac flakes, one marked ‘Amber, dewaxed.’” She lifted and set them aside one by one, naming the mass as she went. The jar that had rolled was on its side under the others, centered in a ring of faint dust disruption exactly the width of its belly. The label was handwritten, a steady dark print: LUMINOUS ACRYL. Below, a second line: Zinc sulfide/stront aluminate, green.

She didn’t touch it yet. “Jar present: luminous acrylic compound. Manufacturer label missing—handwritten. Iris’s hand matches? Likely. Exterior shows… hang on.”

She lowered the headlamp beam and angled it. The ridge on the jar’s lid had that slight drag of dried medium. Around the threads, an irregular meniscus showed where something had dried and flaked, then been wiped: little commas of residue caught in the cut of the cap.

Mara leaned the recorder to her shoulder and called up the line on Rourke’s radio. The initial hiss filled the closet and made the mop heads look like they were listening.

“Rourke.”

“Go.”

“In the art/maintenance closet off north service. I’m looking at a jar labeled ‘Luminous Acryl—Zinc sulfide/strontium aluminate, green.’ Handwritten label, likely Iris’s. Residue on cap says it’s been opened recently. I’m going to bag it. I need prints on the jar, lid ridges, and the bin rim. I also want timestamps on door opens and whoever’s been in this closet the last forty‑eight hours. If there’s no sensor, we’ll have to go to hallway traffic or staff statements.”

He didn’t sigh—Rourke had discipline—but she heard him re‑seat his pen. “Copy. Bagging authorized. I’ll ping Maureen for movement logs and check the wing cam. You said luminous green?”

“Green that blooms under UV and after some exposure. There’s a second item possibly stashed. Stand by.”

She clipped the radio back and made herself move slow: clean bags from her kit, evidence stickers, a little coil of barcoded seals. She laid one open like a sterile bed on the shelf itself and, with nitrile on nitrile, lifted the jar by the glass shoulder, avoiding the lid, and set it in the bag. The weight was not full, not empty. It landed with a thick sound—paint has its own gravity. She fed the zipper track together, smoothed the seal, and signed across the sticker.

The headlamp’s cone caught something at the floor baseboard: a small black cylinder pushed between the mop bucket wheels and the wall, nearly flush with the kickboard. Not thrown. Placed. Mara crouched, bent, and, without altering her breathing or the beam, said, “And here we are.”

She reached around the bucket with the long tongs from her kit—chrome, ridiculous, perfect—and teased the cylinder forward until she could see the knurled grip. It was a flashlight the size of a cigar, body black, bezel scratched to raw aluminum at three points, the sort of cheap tactical that lives in glove compartments. The tailcap had a tiny circle of luminous tape, half peeled.

“Object: UV light, pocket size,” she told the recorder. “No brand visible without rotation. It’s placed against the baseboard, not dropped. Dust disturbance is a neat trough the width of the body. I’m seeing fine residue on the bezel edge—likelihood of paint transfer, green spectrum, non‑visible under white.”

She didn’t thumb it on. She didn’t need to. The bezel threads also carried the little bright flecks she knew how to see if she wanted to—she didn’t. She slid a bag around it with the tongs and let it roll into its clear cell. The bag crackled, the sound loud and thin. She sealed and labeled.

The maintenance shelves on the left had a coffee can full of screws and a mason jar of old keys. The closet smelled like Pine‑Sol and, under it, like damp cardboard. She kept going, calm, deliberate.

“IRIS – PIGMENTS A‑D,” she said, because the act of naming kept things straight under stress. “Cadmium red medium, cad yellow, cobalt, two bags of titanium white, one with a tear taped in Iris’s blue painter’s tape. Nothing suggests luminous powders in that bin. E‑K has four jars of flake graphite, a bag of iridescent mica. Not relevant.”

She put the bins back exactly to the ghosted rectangles their dust had left on the shelf. The bucket’s wheels left two little C‑shapes in the dust where she had moved it; she photographed those, because the act of being boringly thorough is how you beat someone with tricks.

A rattle outside made her look at the door—only the house settling, a draft moving under at ankle‑height. She set her boot against it again and kept the beam shallow. She didn’t love closets; she didn’t love the small thinking they encouraged. She kept to the work.

“Rourke,” she said, thumb back on the radio.

“Go.”

“I have the UV flashlight bagged and marked. I’ll bring both items to the library table for processing. Can you get me timestamps for the blackout hits tonight? When the north service hall went dark and when power cycled. That window’s relevant to who would have needed a UV source.”

“Copy. I can get the gene set logs now. You want prints on the closet door?”

“Handle and inside face at latch height. And check the drop cloth bundle on the right—someone used it recently, and I don’t want to lay my hands on it. If you can send Alana with dusting, we can work in sequence.”

“On her way.”

She put the radio down, shut the recorder off to save what battery she could, and let herself be quiet for thirty seconds. The quiet changed the room; it felt colder and more honest without her voice in it.

She turned it back on and kept it in the stand. “Assessment: The presence of luminous paint in Iris’s labeled bin is consistent with her work on the art program. The UV flashlight in maintenance is not surprising in itself—housekeeping uses UV for leak checks and housekeeping—” she checked the shelves and said it again to clean it “—housekeeping uses brighteners and would have reason to keep a UV source. However, the placement of the flashlight—deliberate, concealed behind the mop bucket, not in the tools bin—pushes this out of coincidence and into intent. The lid residue on the luminous paint jar suggests recent use. The cap threads show wiped residue consistent with someone cleaning the edge. I’ve photographed and bagged the items under chain‑of‑custody. Request: fingerprints on jar lid, jar shoulder, bin rim, closet door outside and in; also on the UV flashlight body and tailcap. Additional request: timestamps for any access to this closet in past forty‑eight hours and, if available, Iris’s studio logs to confirm when she last used luminous compounds.”

She reached up for the top shelf, running the beam across the lip where people toss things when they shouldn’t. There was a box of chalk, a spray bottle full of water, a sack of clothespins, and an old, flat pizza box the color of damp cardboard. It looked insultingly out of place. She took a photo and left it alone.

Footsteps came thinly through the door—a voice, then Alana’s shorter reply, direction skeptical even when agreeable. Mara opened the door with a wrist to keep her glove back.

Alana, in her powder apron, peered in. “You called it,” she said, already tired. “What am I ruining tonight?”

“Luminous paint jar in here and a UV flashlight by the mop bucket, both bagged. I need the closet handle and the bin rim, please. And whatever prints you can lift from the flashlight once we get you a stable surface in the library.”

Alana looked at the shelf, the bags, then at Mara’s neat little battlefield of labels and notes. “You keep making me grateful for my life choices,” she said, and then, to the swag of drop cloths, “Don’t move.”

“Also,” Mara said, because she knew what came next if she didn’t lay it out cleanly, “for the record: Iris labeled the paint. That tracks with her inventory. The flashlight’s placement is the red flag. Log it as found concealed, not shelved. No assumptions attached.”

Alana snapped on her dust mask. “Stick around?”

“I’ll run these to the library and print the forms. Bring your lifts there. Rourke is pulling timestamps.”

“God bless the paperwork,” Alana said, and snapped a photo of the handle.

Mara stepped past her into the hall, the closet air trailing out in a smooth, cold ribbon. She didn’t look back; she didn’t need to. In her hands, the two bags knocked against each other, clear parcels with their ugly little calm. She told the recorder the time again—twenty‑two fifty‑nine—because it mattered, and then she shut it off and moved.

In the library, under the battery lantern and the map of the grounds, she set the two bags on the clean surface she’d kept sacred since she arrived. She wrote the evidence entries in her even hand: Item 7‑3A: Jar, glass, labeled ‘Luminous Acryl—Zinc sulfide/strontium aluminate, green.’ Found in Supply Closet—Art/Maintenance, Shelf B‑2, bin ‘IRIS – SOLVENTS/VARNISH.’ Item 7‑3B: Flashlight, UV, black, pocket size; found behind mop bucket, floor level, against baseboard. She initialed, time‑stamped, and slid the forms alongside.

The generator pushed a little more sound into the library’s plaster. A slip of fog licked the north windows, low and patient. Mara opened the radio again.

“Rourke.”

“Go.”

“Evidence logged: 7‑3A and 7‑3B. I want fingerprints on both, plus the closet door. I want timestamps for closeted access. Also include Iris’s last sign‑out of pigments if Maureen has that handy. I need this clean.”

“You’ll have it. Anything else?”

“Yes,” Mara said, and her voice stayed level. “We can take ‘haunting’ off this one. Put this under hoax until contradicted.”

“Copy,” he said. No argument, no commentary. The radio timed itself out with a tidy click.

She sat down, crossed off a column that needed crossing, and kept her eyes on the forms until the ink looked like it had always been there. Outside, the house went on with its ordinary, and she let it. She had the jar. She had the UV light. She had the time. The rest would come in under names and dust.


==================== SCENE 24 ====================
The foyer had become a harbor in the blackout, a cavernous mouth taking in wet people and letting them drip and steam under the little generator’s pulse. Wind pressed a palm against the glass of the entry like someone thinking about coming in. Coats hung from the hall tree in their sodden colors, boots rucked their tides along the baseboards, and the tiled compass rose at the center of the floor gleamed with tracked salt.

Mara wiped her hands dry on the towel she kept clipped to her belt and came in from the corridor with her bag zipped and squared. The lanterns the staff had set on the newel posts made the banister glow like an instrument panel. The grandfather clock’s pendulum ticked and stalled, ticked and stalled, losing a second, stealing it back.

Julian stood under the balcony, talking in a low voice to Maureen at the secretary desk. His tie was off; his shirt was open at the throat; there was a ragged dark drip at his elbow where his sleeve had met a doorframe and lost. He was all polish except where the day had scuffed him. When he saw Mara he finished his sentence to Maureen with a little mouth-tilt that registered as a smile from a distance and nothing like it up close.

“Mara,” he said, pivoting, “I was about to come find you. Is the library still your war room or has it ascended to—what is it you call it? An evidence temple?”

“Sanctuary,” she said, and the word landed dry between them. “We need to talk. Briefly.”

“That sounds like a threat and a mercy.”

“Not a threat.” She glanced past him at Maureen, and at Cass curled on the leather settle with her hoodie up and earbuds in, one bright nub winking blue in the dark. Owen had taken the foyer bench apart earlier to tighten something, and its bones leaned against the wall like a cot waiting for its patient. Alana’s toolbox sat heavy by the door.

Mara shifted so the newel post blocked them from direct view. “Here is what I’m prepared to say in a public space: the widow’s-walk door has prints on it that read under UV. I have the paint jar and the light. They’re logged. I’ve asked for runtimes, sign-outs, and prints. It’s a staged effect.”

Maureen’s pen paused mid-figure. The top of her head didn’t move; only the pen did, and then only to click once. Cass kept breathing, a small fog at the edge of the settle.

Julian’s smile ratcheted. “What a wonderfully neutral announcement.”

“I’m keeping it neutral.”

“Until when?”

“Until I interview the person who can speak to those two objects without you or anyone else in the room.”

He rocked back on his heels slightly, hands in pockets. There was a tiny cut on his thumb, a pantry kiss of something sharp. “You’ll forgive me for hearing ‘ambush’ and ‘unfair process’ there.”

“You can hear what you like. I’m telling you the stagecraft is off the board as a mystery. You asked me to treat your house like a client. My advice is to keep certain findings out of group conversation until they’re clean.”

“Your advice, which I appreciate,” he said, the word appreciate fitted with a hinge that wanted to snap, “competes with my obligations. I have staff downstairs trying to sleep, a board on remote aspirin, a detective who is, how did you put it earlier, hungry, and a property whose reputation—”

“Which is not a living organism,” she said, level. “It doesn’t need your bedside manner. People do.”

Maureen’s pen clicked again, involuntary as a swallow.

Julian dipped his head once, acknowledging the hit without stepping back. “All right,” he said. “You have paint you believe was used.”

“Not belief. A jar labeled with the compounds that produce persistence under UV and charge under ambient. It fluoresces. The hand sizes on the door correspond to adult spans. The light was placed behind a mop bucket where it would warm the paint to bloom when someone flipped it.”

“And ‘someone’ is—”

“A placeholder. I’m not doing ‘someone’ in the foyer.”

“Charming.” His eyes traced the lantern lit up her shoulder, the zipper of her bag, the mud freckles on her boots. “What do you want from me besides the privilege of internally combusting?”

“Iris,” she said. “Half an hour. No chorus, no counsel, no recorder unless it’s mine. Tomorrow morning at ten in the library, while we still have enough generator to process prints. If she’s asleep, let her sleep. But she’s first.”

Maureen’s pen made a small bracket at the edge of her pad. “She’s on the early linen rotation,” she said, looking at the paper rather than them. “She’ll be up by six unless the machines are cut.”

“Ten,” Mara repeated. “Privately.”

Julian shifted his weight. The cut on his thumb whitened as he pressed it against his palm. “You’re skipping some steps here.”

“No. I’m choosing the order. I’m doing you the courtesy of not putting ‘hoax’ on the group radio and letting it ricochet. That’s your benefit. Mine is that I get clean testimony without an audience or a script.”

“Testimony,” he said softly. The word tasted wrong in his mouth, legal and wrong.

“You wanted me to say ‘conversation’? That’s fine. Conversation.”

He looked at the compass rose under their feet, at north made of black tile and east of cream. The generator gave a low hiccup and recovered. Somebody opened a door down the hall; wind came in like a thought and closed again. When he lifted his head, the polish was back where it had slipped.

“If I agree to this,” he said, “you’ll keep this”—he touched the air between them, a little shimmer of signal—“out of the communal mouth?”

“I’ll keep it where it belongs.”

“And you won’t go theatrically announcing that my staff is painting specters on the widow’s-walk door.”

“That’s loaded,” she said. “Strip it down: I will not announce. I will not accuse. I will ask questions and listen. After that, we do the next right thing.”

“Is that a trademarked procedure in your line or a personal ethic?”

“Both.”

Behind them, the settle squeaked as Cass rolled onto her back. She kept her eyes shut. Maureen set her pen down and rubbed at a cramp with her thumb and forefinger.

Julian took a breath he didn’t quite want. “Ten,” he said. “You’ll have your half hour. I’ll, ah, keep my tongue joinable to the roof of my mouth until then.”

“I appreciate it.”

“You will enjoy enormously reminding me if I fail.”

“That won’t be necessary.”

He glanced at her bag. “The items are secure? Before you blister me about the hallway not being the place, let me ask it: they’re not… wandering.”

“In the library, under logged seal. Rourke has the chain.”

“Of course he does.” Julian’s mouth thinned. “I’ll tell you something you can ignore: our board chair believes in omens. He would have loved your handprints. I suppose I should thank you for ruining his evening.”

“You can thank me by not recycling the word ‘haunting’ in my hearing.”

“Done, until it becomes convenient again.”

She let that pass. “Anything else you need from me tonight?”

“Yes.” He glanced past her to the brief flicker of lightning far off that made everyone in the foyer pause inside their own skulls. “Tell me we’re not going to discover that whoever did this—this craft—also did something worse.”

She held his gaze without letting it anchor. “I’m not telling stories,” she said. “I’m telling you I’ll keep this tight. That’s what I have.”

“That’s never as soothing as it sounds in cop dramas,” he said lightly, and for a second something unperformed came through—the fatigue of holding a building and all its veins in his head. “Ten,” he said again, rehanging his mask with a little internal click. “I’ll make sure she’s there.”

“Don’t prime her.”

“I don’t coach witnesses, Mara. I’m not silly.”

“We’ll see.”

He started to step back, then held up one finger in a little coda. “Oh. If you could let Rourke know—on the record—that any forensic activity in staff spaces must be cleared through me, we avoid more… stepping on toes.”

“I’ll let him know you prefer courtesy,” she said. “It costs nothing.”

“And earns interest.” He nodded, the conversation slotted into some private ledger, and turned toward Maureen. “Ten,” he said to her. “Mark it.” To Mara, over his shoulder: “Good night. Or the approximation thereof.”

“Good night,” she said.

He went, shoes whispering on the tile, his shadow stepping twice on the far wall before he was gone down the corridor.

The foyer exhaled. The pendulum ticked and stole, ticked and stole. Cass cracked one eye and then let it drop. Maureen made the bracket into a box and filled the box. She didn’t look up.

Mara stood there a second, looking at the wet boots and their distances. The wind had found its own rhythm. In her bag, the paperwork sat under the zipper, a small pack of edges, straight and satisfied.

On her way back to the library, she paused at the base of the stairs and radioed low. “Rourke.”

A crackle, then: “Go.”

“Evidence stays as logged. No chatter about the prints. I have a ten o’clock with Iris in the library. If anyone asks, it’s a cataloging review.”

“Copy. You need a warm body there?”

“No. I need it quiet.”

“Copy,” he said. The radio clicked off.

She tucked the radio away and took the stairs up with her hand on the rail, counting her own steps out of habit. In the foyer, the compass rose kept north and nobody made a show of following.


==================== SCENE 25 ====================
The morning had the wrong color—blanched, like paper soaked and stretched thin—and the library’s high windows kept passing that light back and forth as if unsure it belonged here. Mara had pushed two study tables together and spread the house plan across them—blue vellum over yellowed revisions, a transparency of exits and voids. A length of neon surveyor’s twine held the corners like a crosshair. Next to it: a legal pad with columns, a Sharpie, a roll of blue tape, a stick of grease pencil that left fat, obedient marks on glass.

Owen came in still in his windbreaker, hair wet, radio clipped at an angle. He had the big ring of keys hooked to his belt and a canvas tote of odd metals that clanked when he set it down: hasps, shims, two four‑inch barrel bolts, a bag of Tapcons, a driver with the bit taped in so it wouldn’t slip.

“Six hours,” he said. He was already peeling off one glove. “Tide book says peaking just after two. Surge on top of that if the fetch sits right.”

Mara nodded once and pulled a red pencil line down the long ocean face of the plan. “No foils. No other hands. Just us. Zones?”

“East galleries, the conservatory link, the staff corridor that runs south—those will take it first. Funicular doors at the boathouse head, too. If the current gets into that runout, we’re pulling it up for a month.”

Mara put a numbered dot at each place, then circled the library and hallways scope in orange. “We triage and stage. You prioritize mechanical and anything that compromises structure. I’ll take doors and windows on public rooms. We’re short on bags?”

“We’re short on everything that isn’t gossip.” He tipped the tote and produced a wrinkled inventory sheet. “We have forty‑two fill bags, sixty‑ish loose sandbags, half of them torn or frayed. Two pallets of mulch, which is not helpful except as mass. Plywood? Three sheets half‑inch, one three‑quarter. Tarps, yes, but they’re like dresses on a boat if we can’t fasten them. The good screws we’re saving for the generator shed if—” He caught himself. “Just—saving.”

“Duct tape?”

“Enough to mummify one medium‑sized liar.”

She wrote: 42 fill / 60 decent / 20 questionable / 4 ply / tarps: yes / tape: ok. “How many bodies?”

“Four I can trust not to wander off.” He ticked them: “Maureen’s nephew is back from Gloucester; he’s strong and will do what he’s told. Theo’s out with Cass but can be recalled if you insist—”

“No,” she said. “Keep him where he is.”

“Right. Lyle from maintenance—”

“The one with the knee?”

“Left knee, but he’ll dig if you give him the shovel side and a reason. Iris offered, but the greenhouse roof is… anyway, she’s a runner, not a weight.”

“Then it’s us.” Mara tapped the funicular shaft on the plan. “Lockout before we get precious. If someone tries to use it as a shortcut up from the slips while we’re sealing, we lose the lot.”

He looked at her, measuring the hour against what they could actually touch. “Lockout first. Then close the eastern sash in the map room and the microfilm alcove. Those panes birth water with a crosswind.”

She wrote the list as a numbered ladder. When she finished, she folded the bottom of the page up so only one through five showed. “We count this out loud,” she said.

He nodded. “One: Lock out the funicular at both ends. Two: Bolt and bag the conservatory link door. Three: Board and tape the east gallery sashes. Four: Stage sand at the staff corridor threshold and the library’s ocean door. Five: Clear drains on the south run and set the pump—if it still has the kick.”

“Six,” she said, ignoring the fold. “Assign routes and mark breach points as we see them. We photograph everything as we leave it.”

He took a Sharpie and drew a fat X over the ballroom on the plan. “Ignore this entire wing for now. It’s high and sealed.”

“I don’t like ignoring it.”

“You can dislike it and still do it.”

They walked, the library’s air heavy with paste and old bindings. The hallways had already learned the day’s posture: doors cracked to listen, drop cloths bundled out of the way, a ladder like a quiet person standing against a wall. Mara carried the grease pencil and the tape; Owen took the tote and the keys.

In the eastern hallway, the windows rattled in their frames with the distant ocean. The sound found the letters carved into the wainscot and made them hum. Mara drew an arrow on the first pane’s corner and wrote E1. She could feel in her arms the old training settling in: number, assess, move; don’t fall in love with a solution when the material won’t take it.

At the funicular landing, the door wore a lacquered politeness. Owen set the tote down and dug for the hasps. He checked the jamb, then the throw of the lock, then the slop in the door. “There’s a trick,” he said, half to himself. He palmed the key, threw the lock, opened the door six inches. The smell came up cold and mineral, like the inside of a shell. He leaned and looked down the rail run to where it bent out of sight. “If water comes this far, it’s already over. But we keep the toy out of reach.”

Mara took pictures: hinge side, lock side, the key position. “Tag it.”

He held up the lockout tag, bright yellow with a place for a signature. He wrote his name, the time, the reason—Storm—then clipped it through the shackle and spun the combination he’d taped to the back of his hand. Two hasps. Two padlocks. He slid the strike plate and sank one Tapcon at a diagonal. The metal sang when the driver bit.

“Other end,” Mara said. “I don’t want anyone creative on the boathouse side thinking they can beat the tag from here.”

They went down the service stairs to the lower landing, where the air had already cooled and the stairwell sweated. The cement steps had that crouching damp that turns ankles. Owen locked that door too, set the second tag, then put the keys back on his belt with a finality that had nothing ceremonial to it.

“Picture,” Mara said. He held the ring up for her phone: the tag, the time, the key count.

Back up. The hall ran long to the east, to the galleries that showed the ship paintings. The first window there had a sash cord that had been mended twice. Mara shoved her thumb under the meeting rail and found the rot she remembered in the weather bead.

“We can tape it with the plastic and then set a strip over the muntins,” she said.

“And accept that it will cry and we’ll lose the paint,” he said. “It’s that or lose the wall.”

They moved in practiced ways. He measured; she tore and smoothed the plastic. He pre‑drilled; she handed screws. The driver’s noise, sharp and short, snagged in the ceiling roses and came back in little aftersounds. Mara pressed the plastic into the corners with the back of a spoon and then ran her finger along the tape edge, sealing the crease. She wrote: E2 sealed 08:13 MO/MM.

“You’re neat,” he said, not teasing.

“It makes checking easier.”

“There’s an argument that this whole house would benefit from your compulsions.”

“There’s an argument that this whole house would benefit from fewer windows.”

They taped three in a row, skipped the stained glass by the stair because the lead would not like anything she did to it, and answered the radio twice with short acknowledgments: copy, copy.

At the library’s ocean door—a set of French doors with a habit of gapping—Mara dropped to one knee and checked the threshold. The strip that ran under the sweep had always been tired; now it was resigned. She could see daylight in a seam no daylight should find.

“Bag it,” Owen said. “We can set the first course tight to the wood and wedge it with the dowels.”

“You’re asking those hinges to hold a lot of hope.”

“Not hope. Mass.”

They set a line of sandbags like small animals crouched in a row. Lyle came from the back with a dolly piled with bags—more than she expected—and dropped his hand to the stack as if to feel their heartbeats. “You want the seam staggered?” he asked.

“Like brick,” Mara said. “And twist the ties under, so we don’t get wicks.”

Lyle grinned and did it right. He had the knee brace, and it squeaked when he bent, but his hands were strong and didn’t shake. They built two courses and stopped. Owen stepped back and eyed the handle height. “Third row would pin it,” he said, “but I’m not sure the floor likes that much water pressing right here.”

“We’ll stage the third in the hall and wait for the glass to say please,” Mara said. She wrote on the tape: LIB OCEAN D1 2 courses staged 08:41.

Down the staff corridor, the smell changed: metal, bleach, the stutter of a pump trying to decide whether to live. Owen cocked his head and then turned for the service door to the south run, where the drains took what they could of the lawn’s runoff.

“Moment,” he said. “If this goes off, we all wade.” He went, and she followed, because the list didn’t breathe if they let the mechanicals fail. The pump sat like a lecturing toad, its hose asleep. He checked the power, the priming, the intake. “Fuel’s low,” he said without dramatics. “It’ll give us fifty minutes at this load.”

Mara watched the flow pick up, then steady. “Fifty gets us two more windows. Three if we keep our heads down.”

His radio chirped. He answered, listened, frowned. “Conservatory link is weeping under the sill,” he told her. “Iris says the inner door swells and won’t seat.”

They cut back through the hall, their footprints indexing the dust. The conservatory link was a little throat of a corridor, glass above and to the sides, with the small, expensive arrogance of a nineteenth‑century graft. The outer door had a visible lemon of light at the bottom corner where the weatherstrip had died in its sleep.

“Bags and a wedge,” Mara said. “Tape won’t stop capillary. We need something that resists in bulk.”

“Do we have wedges?” Owen asked.

She looked at his tote without hope. “Do we have wood?”

He tapped the tote, produced a slat of scrap oak that had done three lifetimes: cradle, shelf, mystery. “One,” he said. “Make friends.”

They pressed a sandbag into the corner and drove the wedge against it until the rubber of the weatherstrip finally admitted there was a world beyond its opinions and flattened. She felt the cold pull through the glass as the wind changed. She marked the door: CONS LINK outer wedged + bagged 09:12. “Inner door?”

The inner door stuck as promised. Owen put his shoulder to it and leaned, and it moved a finger’s width and stopped like a mule. “It’ll budge with heat,” he said. “Which we don’t have time for.” He looked at the frame. “Or we cut the strip.”

“Don’t cut,” Mara said. “We need it later.” She took the roll of blue tape and made a gasket, wetting the tape with her thumb as she laid it over the contact points. Crude, but responsive. The door settled against it and made a noise like agreement. She wrote: inner seated w/ tape 09:18 future replace.

They were clocking the route when the first real edge of the ocean came in. It arrived in sound, a low thickening under the regular racket. Owen looked down the hallway toward the east and then back at the list in his head. “Map room,” he said. “Then microfilm. Then the staff corridor threshold.”

“Funicular’s done,” she said.

He nodded once—one weight off the scale, a dozen still on.

In the map room the glass had more respect for itself than the hallway sashes did, but the casements had a defiant habit of grinning at their own corners. Mara smoothed tape and laid plastic triangles in the upper panes where they loved to fail. Owen tightened the stays until they squealed.

“You always write the time,” he said, reading her tape flags.

“I always want to know how much time I lost,” she said.

A gust shoved the east face of the house and went away like a mood. The tape made that wet kiss sound protective things make when they take a set. Mara finished the corner and looked at the plan she’d brought folded in her pocket. “We skip the northeast breakfast room,” she said. “It’s high and not our remit.”

“It’s also where the staff cut through to the pantry,” he said. “If they leave it open—”

“I’ll put a tag on it. That’s what the tape is for. We can’t do them all.”

They stopped in the hall by the microfilm alcove. The little aluminum window there had the ugly self‑confidence of a retrofit. Mara marked it, sealed it, and wrote MFLM A sealed 09:37, the pen writing on tape with a hiccup where her hand caught the frame’s burr.

Lyle came back with the dolly again, knee squeaking. “Mulch,” he said, tapping the plastic‑wrapped bags with a flat palm. “It’s not sand, but it’s weight.”

Mara blinked at them, recalculating. “We can use it as a second tier behind the sand,” she said. “If it gets wet, it swells and locks. We bleed the water between the two.”

Owen nodded. “Put it at the staff corridor threshold. That door’s all talk and no bones.”

They moved as a unit now, tasks clicking. At the staff corridor, where the back door met the long hall of the service rooms, they laid a line of sand, then two mulch bags sideways behind it, then a line of sand again. It made a little topography that pleased the eye in a way that had nothing to do with beauty and everything to do with function. Mara stepped back and took two photos—one wide, one close. She wrote on the tape: STAFF CORR thresh: 2/2 layered 10:02.

“Supplies,” Owen said, looking into the tote. “We are out of decent screws.”

“Use the Tapcons on anything we want to take back down. Save deck screws for permanent annoyances.”

He grunted. “I’m almost enjoying your taxonomy.”

She lifted her head. “We’re losing time.”

He stood straighter. “Then we stop talking about enjoying anything.”

They pushed through the next twenty minutes elbow‑deep in tape and fasteners, marking every move. On the last of the east hall windows, the putty sighed and came away in her hand. She pressed it back like bread dough and taped over it with a little guilt and no apology.

By the time they turned back to the library again, the list had bled to the bottom of the page. Mara unfolded the crease and read the six and seven as if she’d been hiding treasure from herself. Six: Assign routes and mark breach points as we see them. Seven: Photograph and log.

“Zones,” she said. “Name them so anyone can talk about them without pointing. East Hallway A through D. Gallery. Library Ocean. Staff Corridor South. Conservatory Link. Funicular Upper and Lower—marked.”

Owen wrote the letters on blue tape and stuck them on the walls near the head of each place. The library table got a key with the same names and a sketch with the arrows that made sense if you knew the bones of the house. They assigned bodies against them: Lyle to Staff South with the dolly and the mulch; Maureen’s nephew to East Hall with tape and a rule; Owen to chase mechanical far ends; Mara to keep the door list and do eyes on the library face.

He checked the tote again, like a parent counting snacks on a bus trip. “We are out of wedges,” he said. “We have four bags full of sand left and ten light ones that will break if you ask them to be brave. Duct tape is half a roll. The good tarps are two. The third tarp is a laundry bag with a secret.”

“List it,” she said. She wrote: sand 4 full / 10 light; tarps 2 good + 1 not; screws gone; wedges 0. Next to those she put small angry hyphens.

“This is not enough,” he said, not like a plea but like a fact.

“It’s what we have,” she said. “We can pull the old theatrical drapes from the lecture room and cut them into strips to push into gaps. That’s an hour we don’t have unless someone else cuts.”

He thought, eyes moving without seeing the library. “I’ll send Maureen’s nephew with a box cutter and a broom handle. He can make them on the way back from the east.”

“And the ropes?”

“I have one coil and a morals problem about using the antique bell pull line.”

“Use it.”

“Copy,” he said, because the radio had made that word contagious.

He wrote the priorities big and simple on a clean page and taped it at eye level at the head of the library door:

1. Funicular: locked and tagged both ends. Done.
2. Conservatory Link: outer wedged + bagged; inner seated. Done.
3. East Hall A–D: sashes taped + plastic; stained glass bypass. 2/4 done.
4. Library Ocean D1: 2 sand courses set; 3rd staged.
5. Staff Corridor South: layered 2/2 set.
6. Map Room + Microfilm: sealed. Done.
7. Drains/Pump South Run: live; 50 min fuel as of 09:05.

Under that he wrote: Supplies short. Sand 4 full/10 light. Screws low. Wedges 0. Tape 1/2 roll. Tarps 2 good. Request: drape strips, rope.

Mara stood with the pencil poised. “What did we miss.”

He didn’t answer for a beat. Then: “The east stair landing casement. It hides under the turn. It spits when the wind finds the notch.”

She marked it: East Stair Turn casement: tape strip. Then she looked at her watch. “We have four and a half hours,” she said. “The water will arrive whether we do or not.”

“Don’t say arrive like it’s a guest,” he said. “Say: it will try the doors.”

“It will try the doors,” she repeated, and for a second the language made her feel like she’d scraped a splinter out of her own hand.

They split again. Lyle wheeled away with the dolly. Owen went to the stair turn. Mara headed for the lecture room to pull drapes. The hallway’s long runner bunched under her foot and she smoothed it without thinking, a habit that annoyed her even as it calmed some corner of her.

In the lecture room, the drapes were as heavy as a sleeping animal. She rolled them on the floor, cut long tongues with the box cutter, and wrapped the strips on her arm like bandages. When she came back to the library, Owen was at the table again, his finger on the map’s line where the cliff edge met the house’s eastern wall.

“We won’t get to the breakfast room,” he said. “Or the north servants’ entry. We won’t touch the greenhouse.”

“It’s out of scope,” she said. “It’s out of time.”

“It doesn’t care about our scope.”

She put the drape rolls down, then pressed one into his hand. “Give these to the people who can make them matter. If someone comes to you asking where to put their hands, send them to a name on that list, not a hallway. No one wanders. No one improvises with a crowbar.”

He turned one of the drape strips over his knuckles like a coin. “You’re not charming when you’re right.”

“I’m charming exactly never.”

This time, his laugh was an acknowledgment without warmth. “We’re short on sand,” he said again, not letting the words go slack.

“We’re short,” she said, “and we have a list.” She tapped the taped priorities. The Sharpie marks looked like they had their own pulse.

He took a breath that was plan rather than air. “All right,” he said. “We move.”

As they went back into the hall, she saw the library’s map, the transparent sheets aligned under the twine crosshair, the taped‑up edge of the legal pad curling like a little wave. It wasn’t pretty. It didn’t need to be. The house felt like it had been told what to do.

Outside, the color of the morning did not change. Inside, they set their last numbers and tried not to watch the clock.


==================== SCENE 26 ====================
They hit the west wing with their arms already tired, the hallway full of the smell of old carpet and new wet. The windows along the gallery were tall as doors, pallid light coming in slick and smeared. A rattle threaded the panes—each gust finding a different loose part of the house and plucking it.

“Veranda first,” Owen said, hauling the coil of tarp rolls from his shoulder and nudging the toolbox with his knee. “The shutters are still up on this run. If we get them closed, the leaks won’t matter as much.”

“‘Won’t matter’ is a lawyer phrase,” Mara said, shoving the box cutter into her back pocket and grabbing the strap of the tarp coil with him. “We’re aiming for less ruin.”

They kicked through the service door to the veranda. The door fought back, a backhand of wind slapping it open so hard it bounced. Outside, the veranda ran like a shallow pier along the west face, white-painted boards gone gray with salt, balustrade scabbed with rust at the screws. The ocean beyond the cliff wore the same color as the sky: a hard, thoughtless pewter. Rain picked a slant and stuck to it. The gust hit crosswise, shearing so that anything lifted wanted to leave.

“This is going to be fun,” Owen said without irony. He dropped the tarps and knelt at the first shutter. Its iron hook was fused to the wall plate by paint and neglect. He knocked it with his knuckles, then with the butt of his screwdriver, and got a flake-snow of old enamel for his trouble.

“Try the pry bar,” Mara said. She slid the bar’s mouth behind the hook and leaned. The paint gave in a sighing strip, the hook jumping free so suddenly she had to hop back to keep from smacking her own shin. Wind slammed the shutter against the siding like a warning.

“On my count,” Owen said, flattening his palm to the wood. “One—two—”

They pulled outward and let the wind help, the shutter a sail in their hands, the hinge protesting with a long animal squeal. The two halves didn’t quite meet; age had warped them, and the center line was a crooked kiss.

“Dogs,” Owen said. His hair was already wet enough to stick to his forehead, making an angular V over his brow. He pointed to the wooden brace set above the window. “They’ll hold it closed if we can get it up.”

Mara got her shoulder under the brace and lifted. It was a simple drop-dog, heavy oak with a rusted ring, stiff in the slot. Owen pressed the shutters closed from the outside. His knuckles went pale. On her second lift the brace scraped into place. The ring quivered, then settled.

A historical house wanting to behave, she thought, and reached for the next set.

They moved down the veranda like that, station to station. Each window was a new personality in the same terrible family. Some hooks yielded to their arguments; some required the coaxing of the heat gun, which wouldn't stay lit in the wind without Owen cupping it like a candle and swearing under his breath. Rain slashed under the veranda roof in sheets and somehow also in threads. When the wind shifted, the spray came up from below, salt air and surf like aerosol. The wood under their boots went from damp to slick to treacherous.

On the fourth window, her pry bar slipped. It knocked sparks off the stone like they were inside a struck bell. The bar went skittering toward the balustrade, hit a spindle, and ricocheted back mercifully. She trapped it with a boot and laughed once, out of relief more than humor.

“Don’t,” Owen said, not looking up. “You make that sound and something else jumps.”

“Not a believer in morale, noted.”

“Not a believer in tempting the building,” he said, then winced as the shutter slammed his thumb and sucked the breath in between his teeth.

She glanced at his hand. The nail was flushed, already a little crescent of blood pooled under it. “You good?”

He shook it out. “It’s attached.” He pushed the shutter closed again, jaw set, and they muscled the dog down together.

Between sets, she cut tarps. The rolls were heavy blue plastic that smelled like a pool cover. She zipped lengths with the box cutter against a paint-scarred bench and threw them narrow over the veranda roofline to make impromptu driplines, weighting the ends with whatever they could trust: two buckets of gravel, a coil of chain, a brick that had once been decorative and now was just a brick. She twine-lashed the midpoints to the gutter brackets. The tarps rattled and billowed, but they diverted the worst of the water away from the window casings. Anything that made rain go elsewhere counted.

Inside the west gallery, pale rugs were ghosting up water at their fringes. There were little islands forming on the hardwood where the wind had found cracks and made them into spouts. In one double sash, a steady leak had worked itself into a run, the water tracing a clean path through dust and pooled at the baseboard in a quivering bead that refused to drop. She pushed a towel into the gap, then another, then wedged a cut strip of drapery along the sill. The cloth darkened with a speed that was discouraging.

Owen came in with his shoulders stacked with rain and pointed at the ceiling. “There.”

A brown bloom had appeared over the window, weak tea seeping through plaster where the veranda roof flashed into the wall. The house had been patched there before; the paint made a cracked map of previous trouble.

“How much roof tar do you have?” Mara asked.

“Half a bucket. I’m saving it for the northeast corner. We’ll make do with tarp over tarp.”

“Double it now,” she said, already cutting a second strip. “If we leave it, everything we did with the shutters is a joke.”

Outside again, the wind forced them to speak in each other’s faces. He went up the veranda ladder, hooking his boot under a rung, then belly-crawled the short stretch to the leaky seam. The roof boards were as slick as a fish. “Pass me the strip,” he said.

She fed it up, but the tarp tried to become a flag, snapping her wrists. He grabbed the edge with one hand and almost lost the bucket with the other. For a breath he was held up only by his kneecaps and a curse. She grabbed his work belt and anchored him until she felt his center of gravity come back down.

“Okay,” he said into the roof. “Okay.”

He smoothed the tarp flat as the wind allowed and screwed in fender washers at the edge, the driver whining high as it skidded. Each screw walked across the plastic before biting, leaving crescent scars. When he swung back down, grime striped his mouth where he’d wiped with the back of his glove.

“Add that to ‘don’t do that again,’” he said.

“Get in line,” she said. “There’s a backlog.”

They worked with the kind of efficiency that feels like a fight. When a shutter’s lower hinge was seized entirely, they abandoned persuasion and added a diagonal brace across the outside face, lag screws through slats into the frame. When an upper window had lost half its glazing putty and trembled at their touch, they made an X of packing straps over the glass and tightened the ratchet until the pane sang. A song, yes, but it would hold.

The tools didn’t enjoy the weather. The driver bit at the screws and barked out; the hammer stung their hands with the ricochet of missed nails. Twice, Owen had to fish a dropped bit out of the scupper with his fingers while cursing water that was the temperature of confidence, which is to say, none.

They were halfway along when a sideways hit of wind stole her hat clean off and sent it cartwheeling into the rose tangle on the slope below. She watched it go and then let her shoulders drop. Owen, seeing her expression, said, “You did not love that hat.”

“It was just a thing on my head.”

“Good. Hate to turn back for it.”

She snorted. “You’re not turning back for anything.”

“Not until we’ve got this side quiet,” he said, and that was exactly right for the size of the job.

At the veranda’s corner, the face of the house jogged. The next two windows had louvered shutters that had never liked each other. The left one dragged on the decking; the right one’s hinge barrel had a split pin. Owen bent to it, found the split, and pinched it back with the pliers until the metal squealed. He looked pleased with himself for a second, then slid his thumb down the seam and made a face. “It’s wet inside the hinge.”

“Check the mortise,” she said. “If the wood’s gone soft, we brace now.”

He dug a screwdriver into the pocket. The tool sank further than it should. He raised his eyebrows. “That,” he said. “That’s a sponge.”

“Okay. Dog it and add a brace across the outer faces, high, to take load off the hinge. We can’t replace it in a storm. We can get the pressure off it.”

They did. The brace spanned both shutters like a seat belt. It looked ugly; it worked.

Between the seventh and eighth window, she realized her shoulders were clenched high enough to ache. She rolled them and almost dropped the box of screws. The plastic box slid over the slick paint and headed for a wall gap where the veranda boards didn’t quite meet. She stabbed a boot out and blocked it, then saw the knot of screws that had erupted under the lid, handfuls of silver babies rolling toward the crack.

Owen lunged and clapped a palm over the gap. “Gimme your cap,” he said.

“It’s in a rosebush.”

“Your other cap.”

She unzipped a pocket and produced the lid of the five-gallon bucket. He shoved it under his palm just in time to catch a spill of screws. “We’re not doing that again either,” he said, voice flat.

“You’re building a list,” she said.

“I’m building a church of the obvious.”

Back inside, they hit the sash with the worst leak. A tiny crack radiated from the lower right corner, impossible to see until water showed it to them. The pane purled with each gust, the glass flexing like a muscle. Mara touched the crack with a fingertip and felt the wetness come through. “Tape,” she said.

He handed her a roll of clear tape and the painter’s version, blue. She laid strips across the crack in a ladder and then overlaid them with the blue, then overlaid that with packing straps and a ratchet. The ratchet clicked and clicked until the world seemed to be made of that sound. The pane stopped pulsing. It didn’t look righteous, but it looked less doomed.

“Two sides left,” Owen said, glancing down the hall. His voice was scruffed by fatigue.

“Think in halves,” she said. “One and one.”

When they reached the last of the west windows, the wind came in oblique, lifting the edges of the tarps they’d already set like someone picking at a scab. The veranda boards were a film of water. A dropped washer slid ten feet and rattled against a post. Owen went down on one knee and stayed there a second longer than was about balance. His breath made steam.

“Hey,” she said. She tapped her forearm with two fingers. “Hydrate.”

He drank from the shared bottle without arguing and passed it back. The water was the temperature of the room, which was to say, cold. The salt on his lips made it taste like broth.

They pulled the second-to-last shutter closed and the dog took it like it was meant to. They both exhaled and looked at the final window. It was a narrow pair, more decoration than light, a finicky Victorian idea. The shutters here were purely aesthetic: cutouts in the shape of pointed leaves. Pretty, and presently stupid. The left one wouldn’t meet the right by a full inch. Someone had planed it thin at some point and taken away what it needed.

“What do you have,” Mara said, scoping the options in her head. “We need a spacer.”

“Shims,” Owen said, already rummaging in the box. He came up with three cedar wedges and one composite wedge, the kind that never shrinks. “I’ll hate myself later if I use the good one.”

“Use the good one,” she said without patience. “Later can loathe you in a dry room.”

He slid the composite shim into the space and rapped it in with the butt of the driver. It locked the shutters together snug as a clamped mouth. The dog dropped. For a moment, everything in the west gallery sounded a little less like an argument. Rain still wanted in. Wind still made its case. But the house had fewer tongues to speak with.

They weren’t done. Inside, they went window to window, checking the seams. Mara pressed the heel of her hand along each sill the way you check for heat on a door, feeling for wet. She made little piles of soaked cloth at the baseboards and swapped them with dry, then marked the worst spots on the legal pad she’d jammed into her jacket, because if she didn’t put a name on them they were too easy to forget.

At the narrow windows with the leaf cutouts, a drop formed, gathered itself, and fell. She held the pad under it a second, let the dot bloom and spread a pale halo. “We’re not done” would have been peculiar, because of course they weren’t, but this needed acknowledgment. “Two panes,” she said. “Still vulnerable.”

“Which,” he said, wiping his face with his sleeve. His freckles were brighter when the rest of him went gray. “The cracked sash. And this leaf idiot.”

“Leaf idiot,” she agreed, grateful for the stupid name. “We’ll babysit them.”

“We’ll babysit everything,” he said with a breath that was not a plan so much as a truce with a bad hour.

They did one last pass of the veranda, hands on the braces, the dogs, the tarps, imposing order with touch because checking visually was deceptive in this kind of light. She tugged a strap and felt it pull like a muscle. He kicked the bottom of a shutter and listened for the shiver that meant loose. They heard it twice and corrected it twice. On the third check, nothing rattled.

Inside, the west wing dimmed by degrees as the shutters made their little darknesses. The gallery looked like a place between. The rug runners were fat with absorbed rain; the air smelled like wet rope and plywood. They’d slowed the water at the roof seam; the brown spot on the plaster wasn’t growing as fast. The tape ladders on the cracked pane held. The leaf idiot bled, but slowly.

Owen leaned against the wall and set the heel of his hand on the bridge of his nose. Mara stood with her hands on her hips, shoulders sharp, breathing around the harness of her own damp jacket. He dropped his hand and looked at her.

“West is sealed,” he said, not triumphantly. Just a field report.

“Two panes still want attention,” she said.

“We’ll give it,” he said. He picked up the bucket lid full of captured screws like it weighed what it did, which was not much, and signaled toward the far door with his chin. “South corridor next.”

“South corridor next,” she echoed, and they moved out of the dim into the dim, leaving the west to its small noises and their work to stand without them for a while.


==================== SCENE 27 ====================
They hit the great doors at the front like dockhands, not guests. The vestibule glass was already beaded with salt, a glittering fur on the inside of the panes where the spray had found any imperfection. Outside, the drive ran a thin sheet of water back toward the cliff like a mirror somebody had tilted wrong. The flagstone step had made a creek of itself, too even to be an accident, still creeping.

“Front first,” Owen said. He didn’t wait for assent. He flipped the bolt, threw his shoulder into the door just enough to slit it, and shoved a heavy plastic drop cloth under with his toe like he was feeding a pizza peel. Wind shouldered the gap and blew someone’s lost ticket stub in on a little cyclone. Mara grabbed it and jammed it into her pocket so it wouldn’t make a mess later.

“Let me lay the sill,” she said.

He crouched and snapped open the crate of sandbags like books. These were the canvas ones, patched at the corners, stenciled with a quarry name that had silted off in a dozen storms. He lobbed one to her. “Pyramid, not wall,” he said. “Three-two-one, kiss the seams.”

“I know,” she said, already setting the base course tight to the saddle so the canvas wrinkled into the bevel. “You like them polite.”

“I like them water-right,” he said. He slid the plastic over the threshold, flaring it into the vestibule and up the inside of the doorframe. “Hand me that tape.”

She tore tape off her wrist where she’d cuffed it earlier and boxed the corners, pressing tape into the wood with the heel of her hand. Her fingers smelled like glue and wet coin. The wood was swollen and cool, thick-skinned. “How many for the base?”

“Six,” he said. “Then four, then cap with the pigs.” The pigs were the long noodles of sand, flexible as sleeping snakes. He placed them like caulk in a line along the seam.

She pressed the bag seams flat and pulled the plastic up and over, smoothing air out with her forearm. A rivulet ran down the exterior of the door at the exact joint where the brass mail slot had been painted shut thirty years ago. It sounded like fingers drumming. She could feel the vibration under the flared plastic.

“Water line’s over the apron,” she said.

“By an inch,” he said, looking out through the glass slit at the drive where leaves swam like fish. “We’ve got, what, two hours?”

“One-forty,” she said, checking the watch she’d taped to her sleeve. “If the chart wasn’t lying and the wind doesn’t goose it.”

“Wind always gooses it,” he said, but not as complaint. He hauled the second course up. “Knees, not back.”

“We’re both knees,” she said, planting, lifting, positioning. The canvas gave a little each time like it was breathing. The smell had changed; the brine in the air had gone tinny, like a battery going to coins.

He glanced at the transom. “You want the felt up there or are we trusting your tape job.”

“Felt. It makes me feel like a person,” she said. He grinned with his mouth closed, which was his way when he was working at speed. She climbed the step stool, jammed the strip of adhesive felt along the frame where the transom met the molding, and pressed hard enough to make her fingertips go white. The wood drank the pressure.

He rapped the first course with his knuckles. “Kiss it,” he said again, softer, not to her but to the line. He was superstitious like that about materials. She didn’t mind. She liked the idea that things obeyed if you were polite.

They were on the second course when water, fast and sneaky, shot a thread under the weatherstrip at the far right jamb. It wasn’t a drip so much as a seam going dark, a precise handwriting of damp moving into the vestibule tile. Mara took a hand towel from her shoulder like a bartender and pressed it there. The towel wicked fast, turning from white to gray along a perfect diagonal.

“Hello, creep,” she said.

“Left hinge,” Owen said, abandoning his bag to drop to hands and knees. He ran a finger along the bottom gasket, found the notch with the accuracy of someone who has mapped this exact problem in autumns past. “The hinge plate screws never sit flush. Every year it’s this one.”

“Add it to the list,” she said.

“It’s on the list. It’s always on the list. Grab me that strip of neoprene.”

She passed it and he stuffed it into the seam like a host stuffing a napkin in a door. The sound of the wind through the gap dropped half a note. The creeping water slowed, then stopped. The towel went from wick to mere damp.

“Okay,” he said. “Back to the pyramid.”

Three-two-one. It was rote and it was not, because every bag had a personality, a corner that wanted to curl, a seam that wanted to split. They coaxed them into compliance. The third course went on and the cap pigs snaked into place with ugly elegance, and she taped the plastic tight and then loose, tight where pressure would bite, loose where it needed give.

“You take the kickboards,” he said, already pulling the U-shaped boards they used like braces. He set one on each side, wedging against the doorframe. He had chalk lines tattooed on his palms from the work they’d done on the shutters; the chalk stained the boards when he slid them. “One more tarp over the interior for crawl.”

“Crawl?” she said.

“Capillary crawl. Through the tile, through the grout. It all loves a path,” he said. He slapped the tarp down like he was making a bed.

She fanned a handful of play-sand—the cheap bag with a faded cartoon shovel on it—along the inside seam where floor met bag wall. He looked at her.

“Psychological sand,” she said.

“It’s ballast,” he corrected, but his eyes said fine, yes, superstition has uses. He checked the bar across the door and put his shoulder into it for the feel of it. The feel mattered more than anything. It had a give that was honest. “Front holds,” he said, and knocked wood once with a knuckle.

“Front holds,” she echoed.

A gutter coughed outside, spewing a new creek into the downspout that missed its shoe entirely and made foam in the gravel. The current spoke a language Mara didn’t like. She shrugged into it anyway. “Service next.”

“Service next,” he said, clipping the empty plastic roll to his belt like a weird trophy. He scooped up three more bags each, one on each shoulder, one held like a toddler on a hip, and they moved fast through the hall, skirting the rug islands they’d made earlier. The house shifted in their peripheral vision like an old dog getting comfortable.

The service entry was meaner than the front by miles—a narrow door that had been rebuilt four times and never right, its sill lower than it should be, its slab always wanting to swell. There was a rusted bell pull on the left that had called nobody for thirty years and a boot scraper that looked like a piece of the shipwreck dragged up the cliff.

“Serious exposure,” Owen said, breath tight. “This is the one that lies about being sealed.”

“Don’t personify,” she said. “It’s bad for morale.”

“I’m telling it what it is.” He kicked the bottom plate and listened. The hollow sound said there was air under the threshold where there shouldn’t be. “We need lumber behind the bags.”

“Risers?” she said.

“Risers,” he said. “That scrap of 2x under the stair.”

She dove for it, knock-kneed in her slickers, and shoved it into his hand. He crouched, braced it against the inside of the threshold, and wedged it with shims. The wood squealed and then seated with a satisfied pop. He looked up. “Give me the foil.”

“Not a pantry,” she said, but she gave it to him. He lined the riser with the reflective insulation and pinned it with roofing nails so the plastic edge wouldn’t cut on wood. It was ugly and clever. The first crash of water against the other side of the door made the whole plane shiver, but the riser took it, shared it with the floor.

She had the base course laid before he could say pyramid again. “Three-two-one,” she said. “We’re on the clock.”

The clock answered by sending a slick of brackish water under the left corner, where a sliver of daylight was visible if you put your cheek to the tile and squinted up. She didn’t need to. She could smell it turn—the freshwater tin diluted by the mineral stink of the gulf. It tried to choose a path to the grease trap and got confused on the wrong side of the room, pooled, and then remembered gravity.

“Go,” he said, low and fast. He had the pigs in both hands and laid them like a brace right at the leak. She was up on her hands and knees with the mop like a squatter in her own kitchen, sweeping back toward the drain they’d taped over an hour ago and forgotten to check, and—there—the tape still held.

“Another towel,” she said, subject and imperative at once.

He slipped one into her hand without looking, his attention on the corner where the jamb met the floor. He drove a cedar shim into the seam with the heel of his hand and that tiny act bought them three minutes. She patched with a strip of pond liner, thick and black and unyielding. They beat it into acceptance and taped the edges.

He was counting bags under his breath, not numbers so much as beats. “Seven, eight—no, that one’s light, ditch it—nine.” He flung the failed bag aside. Sand pfft’d a little like a sigh. He took a better one, set it, and pressed. “You trust this.”

“I trust this,” she said.

The rising surge hit again, a push-pull that made the door breathe in the frame. The bell pull trembled a little. The little window at the top of the door, four panes and a crosspiece like a church, had a line of condensation that ran until it found a nick in the putty and just stopped, as if it had remembered manners.

“Hold, hold, hold,” he said, not to the door but to the arrangement, the geometry of it, the logic. She put her palm to the center of the tarp and felt the cold translate through her bones. Somewhere behind them, the generator coughed once and then caught and then settled. It changed nothing about what they were doing and it changed everything about how the house sounded.

“Say it,” she said.

“Service holds,” he said. He tasted it, then said it again with a little more throat. “Service holds.”

She didn’t echo, because the corner where the baseboard met the tile had a line of darker grout. The capillary crawl had started, fine as pencil. She crouched and put her finger there and felt the chill kiss her skin. It wasn’t a failure. It was physics.

“Crawl,” she said.

“Expected,” he said. He reached for the bundle of painter’s rags, stuffed them along the inside like a gasket, then poured a stingy line of hydrophilic gel like icing along the culprit joint. The gel puffed slightly as it drank. The seam went from pencil to blur to nothing you could name.

She sat back on her heels, both of them wet to mid-shin. He was as wet, his hair pasted to his forehead, freckles branded brighter again. They both breathed like they were laughing except they were not.

“Front,” he said.

“Front,” she agreed.

They ran it back like a loop, checking for what had changed just because they’d left. The front pyramids had slumped by a fraction, not surprising, canvas settling. She patted them back into obedience. The towel at the right jamb had been abandoned in its damp state; she switched it for a dry one with the efficiency of a nurse. He touched the transom; the felt still clung. The gore of water outside the door was louder, but the interior song was soft and even. He nodded once.

“Okay,” he said. “We buy ourselves twenty minutes of okay at a time.”

“Fifteen,” she said, glancing at her taped watch. “The creep is an optimist.”

“Fine. Fifteen,” he said. He leaned his head against the doorframe for one second, then pushed off. “You hungry?” It was a practical question, not social.

“Not in a way that’ll help,” she said. She wiped her hands on her thighs and left two witch marks on the slick fabric.

He pointed at the yellow bucket by the service entry. “Give me the mop water. Bleach cap in. We don’t want a bacteria party in this.”

“You always say party when it’s disgusting,” she said, but she did it. She snapped the bleach cap and tipped a measured glug into the clouded water. The smell stiffened the air. She swept again along the base and under the riser and into the drain dip where water always liked to pretend it was going to be a lake.

He lugged the extra bag pallets just inside and laid them like a second wall, a fail-safe. He wasn’t calling it that; he just put it there. She took the legal pad out of her jacket pocket, made a quick map that was three rectangles and a line and a dot, and wrote: FRONT THRESHOLD: HOLDING W/ SEEPAGE RIGHT JAMB. SERVICE: HOLDING W/ CREEP AT LFT CORNER. TOWELS ROTATE 15. She taped the page at eye level where anyone with hands would see it.

He caught the tape with a finger as the wind shook the door and smoothed it down. “We’re the anyone with hands,” he said.

“For now,” she said. “South corridor later. Funicular after that.” She wasn’t getting ahead of herself; she was measuring distance.

He bent and adjusted the pig on the service entry one more millimeter. “You’ve got leaf idiot on your jacket,” he said, flicking a green smear where it had found her in the last room.

“Leaf idiot is part of my look,” she said.

“Mm,” he said, mouth closed, and his eyes flicked to the line on her taped watch and back to the door. “Give me the timer,” he said.

She set her phone for fourteen minutes and put it on the crate where it would vibrate loudly against the wood. He checked both doors again with his hands, as if they were horses you talk to and keep from spooking. She put a palm flat to the interior plastic at the service entry and felt the cold seep across and into her skin again, less a rush now than a presence.

“Safety, such as,” she said.

“Exposure, such as,” he returned. It was not gallows humor; it was a ledger.

“You ready to babysit?” she said.

“We’ll babysit everything,” he said, and this time it was a plan. The timer face glowed. Outside, the water climbed its fractions. Inside, the thresholds held, and the house breathed its damp, its cleaned edges, its slow seep in fine gray lines that could be named, watched, and kept.


==================== SCENE 28 ====================
The funicular house crouched like a hunched shoulder at the cliff’s lip, tar paper scabbed over old shingles, its little window salted blind. The rails dove away from it, two dark lines vanishing into blown spray, the counterweight box muffled in canvas that snapped and smacked with every gust. Rope flags strung along the safety chain were at permanent attention. The whole cliff face throbbed with the sea’s iron throat a story below.

“Your lights are still on,” Mara said, nodding at the pane. The bulb inside had the yellow of a tooth.

“They’re on because Theo keeps forgetting he leaves them on,” Owen said. He had to keep a palm on the door to keep it from opening itself at the wrong moment. “Or because he thinks moths are employees.”

“Or because you say ‘moths are employees’ in meetings and everyone writes it down,” she said. The gust kicked. She leaned into the slant of it and hooked an elbow around the doorjamb, the wood slick with brine.

He put his shoulder to it. The door gave them six inches and snapped back, a dog playing possession. He grunted, braced his boots on the concrete lip dark with algae from high spray, and shoved again. She slipped inside first, grabbing the handrail, and pulled while he heaved. The door banged inward and they stumbled check-to-check into the tiny room.

Inside, the funicular house was barely larger than a closet wrapped around a control cabinet. The room carried the smell of ozone, old belt dressing, and damp cardboard. A coil of frayed manila rope hung like a museum exhibit. The control box wore a red sticker from some electrical company with a phone number with a town code no one used anymore. A pair of marine radios sat on a shelf under a rag. A line of dead flies held the sill like barricade soldiers, little legs fur-bristled with lint.

Mara shook off the gust and closed them in. The wind gave the door a last slap and sulked away. The bulb hummed. Water hissed at the rocks below.

“Okay,” she said, setting her kit on the only flat thing that didn’t look like it would bite—an old surveyor’s case with a cracked corner. “Lockout, tag-out, and you get the honor of killing your darling toy.”

“It’s not my darling toy,” he said, but his tone softened when he put his hand on the cabinet. “She just doesn’t kill strangers if you treat her like an old lady in heels.”

“We are going to take her heels and hide them from her. That’s the point,” Mara said. She pulled the folded checklists from her jacket pocket, weighed them flat with a roll of pink tags. The tags had little wires and holes that had seen a lot of winter. She set her phone face down on the case, the timer screen still running from the last door. The display read nine minutes left on the towel rotate, like a tiny metronome ticking in another room of the house.

Owen pulled his gloves from his back pocket and put them on. He had that set face he wore when machinery needed to be told what to do; the part of him that had learned as a kid not to put fingers under anything that could decide it wanted them for itself. He reached for the cabinet latch. It was a dull chrome with a lock that had been bright once a decade ago. Crystals of white salt perched in the keyway like the crystallized sugar on a cheap doughnut.

He put the key in. The key stopped halfway in and argued about going farther.

Mara dug out the little blue can from her kit and shook it. She covered the lock face with her hand to make a cave and shielded the spray. “Salt has decided this is its invention,” she said, half to the room, and misted the keyway until it drooled brown.

“Say when,” Owen said.

“When,” she said. He turned, careful and steady, then backed off a degree and forward again, coaxing it like it was a stuck ankle. The lock thought about their proposition, held a committee, then gave half a quarter-turn with a dry scream.

“Good,” Mara said. “Again.”

He wiggled the key, pressed in, and it went. The lock turned grudgingly, and the latch gave up like a jaw. He opened the cabinet.

The inside of the box was a grid of breaker handles, white labels curling at the edges, wires marching up in bundles that had been tidy once. Someone had put a strip of tape inside the door with sharpie notes: MAIN, PUMPS, LANTERN, BOATHOUSE PENDANT, AUX, RADIO CHARGERS. Someone else had crossed out and overwritten, so each note looked like it had a ghost behind it. A laminated card velcroed there read: LOCK OUT/TAG OUT—SEQUENCE. Owen ran a finger down the steps. His nail was chewed short and black with the day.

“It should be MAIN, then AUX, then Pendant,” he said. “Then physical chocks at the car. Then lock the hasp. Verify zero.”

“Let’s do the romance version where we actually read the voltage,” Mara said. She took the non-contact meter out of her bag and clipped it to her jacket with the tether. She looked at the throat of the house. The room had galvanized conduit that toured the ceiling and ducked in a corner down to an ancient junction box where salt had dined more than once. She clicked the meter on. It peeped. “Talk to me.”

“Main first,” Owen said, and put his thumb on the big handle. He took a breath that had nothing to do with fear and everything to do with habit. He flipped it down. The hum in the bulb died. The bulb kept glowing, dimmer now, coasting on the last dribble of warm filament. The sea got louder in the vacuum where the electricity had been.

Mara held the meter near the cabinet bus. It stayed quiet. She touched the pendant cable where it came up from the floor—fat, black, salted at the strain relief. The meter gave a little yip and then shut itself up. “Stray,” she said. “Not load. Keep going.”

“AUX,” Owen said, and threw it, then “Pendant” and “Boathouse.” Each click down had the satisfaction of snapping shut a series of mouths. The bulb went dead for good. The meter stayed quiet everywhere it should, yipped at the line coming in. Outside, a gust rattled the rails; their tiny building creaked its ribs.

Mara put the meter against the pendant plug again and waited. Then she pushed the plug out of its socket at the control box and dropped the cord down, coiling it and securing it with a zip tie around the coil and a nail on the wall. She wrote on a tag: PENDANT DISCONNECTED; MAIN+ AUX+ BOATHOUSE OFF; VERIFIED ZERO; M.K. / O.B.; TIME:—and checked her watch–“Six fourteen,” she said, and wrote 1814. She signed and clipped the tag to the coil, then threaded its wire through the cabinet handle and the hole in the lockout hasp.

“Get the blank?” Owen said.

She had the red lockout hasp with its many holes. He flipped it open, slipped it over the cabinet latch so the door couldn’t close, then shut the hasp and clicked his own padlock through. The padlock looked absurdly clean against all that salt. He wiped the face with his glove and tapped it like a benediction. “My lock,” he said. “No one touches.”

She opened her little brass padlock and added it through the second hole on the hasp. She pulled once to make sure it took. “Mine,” she said. She hung the pink tag under hers. Just then the wind cracked against the door, and the whole cabinet sang a little chord.

“Physical,” she said. “Let’s block the car.”

“And the gate,” he said.

The funicular car itself was down at the landing—Mara could see it only as a suggestion beyond the shuddering little pane, a ghost box in blown spray. Up here, the track emerged through a mouth cut in the planks and crossed the little floor to go out the other side; right at the threshold was the fail-safe brake lever: a steel arm that would jam into the ratchets along the rail if something let go. Owen bent, wiped the rack with a rag, and pulled the lever up. It went with a clunk and a jar. He jammed a pin through the lever hole so it couldn’t drop. He tugged the pin. It stayed.

He took two rubber chocks from under the shelf and wedged them into the notches of the rack where the car would catch if something moved. He hammered each with his fist out of habit until they sat home.

Mara took a chain and a brass tag from her kit. The tag read: DO NOT OPERATE. She ran the chain across the interior threshold at hip height and padlocked it with a third lock. She wrote the time on the tag and hung it dead center.

“Redundancy is kind,” she said.

Outside, the gust did one of those long, steady pushes that meant the storm had switched from arguing to making speeches. The door banged against its latch. Spray blew into the little gap around the jamb and salted the floor in a careless constellation. A drop found Mara’s neck and ran cold under her collar.

Owen looked out at the water because he always did, as if he owed it attendance. The tide had climbed another fraction. The notch in the rock where the boathouse used to be hungry was full. The surface was not a surface; it was muscle.

“This is the part where you say you trust your paperwork,” he said.

“I trust the physical,” Mara said. She stepped toward the cabinet and put her ear against it. The metal was cooling. No hum. She tapped it with her knuckle: a dead sound. She set the meter against the main feed again. No scream. She counted to ten because counting was part of no one’s magic, but it settled her anyway. “You’ll trust it when somebody bangs the pendant and nothing happens,” she said.

He nodded. He dragged the emergency crank off its hook and set it on the floor under the shelf. “If anyone cries to go down the cliff during a king tide, they can cry with their hands empty,” he said.

Mara reached above the door and found the switch for the light. She flipped it even though she knew. Nothing. She wrote on her pad: FUNICULAR: MAIN OFF; AUX OFF; PENDANT DISCONNECT; MECH BRAKE PINNED; RACK CHOCKS; THRESHOLD CHAIN; TAGS M.K., O.B.; 1814. She taped the note to the inside of the door. She added: NO EXCEPTIONS, in smaller letters, and it was not drama; it was a rule she could respect because she made it.

“South corridor after this?” he asked, voice low in the little room, as if the funicular had ears that would sulk if it knew it was only one item on their list.

“South corridor after this,” she said. She pulled the door open. The gust tore it from her hand and slammed it against the stop. The hinge cried out. She grabbed the handle and hauled it back. The wind tried to push them back inside and hold them there.

“Wait,” Owen said. He stepped out into it, braced the door with his back, and pointed with his chin to the gate. There were two fences: one along the cliff, one across the little gravel apron that led to the funicular house. The chain across the apron sagged in a tired loop, last winter’s wind having stretched it. He went to it, tightened the clevis with a wrench from his belt, then took two padlocks from his pocket—one painted green, one red—and ran them through the links so the chain hung taut, the padlocks a stern pair of eyes.

“You color-coded your petty,” she said over the wind.

“I color-coded so my muscles remember what the brain forgets at two in the morning,” he said. “Green for gate, red for kill.” He held up the map of his hands. “There. Now if a bored intern thinks zigzag is a direction, they’ll have to go find a bolt cutter and the audacity.”

She thumbed the pink tag on the chain. Its ribbon rattled and snapped. She smoothed it down and wrote: PERIMETER GATE SECURED—FUNICULAR DISABLED. The pen scratched in the wet and her letters grew fat with humidity.

Another gust came, this one with a little particulate anger in it. It found the corroded hasp on the outside equipment locker and banged it like a drum. She glanced at the padlock there. Its shackle was scabbed with orange. If someone needed the spill kit inside, they’d lose minutes to rust.
