She went to it. She dug the blue can out again, put the straw in, and fed the lock. Owen set his shoulder against the door to keep it from taking her with it. She rolled the lock in her palm to warm it, then turned the key. It stuck, then went all at once, almost skinning her knuckles on the hasp. She opened the locker, checked the contents—absorbent socks in a sealed bag, nitrile gloves like ocean-colored balloons, a little scraper, a roll of white tape, a flashlight that died two seconds after she clicked it on.

She pulled the batteries, shook them out into her hand and back into their box, replaced them from her pocket pack. She clicked the torch again. It went dew-bright. She set it face out on the shelf, so that when—if—someone grabbed this locker in a hurry they’d be met with sight instead of guessing. She left the lock open on the hasp and ran a zip tie through the eye so anyone could yank the locker and not be thwarted by a key in weather. She wrote on the little inner card: LOCK REMOVED FOR ACCESS; MK / OB; 1819.

“You can’t leave it loose,” Owen said, out of habit.

“It’s tagged,” she said. “And you just said ‘if bored intern.’ I say ‘if fuel.’” She held up her hands like a scale. “Safety. Exposure. Call it.”

He nodded once, a pragmatic bow to a better idea. He reached up and tugged the zip tie to feel the give. “Breakaway. Good.”

“South corridor,” she said again, like a train announcement. The tide threw white shoulders against the cliff below and tried to climb up their boots with spray.

They stepped back into the funicular house. Mara took a photo of the cabinet with both their locks in frame, the tags clear, the hasp through. She took another of the chain across the threshold and the pink tag. She took a third of the gate chain with its twin padlocks. She checked them once with her hands, the way she had at the doors—like you touch horses before you leave them. Owen touched the brake lever and the pin out of habit. They did not talk to the funicular like a person, but they afforded it the respect you give a thing that can do harm if you let it think.

“Mostly secured,” he said, as if testing the words for splinters.

“Compound mostly secured,” she said. “Language is part of the engineering.”

He looked at her watch. The line she’d marked on the face for the king tide’s guess sat in the near future like a train on a schedule board approaching. He jerked his chin the way he did when there was no point in being sentimental.

They went out and hauled the door shut behind them. The gust tried one last time to make them look ridiculous and lost. It failed. Owen turned the corroded deadbolt. It stuck halfway, argued, then committed. He put his forehead against the cold, damp door and exhaled. Salt water smell, wood that had drunk too much, the distant oil of machines that waited and would wait a little longer now.

“Say it,” she said.

He took a breath that felt like a crane setting down a beam. “Funicular’s dead,” he said. “On purpose.”

“Good,” she said. She angled them toward the path back to the house, gravel crunchy with damp. The wind shoved. She leaned. They moved like people who had weights in their pockets and reasons in their mouths.

Halfway up to the service side, he touched her elbow. “Hold.”

She stopped. He crouched at the drainage gully that crossed the path, its galvanized grate rattling. He put his fingers under the edge and pulled it up. It resisted and then came. Below, the channel had a worship of leaves and grit doing their best to make it a tiny dam. He scraped with his hand, cleared a clot of brackish, threw it aside. Water tugged at his knuckles like a cat. The channel gulped and then ran.

She crouched with him and added handfuls of sodden needles and a matted chunk of rag that might have been someone’s shirt a season ago. They set the grate back, and he stomped on it once to settle it.

“Now,” he said.

They went on. At the south corridor door, her taped page was still there, their towels doing their bored work, the nice reliable creep at the left corner exactly as she’d named it. She pressed her palm to the plastic sheet. Cold seep. She smiled without showing teeth. He checked the pig in the service entry. He nudged it with the toe and then the hand and let it be.

“Mostly secured,” she said again, this time not to test the words but to pin them where they could do some use.

“Mostly,” he said. He looked at the long side of the house where the shutters were a row of serious eyes, all turned and latched. He looked at the roofline. He looked at her. “Babysit everything?”

“Babysit everything,” she said. She wrote FUNICULAR DISABLED on the big page inside the kitchen where all their pieces lived, and underlined it once. The house breathed its damp, cleaned edges, and slow seeping in fine gray lines. Outside, the water climbed its fractions. Inside, the thresholds held. In the funicular house, the cabinet sat with its jaw open and its two locks on, tagged like a promise. Hard-won meant counted, and wary meant counted again. The compound had edges now. They had given it those. They would keep them.


==================== SCENE 29 ====================
The office had been a copy room before it became an IT nook, you could tell by the scuffed rectangle where a printer the size of a washer used to sit and the smell of burned toner that never left. Now it was a cave of humming boxes and wires taped down in concessions, a folding table pretending to be a rack, a fan clattering against a zip‑tie tail that someone had trimmed poorly. The window was a square of storm: seawater sprayed it and slid down like gray film. The building shivered every time the wind hit right, as if the studs were exhaling.

Cass had a towel under the power strip in a gesture to fate. The UPS made a nervous little sound every minute, a metronome for the way he kept checking the battery icon. On his screen: waveform. Blue river, yellow peaks, the little ants of sound. On a second, smaller screen: a chat window rolling and freezing and rolling again as the bandwidth tried to breathe through rain.

He had the headphones on. The childish hum came in like a stowaway—thin, purposeful, then expanding when he lifted it with gain. Not a song really, just notes shape‑remembered, a rise, a fall, the odd interval that made adult ears go cold. Under it, the prop wash the mic had taken, a flittering metallic shimmer. He put a notch on the prop. He rolled a low cut. He fed the voice through a slapback barely there, the kind of reverb that lives in a stairwell. He opened the spectrogram and watched the ghost‑fruit smear across in pinks and oranges.

“Again,” he said, not to anyone. He looped the section with the breath in it, a sigh between two notes that had to be accidental. It sounded so human he wanted to stand up and look behind him. He didn’t. He slipped the high end down one decibel and it turned from knife to whisper.

The radio on the desk crackled. “Cass.”

He nudged the fader down and pulled the cups back to his neck. “Here.”

“Status?” Wind in Mara’s voice, a scrape of something against her mic like a palm on a door.

“Two minutes away from upload.” He clicked “save as,” gave it a name he wouldn’t regret, then deleted that name and typed another, less dramatic. He stripped the EXIF and killed the GPS in the companion clip. He set it to export. The progress bar ooze‑crawled.

“No,” she said.

“That’s not a status.”

“Don’t do it,” Mara said. Nothing in her tone but sand and decision. “We’ve got the funicular locked, the shutters pinned. Don’t throw meat over the fence.”

Cass glanced at the other window where a weather radar tried to assemble itself, tiles popping in out of order. Peak band over them. Everything white‑green. Branches slapped something outside and he flinched, looking up like the ceiling might step down.

“It’s not meat,” he said. He picked up the mic, set it down again. “It’s the truth.”

“Don’t,” she said, firm and flat. “Not tonight.”

He went back to the waveform, zoomed in further than necessary. “You want quiet compliance or you want a light? Because people—”

“People will come,” Mara said. “That’s the point of people. They come when they shouldn’t.”

“That’s not on me,” he said, and heard exactly how defensive it was. He softened his mouth. “It’s leverage.”

“Over who?” Another static smear. “You post that, you draw the exact ones who’ll think a storm is an invitation. We can’t babysit the fence and bail the house and keep idiots from climbing things while the bay tries to kiss the parking lot.”

He felt the old itch between his shoulders, the one that started every time Julian smiled with his attorney’s eyes and said, We’ll handle it. He spun the knob on the headphones and let the kid‑hum fill the room again. It was wrong to call it kid‑anything. He didn’t know whose throat it had come out of or whose plan it had served, capture mic strapped to cheap carbon fiber, circling above the wet grass at midnight. He knew what it did to him.

“We don’t need them physically,” he said. “We need them watching.”

“The second is the first with lag,” Mara said. “You know that.”

He did. He also knew the admin panel of his channel and the way one video on the right night could rewire a room, make a sheriff remember a policy, make a board member’s altar to optics tip hard. He moved the marker, shaved the front, tightened the tail. He set the title: The Sound Over Harbor Light. He deleted “Harbor Light,” typed “the Point.” He deleted “the Point,” put it back. In the description he stripped the adjectives until there were none. A ten‑second clip captured during storm prep. No persons pictured. Audio analyzed, unaltered except for dB matching. That last part was a lie and he didn’t like lies, so he backspaced it to: leveled.

“Say you get your eyes,” Mara said. “What if that’s all you get? What if this floats five screens deep in a stranger’s phone while they dump their truck at our boundary and film it?”

“Then we’ll film them back,” Cass said. He wanted to keep his voice even. He failed. “Julian wants to sit on everything. He wants Vivian to be a whisper, he wants the dock incident to be a rumor, he wants the child’s hum to be a parlor trick. I’m done letting him drive.”

“You are not the wheel,” she said, and he heard the fatigue under the words like a dull blade. “You want leverage? Make a file, make a list, make a chain of custody I can hand to Rourke. Don’t make a bonfire.”

“You called me to say that or to ask me where the extra batteries are?”

“You know where the batteries are,” she said. She sighed. The radio hissed in sympathy. “Cass. Please.”

He looked up at the crooked poster someone had blu‑tacked to the drywall years ago, some conservation. Shorebird against a blue. Protect the fragile. Funny. The UPS chirped again, a nervous canary. He rubbed at his eyelid with the heel of his hand until lights sparked in the black.

“If we go quiet, we get steamrolled,” he said. “If we make noise, we get options.”

“If you make that noise, the kind of option you get is plastic police line and exhausted nurses,” Mara said.

He stared at the export progress stalling at ninety‑one percent. He tapped the counter with his nail. Once. Twice. He switched windows. The hotspot indicator blinked: solid bar, then half, then full. He had a backup SIM. He had a VPN tunneled to a city where it wasn’t raining sideways.

“You can’t control it,” she said, softer now, like a fall he could sit down for. “Don’t trick yourself.”

“That’s the point,” he said, and realized it was true. He didn’t want control tonight. He wanted a match.

“Cass.”

He slid the fader down on the radio. He didn’t turn it off. He stared at his own reflection in the monitor, a vague ghost in front of the ghost‑fruit smear. He looked like a man trying to look like a man with a plan. He clicked “export” again as if the progress bar might respect persistence.

It ticked forward. 100%. He dragged the file into the uploader, watched the little wheel do its thing. He filled in the alt text: Audio from aerial recorder; low‑freq buffeting suppressed; human voice audible. He toggled comments on. He toggled them off. He toggled them on again because off looked like fear.

He clicked “publish.”

The page stuttered and blinked. For a second he saw a white square and thought the connection had died, and then it populated, the thumbnail he’d set popping into place: night grass, a corner of the clapboard, a block of sky. The counter sat at 1. Then 3. Then 12.

The radio breathed in his ear cap against his neck. “Did you do it?” she asked, and she didn’t even bother to keep the knowing out of it.

“Yeah,” he said. The word felt both too big and too small.

“Of course you did,” Mara said, and then nothing else, as if they didn’t have anything better to say to each other for a minute.

He opened the back end and watched the graph climb like a small hill going steeper. Comments began to generate in a staccato that his phone, pocketed, echoed with little thuds. He pulled it out. Notifications stacked. The ones at the top were predictable: fakes, you made this, the house is haunted, I hear the ferry horn, I hear my ex‑wife. A DM from a local account with a skull profile picture: on my way, bro. Another with an area code he recognized: is that the south side? A third: I can get over the fence easy.

He should have felt satisfaction. He felt it and something else that licked the back of his neck. He typed replies—No trespassing zones are marked. Do not attempt to enter the property. Severe weather conditions ongoing. He pinned a warning to the top. The warning made him feel responsible, which he didn’t like any better.

“Views?” Mara asked.

“Two hundred.” He refreshed. “Seven hundred. Hold.” He refreshed. “One thousand three.”

“You do not sound happy,” she said.

“I didn’t come here to be happy,” he said.

Outside, a gust hit so hard the window flexed. The UPS gave a sustained beep like a held breath, then went back to little chirps. Somewhere down the hallway, a door slammed and bounced and slammed again until some invisible hand caught it. Cass stood up, then sat back down without deciding to.

The clip had been up for ninety seconds. He watched the analytics try to draw a map of watchers and fail. He watched the top traffic source label itself as Direct/Unknown, which was never true; it meant the link was already spreading faster than the report could keep up. Someone with a beat‑up truck would have just texted someone with a better truck. Someone with a cheap drone would launch it in their driveway to see if they could grab their own copy of the sound from the south fence.

The radio: “We got headlights at the county pull‑off,” Mara said, and he heard the bitterness she wasn’t going to swallow. “Two. Make that three.”

Cass pushed the talk button. “I pinned a warning,” he said, like that contributed oxygen. “I’m taking it off my name and putting it on the Point’s archive account. Less—”

“It has your cadence,” Mara said. “And you did it, so it’s yours.”

He closed his eyes and saw the spectrogram still, the breath like a petal. He had taken the teeth off the prop noise tenderly. He had curved the thing with the same hands he used on his coffee mug, on the door latch, on the zipper of his jacket. He had made it more itself.

The chat tile on the second screen brightened as someone wrote him. A reporter he refused to talk to when she called, now switching to text: Can you confirm this was captured tonight? Are you onsite? Does the house acknowledge prior incidents?

He typed: Captured recently. Weather integrity compromised. Property is closed. He didn’t press send. He erased the third sentence and wrote: No further comment. He pressed send.

The number climbed: 2.2k. 3.1k. The clip was ten seconds long; the average watch length was eleven because people were replaying. He shouldn’t have checked that. Checking it made him feel like he’d put quarters into a machine and was watching the lights, and he hated that.

The doorframe to the IT nook was metal and made of three bends and a wish. Someone walked past in the corridor, their steps fast and careful, and then doubled back and put their head in. Iris, hair in a scrunchie and dripping a little, purple nail polish chipped. “Did you do a thing?” she asked, eyes already flinty with the answer.

“Always,” he said, and kept his face blank because what did he owe her.

She gestured vaguely in the direction of outside. “Rourke’s going to—” She caught herself the way a person learning to not say everything out loud does, and swallowed the rest. “Do you want help routing audio to the lobby for the night guard? He said his scanner’s dead.”

“On the shelf, upper right,” Cass said, and watched her go like she was smoke.

The radio again, softer, closer; she’d turned her gain up. “Cass,” Mara said. “You drew them. Can you live with that?”

“You can’t keep asking me to live with being quiet,” he said.

“I never did,” she said. “I asked you not to trade our safety for your following.”

There was no decent way to answer that. He looked down at his hands. They were doing that thing where they weren’t sure whether to make fists.

He clicked into the mod panel and typed a banner in big block letters across the top of the comments: Do not attempt to travel in this weather. This clip is documentation, not invitation. He set it to red. Little hearts appeared at its corner anyway.

“Four cars,” Mara said, to herself more than to him. “Idiots.”

He swallowed. “I’m going to mirror the file to three places in case one goes dark,” he said, because that was something he could do that felt like a responsible act and not a dare.

“Do what you want,” she said, a fine exhaustion under it. “But don’t call it help.”

Static filled the space between her last syllable and everything else.

He found the cursor, fat and blinking, over the “share” button. He clicked “copy link.” He pasted it into the archive site’s entry with boring, blunt labels. He added a hash for tracking. He pulled a second hotspot out of the drawer, turned it on, watched the lights arrange themselves into competence. He rubbed condensation off the window with the heel of his hand and made a greasy circle he could see through: the parking lot a smear, the fence line a suggestion in black on black, the silver flick of a license plate when a car took the curve too fast and corrected with a little fishtail.

The numbers on the video went up again, a little tide in a bright font. The UPS chirped—steady, insistent. Somewhere a breaker thunked when the building shifted. He breathed in the dregs of the coffee in the mug he’d stopped tasting an hour ago.

He put the headphones back on and listened to the ten seconds he had decided the world needed to hear. The hum, the breath, the smallness masquerading as a wide‑open warning and the other way around. He closed his eyes to it completely, not to be dramatic but because for those ten seconds he wanted to be nowhere but inside the sound he had let out. The clip ended and the default silence of his software felt too loud.

He lifted the cups to his neck again. The room came back. The radio hissed.

“Views?” Mara asked.

“Five thousand,” he said. He hit refresh. “Eight.”

He rolled his chair back and hit the “lock” on the tower because that was the kind of control he could still exercise. The wind hit the window again like a palm. The graph climbed. The comments argued, adored, threatened, speculated, solved, warned, begged, bragged, invited themselves. He watched all of it and none of it, and when the screen saver tried to come on out of habit, he twitched the mouse to keep the thing awake, as if that would keep all of this from sleeping, too.


==================== SCENE 30 ====================
Wind made a drum of the north lot, the chain-link fence buzzing like an instrument someone had tuned too tight. Cass jogged the last stretch along the gravel, the headlamp on his forehead juddering sick light across wet weeds and the prongs of rebar where an old sign used to be. The rain came at angles, chopped by the gusts; it stitched cold through his jacket and found the skin at his wrists. The radio on his hip sizzled, then cleared.

“Where are you?” Mara asked. Her voice was small from the battery but crisp with something that wasn’t weather.

“North lot,” he said, breathing into his sleeve to block the wind. “We’ve got people.”

“People how many?”

“Enough.”

The fence threw shadows, then bodies made of those shadows. They came together and apart, sliding along the fence line in slicks of dark. Headlights from cars cocked at the ditch cut long blinds across them. Someone wore a poncho the green of emergency exit paint; someone else had a scarf over their mouth like a train robber. Phone screens bobbed bright as jellyfish. A jackknifed pickup idled with wipers slapping—three teens in the bed rat-tat-tatting the fence with the broken halves of a pallet.

Cass thumbed his megaphone on and it didn’t work—waterlogged or dead. He did it the old way, cupped his hands and let his voice go.

“Private property,” he yelled. “Back off the fence.”

“Where is he?” a woman shouted back, cupping her own hands, her hair pasted to her cheeks. “You play a child’s voice like bait and think nobody comes?”

A chorus lifted, ragged but glad to have found anything to say in unison. “Let him out! Let him out!” The words rode the gusts, broke on the wire and fell into the weeds.

“Jesus,” Cass said, to himself or the fence.

A boy with a hoodie tugged down low along his eyebrows stepped forward and slapped the chain link with both palms. It shuddered, a snare hit. The clap echoed off the aluminum siding of the equipment shed and came back like an answer. Someone else picked up the rhythm with a length of PVC, bang, bang, bang. Another voice, nasal and practiced, talked directly into a phone camera.

“If you’re just joining,” it said with soothing cadence, “we’re at the property line of the so-called sanctuary. We have proof of audio—what they’re calling the Drone Child, remember? They’re refusing to let us verify his welfare. Hit the share, hit the share.”

“Go to the south gate!” a different voice yelled, from a car window. “They got a blind spot there.”

“Not tonight,” a stocky guy in a camo jacket said, gripping the fence, reading the posts with the practical eye of someone who had built other fences. “The tide’ll take you.”

Cass moved along the line until he was opposite the camo jacket. “Hey,” he said, so close the guy could see the rain on his eyelashes. “You want to do this the safe way or you want to do it the way that gets you arrested and electrocuted by a drunk transformer? Back up.”

“Show us the kid,” the guy said, not unkind, just as if he were reminding Cass of a routine. “Or open the gate and let a licensed advocate in. Your choice.”

“There is no kid,” Cass said. “You’re here because you fell for a stunt. Get off my fence.”

“On your fence!” somebody laughed, high and delighted. Sticks rattled against wire. “He said my fence like we’re not all paying taxes.”

Another chant tried to start, different cadence: “Aud-it, aud-it.” It got strangled by the wind and turned into laughter, then the original chant swallowed it, heavy and stupid as a drumline. Let him out. Let him out.

Phone-light collected on Cass’s face and made him a shape to aim at. Comments bubbled up at the bottom of screens—he could see them from the signatures and flicker. He could see his own upload in a thumb on more than one display, paused at the waveform spike. His ears did that thing where they isolated: the wind, the fence, Mara’s radio hiss, his own breath. He opened the radio up with his shoulder.

“Do you have eyes?” he asked.

“Briefly. I’m coming up lot side,” Mara said. “Don’t engage. That’s—” The radio clipped on the gust. “—your last warning, Cass.”

“Copy,” he lied.

A woman in a yellow slicker shoved forward and pressed her phone camera into the diamonds of the chain link as if she could push it through. “Look at this,” she said, narrating to her followers. “Barbed top. Spikes. This is not rescue, this is containment.”

“You’re going to cut that fine jacket,” a voice warned from behind her, amused.

“Back off the fence,” Cass said again, and he changed his tone, laid on lawyer words like sandbags. “You’re trespassing. If you damage this, that’s a felony. If you incite others to damage it while you’re streaming, that’s distribution of—”

He didn’t get to finish. The camo jacket man leaned his weight in, and two teens to either side took his cue like fish taking a signal from a school. The fence bowed. The posts hissed in their sockets. The top rail popped a zip tie loose with a white spit of plastic. A voice whooped like someone had won a stuffed animal. Cass felt the give in the wire in his hands, the way a seam feels when you’ve bent it once too many times.

“Stop,” he said, and said it again two inches from the man’s nose.

“Then open,” the man said. Rain beaded on his beard and made commas. “Five minutes in and out. Camera on. No one has to break anything.”

A car horn went off and kept going—a nervous alarm, the kind that only stops when you find the panic button again. In the bed of the pickup, one of the kids developed a rhythm with a ring spanner: three light taps, a pause, one heavy bash. The fence lived. It sang with the push and the weather and all their bodies.

Mara slid into Cass’s peripheral with a hood up and her eyes lined in rain. “Back,” she said, no fanfare, already moving past him to stand where a gap in bodies had opened. “You don’t have consent, you don’t have cause, you don’t have a single binding document that entitles you to put your hands on this fence. Back.”

“You scared of us seeing what you’ve got in there?” the slicker woman asked, angling her phone to catch Mara’s face. “Say your name for the record.”

“No,” Mara said.

“Doxxed already,” someone said from the dark. It wasn’t true—Cass could tell from the stupid brightness of the claim—but the word itself had weight. The group surged like water will surge when it finds the shallow. The chain link took it; one of the bottom ties popped with a metallic squeal.

“Rourke is on his way,” Cass said, bluffing a cop he didn’t have. “This is your one chance to not learn what a night in county feels like.”

“County is where the truth comes out,” the nasal voice said, turning his phone to score an angle that included the sign with the property’s name, the fence, and the suggestion of big house lights somewhere beyond. “We’re safe. We’re streaming. You can’t just—ow.”

He’d got too close to one of the posts and a rebound had scraped the skin off his knuckles through the chain link. He sucked the spot theatrically and held it to camera. Comments flew: blood drop emojis, a chorus of we told you so’s, people asking for the address again.

The chant stretched, thinned; the wind took it and made it strange. Let him out became let—out let—out let—out. The fence made its own music under it, a deep chord with wire harmonics. Cass had a stupid thought that if Vivian could hear this she'd be furious at the noise and then pushed that away hard before it could turn into anything.

Mara’s shoulder hit his. “Back,” she said to him now, low. “Get off the line.”

“I started this,” he said, an admission without aesthetics.

“And you’re going to finish it by getting me a clear shot of their plates,” she said.

He saw then what she’d been doing when the voices swelled: her phone held low, under the fence seam, catching rain-painted reflections on bumpers and wash-out letters on tailgates and the badge of a truck that would matter later to somebody else, not to them now. She kicked a rock so it would click under a car and make the driver look down. When he did, his face was clean in the light for a second—baby cheeks, a freckle constellation, the private concentration of someone trying to remember which pocket held the fob.

“Move,” she told Cass, and he moved. He took three steps back and the fence leapt forward into the space he’d made, buoyant with hands. The top rail bowed; one of the pipe couplers sheared its last rust bit and let go with a bang. The panel leaned, angled like a crooked tooth. A kid shrieked like the sound itself had caught her.

“Whoa whoa whoa,” camo jacket said, getting scared of the physics again, which was an honest fear. Too late to be useful. The whole section came in a foot and the bottom clip ripped out of its concrete shoe. The panel didn’t fall; it bent and locked, perfect for what they wanted.

A dozen phones lifted at once. The noise went up another resolution. The nasal man pivoted into the breach he’d been describing on his stream in his head the entire drive.

“We have an opening,” he said, his voice rich with discovery he hadn’t earned. “We have an opening.”

“None of you set a toe,” Mara said, and there was something old and authority-shaped in it that made three people actually do what she said.

But not all of them. A young guy with wet bangs and a sweatshirt with a grief counselor’s logo ducked into the angle and got stuck by his hoodie. He panicked and wrenched and tore it free with the noise of teased tape. He stood inside the property line for two seconds, staring around at the dark trees as if they would answer the chant, and then he took his own phone out and made his own feed of being inside. His comments went berserk, hearts breathing across his screen like gills.

“Out,” Mara said to him, as if he were one of her divers who’d gone too deep. He looked at her, at Cass, at every lens, and chose a pose instead of a choice.

“We’re in,” he announced, and his voice broke on the second word.

The chant didn’t need them anymore. It was a machine they had built that ran without hands. The banging recommenced in bursts, less rhythmic now, more like applause that had forgotten why it started.

“Back up,” Cass said to the group at the panel, but he said it without hope or anger. He said it because it was the last right word left to say and leaving it unsaid would be a lie he’d have to carry around.

Behind the fence, the grounds ran away toward the slope and the black spam of the trees. The house lights were distant, made soft by rain. Between here and there, a service road glistened, its puddles mirrors that shattered with each new drop. He thought of the ten seconds he’d uploaded, the smallness masquerading as wide-open warning, and how the world had made a thunderclap out of them. He thought nothing after that.

The panel scraped along the gravel with a terrible lip-smacking noise as someone tried to lever it wider with their shoulder. Alyssum and bluet ripped into stems and stuck to wet knees. A car alarm went off again. Sirens did not appear. A drone, somebody’s pet store one, lifted unsteadily above the lot and bobbed in the buffeting, its red eye recording.

“Smile,” the nasal man said to Cass, stepping into the light of another guy’s phone. “Legacy media meets citizen press.”

Cass smiled with all his teeth and none of his kindness. “Tag me,” he said, and held up his own phone with his handle lit up like a flare.

The man’s grin slid. “You wish.”

“Yeah,” Cass said, and he couldn’t make himself stop. “I do.”

Wind punched through, harder than any so far, and sent spray sideways. The wick of rain in all their sleeves went cold at once. The chant stumbled. The camo jacket man took his hands off the wire because they hurt. The kid inside tripped on a rut and almost went down. Mara’s phone vibrated and she cut it off with a knuckle, eyes never leaving the breach.

“It’s already everywhere,” someone said with satisfaction, showing a screen to their neighbor, who made a noise like dessert. Cass could see the metrics without seeing them, the tide and the rise and the comments eating each other, the shares hopping counties, the snips going up on purple backgrounds with white captions calling them monsters.

He heard his radio breathe. Rourke’s name did not appear. No cavalry assembled. The fence held on a crease and would not unbend.

“Back,” Mara said one more time, and if the weather or the weight of bodies heard her or if anyone cared, it didn’t show. The panel canted like a hinged jaw. Their faces shone, rain and screen light and glee. Somewhere in the lot a horn hit a lower note and stuck there, like the beginning of a siren that never grew into one.

Cass let his headlamp fall around his neck. He put his phone up anyway, caught the angle of the bent panel, the wet hands, the phones raised towards themselves. He didn’t frame; he only pressed. And the footage went out across the dark, and the dark took it, and it spread.


==================== SCENE 31 ====================
Cass shouldered into the IT nook like the room might try to push him back out. The house’s bowels were humming: the UPS box ranting in its beeping Morse about battery life, the server fans making their small, panicked whir. His headlamp hit the dust filaments in the air and the scabs of tape on cable bundles that had been labeled and relabeled until the ghost of every organizational scheme remained.

Water made squelch noises in his shoes. He left comet tails wherever he stepped. The damp had crept into the fiber patch, gathered in tiny meniscus smiles where the plastic ports jutted out. Somewhere in the rack a tiny green light blinked a metronome at odds with the storm-beat rattling the window above the ductless mini-split.

“Okay,” he said aloud, like the machines needed warning. “Okay. I hear you.”

He dropped into the chair. It protested in a slow, obscene creak. The iMac woke like a lake surface when a fish breaks it—one ripple and then light everywhere—and the dashboards he’d left up were now berserk, numbers stacking like bad headlines. His post—the ten seconds and the cheap red-eye drone inside the cheaper ones—had metastasized. He could watch the external pings hit like sleet: 10.8k concurrent; 12.3k; something that should have taken a day, then an hour, now five minutes.

His notifications ached. On the far screen, where he’d left a secondary for comments and DMs, the column scrolled without him, hands-off, a torrent pulling its own levers. He pawed for a towel, found none, wiped his hands on his jeans, and clicked, the little wet squeak of skin on mouse.

Having a moment. You made a CHILD CRY.

“Cool,” he said flatly to the air.

He switched tabs. The social admin panel had his avatar like a target. The queue flooded; his moderation flag count was in four digits. He selected “Hold potentially violating comments for review,” and the toggle gave him the polite digital click of something that understood nothing.

Notifications at the top:

- Your video is now trending: #2 in Region.
- Your video has been added to a news carousel.
- 27 creators used your audio in the last 5 minutes.

He didn’t click through any of them. The comment ghost-window fluttered anyway, a thin list of hooks:

Where is this? Looks coastal. I can hear waves. Where are the BOSSES NOW?

He saw the first dox-laced one the way you see the first roach and know there are more.

Check the MLS. Harbor Light Foundation had an open house in 2017. That siding.

He froze, then grabbed a keyboard and did what he should have done fifteen minutes ago: kill the post. Delete. The button was a little euthanasia he’d done a thousand times. It spun a pallid little spinner. Then: Error. Try again.

“No,” he said, a laugh that got snagged on a wire.

He tried again. The spinner spun like a parasite. Error. Then a fresh ribbon sliding over the top: Your video has been reuploaded by 182 creators in the past 10 minutes. View derivations?

“Don’t,” he told the machine. “We have enough pain.”

The radio on the shelf crackled and breathed, a man asleep with congestion. “Rourke,” Cass said, thunking the button. It sank like a molar giving way. “Rourke, you copy?”

Scrape-noise. No voice. He twitched his headlamp down to cut the glare. He could still taste rain on his upper lip. He moved to the router, checked the wan lights like he could will redundancy into them. Outside the slit of window you couldn’t see anything but the slashes of rain hitting the glass and running like little metrics, altering their slope depending on the gusts.

A message pinged across all his screens at once with the special sound he’d set for “core”: Mara.

– Where are you.

– IT. Trying to get the post down.

There was a pause long enough to be worrying. He pictured her outside the fence, chin up in the pellets of rain. He pictured the panel hinging. He closed the image like an eyelid and brought up the security cam grid. Half of them were smeared by water, the IR giving everything a dreamy eclipse. The boundary cam showed what looked like static until he realized it was people: pixels of hood, umbrella ribs like spider legs.

Another admin pinged. Iris, with her lowercase disasters.

– they are posting the road

– what

– google maps, cass. someone put a star on us. QA3W… whatever. with “ghost kids.”

He felt the swoop under his ribs. He punched the keyboard sloppily and the house’s wireless daemon told him, primly, that there were ninety-seven devices within footprint. “No. No, no.” The funicular lockout earlier had felt like a clean switch. This wasn’t switches. This was someone unzipping a bag of rats on a picnic.

More comments pulled through despite the filter:

here: 42.6,-70.7. i can hear the foghorn.

From Zillow!! LOL. It was for sale once. You can see the fence line in the drone. SAME ROCKS.

He clicked into the back-end of the org site, which had been a stern brochure for decades: a cluster of static pages and a calendar that never worked right. The “Donate” button had a new badge, though, a red dot he hadn’t coded. He clicked like he was lifting a scab and found the donor portal full of chat, alive. Someone had embedded a public comment widget through a lapsed plugin and now: a running cut-rate forum.

take a left at the weathered lobster trap. there’s a breach in the chainlink behind the blue spruce.

He felt heat in his face that wasn’t the room. He went for the plugin registry, saw it had been last updated in 2019, swore, toggled “deactivate,” got another spinner. A stray strand of wet hair kept sticking to his cheek; he blew at it and tasted salt and dust.

The radio made a cough again. Owen’s voice ghosted around a cell tower: “B—ck—p— f—l.”

Cass’s fingers got more reckless. He put parameters on the firewall that were more hope than best practice. He throttled traffic from outside the region, then added domestic IP blocks he knew would cut off some donors and did it anyway. He turned on the kill switch for the link aggregator he’d worked so hard to get integrated for, god help him, discoverability. He un-discovered them.

The comments on his personal account didn’t care about firewall settings. They kept drawing up from some infinite below, floodwater in a stairwell you’re not climbing fast enough.

— don’t let them gaslight you, this is elder abuse

— post more of the kid pls

— @CassYouSir you lied it’s a Synth

— harbor light, gloucester??

— I’m outside rn in the parking lot lol

His breath snapped. He clicked the profile of the last one. A blurry header of rain across a windshield. A profile picture of a girl with a ring light and a red beanie. Current live: “Outside the Ghost Place” with a view count that was wrong, that was huge.

He opened it. Her voice flowed in, nasally sweet, breathless. “Guys, okay, it’s like actually haunted? The cops are not doing anything. Are we allowed to go in if it’s— ooh! Look—”

The camera jostled and caught the blue spill of a cheap siren light—no, just a toy strobe on someone’s car. People’s faces slurred by, more phones held up like communion wafers. Someone’s hand slid along the chain link. A boy in a clear poncho, hair plastered to skull, yelled, “Tag Cass!” and the live exploded with a new wave of comments and hearts, red and suffocating.

He shut it, like closing a mouth.

The house map on the monitor next to him—something he’d put together with labels while waiting out an earlier gust—lit up with yellow as the door sensors began to jitter. They were just alarm contacts, just little magnets that decided to make it his problem whenever they lost their temper in bad weather. “Don’t you dare,” he told the icons. “Don’t do theater.”

His phone vibrated so hard it hiccuped across the desk. Rourke, finally. He lunged for it with a wet hand and got a faceful of the emergency network message instead: Due to storm conditions, response times have been significantly delayed. In case of life-threatening emergencies…

“I know,” he told the recorded calm. “I know.”

He jabbed comeback texts to the police liaison he had a number for: serious numbers, not the lobby desk. They delivered, were read, and then sat, turning a color it hurt to look at.

The far end of the rack made a different sound. He knew his hardware like voices. The NVR, the cheap one he’d swapped in when the good one coughed in May, whined. He turned. Its status LEDs had gone Christmas, red and green alternating like it wanted to be festive at the worst possible time. The admin page on his screen blinked with a prompt: New login attempt from external IP: allow?

The cursor trembled. “Who gave you an external?” he asked it, and that was to himself; he knew who, he’d opened a port two weeks ago because the board wanted to be able to see “what we’re paying for” during the fundraiser and he’d meant to close it and he hadn’t because one hundred other things. He hit “deny.” The same prompt reappeared with a slightly different address below it. He denied that too. Then another. The prompts began to ladder. His finger got dumb on the trackpad.

He dove into the router settings again, found the NAT table, saw the forward still sitting there like a barn door on a windy day. He clicked the X. The page tried to reload and stalled as if considering whether to humor him.

From the comments shored up along his personal feed:

— can confirm fence is cut by the old boat. thanks Kayla

— if you’re inside go up the left stairs there’s a window unlocked in the greenhouse hall

— my cousin did a wedding there and there’s a cellar door behind the hedges we got drunk in. u can slip a card in the latch

He said “no” again, to the room, to the hedge, to the cousin. He pulled the comms. “Mara, they’re posting entrance points.” He didn’t wait. “Mara? They’re posting the goddamn—”

“Busy,” she answered, breath like wind. Movement sounds. Someone else yelling near her—Owen, probably, or that kid with the good shoulders who always had paint on him. “We need Rourke.”

“He’s a voicemail where hope goes to die,” Cass said. His laugh had buzz on it now, interference from his own chest.

“At least call the police—”

“They sent me a pretty banner. It says good luck and consider making an emergency plan.”

Silence. Then, softer: “Okay. Lock the interior doors. Kill everything you can kill.”

He spun in the chair and made the IT equivalent of a sign of the cross. “Amen.”

He did it: killed the interior Wi‑Fi SSIDs, forcing anyone with stray devices off the network; killed the guest SSID so the ninety-seven devices would have to live on their own battery and their own data. The storm pressed against the window, a palm. The UPS beeped again like a trapped cricket. He moved to the ancient metal cabinet under the desk that held the old maintenance laptop and the binder with passwords in a laminated sleeve labeled, in someone’s neat handwriting, DO NOT TOUCH. He touched it. He flipped with fingers that kept sticking. He found what he needed and regretted every time he’d said, “I’ll remember.”

On the other screen, his platform DMs were flaring, requests from accounts with blue checks and accounts with dog pictures and accounts with God in their bios. One stood out by tone, a neat, transactional directness from a journalism outlet he’d worked with before: We’re live. Want to come on and contextualize?

He barked a single syllable of a laugh. Contextualize a hurricane.

He typed anyway: Not now.

He hovered over the slider that would set his account to Private. The thing he had built like a little precarious house of cards, the only stack of anything he’d made with his own hands that anyone seemed to care about, and the idea of flipping one hidden toggle and making it a dark room where it could not feed or be fed made his stomach flip on itself. And yet—

He dragged the slider. The page refreshed. Locked. The flood of comments kept coming because the mirror clips were their own aqueducts.

A new alert on the NVR flea-circus: Login successful. He stared hard enough for his eyes to water. The camera grid shivered. For a heartbeat it was still his hallway, his pathetic foam-core labels with arrows for “kitchen,” “laundry,” “back stairs.” Then a gray box bloomed in the lower corner, a second stream overlay, some script kiddie’s idea of a watermark: @coastgh0st, 12k watching.

“Get out of my house,” Cass said to the moveable type. He bounced back to the router page. The NAT line still wouldn’t die. The page lolloped in the storm-lag of a shared line hammered by a hundred devices and wind. He slammed the page in a new tab, a different browser, the way you jiggle a lock that’s striped. The fan behind the dust grille hiccuped like it was choking on lint.

He pulled the power on the NVR.

Every camera in the grid went to black on his screens. On someone else’s, he imagined, they went to black too but with a thousand people watching, leaving the impression of secrets behind the dark like a velvet rope. That wasn’t what this was. This was a man who had yanked a plug so nobody else could see the hallway leading to the greenhouse with the crack along the tile where the old water had lifted it years ago and never settled back.

His phone buzzed with a text from a number not in his contacts:

— you’re live on my live rn

He got the picture a second later: the nook’s window, exterior, that slit of glass now a bright rectangle. His own silhouette ghosted in it, a cutout of a man hunched over a glow. He stood up reflexively and the silhouette stood. The phone dinged again, another picture—this one wider, showing the slope under the window, the way the downspout jetted water, the little metal grate where the gutter fed into a buried pipe. The caption: do we tap?

His mouth went dry, a reverse of everything else in the world being damp. He typed back because he could not not: Who is this?

Three dots. He watched them roll like pebbles. Then:

— look outside

He did the opposite. He went to the breaker panel and threw the bottom row. The mini-split died. The UPS went ecstatic. The room went quieter, somehow, and the storm got louder without changing at all.

The radio: “Cass.” Mara again, clipped around the edges. “We’ve got three inside the greenhouse. Tell me you cut the cams on purpose.”

“I cut them.”

“Good.”

“They’re on their own streams anyway,” he said, and immediately wished he’d kept that inside.

“Of course they are,” Mara said, and whatever else she said was drowned by a spike of interference that sounded like steel wool on teeth.

He turned the little crank on the old battery lantern he kept in the rack just to make himself useful, a ritual. The plastic gears made cheerful peep noises, the kind only three people on earth could find comfort in.

He pulled up a different set of maps. Not for them, for him: cut sheets Owen had given him about interior doors that still locked. The notes in the margins were an evolution of handwriting—past caretakers, a past life of the house recorded on paper with ballpoints and a highlighter once the color of a tennis ball. He thumbed locations into his head like he could walk them all at once. He could move through here without his body: past the server rack, to the hall, up one flight, left to the greenhouse corridor. His body would have to follow slower.

Before he moved, he tapped back to the org site again. The public page, the one with the sepia header and the mission statement you could smell the boardroom on, now had a new banner some helpful soul had injected through the comment widget: LIVE FROM INSIDE. A link. He clicked against his better sense and got—of course, of course—the greenhouse live, from someone’s phone propped drunkenly against a watering can, the rain overhead a silver noise and the glass sweated over so everything looked like a painting someone had breathed on.

Two hooded figures moved through the frame. One had the hungry, chest-forward walk of some people with cameras; the other held their phone out as if it emitted heat. “Say hi,” the second one giggled, and a third face leaned in, an oval with mascara on its last legs. “Hi Cass!” she sang, into the feed, into the bone of the house.

He didn’t say anything because they couldn’t hear him and because if he tried, he’d be talking to every echo chamber at once. He hit “report.” The platform said, Thank you for helping keep our community safe. He closed his eyes for half a second, saw a red tide press against the inside of his lids, opened them again.

He grabbed the ring of interior keys off the hook—a heavy, sad chime—and his flashlight. He slung the radio. “Going upstairs,” he said. “Shutting it down.”

“Copy,” Mara said, and then she added, with the calm of someone making a list while a fire alarm went off, “Police are a no. Rourke is a no. It’s us.”

He stepped to the door and stopped, hand on the knob. The iMac was still hot against his side, its light making a tissue-pale rectangle on the wall. He turned back and did the only obvious thing left: he unplugged it. The screen went soft, then uh-uh, no, and then no more. The room’s hum was thinner minus that fan—less like a hive, more like a throat clearing.

His notifications, his feeds, his careful little network of glass cups set out to catch rain, all went silent with the speakers. Outside, the rain did not.

He opened the door and the hallway air hit his face cold and green, full of plant-rot and metal, and the storm sounded like the ocean hammering a bowl. He started up the stairs two at a time, keys chiming. Behind him, in the tiny room, the dead screens reflected him for a second longer than they should have, then let him go.


==================== SCENE 32 ====================
The corridor narrowed into a cul-de-sac of velvet and dust where the ballroom doors loomed like two slabs of night. The parquet here cupped like a tired palm under the runners, and the air felt slow, sour with old glue and a hint of brine. The storm had ratcheted down to a soft drill on the north windows, the house settling into the ache after clench. Mara’s breath fogged in the beam of her flashlight, a quiet flare in the chill.

Owen set his toolkit down and let his hand rest on the carved escutcheon, feeling for play. “Swollen,” he said. “Painted shut, then swelled. And they’ve run trim screws through the stile. See the fill? Someone didn’t want curious hands.”

“Or opportunistic hands,” Mara said. Her voice was low, more for the sake of the hush than any fear of being heard. “How’s our patience level with trim screws.”

“I’m full of love for them,” he said dryly, squatting. The beam slid to follow his shoulder as he leaned in. “Half-inch sheet-metal anchors into plaster, three per side. Somebody was in a mood.”

“Julian would have had a mood about this room being a line item,” Mara noted. She pinched the seam where paint met paint and felt the brittle give. “You hear them?”

“Only the drip through the west gutters,” he said. “Pumps are holding for now. Transfer tank’s at twenty percent. I don’t want to be heroic.”

“Don’t be,” she said. “Be neat.”

He looked up at her. “If these doors bind and the jamb splits, you get a torsion on the header. If the header’s already compromised, you get a cascade. Ballroom spans are stupid. Whoever designed this wanted to show off volume, not sense.”

“Understood.” She tapped the top hinge pin with the butt of her screwdriver, listening. The metal answered dully. “We need in.”

“We need in,” he agreed, and it wasn’t resignation so much as an inventory added to the list.

He opened the kit, pulled out a painter’s multi-tool, a narrow pry bar, a wooden wedge. “We go from the latch side, break the paint seam, remove the screws from the trim—with a miracle—and try to lift the leaf. If the slab is truly swelled, we cut the paint all the way down. If the jamb moves, we stop. No arguments.”

“No arguments,” she echoed.

Mara crouched, working the razor around the gap with methodical pressure. The paint cracked with tiny, happy sighs, curling like fingernail peelings. Owen backed out the first screw in the applied stop; it complained, then came, threads spitting old chalk. The second was more stubborn. He set the screw back down on the tool roll like it was something that had a claim on the world.

“Why seal a ballroom?” Mara murmured.

“Pigeons, taxes, ghosts,” he said. “Pick two.”

“Ghosts eat taxes.” She slid the blade deeper. “Iris said the plaster here went bad before her time.”

“Meaning before three other caretakers,” he said. “Meaning no one wanted to pay for scaffolding.”

He got the next screw to turn, the driver whining. “You smell that?”

Mara did: a cold, feathery rot, not fresh, layered—drop cloths, wool, old flowers. She thought of masks in a museum and didn’t say it. He nudged the stop off and set it gently on the carpet runner. The exposed seam looked more honest now, less like a drawn line.

“Okay,” Owen said. “Let’s see if she’ll give.”

They both had their shoulders angled, the pry bar set under the latch. Owen breathed out, set the wedge, and levered. The door answered with a wet pop and then a sound like a paperweight sliding across sand. The wood stuck again, a stubborn catch. He repositioned, lifted, and Mara felt the slab move in increments, a tide measured in fractions.

“Hold,” he said softly. “Listen.”

They froze. The house did not shift a protest from the header; no tick, no bragging creak. Outside, the rain scratched the windows like fingernails of seaweed.

“Good,” he said. “Again.”

They worked. She leaned her weight, he feathered the bar, their bodies a metronome. Another screw came, another stop piece tucked away. The gap widened enough to take the flat of her hand. Cold air spilled out, attic-cold, clean in the way of things left undisturbed for too long.

Mara slid her hand in and found the chain. “They chained it from the inside,” she said. “That’s jaunty.”

“Can you see the hasp?”

“Left. Two inches.” She pinched the chain, lifted, and felt the rust bite. “It’s set shallow.”

Owen handed her the bolt cutter. It looked ridiculous in the corridor with its bright yellow handles, toy-like until you put it to metal. She angled it blind, found the links by feel, and bit down. The pressure went from resistance to surrender with a neat, blunt blink. The chain fell with what felt like too much relief.

“Is that going to rain door?” Owen asked.

“Only if you’re not looking.”

They both were. He lifted a hair; she countered with the wedge. The slab moved, then stopped at something deep in the sill. He set down the bar and put two fingers on the seam, feeling for nails punched flat and painted over. “Whoever sealed this was thorough.”

“Whoever sealed this had time,” she said.

They cut three more paint bridges. He stepped back and to the hinge side. “I’m going to tap the pins. If one jumps, we back off.”

He set the screwdriver tip under the cap and gave it a persuader’s kiss with the hammer. The pin rose like an arthritic joint, one millimeter, two. He caught it reflexively and nodded. “Top,” he said. “Middle.”

Mara watched the frame while he worked. Nothing sagged. The storm’s murmur turned to a distant sweep, like a theater curtain drawn for a final run-through. On the floor, the line of dust at the door’s bottom edge was thick enough to have its own geology.

“Last one,” Owen said. “Okay. Lift.”

They did, and the door, suddenly depiction rather than boundary, stood proud of its jamb. It rotated open a foot, then two, the hinges whispering a long-neglected secret. Dust spilled in a visible wave, stippling the beam like snow.

The smell was stronger inside—damp canvas and the iron of old light fixtures. Mara clicked her flashlight to a broader spill. The ballroom was a pale city of mounds under drop cloths, each shape anonymous but self-declaring: a grand piano under a crisp tent; a stack of chairs like vertebrae; a mirror as tall as two people turned against the wall and veiled. The ceiling lost itself in shadow, gilding peeping like shy teeth. The far windows were shuttered from within, bars of wood bolted across. One had warped, and the faintest gray of night seeped around the edges.

Owen stayed in the threshold, shoulders squared as if to guard against an invitation to sprint. “I don’t like the span.”

“You don’t have to like the span,” she said, stepping onto the first floorboard.

He put a hand out and she stopped. He tossed the wooden wedge onto the boards. It bounced once, twice, and settled without drama. He nodded. “Cut to the right, keep to the beam line. Feel with your feet. Anything that sounds like a drum, stop.”

“Copy.” She kept her steps small, listening to her own weight come back to her through the wood. The dust under the drop cloths had settled so uniformly it looked intentional, like a frost. Her beam found the edge of the piano cover and slid along it, catching the nameplate under the cloth’s drag: the letters were reverse in the shine of lacquer.

Owen came in behind her, less comfortable but competent, scanning at the cornice level, then the floor, then his breath. “We’re in a lake of ‘no,’” he said quietly. “Pretend the corners are weaker. Pretend, but also believe.”

“Believing,” she said. Her light lifted to the wall where the old portraits hung, two covered, one uncovered—a man in oil with a petulant lower lip and the high brow of someone who wrote stern letters to newspapers—except the paint above his heart had blistered and run in a vertical bloom, a ruddiness that had dried into long tears. It looked like the wall had bled around him and then thought better of it.

“Water ingress,” Owen said preemptively. “From the roofline. It runs the lath, comes out where the canvas is stretched loose. Looks bad. Is bad. Is also not a ghost.”

“I wasn’t auditioning ghosts,” Mara said, though the sight stung. She adjusted the beam. The portrait’s frame was a sunburst carved with compulsive patience. The runnel had left a pale track through the gilt like snail trace.

The room’s hush was its own organism. They moved through it carefully. On the far side, where the windows gave onto the bluff, the shutter bars showed the penny heads of hand-sunk nails. The bars themselves were new enough to be splinter-bright where the planer had nicked them. Someone had been here after the last plaster repair. Someone who hammered like they were apologizing.

Owen crouched by a floor vent, peered into the darkness, and lifted the grille with two fingers. “Duct void,” he said. “No movement. No critters.”

“Good,” she said, and then, “Help me with this cloth.”

Together they peeled the dust sheet off the piano, folding it in on itself so it wouldn’t inhale its own grime back into the room. The instrument beneath prompted an involuntary intake of breath: walnut, the keys yellowed, the lid closed and locked. On the surface, someone had left a fan of programs, the paper turned the color of tea. The dates went back far enough to make your teeth cold.

“Structural?” she asked.

“Piano’s heavy,” he said. “But it’s aligned with a joist. They did that much right.”

They worked along the beam line, uncovering islands: a sofa with its horsehair bravely intact; a low table with water rings that mapped seasons; a stand of candelabra bent as if they’d been listening. Every new shape made the room make more sense.

At the door, he had wedged a doorstop under the bottom edge as if to promise it would not think about shutting on its own. He checked it now, nudging with the toe of his boot. “We’re safe here. If weather goes again, we leave.”

“Agreed.” She walked the long wall, stepping over the meek, blistered bumps of buckled boards. Her light landed again on the weeping portrait. Up close, the canvas edge had a touch that wasn’t accidental—someone had tacked a narrow velvet ribbon along the inner frame, something a former lady of the house might have thought tasteful. The ribbon had bled dye once upon a time, and the bleed had tracked the water line into a suggestion of something else.

Mara put her palm against the paneling just to the right to feel the temperature. The wood was cool. Her fingers found a ridge, then a seam, then what might have been a button beneath paint. She stood very still, then took her hand away as if the wood had teeth. She didn’t press it again. Not yet.

Owen’s light drifted back to her shoulder. “What are you thinking.”

“I’m thinking we’re in,” she said, and the relief in the words was equal parts access and reprieve. She turned a circle slowly, mapping the room to her eye. “We’re in and nothing collapsed. That’s our win.”

He gave a small, rueful breath. “I like wins.”

They stood in the hush, listening to the east gutter cluck and the ocean speak its slow, indifferent grammar against the bluff. It was almost easy to imagine all the shapes under sheets as guests asleep after a long party, but neither of them went there. Mara tilted her head toward the door with a glance that meant tools; Owen understood and went to fetch the kit, his boots ticking gently on the safest line. The wedge at the threshold held firm, the door graced a patient angle, and the ballroom’s dust drifted in their beams like a quiet weather they had made themselves.


==================== SCENE 33 ====================
They worked without speaking at first, the kit open on the piano’s closed lid like a surgical tray. Owen handed her the slim pry blade and the little dental mirror on a telescoping stick, then tucked the headlamp under his cap brim so its beam held steady.

Mara angled the mirror along the portrait’s frame. Up close, the velvet ribbon looked like bruise-dark fruit skin, seamed and puckered where tacks bit. The “tears” were not only dye; the streaks carried silt, a fine grit that had ridden water and settled in thin commas. She followed them with the edge of the blade, reading the house’s handwriting.

“See this?” She touched the stain where it broke, then resumed lower, three inches to the right. “Not gravity. It jogs. There’s an interrupt.”

Owen leaned in, careful to keep his weight over a solid strip of floor. “Channel behind? Or a join.”

“Or both.” She pressed the pad of her thumb against the panel just to the right of the frame and felt the faintest give. Not loose—sprung. “Hold your light.”

He did, and she worked the blade into the hairline seam she’d felt earlier. Paint crackled like old pastry. The house gave them a small sigh of dust.

“Wait.” Owen’s hand came up. “If it’s a latch, we don’t want to shear it. You want the scope?”

“Yeah.”

He fished out the borescope—a stiff cable with a tiny camera—and fed it into the seam. The phone screen bloomed with a claustrophobic tunnel of wood grain, a spiderweb, an old wasp nest the size of a thimble. Mara guided while he steadied the cable.

“Back a hair,” she said. “There. That shadow.”

The lens caught a dull square half-swallowed by cobweb. Metal behind paint, with a shallow notch.

“That’s hardware,” Owen said. “Somebody painted it over with everything else.”

Mara withdrew the scope. “I felt a button under here before, under the paint. They laid ribbon to hide the outline.” She tapped the ribbon’s edge near the portrait’s lower right corner. “The bleed tracks to the latch line and then… jogs.”

“Meaning the water came through, found the latch seam, and ran along it.”

“Meaning the seam’s continuous. Meaning there’s a way in.”

She tested the paint’s skin with her thumbnail. The make-do fix of a dozen caretakers resisted, gummy and stubborn.

“Heat?” Owen asked. “Softens paint. Gentle.”

She nodded. He produced a small heat gun, the kind used for shrink tubing and old varnish, no more threatening than a hairdryer but with purpose. He fixed a scrap of flashing as a heat shield to protect the silk at the portrait’s edge, then warmed the bottom run of paint in patient circles. The paint slackened.

Mara slid the pry blade in again, this time with a little more kindness. The blade found the notch. She felt the plate of something move beneath, the tiniest click. Nothing yielded yet.

“Feels sprung,” she said.

“Let me feel.” He took the blade, set the tip just proud of her mark, and flexed. The panel lifted a fraction, then settled as if deciding not to embarrass itself.

“Pressure’s wrong,” she murmured. She flattened her palm across the panel and felt for the way wood speaks when it’s trapped—resistance in a crescent, not a wall. “It wants to go left, not out.”

Owen looked at the floor. “Slide, not hinge. Swollen rails.”

“Delicate.”

He grunted assent. “I’ll do a French cleat shim. No prying.”

He dug into the kit again and came up with thin plastic wedges and a length of painter’s strapping. He looped the strapping around the lower right corner where the ribbon hid the edge, giving them something to pull against that wasn’t the silk. He eased a wedge into the seam at the opposite corner, just enough to break adhesion without torque.

“On three,” he said. “Nudge, not haul. If it moves, we go with it. If it doesn’t, we stop.”

She took the strap, wrapped it around her palm twice. “One.”

“Two.”

“Three.”

They leaned together. The panel resisted like a sleeping animal, then gave a damp, reluctant pulse. Paint unzipped with a soft tearing noise. The ribbon trembled. The panel slid a quarter inch left, then halted against swollen grain.

“Don’t force,” she said, breath threading. “Let it remember.”

He laughed once, quiet. “Talk to it.”

She loosened their angle, felt the weight translate. They eased again. A second quarter inch. Cool air breathed up from the seam—old, mineral, unperfumed. The ocean pronounced itself a little louder through that hairline.

Mara’s shoulders softened. “There’s void.”

“Good. Keep going.”

One more nudge. The panel moved with more confidence, then stuttered. She heard a tick inside, the catch of a spring that had not sprung in decades.

“Stop.” She listened. The tick didn’t repeat. Nothing fell. “We’re okay.”

He withdrew the wedge and slid a second one where the panel had hung. “We’ll lift the swell,” he said. “If we fight it, we splinter.”

He warmed the seam once more, low and slow. She pressed her palm over the paint just above the ribbon and felt the skin soften under heat, like frost in sun.

“Try,” he said.

They drew again, and this time the panel obeyed. It slid left in a hushed, steady run, paint dust and ribbon lint rising like a tiny storm. When it reached the end of its travel, it thumped—quiet, decisive—against a stop.

Behind it: black. Not empty black; black that had depth and structure. The smell came first, a clean cold that had avoided the mildew in the rest of the room. Something metallic, too, the faint iron of nails and the real chill of stone deeper inside.

Owen lifted his headlamp beam and skimmed it in. The light hit stacked lath, then vanished into a vertical shaft. Farther in, the shaft opened: a pocket tall enough for a person to stand, a tight corridor or closet cut between studs and wrapped in old tarred felt. On the right, within hand’s reach, a small lever glinted—mate to the painted-over square they’d freed.

Mara steadied the panel so it wouldn’t rebound. “Hold it,” she said. “I’ll wedge.”

He passed her two more shims. She set them, careful to keep pressure even and not crush any of the delicately rotted paint lips. The panel stayed.

“Light?” she asked.

He offered the headlamp. She clipped it to her collar and slid sideways into the opening until her shoulder touched the cold felt. Dust kissed her cheek. Her beam found the lever again, then a shallow wooden shelf below it with a brass keyhole plate and no key. The shelf was clean except for a perfect oval where something had rested a long time and was now gone.

“Latch is mirrored inside,” she said. “If someone was in, they could close from within.”

“Good design for a secret, lousy for fire,” Owen said. His voice sounded different here, tamped by the absorbent hush.

“Feel this.” She guided his hand to the felt. It held no damp. The room’s wet had simply washed around this space and not through.

He whistled under his breath. “They sealed it right.”

She checked the plinth line with her fingers and felt a little turnbuckle in the corner, a simple brace that had been loosened by the swelling and now hung slack. She spun it twice and the panel settled a hair. Not dangerous—graceful, like a curtain coming to rest.

“Let’s get it open fully,” she said. “I want both hands free.”

Owen reset his stance and they walked the panel as far left as it would go, then set a wooden spreader bar he’d fashioned on bigger jobs—a length of oak with rubber feet—between panel edge and adjacent molding. He tightened it just enough to hold, no more.

“Tell me when that feels wrong,” he said.

“It feels right.”

He angled his light past her shoulder. The void beyond the initial pocket ran deeper than their beams could define immediately, but the first six feet were clear. No spiders big enough to have names, no critter nests, just a brushed track in the dust where a body had taken this path before them a long time ago. The portrait’s backside loomed above, stitched linen and stretcher bars like the ribs of a boat.

Mara reached for the interior lever and tried it. It moved with a neat, pleased click. Behind them, the painted-over square on the outside made a sympathetic cluck, as if agreeing. The mechanism was intact.

Owen smiled, and the smile changed him from caretaker to boy for a second. “There she is.”

“Truth where they hung a face to hide it,” Mara said, not meaning to sound like a toast.

He handed her a dust mask and pulled one over his own mouth. “After you.”

She leaned in, set her boots on the hidden floor plate—the wood was sound—and stepped through. The air wrapped her wrists in coolness. She heard her own breathing, thin through the mask, and the far-off vocabulary of weather beyond the walls. Owen slid in after her, shoulders filling the opening, then turned sideways so he didn’t scrape the molding.

They stood together in the narrow space, their lights crossing. On the left, an inch from Mara’s knee, a vertical run of old conduit marched downward like a tidy stitched seam. On the right, the felt peeled back in one neat rectangle, tacked to a batten—no accident, no animal, a human cut. That opening looked inward to a darker hollow, roomy and waiting.

Owen’s hand hovered over the edge of the peel. “You got photos first?”

“Yeah.” She took three quick shots, then another with the scale card. “Okay.”

He eased the felt back. Old air rolled out—the kind of cold that stays no matter the season, a collector’s cold. Their lights went in.

The wall void was there, unapologetic, all ribs and shadow. It wasn’t a narrow chase but a true cavity: a deep box framed between studs and lathe, descending and ascending beyond their immediate sightline. A small ladder was fixed inside, narrow rungs like piano keys, the wood unworn in the center and smoothed on the edges by hands that had kept close to the sides.

Mara let out her breath. “We’ve got it.”

Owen looked back to the ballroom, the sheeted furniture, the patient doorstop. He adjusted the spreader bar once, minutely. “And we didn’t break her.”

“Not a splinter.”

He grinned behind his mask. “Let’s see what they thought we wouldn’t.”


==================== SCENE 34 ====================

“Hold your beam low,” Mara said. “Rake it. I want the topography.”

Owen angled his flashlight so it glanced along the interior face of the cavity. Dust took shape—dunes and combed tracks, islands of untouched fluff, a shallow tide line where air had moved past for years.

Mara pulled a roll of Tyvek from her pack, tore a strip with her teeth, and laid it down across the floor plate inside the opening, sticky tabs outward, making a clean threshold. “Step on this if you have to step. Nothing past the line.” She kept her voice light. “I’m not paying to rebuild the eighteenth century with our boot prints in it.”

“My boots are a bargain,” he murmured, but he stayed on the Tyvek.

She snapped a series of overview shots: portrait backside, their entry angle, the peeled felt tacked to the batten, the cavity yawning beyond. Then she moved in close—edges first, always edges. The cut in the felt was surgical, a box knife with a steady hand, the weave clean and tight where it parted. The tack heads along the batten had a faint crescent of lighter felt around each—recent disturbance marked itself by absence, dust halos where fingers had pushed. She got them from three angles with the scale card laid in the same plane.

Owen breathed quietly behind his mask. The house breathed louder: a drawn-out draft that rose up the cavity like a voice running scales.

Mara set her flashlight on its magnet so it stuck to the conduit, giving her both hands. She took out the little mirror on a telescoping wand, angled it into the darker corner where the ladder hid. The rungs were narrow and square, sixty years of air and maybe six years of hands. The centers held a skin of untouched dust; the outer edges looked polished by caution, someone keeping their weight to the side.

“Rungs are kissed on the flanks,” she said. “Center’s still furred. Whoever uses this doesn’t trust the middle.”

“Would you?”

“Not today.” She took photos of each rung in ascending order, a strip of exposures like piano keys.

The opening into the deeper void was neat but not sterile. Along the lathe at the lip, there were scuffs of paint transfer—white-gray chalk—where something broader than a hand had passed and nudged. She touched nothing, only eased the scale into the plane and shot it with the light raked from below, switching to oblique, then dead-on for documentation. In one shot, a single pale hair had stuck to the splinter spike of a lathe strip. She paused, looked at Owen.

“You want it?” he asked softly.

“I want to leave it exactly where it is until Rourke can pull it with the kit. Photographs will satisfy him for now.” She pulled a sticky arrow from her flags, wrote HAIR—DO NOT TOUCH, and anchored it to the batten, the arrow pointing in without crossing into the void.

He nodded. “He’ll be glad you didn’t pluck.”

“It’s pretty,” she said. “Let him have the pleasure.”

She shifted to the left edge, where the conduit ran like a stitched seam. Old cloth-wrapped cable, tidy straps, dust curling off the strap screws in petrified petals. The screws had untouched crowns. She took a close-up of one head, the dust granules distinct like sugar. No new screwdriver chatter, no torque sheen.

“Untampered hardware,” she murmured, taking another.

The bottom of the cavity showed a seam where the baseboard had been backed with a thin spacer. On the spacer’s upper face, someone long ago had run a fingertip through the dust in a straight stroke, perhaps to test. That stroke had softened at the edges since, a small drift filling the trench. She shot it, noting the softening.

“Time cost,” Owen said, reading her mind. “You want the whole cavity before you lose the light.”

“Batteries, not light.” She checked the bar on her camera. Two left on this battery, a full spare in the pouch. “We can do it if we don’t start touching things. I’m not pulling anything. We document, then close.”

He looked relieved. “Music to the plaster’s ears.”

She pulled a small UV flashlight and clicked it on. The cavity went into bruise tones; old varnish on the back of the portrait came alive; a few flecks along the ladder rungs glowed faintly. Skin oils. She photographed them with the UV, then repeated with visible light for reference. Along the inner lip of the opening, a series of faint arcs traced where fingers had curled over the edge. The arcs were intermittent in UV and steady in dust, which told her someone had used gloves at least once, and once not.

“Two kinds of touch,” she said, more to the record than to Owen.

“Gloves and bravado.”

“Or gloves and forgetful.” She switched back to white light, took her shots, then slid the mirror deeper.

“How far does she go?” Owen asked.

“Down past the level of the ballroom floor. I can see a cross-brace. Up is maybe four feet, then a header. It’s a box, not a shaft. Storage, not passage.” She took a general shot to catch what the mirror showed, checked focus. “You’re not crawling in there.”

He lifted his hands. “Not volunteering.”

She took a breath through the mask, felt the cool air gather around her wrists again. “We need a raking sequence along the interior face. Start at the top, sweep down. Slow.” She passed him the second light. “Keep the beam at the same angle.”

He set himself, patient, and dropped the beam a half-inch at a time while Mara clicked off frames. On the third sweep a pattern rose out of the dust—parallel lines, shallow and even, as if something with a ribbed edge had rested against the interior face. She got in tight and saw the faintest imprint of a corner: a rectangle, small, the size of an envelope box. The ribs were from the card stock ridges.

“Somebody staged a little shelf against this face,” Owen said, seeing it as she did.

“Or rested something while they worked the lever. No drag marks, just set-downs.” She photographed it with the scale, then with the oblique LED to carve the shadow. “We are not going to rebuild it.”

He grinned with his eyes. “Which is to say I am not going to try to see if something still sits there.”

“You’re doing great by doing nothing.”

He made a small show of holding his hands higher away from the edges. “My mother would be shocked.”

Mara shifted her weight, felt the Tyvek crinkle under her boot. Beyond the immediate permission of the opening, the rest of the house waited with its covered furniture and its sense of held breath. The cavity held its own.

“I want the tool marks on the lever,” she said. “Then we close up and write it down.”

She laid the scale along the interior lever, careful not to touch the working part. The metal had fine scratches, directional, a little crescent scar where a flat object had slid off and bit. That scar had bright metal at its core, not yet gone dull, but a film was already starting. She shot it from three axes.

“Flat spatula?” Owen said.

“Flat something.” She took one last shot of the lever with the outside square visible beyond the portrait’s back, then turned the camera to the hinges on the cut felt. “You see how clean the cut is? No fray. That’s practiced.”

“Stagehands,” he said. “Or someone who makes models.”

“Or someone who was careful.” She smiled a little behind the mask because the habit of speculation was like chewing gum and she was letting it flavor her mouth without swallowing. “We’re still not touching.”

“Ma’am, no ma’am.”

She clicked the camera off long enough to swap the battery, slid the spent one into a labeled envelope, and spoke the date and time aloud as she seated the new one. She took a step back onto the clean Tyvek and did a second round of wider shots, so the set of the opening sat in context with the painted-over square and the spreader bar bracing the portrait’s hinge in the ballroom beyond. Owen adjusted the beam to keep his own shadow out of frame.
