“How many?” he asked as she checked the counter.

“Eighty-one. We’ll back them up now, then again when we get to power.” She pulled the second card, mirrored her shots, labeled that card with a fine-tip and a strip of tape. She sealed it in a small bag with a signature across the seam. Another bag for the first card went into her inside pocket. “You’re a witness,” she said. “Initial where I marked.”

He took the pen, leaned against nothing, and signed in a small precise hand. “Certified.”

She looked once more into the void. The ladder’s edges caught the light like piano ivories in a closed room. The dust held itself on its own terms. The hair on the lathe made its own polite declaration by simply existing.

“Okay,” she said. “We close. We leave it as we found it.” She angled her camera for one last shot with the felt held back, the ladder visible, the arrow flag pointing. Then she touched the tack heads with the back of her fingernail, feeling their coolness without pushing them in, the way you tap a sleeping shoulder to say you’re leaving the room.

Owen held the felt while she eased it back into place, careful to lay it on the same tack shanks without pressing. The cut met its cut. The line vanished into the old wall like a healed seam.

“Spreader,” Owen said, turning. He reached out and gently loosened the aluminum brace he’d wedged against the molding, holding the portrait open. As the pressure eased, the portrait’s back came down to meet its frame with a soft, steady settle. The sympathetic cluck from the painted-over square answered again, tidy, correct. The mechanism was home.

Mara stood on the Tyvek one more second, scanning. No new scuffs, no fresh streaks, no footprint strayed over the line. The dust had not bloomed where it shouldn’t. She peeled the Tyvek up by its clean tabs and folded it inward so whatever dust it had caught stayed caught. She slid it into a bag and labeled it VOID THRESHOLD—BALLROOM—WALL PORTRAIT—10. She looked at Owen. “That’s to keep us honest.”

“Always a good look on us,” he said.

She slotted the labeled bag into her pack, zipped that pocket, and clicked off her light. The cavity fell away into natural shadow. The house’s weather-words came back in, soft and indifferent.

“Want to tape a note?” Owen asked, already pulling a strip of linen tape from his pocket.

“Yeah.” She printed in block caps on a card from her kit: DOCUMENTED. DO NOT OPEN. ROURKE—EVIDENCE HOLD. She dated it, added her initials and Owen’s, and taped it to the back of the portrait frame where anyone tempted would see it before their hand got clever.

They stood a second, masks down around their throats now, air tasting like old plaster and linen. Owen looked at her. “You good?”

“We got it,” she said. “Photos secured, void intact.”

“House thanks you,” he said, low and pleased.

She patted the frame once, not quite a touch, then turned for the library with her pack. The storm moved in its own grammar out beyond the windows, but here the wall was closed, and what lived behind it sat preserved in its own chosen air.


==================== SCENE 35 ====================
The next morning the kitchen felt like a stage set left on overnight: the long butcher block still damp in a clean oval where a pot had sat, a ring of salt white on the slate by the back door, towels hung to dry and crisp with last night’s air. Light came in thin as paper through the east windows, the sky outside a flat pewter. The generator’s thrum had settled into a low cough, off and on, a smoker clearing its throat.

Owen had both hands braced on the farm sink, watching the bottle under the tap spit and struggle. “That’s not pretty,” he said, closing the tap and holding the bottle up. The water inside was clouded with a faint silt swirl that refused to settle. He leaned close, sniffed, made a face. “Vegetal. Not chlorinated. That tells us something.”

“It tells me I’m not making coffee,” Mara said. She set her pack on the chair nearest the hatch and unlaced the rain shell bundled on top. Her fingers were stiff from the chill; she flexed them, feeling the joints click. “What’s the reading on the tank?”

Owen cast a look toward the utility closet where the old analog gauge had been taped to the door in somebody’s tidy handwriting years ago. “Stuck at one quarter since last night. Could be true, could be wishful thinking. Pump’s been cavitating. I keep priming it and she keeps lying to me.”

“The cistern’s under us,” Mara said.

“Old stone collar. The hatch was put in after the war.” He nudged the boot rack aside with his foot, exposing the darker rectangle cut into the scullery side of the kitchen’s floor. The wood around it had a different line to its grain, newer by a couple of decades, more yellow under the varnish. “Old times they hauled from the wellhead out back. Then somebody got elegant and piped it. And here we are.”

She looked at the hatch: flush with the floor, two iron pull rings, a shallow lip where dust had gathered like eyeliner. Someone had chalked a small X at one corner, the kind of code that meant nothing now and everything to the person who’d written it. Owen crouched and ran his hand along the seam, feeling for tacky edges.

“It’s not painted shut,” he said. “There’s felt under the lid. For odor, originally. They were thinking onions. Not other things.” He lifted his eyes to hers. “We can put it off. Rourke can bring his team.”

“If the water’s bad, we need to know now,” she said. “People are going to brush their teeth. They’re going to cook. Whatever’s in there, or not in there, we need to confirm before noon.”

“Right,” Owen said, with that short, resigned nod he used when the obvious was also the unpleasant. He straightened and went to the drawer with the box of nitrile gloves and the roll of painter’s tape. “Masks?”

She pulled two from her pack, the charcoal cartridges clicking onto the halves with a pinched twist. “If it’s strong, we back off. We don’t go all the way in. We look, we don’t touch. Visual only, initial note. You’re holding the light.”

“Lucky me,” Owen said, but his voice had softened. He took the mask and leaned his forehead against his wrist as he adjusted the straps behind his head, finding the fit by feel the way someone ties a familiar knot.

They put on gloves. Owen flipped the painter’s tape loose from the roll and made two strips on the edge of the hatch so they could lift without palms on wood. He planted his boots at shoulder width and put his fingers in the rings. “On three,” he said, muffled by the mask. “You good?”

Mara pulled her own straps tight and pressed both valves with her fingertips. The mask sucked in and held. “Good.”

“One, two, three.”

The hatch came up with a sound like a wet kiss separated, the old felt seal breaking. A thin burp of air rose and then—another wave, thicker, assertive, like the worst kitchen drain in the city multiplied, a sweetness undercut by rot. It hit the back of her throat and she swore into the rubber muzzle.

“Jesus,” Owen said, eyes squinting. He held the lid at a slant while the first roll of air coughed up and out. She watched for pressure change, for that invisible writhe that means gas, felt nothing beyond the stink and the way the kitchen went small around it. The lights in the ceiling suddenly looked too clean.

“Hold,” she said. He did. They waited another ten seconds, watching nothing. The smell didn’t elapse; it sat and made a chair, settled in, insisted on its own reality. Owen’s eyes watered behind his safety glasses.

“Okay,” she said. “Slow.”

He lowered the hatch the rest of the way open and propped it back with the chock he’d set aside. The opening revealed a square throat into the floor, rough planks around a concrete collar. Moisture had beaded up under the lid and now slid down in sluggish tears. The smell was worse at the edges.

“Light?” she said.

Owen switched on his work lamp and tightened it one-handed to the bracket at the edge, tilting it down so it would throw a broad, low cone rather than a tunnel. “Don’t lean,” he said.

“I won’t,” she said, and they didn’t. They approached the edge in increments, breath nasal, careful. She braced one hand on the counter as if the floor might tilt. She’d descended enough old ships’ hatches to expect ladders, and there was one here, bolted iron rungs down a shaft of poured concrete. A film had formed on the rungs: brown-green, faintly luminous if only because the light turned everything below into another country.

The cistern wasn’t far. She could see the water’s skin from here, uneven, dull. On the right, a floating shape that was familiar in the same way the smell was familiar, and both of those things were why she let her eyes move carefully, one quadrant at a time, as if patience could alter content.

“You okay?” Owen’s voice was low and uncharacteristically formal. He’d set the lantern so both of them lit the pit but not each other’s faces.

“Fine,” she said, and swallowed. Her mouth had turned slick with saliva, the body’s ancient prep to reject. She breathed slow and counted the tiles on the backsplash until the urge receded, then looked again. The stone sides of the cistern were old blocks mortared and skimmed; a green beard had grown in vertical runs where leaks had been or where condensation always condenses. The water itself had a life on it: threads, skins, a thrift-store sweater of algae. A scum had gathered thick near the outflow pipe. In the center it held a sheen that winked and went as the surface took an almost unnoticeable move.

She smelled the sweet note again, one layer above the other, and then her eyes adjusted to the darker on darker. The floating shape wasn’t the shape. The true shape was below it, mass below scum. She realized she had been avoiding seeing down, her eyes skittering off the deep like a nervous shorebird. She forced them and saw the thing under the water that shouldn’t be under the water.

An elbow first, or what read as that: a bend, pale, not-stone, with a line along it where some fabric had bunched. Beyond it, a shoulder turned just enough to make slope into a human slope. The water magnified and shivered it: familiar geometry aberrated by a slow, thick lens. Hair fanned and caught along the intake grate like river weed.

“Oh,” said Owen, not a word, just air shaped. His hand went to the edge and then stopped because he’d told her not to lean. “Mara.”

“I see,” she said. The words came out flat, from the efficient part of her brain that rose in situations like this and moved her hands correctly. It wasn’t calm; it was a window someone had slid open in winter, cold air rushing and everything else going silent.

They took a beat. The kitchen noises that weren’t the kitchen asserted themselves: the house’s weight shifting on its beams; a draft through the bad patch of the west window; far down the hill a gull that had gotten lost and found its cousin and shouted about it. Close by, only their own careful breaths.

“How long?” Owen said, and then shook his head, as if that were too far down the road. He stayed close, shoulders hunched a fraction, every tendon in his forearms a line under the glove.

“Doesn’t matter until it matters,” she said. “We document we saw. We do not go down.” She could hear herself taking inventory aloud and let herself. It was better than thinking about the cold around the spine below, the way the water had taken away what warmth had made it person and now made it object. The human body was so much smaller when not alive. And yet the cistern had made it large in another way, a problem that filled a single room and then radiated out into the day.

“We shouldn’t be breathing this,” Owen said, a quick scrabble note in his voice. “If there’s decomp in the water, that’s in the air. I should—vent.”

“Back door,” she said. He peeled away to push it open with the back of his glove, then a wedge of outside light and wind slid in low along the floor, corralling the smell and shouldering it toward the drains. It didn’t leave; it simply agreed to share.

“Rourke,” he said.

“In a minute,” she said, because she wanted the initial photos without anyone walking into the frame yet. Because she wanted to look again and know she wasn’t seeing a trick of light, or a scare that the house had put in there just because it could. She put a fresh card in the camera from habit, even though the one in there had space. She didn’t want this file touching anything else.

Owen shifted closer again, the lamp warming his glove where he grabbed the stand to drag it half an inch, the better to throw light under the scum. The day threw its own milk light down beside it, and the two whites argued as they hit the surface; she adjusted the exposure and let the argument be part of the record.

She took shots: the hatch as found and opened, the iron rungs with their slime, the lip of stone where someone’s boot had scuffed last week and made a pale crescent. She framed the water’s surface to include the intake pipe on the far wall and the floating trash that should not have been there—a cigarette filter bobbing like a tiny buoy, a warped mason jar lid, a piece of twine with a knot that had held something small and had let it go.

Down. She leaned her camera out enough to catch what was below without being stupid about balance. The lens turned what her eyes had been arguing with into flat truth: the pale limb, the shoulder, the fabric’s color trying to assert itself through water’s brown lens. A hand? She couldn’t see a hand. Heads turned wrong underwater; gravity and buoyancy fought and made strange theaters. She didn’t catalogue parts. She took the images.

“Tell me when,” Owen said, because he could not not look and could also not look alone. She clicked off another set of exposures and then lowered the camera. He leaned forward and made himself see what he had already seen and denied. In his shoulders she saw the moment of acceptance, the way the body takes a blow and finds its new shape around it. He put his hand on the counter and left it there, the glove squeaking.

“Okay,” he said into the mask. He swallowed. “Okay.”

“We close it?” he asked, after a breath. “Seal the air? Or do we leave it to vent? I don’t know which keeps people safer.”

“Back door open, hatch closed,” she said. “We mark it and keep everyone out and upwind. We put a chair over it and a note. We call Rourke from the hall.”

“Right,” he said. He didn’t move. He looked down like there might be another instruction written at the bottom and the body might contain it.

Mara exhaled through the side of her mouth to fog her own mask and tamp down whatever had risen. She let her eyes flick sideways to ground them: the row of knives magnetized to the backsplash, the ceramic canister marked FLOUR in a script that used to be called cheerful, a spray of rosemary in a jar darkening the water as it steeped. The rosemary would smell like something else now, if she held it to her nose.

“We didn’t hear anything,” Owen said suddenly, surprising her. “Last night. I was in here half the night with that pump. I didn’t hear. That’s—” He pinched his lips into a line. “We wouldn’t have, I suppose.”

“You can’t hear through water and stone what you think you can hear,” she said. “The house eats sound.”

He winced-sighed, and some small tension left him only so better tension could take its place. “You want a ladder guard?”

“Yeah,” she said. “Tape across the rungs. No one steps down without PPE and Rourke’s say.”

He moved then, practical again. He reached out and tore two strips of tape and made an X across the top three rungs, the adhesive grabbing even on the damp metal. He added a second X below, belt and suspenders; if anyone tried to go down they’d have to commit to breaking something to do it.

She took one last image. The camera’s click sounded indecent in the wide quiet. She capped the lens, slipped the body into its bag, and then wrote the words on the evidence card with her Sharpie: CISTERN—KITCHEN—HATCH—OPEN—BODY SIGHTED. She wrote the hour, the date, the notch of the wind she felt coming under the back door. She wrote her initials and then Owen’s, and his pen stroke trembled and left a double line, as if two of him had tried to sign at once.

“Ready?” she said.

“Yeah.” He swallowed again. His eyes were red. When he lifted the hatch, the stink surged, chastened by the new wind but confident in its own persistence. They brought the lid down in a steady, controlled way, careful not to slam. The felt gasket took time to meet. The lid settled with a sullen hush. The smell remained, attenuated, as if someone had left a rotten apple in a drawer and then closed it but the room would always know.

Owen slid the chair over the hatch and set both its front legs hard. He taped the first note she wrote to the chair’s back and a second to the floorboards in front: DO NOT MOVE—HAZARD—EVIDENCE HOLD. He kept his eyes on the tape as he rubbed it down, as if he could will it to become law.

They stood together by the sink then, both of them pulling their masks down at the same time, because the house’s air was still better than the seal and the smell would be with them whether they wore them or not. Air caught in the back of her throat and made her think of pennies and fennel and something else she did not want to name.

“Rourke,” Mara said, and reached for her phone.

Owen washed his gloves in a bowl with dish soap like it would help and then stopped, because it was absurd, and emptied the bowl into the sink and watched the suds go and refuse to go, hanging like ghosts in the neck of the drain. He wiped the edge of the sink with the back of his wrist like that would do anything about anything.

When Rourke picked up, Mara kept her words simple. “Kitchen cistern. Hatch open. We’ve sighted a body,” she said, crisp, the syllables laying themselves down like planks.

“Copy,” Rourke said through whatever morning he had. “You sealed?”

“Closed. Room vented. No one down.”

“Keep it that way.”

She hung up and set the phone face down on the counter and it wobbled on a grain ridge and then found level.

Owen turned to the back door and pushed it wider. Cold came in like a new, better thing, prying into the room, skimming along the floor and out, pulling at the smell. The day outside had found its light. The herb pots shivered. The gull on the hill had changed his mind about something and was telling everyone.

Mara looked at the chair, solid and ordinary on the invisible square under it. She didn’t look at the hatch because she had already looked and now they would not again until the right people were here. She took the Sharpie and printed one more note, her block letters darker now with pressure, and taped it to the refrigerator, at eye level, where anyone going for milk would see it and stop.

NO WATER.
DO NOT USE SINK.
DO NOT ENTER.
SEE ROURKE.

Owen read it and nodded, as if reading it out loud to himself would make it truer. He leaned his hip on the counter and held himself there for one more breath, one more.

“Tea?” he asked suddenly, absurd and not absurd, because hands wanted tasks. “Kettle, bottled.”

“Later,” she said. “Let’s get out of the room.”

They stepped into the hall, and the smell thinned immediately, which didn’t make it gone so much as put it in a different tense. The kitchen, with its ordinary plates and its rosemary and its chair, sat behind them. Under the floor, the cistern held what it held. They stood in the narrow hall with their hands by their sides and the house did not care, which was, at that moment, a strange relief.


==================== SCENE 36 ====================
They stood a while and said nothing. The house clicked once somewhere in its vents like an old watch running down.

“Rourke said keep it that way,” Owen said.

“He also said keep the room vented,” Mara said. “We’ve got a hatch open inside a structure with people in it. If anyone turns a tap—”

He shut his eyes, swallowed once, opened them again. “We’ll make it quick. No touching. No reaching. Light only.”

“Light and a probe,” she said. “Confirmation, then we get out and lock it.”

He looked like someone who’d been told to open his own mouth to check for a broken tooth. Still, he went to the pantry and dragged out a telescoping pole, the kind painters use for ceilings. He rooted through a drawer and produced a stainless meat hook that had never known a side of beef in its life; he taped it to the tip with blue tape, then taped over the tape with duct, layer on layer until the end looked like a padded spear.

Mara took the headlamp from the peg by the door and clicked it on. It stuttered, then held. She tested her phone in her pocket—one bar knuckled over to zero, then back. She didn’t like the way the signal acted like a tide.

They went back into the kitchen. The air was sour-sweet in the way of wet things that used to be alive and had opinions about it. The note on the refrigerator looked too declarative, a poster for order in a room that had other ideas.

“Open and close,” Mara said.

Owen set the pole down, palmed the hatch ring, and lifted. The wood gave up a gasp as the seal broke. The smell came up like knowledge. Mara put her glove to her face for an instant and then forced her hand back down. She knelt and set her headlamp for flood, angling herself so the beam wouldn’t glare off the wet stone.

The cistern was a black mouth, cold breath sailing up. The beam found the mortared wall, the curved belly of the chamber, the rag-sheen water pushing against the brick with a little lap. And there, where they’d seen shape before, the light made accuracy: a shoulder blunt with a wet shirt on it, a gray arm rising to nothing, hair snarled and streaming, caught where the flow narrowed under a low stone shelf. The skin had the color of dishwater. It was so ordinary and so un-ordinary that the brain did a lurch and then settled like it would later.

Owen took a breath he didn’t want and inched the pole forward, guiding it with small corrections so it didn’t clank against the edge. His hands were steady in the way of a man lying to his hands.

“Hook the fabric,” Mara said, soft. “Don’t catch—don’t lift. Just proof.”

He touched the hook to a fold of shirt. The fabric gave the way wet cotton gives, heavy as sand. The meat hook slid off and tapped stone with a gentle, obscene ring. Owen tried a sleeve. The pole tip nudged and the arm obeyed gravity and not him and then stopped, held by whatever held the rest, wedged tight under that low shelf. The hair moved like grasses in a tide pool; the face stayed turned away, cheek to stone, a careful denial.

“That’s a person,” he said, and the two words landed as if they’d been looking for somewhere to land.

“Pull back,” Mara said. “We don’t move them. We don’t try.”

He drew the pole in, slow, as if anything quick mattered. The hook came up trailing a thread of something that wasn’t thread; he wiped it off with a paper towel and then looked at his hands like he’d used them wrong all his life.

She swept the beam around once more—stone ladders built into the wall, slick and greened; a scum of floating rosemary needles and a little square of white that might have been a label once. She cut the light.

Owen lowered the hatch as if it were a lid on a box that could talk.

They backed away, breathing the less-bad air. He locked the hatch with the hasp that had never been used for anything but keeping the hatch open. It looked wrong, and that was just how it would be.

“Call,” he said.

“Landline first,” Mara said. “Then cell. If both fail, radio.”

The house line had its own voice, a solid old dial tone that should have been a comfort. She punched in 911 and listened. The ring went to static, then to a woman whose voice sounded like it had spent the night in a metal box.

“Emergency services. What’s your location?”

Mara gave the address, the house name, the fact of the island road. “We have a confirmed body in a cistern, kitchen level. Confined space. Hatch closed, room vented, no one down.”

“Any sign of immediate danger to occupants?” the dispatcher said, and a little pop cracked through the line.

“Smell only,” Mara said. “Gas no. Electrical, we’re on generator. We’ve posted and taped. We’re out of the room.”

“Okay. Medical and fire will be notified. We’re experiencing delays due to road closures and the bridge. Are you able to evacuate the structure?”

“Negative,” Mara said. “Storm conditions, elderly residents, blocked egress. We can isolate the kitchen.”

“Do that,” the dispatcher said. “Do not attempt recovery. If the situation changes, call back immediately. Units will be en route with an uncertain ETA. I’m giving you a case number.”

She repeated it back, wrote it on the note under NO WATER, even though it didn’t belong there. The pen dug a little trench in the paper with the pressure she put on it.

The line hiccuped, and the dispatcher’s voice thinned. “If we lose you, maintain the scene. We have you logged.”

The call ended itself. The phone put up a busy tone like a shrug. Mara set it down and checked her cell. One bar. She typed a text to Rourke with the case number and hit send. It hung, then decided to go. She didn’t watch it like watching would help.

“Fire?” Owen asked.

“Dispatched,” she said. “Uncertain ETA.”

He nodded and didn’t. “Coast Guard might be faster if the bridge is a mess.”

“Try your radio,” she said.

He went to the mudroom and took down the orange handheld VHF that looked like a child’s buoy. He thumbed it on. The little screen woke up. He went to 16 and held the button. “Harbor Master, Harbor Master, this is High Ledge caretaker on sixteen, over.”

Silence full of weather.

He tried again. “Harbor Master or any vessel able to relay to county fire, request welfare check and scene hold at High Ledge main house, possible DOA in cistern, over.”

A different voice came, thinner, probably a liveaboard on a mooring. “High Ledge, heard you. Harbor Master boat is tied and manned but they’re saying surge over the dock. County has it on their board. Advise shelter in place. Over.”

“Copy,” Owen said, and added, because normal talk helped, “Thanks.”

The radio spit white noise like rain; somewhere on the channel, men discussed a line parting. He thumbed off and set the radio back, careful as if it were breakable.

“We’ve done what we can do,” Mara said.

He rubbed his forearm; the glove made a sound like an eraser on a page. “We should shut the basement valve to isolate the kitchen line, in case someone forgets the note. I don’t trust notes.”

“We put tape across the hall,” she said.

“And the storm will take the tape’s glue like it takes everything,” he said. Then, apologetically, “I’ll get the tape anyway.”

They sealed the kitchen door with a strip of caution tape from a roll he kept for fallen branches and found that the tape didn’t want to stick to old paint. He pressed his palm to it until the heat of his hand argued with the cold of the wall and got it to hold.

“Do we tell anyone else in the house?” he asked.

“Rourke will,” she said. “We keep the list of mouths small.”

He accepted that like a posted rule. He cleaned nothing. He straightened nothing. It was a particular discipline, to let a room be wrong.

The generator hiccuped and caught again. The lights flickered and then steadied into a color that made the walls the shade of old teeth. Outside, the gull had given up on commentary; the wind took over. Somewhere, a pot rolled an inch in a cupboard, found a stop.

Owen looked at the hatch door like it could warn him of something if he stared long enough. “I’m going downstairs,” he said. “Main shutoff.”

“Don’t touch the cistern valves,” Mara said. “Just the kitchen feed.”

“I won’t,” he said, and the honest thing about him was that he meant it.

She waited in the hall with the note and the tape and the memory of water moving around a human shape. Her phone buzzed—a delayed text from Rourke: On route. County coordinating. Use no water. Keep people out. ETA depends on bridge.

She replied: Confirmed wedged. Hatch sealed. Kitchen isolated. Case number attached.

She listened to the house breathe and not breathe. Owen came back up with the damp on him that basements give, a smell of cold iron and old salt.

“It’s off,” he said. He looked like a man who wanted to wash his hands and remembered what he’d written on the refrigerator. He held his hands out from his body like it might help.

“There’s bottled,” she said, and then realized how that sounded, and they both let the sentence sit down and be quiet.

They moved the chair tighter over the tape, added a second note on the hall door at face level. The second note was in the same dark block letters and it made the first one feel like a system.

“Sit?” Owen said.

“Just not in there,” Mara said.

They took the two least offensive stools in the prep nook and sat like they’d forgotten how, knees touching anything that wasn’t the hatch. It felt like a crime not to do more; it felt like a crime to do anything at all. The two facts stood next to each other and refused to fight.

The wind leaned hard on the door and the tape ticked once against paint and held. The phone didn’t ring. The house made no promises. The cold kept coming in its particular way, indifferent and clean. Outside, the morning went on, and inside, under the floor, weight pressed against stone and stayed.


==================== SCENE 37 ====================
They stood for a minute in the wrong kind of quiet. Then Mara pulled herself up like the stool was trying to keep her and let it fail.

“Okay,” she said to the room, to the chair over the hatch, to the notes in her block letters. “Perimeter.”

She found the blue roll in her kit, the one she trusted to come off paint without taking the house with it. The first strip ran shoulder to shoulder across the kitchen doorframe at head height: DO NOT ENTER—SCENE HOLD. She wrote the time, the case number, and her initials. The ink bled a little on the damp paper, but it was legible. She added a second strip lower, set like a belt. Kitchens invite freestyling; two lines made it look less like an invitation.

Her phone vibrated in her pocket with that little bug-buzz that always made bad news sound like a small insect; she answered without looking. “Mara.”

“Rourke,” the voice said, wind-muffled, engine under it. “Status.”

“Hatch sealed. Kitchen isolated. Cistern feed off at the kitchen. No one in or out. I’ve got notes up, tape up.” She watched her hand keep writing while she spoke, the muscle memory of caps and slashes. “We’ve got visible wet transfer on tile around the hatch. I am not crossing it until you bring suits and booties.”

“HVAC?”

“Kitchen fan off. Window closed. Generator’s feeding overheads—if you want lights out, say it now.”

“Keep lights. No water. No drains. No disposal. County’s patching me to the ME. I don’t care how thirsty anybody is.”

“Copy.” She stuck another note to the refrigerator at eye level because people read refrigerators. Then she wrote it a second time at child-height because people forget to look up. Use no water. Kitchen hold.

“Gear?” Rourke asked.

“Inadequate,” she said. “I’ve got nitrile gloves and two antiquated paper masks. No Tyvek. No covers. No goggles that haven’t been in a glove box since the Obama years.”

He grunted. “Then we do a museum rope, not a garage tarp. Line of sight only.”

“My plan.” She looked at the hatch, at the chair over it like a gentleman at a puddle. A wet print had ghosted out from under the hinge side—someone else’s tread, not the lug on her boots, not Owen’s. The grid of the tile picked it up and held it like a cheap stamp. Her fingers twitched for the camera, then settled. Not yet. “I can mark a dry side. Nobody crosses. Not even me.”

“Good. I don’t want your boot marks joining the chorus. I want the chorus we have.” A pause. The engine noise got brighter. “Text me a perimeter shot. If you can get anything with scale from outside the line, do it.”

She ended the call and did the thing her hands had been begging to do. The wide lens. The ISO punched up to compensate for the old-teeth light. She stayed on the dry side and shot obliques of the hatch corner, the chair legs, the tape edges, the scuffed grout. She dropped a metal ruler just inside the dry zone and bumped it with a wooden spoon until it sat half a tile from the nearest wet trace. The spoon was sacrificial; the ruler could be wiped.

She narrated under her breath as she worked, not for the record so much as to keep her mouth busy. “Entry one, kitchen threshold—north door. Initial seal note. Time stamps. Entry two, hatch area, obstruction with chair. Evidence of wet transfer. No contact beyond visual.”

Owen hovered at the hall mouth and didn’t come in. He had learned the little bit you can teach someone in twenty minutes. “Can I get towels?” he asked, guilty in advance.

“No towels,” she said without looking up. “Nothing absorbent. Nothing that turns evidence into opinion.” She pulled a box of contractor garbage bags from the island drawer with her clean hand and set them like a black accordion across the tiles to mark a second boundary. Plastic sagged and sighed, a cheap little moat. “If you have painter’s pyramids, bring them to the hall. We’ll keep our boots on them.”

“I have two,” he said, and then, “I have four if one is missing a corner.”

“Four is plenty.” She tore off more tape. “And breathe through your nose. It keeps you from volunteering to do something dumb.”

He snorted and disappeared again, the reasonable sound of a person who knew some part of this was theater with a purpose and some part of it was worship.

She made herself inventory out loud as if Rourke could hear through walls. “Visible hose coupling on the hatch edge. Scuffing on wood frame—fresh, no dust settled. One glove print on latch plate, right hand, probably modern. Segregated wet from dry by three tiles and plastic barrier. No bagging. No touching.”

She gingerly rolled the island stools to the edge of the plastic and made them into pylons, tape ribboned between their legs. She wrote KITCHEN HOLD across the tape itself in block letters, not necessary, but she had learned that some people respected words more than physics.

Her phone lit with a text. Rourke: ETA forty. ME fifty. Deputy thirty. Bridge cleared one lane.

She texted back a top-down shot of the room, her scribbled little map annotated with arrows. She added: No fans/no drains/no ice machine. Witnesses kept out. She hovered, then wrote: We need PPE on arrival. No casualties here beyond the obvious. She deleted that last line—stupid phrasing—and sent the rest.

The generator hiccuped again and she cut her eyes to the ceiling. The bulb over the sink was the wrong yellow, the wrong warmth. She walked to the fuse cabinet, rested her fingertips on the metal without opening it. “You keep breathing in a safe way,” she told the panel like it had a say.

Owen came back with the pyramids nested like weird orange candy and set them on the hall runner. He balanced his boots on two and held out the other pair to her, arms stiff like he didn’t want his sleeves to graze the air. “Do you want me out entirely?”

“Out of the kitchen,” she said. “Stay where you can see. Block anyone who thinks a refrigerator is a right, not a privilege.”

“Copy,” he said, and didn’t even try to make it cute. He put his hands in his jacket pockets to keep them from reaching for the kitchen by habit.

She clicked her camera again and again, shallow angle to grab the sheen on the tile that told you the shape of damp. She took an establishing shot of the kitchen sink, control knobs turned off, a note taped right on the handle anyway. She photographed the drain for no reason other than that it existed and she wanted no gaps in the record a bored lawyer could step through.

Her glove snagged on the roughness of the hatch frame where somebody had worried it with a wrench or the impatient edge of a shoe. She held still, pulled back slowly so she didn’t drop a thread, and examined the glove—no tear. She made herself breathe.

The house did that inward sigh old houses have. A pause, then a crack as wood flexed back to its idea of straight. Something small skittered in a drawer and stopped. From the hall, Owen made the sound of a man remembering coffee and letting the thought go like a balloon. He said, “I’m telling Cass to keep people out of the service corridor. She’s good at theater.”

“Tell her no film on the kitchen,” Mara said, and let her voice carry. “No stills. No doorway shots. Nothing through windows. I don’t want an accidental zoom over someone’s breakfast turning into a tour.”

“No film,” he called back.

“And if Julian tries the legal voice, the answer is no and also no. He can call Rourke if he wants the long version.”

A beat. “Understood.”

She took her pen and wrote a third time on the door: If you need something from this room, you need permission. Then she underlined need. Above it, she wrote: Hands off. She underlined that, too, and drew a small box around it as if it mattered.

The cold was the kind that cohabits with fatigue; it didn’t bite so much as lean. She rotated her shoulders and shook out her wrist. The urge to lift the chair and get one more look was a physical itch. She looked anywhere but at the hatch and found evidence in every ordinary thing, the way your mind does when it knows it’s not allowed to touch: the chip in the cutting board, the rust arc on a dish drainer foot, one strand of someone’s hair snagged on the low corner of a cabinet. She let them be junk. The area was a room; not every room was a crime. This one was a crime scene and a kitchen both and she had to let it be both for now.

Her phone rang again and she picked up on the first chime. “Mara.”

“ME,” a new voice said, clear and uninterested in pleasantries. “Rourke’s looping me. Decomp?”

“None apparent in air. Body underwater. Cistern closed since at least six, seven hours—could be longer—but I’m not opening it to get you a better assessment. We’ve got active wet transfer on tile. I’m maintaining a photolog from dry side only. No bagging.”

“Good. No bleach stink?”

“No cleaning agents. No fresh mop stripes. No damp beyond the immediate hatch footprint and the shoe transfer. Water feed is off this room only. I haven’t touched utility beyond that.”

“Clothing on the decedent?”

“Unknown from here,” she said, and felt the lack like a stone in her shoe. “I can see an arm. Color looks like cold water. That’s it.”

“Do not go fishing,” the ME said, already bored by the stupidity of other people. “Tape it and sit on your hands. Keep the house calm.”

“It’s as calm as this house gets,” she said. “See you soon.” She hung up before the part where they said their names like they hadn’t already.

She put her gloves flat on the island and looked at them. Two gloves. Two hands. A roll of tape. A camera, old and reliable. A ruler. A spoon with a job it hadn’t expected. It felt like trying to win a flood with a pocket.

Owen drifted back into view and stayed in the doorway. “They’ll bring the suits,” he said, and then caught himself and adjusted. “They’re bringing the suits.”

“They are,” she said. “And we’ll not make a mess they have to solve.” She put a second strip of tape at floor level across the threshold like a small note to shoes: don’t be stupid.

Something bright and careless banged against the back door and then skittered—wind, loose something on the porch—then the sudden knock of it finding a rail. They both looked that way and then looked carefully away and did nothing.

Mara took one last circuit of the room without crossing her own lines. She checked the window latch. She read the notes she’d written as if a stranger had, checking for clarity. She lifted the corner of the plastic barrier and peered at the seam where dry met wet, then set it back down and smoothed it like a bandage.

She took two steps back from her own work and photographed the entire thing again from the hall, a wide angle that made the room look like a model in bad weather. The yellow light threw everyone’s skin off. The chair over the hatch cast a clean-edged shadow. The tape lines read as bright declarations in a place that had never been keen on rules.

“Nothing in, nothing out,” she said, mostly to herself, and capped her pen. “Hold holds.”


==================== SCENE 38 ====================

The kitchen wore the kind of hush that only happens when two people are trying not to breathe on something. Wind shouldered the back door again, rattled it, then eased off as if embarrassed. The yellow overhead swung a fraction. The chair over the hatch threw its square shadow across tape and linoleum like a cheap sundial.

Mara pulled the camera strap over her head, thumbed the power, watched the tiny battery icon do its little lie. “We do this once,” she said. “No re-staging, no touching, no ‘one more angle.’ If it isn’t worth a shot, it isn’t happening.”

Owen nodded and lifted his hands away from his jacket pockets like a man performing a magic trick involving nothing. “We’ve got… what? Fourteen minutes before the parade?” He meant Rourke, and the county, and the zippered suits.

“Nineteen on the ME. Rourke sooner.” She slid her gray card out of the bag and held it up to the jaundiced light. The color in the room had a fever. She made herself trust the camera. “ISO eight hundred. F-four. Don’t let me goose it.”

He breathed assent as if that were some authority he had. The wind found the porch loose thing again; the clack and skitter sounded like an impatient dog. He glanced that way, didn’t move.

Mara crouched at the threshold and tucked a folded dishtowel under her knee to keep from tracking dirt onto her own tape. The rust hazard of the cistern breathed up at her: iron and algae and the faint metallic nickel of blood that might have been imagined or historical. She angled the lens down and waited for her hands to quiet. The camera blinked a little red number; she told it yes.

“Two-three-three: kitchen overall, hatch barricade, line placement.” She said it low, like reading a grocery list to a god who misheard. Owen held out the time without her asking.

“Nine forty-one,” he said. He set his watch on the counter like it might be dangerous nearer the line.

She worked the angles fast and clean, tilting to avoid the hot flare of the bulb. The plastic sheeting she’d taped over the hatch corner tarped a little under the warm air’s insistence. She shot the transition line where the water had kissed up onto the tile, discolored it, then decided not to be precious: she eased the spoon under the plastic edge and lifted a finger’s worth, just enough to kill the glare.

“Two-three-eight: debris tide mark east tile, scale.” She slid the plastic ruler up to the threshold, squared to the grout. “Half-inch organic band, seed husks, green scum, silt. Take me a memory: you see a straw?”

“Grass, not reed,” Owen said automatically, peering without leaning. He kept his hands linked behind his back. “And pine needles. Cistern’s got the gutter feed, so—”

“Don’t—” She stopped herself from saying “think.” He wasn’t doing that to the evidence; he was trying not to.

She took tight shots of the line: the small crescent of oiled pollen like a yolk ringed on a plate, the fuzzy mat of algae hairline on the concrete lip inside the hatch, the single dark grit of something heavier that had settled out and stuck. She got her scale in frame, then pulled it back, then got the overall again.

“I’m going to need lift,” she said. “But no lift.” She stared at the geometry: hatch, chair, distance, body half in view. The arm she could see was draped against the curve, pale in that silver-blue way that makes eyes want to say gray.

Owen stepped to the pantry and came back with the big aluminum baking sheet without being asked, and held it up at chest level, an angled mirror for a sun they didn’t have. The overhead bounced off the dented surface and slid into the cistern, opened shadows. The glove on Mara’s shutter finger squeaked against the body of the camera.

“There you go,” he murmured. His wrists trembled a little with the hold; he adjusted, set his elbows on his ribs like a brace.

“Good. Hold. Hold.” She leaned into the viewfinder. The body’s forearm floated just under the surface, the skin beyond waxed with maceration: washerwoman wrinkles barbering the pads of the fingertips, the nail beds bleached. But where the arm bent against the concrete lip, out of water, the skin lay slack in little tents, gravity’s fingers pinching.

“Two-four-six: right forearm, immersion changes, skin slack at antecubital, no manipulation.” Her voice stayed level. She could feel Owen listening for anything in it that would say something was wrong and resisted the impulse to give him that handhold. “Two-four-seven: palm, maceration, nail bed detail.”

“Time,” Owen said. “Nine forty-four.”

“Say it again when I forget.” She eased the lens deeper into the hatch’s mouth, careful to keep her shoulder behind her own tape line. She took a breath through her mouth, not because of the smell, but because that made it uniform.

The lividity showed in curious ways. The far shoulder and the side of the face she could see had that dull wine staining where the body’s small rivers had settled. It pooled along the low side, not the shoulder that should have been most dependent if the body had taken on water in a standard drown. She wasn’t deciding; she was framing.

“Two-five-three: posterior face and shoulder, mottled lividity, dependent plane,” she said. “Angle one. Two-five-four: angle two with scale.”

She laid the ruler across the concrete lip, carefully cantilevered so the markings floated in frame without touching flesh. The numbers were backwards from her angle but readable later. She took the shot.

“Your light,” she said. Owen tilted the baking sheet; the silver flood slid deeper, lit the shadow under the jaw, brought up the purpling as something that wasn’t just the room’s bad idea of color.

Owen swallowed. “Nine forty-six.”

She thanked him without saying it.

“Skin turgor,” she said, half to the camera, half to the record. “No pinch performed. Visual only. Gravity tenting present at upper arm.” She made the shot; the camera chirped, pleased to be useful.

He shifted his stance and the baking sheet scraped against the doorframe. They both stopped breathing. Nothing moved below besides the slow lap of gray.

“Okay,” she said. “Now the hose.”

Owen didn’t move. “I didn’t— It’s just— We had run it down last week to drain off the overflow. It’s—” He ran out of words rather than breath.

“It’s there,” she said, neutral. She angled left and got it: the lay-flat hose, green with age grime, looped in arcs by the sink, one end disappearing into shadow, the brass coupling gleaming dumbly.

“Two-six-one: pump hose path, kitchen floor to hatch,” she recorded. “Two-six-two: coupling detail, manufacturer stamp, tool marks.” She knelt to the coupling and swallowed. The light took the serial numbers clean: DIXON 3/4, the teeth marks of a wrench chewed around the flats.

“Time.”

“Nine forty-nine.”

“Hard shadow. Tilt.” He tilted the baking sheet again, and the brass stopped flaring, went readable. She got the shot of the hose’s exterior too—an odd rough-scuffed band where someone had cinched it, or where it had worn against a lip. She zoomed in on the interior end where it vanished toward the hatch and took a last overall to stitch it later into a path.

Outside, the wind chose the house again, dragging a palm of air along the clapboards. Owen flexed his fingers and the sheet flashed a single bright bar across the room like a fencing foil. He shifted it down.

Mara glanced once at the clock she’d taped to her notes and then back. The camera’s buffer lagged a half second, a small stutter that felt like vertigo. She didn’t curse because cursing was for choices and she had none.

“Lividity mid-thorax?” she asked, acquisitive but not hopeful.

Owen squinted into the hatch with him still holding the sheet at that awkward saint’s-painting angle. “From here I can see right flank. Pooled. The other side looks pale. There’s a snag on the shirt—” He stopped himself. “There’s… cloth.”

She changed her posture and shot it blind, trusting her frame: the lips of the cistern, the right flank darkened; a shred of fabric caught on the concrete pock, an edge like seaweed. She didn’t reach for it. She didn’t want to know if it would come free.

“Two-seven-one: flank lividity, context.” She added, quiet: “Clothing present. Type unknown.”

She dragged the spoon along the plastic again to flatten a wrinkle that had crept under pressure differential, exhaled, took the debris line once more. The tide mark had a thin skin on it already, a slick re-forming as if trying to hide the tell.

“Owen, also the feed. Get me the shutoff. In frame, then out.” She meant the valve he’d closed earlier for the kitchen, visible under the sink like a backed-down menace. He stepped closer than he liked to the tape and bent, careful not to tighten his shoulders as if that would make him smaller. She took the shot of the handle angle, off.

“Two-seven-eight: kitchen feed shut, handle position, tag.” The tag dangled, an old service sticker with the ink washed down to a ghost of red. She shot it anyway.

“Time.”

“Nine fifty-two.”

She went back to the body, knowing she had three more she needed and two she wanted, and there was the conflict: the tiny acquisition of more versus the blunt arrival of other people. She took the last necessary: a wide overall from the hall, showing the hose, the tape, the chair, the plastic, the line, Owen with his ridiculous silvered pan like some minor saint of light. Then the indulgences she could justify later: macro of the debris’ mixed population—pine, seed husk, insect wing—macro of the skin wrinkling at the knuckles, macro of the wrench bite on the coupling.

“Two-eight-nine through three-zero-one,” she said, fast enough that the numbers ran like water.

Owen’s voice, satisfied to be a tool, said, “Nine fifty-five.”

They both listened. The house answered with its own lungs: wind, the arterial rush in the walls where higher floors still had water to give up, the low throat of the storm further out. No approaching voices yet.

Mara backed up inch by inch from the threshold, unhooked the spoon, smoothed the plastic back down so it lay like saran over a bowl, re-taped one edge where the adhesive had given up because of damp. She’d brought extra tape. She used it.

“Look at me,” she said. He did, blinking against the shift from looking down. “I’m going to check focus. If something’s soft, we do it again right now. If it’s all in, we’re done.”

“Done is a word,” he said, but he meant yes.

She spun the camera’s wheel and ran through: sharp at the edge of the debris line, readable numbers on the ruler, the lividity’s marbled border crisp, the hose coupling letters unambiguous. The skin slack at the upper arm was a small, obscene tent. She felt the old place in her chest where satisfaction used to be. It thumped its tail.

“Key shots are good,” she said simply. “We stop.”

She reached for the tape, pressed it down along the threshold one last time, the sound of adhesive finding purchase. She capped the lens. She wrote on the top page of her pad with her neat block hand and put the time and the sequence range and an arrow to remind herself: upload first, double-copy, tag.

Owen put the baking sheet back on the hook like he was putting an animal back in its pen, carefully, without offering fingers. He blew out a breath he must have started holding halfway through.

“Do I stand here,” he asked, “or do I go fetch Rourke before he starts howling on the radio?”

“Stand here for ten seconds,” she said, and let the ten seconds be quiet. Then she nodded. “Now fetch him.”

He went. She straightened the chair over the hatch that she’d nudged a quarter inch with her knee and checked the tape lines again to be sure the prints belonged to this moment and no other. She put the spoon next to the ruler like they were two people who had done a good job and deserved to sit.

The wind banged at the back door again, found the rail, rattled it, let it go. The light swung and settled. The kitchen, for a heartbeat, was a lab, everything contained. Then footsteps came up the porch, hard and official, and Mara laid her palm flat on her notebook and didn’t move.


==================== SCENE 39 ====================
The pump shed was a tin lung rasping in the wind. The padlock had salt welded into its seams; Owen lifted it with the familiarity of someone who’d learned the angle years ago and banged it once with the heel of his hand. It popped. He pushed the door and the smell of old diesel and wet canvas rolled out, clean in its own way—honest filth.

“Watch your head,” he said out of reflex, then immediately, “You know that,” as if this were some version of hospitality.

Mara ducked anyway. The beam from her flashlight slid over the coils: green suction hose, lay-flat blue discharge, thirty feet of inch-and-a-half black with brass couplings, each hung on nails in educated loops. The shed’s floor was oyster-crunch with gravel. On the bench: hose clamp pliers, a set of spare gaskets in a pill bottle, a plastic Folgers tub of clamps. The small Honda pump crouched under the bench with its side panel off, guts exposed.

“You said lettered coupling,” she said.

“DIXON is the one I’ve got on the black line,” he answered, voice abrading against the noise of the wind hissing under the tin. He reached for the length of hose with a two-fingered care, as if afraid the wrong touch would make something true. The brass coupling at its end was stamped in relief, proud letters around the circumference so they bit into your palm if you carried it wrong: DIXON 1-1/2 NH.

He craned it into the light. “That one. That’s the run I use for the north sump—when it’s not sucking air.”

“How recently?”

“Today if I had juice,” he said, then corrected himself. “Yesterday, when the diesel still behaved.”

He took down the hose, and wet grit fell out of the tail. Not puddle-wet, not fresh, but humid with its own history. Mara bent, put two fingers into the end and felt for the lip—there was a slight burr where somebody had dragged it over stone, and a smear of blue-gray paint worn to thumbprint on one facet of the coupling.

Mara lifted the coupling all the way to her face as if scent could be a form of measurement. There was the pump’s ghost smell, metallic-sweet, and under it the mineral cold that was the cistern’s particular signature, algae and stone and old lime. She set the flashlight bite-up on her shoulder like a carpenter, freeing her hands.

“Hold it steady,” she said. “Like a coin. No rotation.” He did. She photographed the coupling face, the letters, the burr, the paint. Then she lined the edge of her laminated ruler against the raised serif on the X and took another shot with the scale.

“You think I’m going to jail for a connector,” he said, very level.

“I think you own a connector,” she said. “And someone pressed it into a person.”

He inhaled to argue and then just held it there, the inhale, afloat in his throat. He looked down at the hose and then at his own hands on it, like he was amazed they would do that—hold something that might wreck him.

“Take the gasket out,” she said. “Let me look.”

He unscrewed the tail, fingers deft even now. Inside, the rubber gasket had a notch out of it like a small bite; it meant you had to crank harder to stop a leak. He showed her. There was a corresponding crescent polish on the brass where someone had overtightened against the fault.

“Yesterday?” she asked.

“Last week. When I noticed it. I haven’t changed it because—” He broke off. Because other fires were burning. Because diesel. Because a house with a hundred problems. He didn’t say it; he just shook his head.

She photographed the notch, the polish. Then she turned the coupling in his hands without taking it. “Again,” she said, and he rotated, quarter turns, clicks of her shutter like a metronome.

When she was done, he eased it back onto its nail. He didn’t let go. He stood with the weight of the hose in his hands and finally set it down on the bench instead.

“Someone took this,” he said. Not a question.

“Someone had access,” she agreed. “And hands. And time.”

He pushed past her to the milk crate under the bench and dug through spare couplings, tapping metal on metal with too much force, as if noise alone could shake free a different answer. He came up with another coupling, same make, same stamp.

“See? Dixon is standard. That could have—” He stopped. The other coupling had letters crisp, edges clean. The one from his working line had the paint smear at four o’clock, the burr, the gasket notch polish. Individual. Fingerprint-level individual.

He put the extra back and wiped his palms on his jeans as if brass left residue.

“Take me to the kitchen,” she said.

They went.

The wind hit them sideways out of the lee of the shed, salty needles. The path was a slick of old moss and now tidal mist; Mara put her boots where he put his boots and it made a sound like trying not to apologize. Owen locked the shed without being asked and put the tiny key back on the ring clipped to his belt.

Inside the mudroom, the house’s damp folded around them again, warmer than the night but dirtier. The kitchen light swung a little on its chain. The tape lines she had laid over the hatch gleamed; the spoon and ruler sat exactly where she’d left them. They were alone, for the moment. Rourke and his sealant were elsewhere, arguing with a radio or a gate.

Through the plastic, the marbling at the shoulder was as she’d left it. The letters in their mirror arc were subdued now that the heat had bled out, but still there—raised impression’s reverse pressed into skin: X, N, a ghost of the O. Owen stood with his fist at his sternum like he’d forgotten to set it down.

Mara set the pump coupling on a towel on the counter. She fitted a clean glove, then another, then drew a fresh sheet of acetate from her kit the size of a magazine and laid it gently over the plastic covering the body. The tape made a polite sound. On the acetate, she marked the visible letters with a fine tip, building a map of their arcs relative to the ruler already in the shot.

Owen watched like a man sitting with a surgeon. He flinched once when the wind hit the back door, then went statue again.

“Hold this,” she said, handing him the ruler to brace against the edge of the hatch, and he took it with two hands like an oath. She brought the coupling close without touching the plastic, orienting the stamp so the lettering’s radius matched the seen curve on skin. At four o’clock, the paint smear fell where the skin showed a slight scrape in the lividity’s pale—abrasion on a dead person reads differently, no bleed, just roughness. The burr’s small tooth aligned with a bite mark in the pressure bruise.

She didn’t say anything. She stopped her breath. She let her hand float in that shallow, careful space and then leaned back to look through the camera again at the juxtaposition: brass over body separated by barrier, letters to letters, curve to curve.

Owen exhaled like the hose had been wound around his lungs and she’d cut it.

“Say it,” he said.

“It’s the same coupling,” she said. “Same make is not enough. This is this. Paint, burr, notch polish. Letter spacing. Depth of impression matches this relief.”

He nodded once, and then again like his neck needed to practice the motion. He stared at the coupling like he could unsay having owned it.

“I didn’t—”

“I know,” she said. Not mercy, not absolution; just a fact put on the counter with the brass. “You don’t need to say that to me.”

“I still—” He closed his eyes and opened them. “I still need to say it.”

“You can say it to Rourke,” she said, and his mouth went hard but he nodded.

She took the working photos—wide, medium, detail—then stepped back and made herself take the boring ones that always mattered later: coupling in situ on towel with evidence tag; tag face with her block letters and time; Owen’s hands holding the ruler, because hands are part of scenes.

“Could be somebody borrowed it when I was sleeping,” he said, words careful. “Could be somebody who knows where my key hangs. Could be they know I do rounds at three and five.”

“Could be,” she said. “What I can prove: this object and that bruise are dance partners. There isn’t a way that skin does this from falling. It needs pressure with letters. It needs time.”

His cheeks went cold white under the kitchen’s yellow. He was shivering and not from wind.

“She was in the cistern,” he said. “She drowned there.”

“She’s waterlogged,” Mara said. “Her lungs weigh what they weigh. But the marks say handling. The hose says handling. The lividity says she spent time somewhere not the water before she went into it. Or after. The order of operations is in here.” She tapped her notebook with the corner of the pen, small and unromantic. “Not in your character. In here.”

“Don’t do that,” he said softly. “Don’t give me a sermon about my character and call it paperwork.”

“I’m telling you where the fight is,” she said, equally soft. “Not in your chest. In evidence.”

He breathed once, twice. The wind licked the back door and let it be. He nodded.

“Rourke’s going to ask why my pump hose shows up on her,” he said. “I’m going to say I don’t know.” He looked at the coupling, then up at her. “And that’s true.”

She put the coupling into a bag, expelling air with two flat palms, and sealed it. She wrote the date and time and her initials across the seam and then handed the marker to him. “Witness.”

He wrote O. L., blocky, like he was twelve and careful for the teacher. The letters looked strange next to hers, two peoples’ names linked by a piece of plastic and glue.

“When we opened the hatch,” he said, “when you told me to breathe… I thought I was going to be sick from what we saw. I thought that was the worst of it.” He smiled without humor. “Turns out the worst is this. This part where it’s math.”

“That’s the better part,” she said, because it was true. “The part you can’t talk your way out of is also the part that can’t be talked away from you.”

He rubbed his forearm, left line of his mouth hitching like a stitch pulled too tight. “You’re a comfort.”

“It’s not my job,” she said. Then, after a second, because he had carried that hose and would carry it again in other people’s mouths, “But I meant it.”

They stood in the swing of the light, two people and a sealed bag. Through the plastic over the hatch, the letters on the skin were still legible. Through the bag, the letters on the coupling were clear. Between them there was a tiny distance measured in cellophane and air and the fact that someone had thought to press metal to flesh and hold it long enough to make a print. The kitchen felt colder without needing to be.

From the hall, a scuff of boot, the clack of something heavy against a doorframe. Owen straightened, squared his shoulders. Mara lifted the bag into the circle of the light and let it hang there for a heartbeat so the brass winked and stopped being anything but what it was.

“Staged,” he said, finally making the word out loud.

She nodded. “Staged.”

He drew a breath that had no weight on it anymore—not from guilt, not yet—but a new kind, the weight of being attached by a hose to a dead person whether he liked it or not. He looked at the tape on the floor, the ruler, the spoon, the plastic, the bag, the notebook; he looked at her.

“Let’s go tell him,” he said.

“Bring the hose,” she said. “We don’t leave our part of the story unattended.”

He picked up the evidence bag like it could bruise, like if he took his eyes off it the letters might decide to mean something else. They waited for one more beat, letting the hum of the house go through them. Then they stepped back from the hatch, and Mara smoothed the edge of the plastic with a single satisfied press of her thumb.


==================== SCENE 40 ====================
Rourke filled the doorway like the house had been expecting him and could finally relax its shoulders. His slicker still dripped at the hem, leaving commas of water that the tile swallowed without argument. Two deputies ghosted at his back, hands already at belts, eyes doing the sweep they were trained to do: window latches, door frames, the bright square of the hatch plastic glowing under the work light.

“What am I looking at,” Rourke said, not as a question.

“A staged drowning,” Mara said. “We have lividity consistent with supine position, then immersion. We have transfer from a brass coupling—Owen’s pump hose—on the decedent’s abdomen.” She held the bag so the stamped letters faced him. “This coupling.”

Rourke’s gaze flicked from her hand to Owen’s face and back. “Mr. Pike?”

“It’s my hose,” Owen said, steadying the bag from underneath with two fingers like any movement might smear the truth. “From the pump shed. I’d know the nick in the ‘4’ anywhere. People borrow it sometimes, but—”

“Stop,” Rourke said. He stepped closer to the plastic-sealed hatch, leaned in, didn’t touch. The dead light of the overhead bulb made the water under the sheet look like hammered tin. Through the milky layer of plastic on skin, the shadowy letters were still there if you knew to read them. He straightened. “Sergeant,” he said over his shoulder, and one of the deputies stepped forward. “This kitchen is now a controlled scene. Full seal. Document ingress and egress starting now. No one in or out without my say.”

“Understood,” the deputy said, already pulling tape and an evidence log from her satchel.

“You’re early,” Mara said. “Usually we have to bribe the house to give people up.”

He ignored that. “What have you moved?”

“This plastic over the hatch. The hose coupling we bagged. The pump hose is there.” She nodded to where the length of gray lay coiled, wet and guilty, on the floor. “Documentation is on this card,” she added, tapping her camera, which hung at her sternum like a badge.

“Hand it over,” Rourke said.

“I’ll mirror it to you,” she said.

He let out a breath that might have been a laugh if there were any humor in it. “Counselors love you until they don’t. Card.”

“Chain of my custody,” she said. “You’ll get an image set with hashes. You can have the physical card when I’m finished writing metadata.”

The deputy with the tape paused, clocked the back-and-forth, glanced at Rourke. He held up a palm to her without looking.

“Mara,” he said, name on its own, heavy.

“Rourke,” she said back. “You want this solved or docketed?”

“I want this not to be thrown out on a technicality when one of Julian’s cousins decides to make a project of me. That means custody. It means you don’t take a souvenir because it makes you feel nimble.”

“I like nimble,” she said. “I like redundant more.”

Owen shifted, a scuff of rubber on tile. “She’s not wrong about the uploads,” he said, like someone offering a towel into a fight. “Power’s spotty. If the generator trips again—”

“Mr. Pike,” Rourke said without raising his voice, and somehow the room got quieter.

Mara set her camera on the stainless prep table, scrolled through three frames to show him the sequence: the coupling against the skin; the lividity pooling in the back, tide lines edging up the flanks; the waterline drag over the chest. No commentary. Just light and sight.

“You’re going to want redundancy on these whether you like me or not,” she said. “We lose them, we lose the ability to say the word ‘staged’ without sounding like we feel it instead of know it.”

He looked, not touching the body of the camera, not letting his gaze move beyond the images he had to absorb. The muscle in his jaw worked. “Where’s your card wallet,” he said.

“In my pocket,” she said, and didn’t move.

“Deputy Quinlan,” he said, finally looking back to the sergeant. “Seal the exterior door. Bag the hose. Photograph all tools within ten feet of that hatch. Note position, orientation, any liquid trails. Lieutenant Ames, get me desiccant, tamper bags, and a formal receipt. We’ll inventory every camera and every phone in this room when we’re done.”

“That’s overbroad,” Mara said. “You want me, fine. You want Owen’s personal phone when he’s on hurricane duty and your radios fail? Ask the storm what it thinks.”

“Ma’am,” Quinlan said carefully, “we’ll log it, issue a temporary.”

“Quinlan can log my temper while she’s at it,” Mara said, but she was already thumbing a menu open, tapping through settings one-handed. “Owen, hotspot.”

“The repeater’s up,” Owen said, digging his device from the chest pocket of his jacket and flipping the cover. The signal light was a stubborn green. “Network’s local only. The uplink comes and goes.”

“Enough,” Rourke said.

“Enough to get thumbnails to an offsite,” Mara said, pairing, setting her camera to push. She watched the status wheel crawl. “You’ll have the primary card,” she added, eyes on the little growing progress bar. “And I won’t be the only human being who’s seen these.”

The kitchen sucked in the humidity from the hall and let it out again around their ankles. Quinlan’s tape gave a sticky rip and then another, running a bright neon line around the door that meant this exit was now a story in a ledger. Owen held the hotspot out between his fingers like a fish he wanted to keep from flopping onto the floor. On the table, Mara’s camera blinked a tiny truth-telling light: sending, sending.

Rourke stepped closer to Owen. “Your hose,” he said, pointing. “Last time it was used?”

“Yesterday,” Owen said. “We were moving graywater so the sump didn’t overflow into the basement pantry. I hung it back on the rack in the pump shed. The rack’s inside; you’d have to go in the shed to get it. It’s not hung by the door for a reason.”

“So someone entered your shed,” Rourke said. “Did you lock it.”

“Yes,” Owen said, then, measured: “You can check the hasp. It’s an old lock. You can pop it with a screwdriver if you’re determined.”

“We will,” Rourke said.

The camera chimed. Mara didn’t look up; she slotted the card tray with a practiced push, palmed out the SD card, and tucked it into a transparent evidence sleeve she pulled from her pack. She sharpied the time, date, location, her initials, and slid the sleeve across the metal to Rourke.

“Primary,” she said. “Twenty-two RAW images. One video clip of the approach. Hashes posted to shared folder, link in your inbox. Secondary card stays with me with redundant copy until lab confirms receipt.”

He took the sleeve with two fingers like it might bite. “You’re very interested in staying useful,” he said.

“I’m very interested in not having to apologize to a corpse later,” she said.

Quinlan came back with the pre-printed seals, the little red tabs that make people think twice about breaking things. “Sir?”

“Do it,” Rourke said. “Hatch gets a double. Log the numbers.” He looked at Mara again. “No one goes under that plastic. No one stands over it except my people. Your footprint in this room is done.”

“My footprint includes my own hands,” she said. “I’ll stand where I need to stand to hold Owen together while you pull his life apart.”

“I don’t require you to comfort my witnesses,” he said. “And I don’t appreciate you positioning yourself as the moral hinge.”

“Then don’t lean on me,” she said, level.

Owen cleared his throat, which was suddenly a raw sound. “I’m right here,” he said. “You can both talk to me like I’ve got a face on.”

Rourke nodded once. “Mr. Pike. I’m taking your statement after we finish the seal. If I learn you interfered with this scene beyond what Ms. Leland has described, I’ll treat it like interfering. That includes the pump shed. That includes any caches of tools, any keys to said caches.”

“Nothing’s cached,” Owen said. “It’s a maintenance property. Tools have racks. Keys are in my ledger.”

“And your ledger is where,” Rourke said.

“In my office,” Owen said. “Bottom drawer, right side, taped to the underside of the insert.”

Quinlan hid a smile under professionalism and added it to her notes.

Rourke turned back to Mara. “You mentioned lividity,” he said.

“Posterior,” she said. “Pooling in the back, sacral area, posterior arms. Minimal blanching. Time window matches the interval between when the generator went out and when we came in. There’s drag on the sternum: waterline abrasion from postmortem immersion. There’s no consistent wash on the coupling mark, which means it was pressed while dry, before the dunk.”

“You have a script in your head to make me sign in the corner,” he said.

“I have a body in your hatch,” she said. “My words are a crutch for me, not a trap for you.”

Quinlan laid the red seal across the plastic over the hatch, smoothed it, wrote the log number in blocky letters like a child making sure the paper knew it was paper. The room seemed to inhale at the moment the adhesive grabbed.

“We’re sealed on the hatch,” she said. “Exterior door is sealed. Window is photographed and latched. Inventory to ten feet is halfway.”

“Finish it,” Rourke said.

Mara pulled her pack over her shoulder and slipped her camera strap fully around her neck. She pressed the edge of one thumb against the table’s brushed metal to leave a soft crescent she could feel later and know where she’d stood.

“You’ll want the hose path,” she said. “Drips leading to the pantry door, then back. It’s messy, but there’s a pattern: lifted coil, carried waist-high, set down, moved again. Whoever did it didn’t want the drag marks.”

Rourke’s stare was as flat as the stainless behind her. “You finished telling me how to do my job.”

“I like to believe you have enough jobs for both of us,” she said. “I’ll be in the hall when you want my formal.”

“You’ll be where I can see you,” he said.

“Then you’re watching,” she said simply. She turned toward Owen. “Do you need water,” she asked.

He shook his head. “I need to not think about letters that happen to be the same as the ones on my gear,” he said. “But that’s not on offer.”

“It’s not your fault a stamp punches ink both ways,” she said, and he huffed something like a laugh despite himself.

Rourke gestured to the deputy not actively sealing, a tall man with a softer face than his crew cut wanted to suggest. “Ames, with Mr. Pike. Don’t let him touch surfaces on your inventory logs.”

“I’m not going to smear your prints,” Owen said, insulted enough that the color returned to his face.

“I know you’re not,” Rourke said. “I’m not letting you.”

Mara’s phone buzzed. She slid her thumb across the screen. Upload confirmed, it read in that glassy, neutral font that didn’t know it had just helped put bones on a story.

She met Rourke’s eyes. “You’ve got the link,” she said.

He nodded, tucking the SD evidence sleeve into an inner pocket like a magician. “And your card.”

“When you receipt me,” she said.

He lifted his chin at Quinlan, who already had the form ready, a carbon pad with tear-out duplicates. Mara took the pen, signed in the box that said Surrendering Party, a phrase she ignored, and took her copy.

Outside the taped door, the hall sounded like itself again: wind flexing the old wood in the walls, a distant thunk from the generator deciding to keep doing its job. Within the tape, the kitchen held its breath under the red seals like a patient under a clean dressing.

“Ms. Leland,” Rourke said, before she stepped over the line, his voice a hair softer but not relinquishing anything. “When I say no one, I mean you, too.”

“I heard you,” she said. “And you heard me.”

“You get itchy fingers,” he said. “I’ve seen it.”

“They scratch when there’s something under the skin,” she said, and then stopped, because they were at the line and anything more would be stepping on it. She looked at Owen. “I’ll be where he can see me.”

“I know,” Owen said.

She stepped into the hall. Behind her, the tape whispered as Quinlan smoothed the last seal over the jamb, and Rourke started calling numbers that would go into boxes, that would be entries in a ledger, that would make a shape out of what had been a panic. Mara leaned against the wainscot opposite the kitchen door, pulled her phone back up, watched the little checkmarks that meant her images lived somewhere not in this house, not in this weather, not in this room she could no longer enter, and let herself breathe.


==================== SCENE 41 ====================
The study had the glow of a glasshouse in overcast—light heaping itself against panes and slipping thinly into the room, pooling on leather and vellum and the green felt blotter like lukewarm tea. Vivian’s study always smelled faintly of bergamot and pencil shavings, of envelopes scored open with a practiced thumb. The wind made the old windows flex; the framed title pages of maritime charts rattled the least bit, as if shifting in their mounts to keep warm.

Mara paused in the doorway long enough to let her eyes count what was out and what had been put away. Vivian had been neat in the way of women who built their own order: straightened spines, trays nested in trays, a shredder with its mouth tape-lipped by someone else’s hand. The big partners’ desk sat with its knees wide, the knees in this instance being plinth drawers, walnut with a blond bead of inlay along the face. A blotter, a gooseneck lamp with a torn silk shade, a pen resting like a docked boat between brass stands. The dictation machine was not on the desktop; what was on the desktop, tucked to the side like a spare thought, was a cassette caddy—empty.

Mara closed the door softly behind her and crossed to the desk. She’d worked out a rhythm for other people’s rooms: look, touch, look again. She opened the top center drawer. Paperclips, regimented. The second held stubs of checkbooks and a blister pack of tabs with days of the week in a tidy Latin alphabet. The left-hand bottom drawer slid out with the modest complaint of well-kept wood; file hangers faced her with their metal lips, white labels in Nora’s handwriting. Estates—M., N., R. Insurance—Boat. Insurance—House. Ewing Quarry: Pleadings.

She went to her knees without thinking about it, her phone already in her pocket, the light in the room more than enough. She pinched the sides of the drawer, pulled it farther than comfortable. It kept going until it didn’t, and then rocked that last half inch on its rails like it had been trained not to jump. She reached back under the bottom file and felt the slat there, the slicker wood. Vivian hadn’t bothered to paint the inside face that would never be seen. Mara held her breath and spread her fingers until they found the flared screw cups. Two? No. Four, tiny and recessed, at the corners where a mouse’s foot would be. She pushed. Nothing. She pushed again, but at angles this time—top right and bottom left—and there it was: the shift of something barely catching, then releasing.

The false back let go as if reconsidering an old opinion. It eased forward a finger’s breadth. She flattened her hand over it and drew it toward her, careful as removing a splinter. Behind it lay a shallow pocket no wider than the drawer itself. A pair of microcassettes in translucent cases sat side by side like pills in a day’s square on a plastic organizer. Each one had a strip of paper inside the sleeve, handwritten in that same narrow, old-school hand: CODICIL—12/ and CALL—ALIST.

Mara’s chest did the thing it did when a piece pitched perfectly into place. She took a photo because that was what she did before touching things, then slid two fingers under the nearest case—the CODICIL one—and lifted it clear.
