She puts her shoulder against the wall, lets the texture of paint and hairline cracks orient her.

“Step count?” Owen asks very casually, like he’s trying not to fill the space she left blank.

“Eight so far,” she says. “Nine.” She makes herself say the numbers as if counting them builds them into something steady.

On the tenth tread there’s a trigger, a reason her skin lift prickles: a fresh chip. The granite there has lost a thumbnail bite from its edge. The broken face is paler than the stair, new and raw. She kneels and draws the light low. In the pale pit is a skid of black—rubber—wedged in the tooth. She leans closer, slow, deliberate—and freezes with a full-body Northing when her knee slides a fraction on grit she didn’t see.

She stops. The micro-slip seems to keep happening in her head after her body has ended it. She finds the wall with her left hand, presses hard. The texture of the paint saves her; there’s something for her skin to map. She breathes once, the kind that includes counting: in for four, out for six. The out is longer on purpose.

“I’m fine,” she says before Owen can ask, hearing how not fine it sounds.

“You’re not on the stairs alone, yes?” he says, which is half a joke and half an instruction.

“Not alone.” She doesn’t tell him the only thing on the stairs with her is a long line of stone and its own quiet. She brings out a brush and touches the bristles to the broken edge, coaxing a single curl of rubber out into her tweezers. The rubber fragment is soft, pliant as an eraser bit. She photographs it where it sits, photographs it again in the tweezers, then drops it into a paper bindle she makes with one hand and licks closed. The taste is just paper and dry.

The tenth, the eleventh, the twelfth. On the turn of the stair the wall is scarred by something harder. A series of parallel tracks—stone on stone, sharp enough to raise a whisper—marker of a buckle? A belt? She steps one tread down, tests with her foot, and shines the light not at the wall but along it. The shallow grooves throw long tiny shadows down the plaster.

“Hold,” she says to nobody but the air.

The ALS turns violet. The smear on the wall haloes and resolves into two teardrops and a finger-width ribbon. Gravity shaped them. People shaped them.

She shoots again. Her flashless shutter takes its time; the stair gives her that time like a grudging favor. She finds a place to put her case a little lower, tests the tread, transfers weight, keeps three points of contact. It’s choreography. You choose to dance like someone who likes their ankles.

“What’s the angle?” Owen asks.

“Steep,” she says. “Narrow. Old.” She doesn’t add the word unforgiving because the stone has no interest in forgiving or not. It just is.

On the next landing—if you can call it that; a wider step where the stair flexes around the shaft—there is the pooled smear. Not drip. Not dab. A place a liquid rested. She feels an instinctive recoil in her shoulders and wants to scold herself for it. She doesn’t. Body is body. It reacts. The smear sits at the inner radius, where someone would have been flung by a miss, and then arrested for a breath by the geometry of the turn.

She squats, careful, almost a yoga, back straight to keep her balance. The pooled smear’s surface is matte, old enough to dry but fresh enough to have shape. There are swirls on its boundary—wiped in a crescent, then again in a backhand. At the edge, the suggestion of threads, a weave pressed into it. She can see the weave as if the cloth is still there.

“I’ve got a pool with wipe,” she says. “Textile transfer visible. Stand by.”

She takes out a microfiber cloth, folded in quarters, and a scale. Center, edge, wipe direction. She talks to keep herself where she is. “Skid marks on tread ten, fresh chip, residual rubber. Parallel gouges on plaster at waist height, direction down. Pool on turn with wipe toward down-run.”

“You want me there?” Owen asks, and the question is careful, because this is the part where pride and sense can make enemies of each other.

“Negative,” she says, and hears the arrogance in it. She softens it. “Not yet. I need clean surfaces. And I don’t want your boots.”

“You don’t want my boots,” he repeats, letting it be the joke so it doesn’t have to be anything else. “I’ll stay in my corner.”

She photographs the pooled smear in sections, making a mosaic to stitch later. In the lower right of the pool where the smear thins, there is a print—two clean arcs with a ridge between. Not a shoe; a heel of a hand, knuckles sliding. She isn’t sure if she says “there” out loud or in her head; either way, it falls to the stone and stays.

She checks her own boots. The soles are clean of new grit; she taps them anyway against the edge of a step to satisfy a rule she made for herself ages ago. The tap makes a dull note that rings in the shaft longer than it should. The vertigo returns like a sympathetic sound, a chord under the note. She leans into the wall again.

Down two more steps the handrail fails. It doesn’t vanish; it just ends, the iron bracket snapped and hanging like a rib. The break is bright at its heart, fresh. The screws have bitten out of the plug. The plug itself—old wood long since petrified—is split. The bracket’s curve carries a smear the color of cinnamon, thin and uneven, with a darker clot where the angle bit. She holds the ALS around it, watching the way light will either drink or throw. It drinks.

“Handrail’s broken,” she says. “Fresh.” She tries to keep her tone neutral. The stairs don’t care about tone, but Owen does.

“Copy.”

There’s a rolled something tucked between the bracket and the wall—a wisp. She teases it free with tweezers and finds what her eyes already suspected: a thread, white once, gray now, a cotton or a blend. She drops it into a bag, scribbles the step number with a grease pencil on the bag because her hands know how to do that while the rest of her is busy not falling.

Past the broken handrail the stairs open their throat onto a wider space—the final flight into the service basement. A cold breath comes up, damper, carrying drainage and iron. The bottom landing is a darker patch framed by the last few treads, and at the base of the wall the pooled smear becomes a puddle that stopped and was then moved. Someone mopped here with something that wasn’t a mop: broad arcs, elliptical, centering on a point as if the cleaner stood there and reached around. She sees the pattern because patterns make themselves visible to her like names to a friend.

She takes one more step to the right, sets the case on a step she can stand over, and leans the ALS beam out over the last rise. The beam finds a rag—creased, mottled, the color of dishwater soup. It is lodged half under the first baseboard plank, where it would be if someone did a hurried wipe and then kicked it out of sight. The fabric’s edges are stiff. She does not touch it. She photographs it in place, then again with a scale, again with the light at a raking angle so the use shows: drag marks, pressure points where fingers pinched.

“Mara,” Owen says, soft again. “You’re quiet.”

“I’m at the bottom,” she says. Saying bottom makes her legs feel it. “Rag under the baseboard lip. Pooled wipe central. I’ve got skid, chip, pull, wipe, and end. This is where it finishes.”

“Don’t rush,” he says, almost a whisper now. “Your shoes don’t like heroics.”

“My shoes hate them,” she agrees, and the exchange loosens something she hadn’t realized had gotten tight.

She extends the telescoping mirror and slides it under the baseboard to see the rag’s underside without moving it. The mirror shows a gummy sheen, a few stuck hairs, one long, one short. She notes the color, ticks the box in her head where noting is all that is asked yet, and retracts the mirror slowly to avoid making the rag shiver.

She stands to reset her stance and the stair flexes again under her in imagination, a sick dunk and rise as if she is on a boat. She puts her boot back one step reflexively. Her heel finds nothing—stone lost to her for a second—and she goes weightless in her stomach while her front foot scrabbles. The ALS lurches. Her shoulder hits the wall in a hard kiss. She makes a sound that is less a word than a rough intake of it.

Her thigh finds the step edge and barks against it. The throughline in her is simple: stop. Hand. Wall. Grip. She looks at her own fingers, white, splayed, the tendons standing like ropes. She lets out an inventory of breath that has waited in her lungs. It leaves, it takes the shake with it.

Owen is already saying her name with that one syllable wound too tight.

“I’m up,” she says, and immediately hears the silliness of that verb on a stair. “Caught. I’m good.”

“Let me come down.”

“No. I’m almost done.” It’s true enough for it to count. The near-miss has made every edge brighter, every distance longer.

She resets the ALS by thumb memory. The pooled smear at the bottom looks back at her without any sentiment at all. She sends the beam across the pattern one more time in a slow arc. The arcs don’t argue; they are what they are. The geometry is clear. Someone came down this stair too fast, too helpless, hit, slid, grabbed, lost purchase again, hit the turn, pooled, kept going. The bottom absorbed. Cleaners came after and didn’t finish. The rag is the failure and the story both.

She speaks into her collar calm and quick, because the calm is a thing you can choose to perform until it becomes true. “Confirming fall site,” she says. “Bottom of servants’ stair. Fresh chips on tread ten, rubber transfer consistent with shoe sole. Broken handrail with fresh fracture and absorbent smear. Pooled smear at turn and base with wipe patterns. Rag partially concealed under baseboard, visible textile transfer at pool edge. I will bag the rag last after perimeter shots.”

“Copy,” Owen says, and that word is a hook she catches herself on.

She moves just enough to shoot the bottom from three heights. Center, oblique, oblique. She uses the tall tripod leg on the case because her hands want something else to do while her hips decide to be steady again. She does not hurry. Efficiency is not speed; it is fewer wasted moves, which is a different thing.

Only then does she get the long tweezers and the evidence bag ready. She pulls the rag out by a corner in a slow, patient inching. The corner is stuck; she pulls straight, not up, to keep fibers from shearing. It comes free with a small sound like something being unpeeled. The smell rises, a sweet coppery nick in the nose that a person could pretend is anything else if they wanted. She does not. She holds that corner while she lifts the bag around the weight of it so the rag falls by gravity without a smear. The bag breathes once as the rag slides in. She seals it. She labels it. Her letters are square and small. The date. The time.

She listens. The house offers her nothing beyond its pipe-breath and a drop ticking somewhere she can’t map yet. The vertigo recedes from a tide to a tidepool. She keeps one glove on and peels the other to run the back of her clean hand along the unbroken paint at shoulder height at the last turn. Not touching the marks, just the room itself. She closes her eyes long enough to let her inner ears catch up with what her feet tell them.

Then she opens them and climbs one tread up, then another, placing each foot like it’s something she has to find rather than something given. The ALS swings at her hip, bumping again, regular as a metronome now that her body has decided to keep time. She says as she climbs, so Owen hears the pacing of it and can agree with it, “Up one. Up two. Up three.”

“Copy,” he says, every time, and somewhere between three and the corridor level the stone under her feet stops being ship-water and returns to being stairs. The case is lighter because of it. The flags in the hallway still look like sky to her. The door gives her her pencil-width opening like it hadn’t almost had her. She sets the case down and breathes, then speaks into the collar with the simple fact of it.

“Site confirmed,” she says. “Almost went funny-footed, but no harm. Evidence secure.”

“Good,” Owen says, and the word carries warmth, which the stair never had. “Come up. I’ve got an open thermos and the story of a sump that thinks it’s a harp.”

She lets herself smile because nothing in this corridor demands she do otherwise. Then she pockets it and goes to work logging the first photo numbers on the stair sheet while the stone cool at her back reminds her of what steadiness costs and what it lets you keep.


==================== SCENE 49 ====================
The servants’ corridor narrows to a pinch point where the plaster bows over the stone footings, an old house’s shrug. At the end of it, the stair punches down—a slabbed seam of cold that keeps its own weather. Mara stands with her shoulder wedged against the jamb, the case latch pressing the meat of her palm, listening to the air under the first flight. It gives her mildew and brass, the dribble-note of something collecting and letting go, and under that the faint oxalic bite of old dust turned wet. Then another scent noses past the others, metallic and sweet as a penny trapped in gums.

“Hold,” she says, to the open space. Then, into her collar, “Under-stair. I’ve got a note of iron. Stand by.”

“Standing,” Owen answers, voice traveling through her from the fabric instead of the air, because the stone eats it. “Sump’s on a lull. I can hear you clean.”

Mara crouches. The first step has a rise cut long ago by heels and buckets, a shallow trough that shiny-polishes under a thousand passes and some. Someone’s scuffed fresh through the patina. That is the first thing—the color chalked into the finish like a cloud fingerprint. The second is darker, and it has traveled—paired spots that began together and then parted, one sliding towards the wall, one toward the void beneath the stairs. She clicks the ALS at her hip to a low setting and drags an oblique beam along the skirting. The light breaks every dust mote into planets. The streaks clarify: wet, then tacky, then a sugar-end sheen.

“Confirming blood,” she says, not raising her voice above the line she would use to ask someone to pass the salt. “Volume small. Transfer, not pool.” She slides two paper tents from her pocket and numbers them. The pencil lead scratches. The noise crawls. “One and two mark the first contact.”

“Copy, one and two,” Owen says. “You want me at your six?”

“Negative,” she says. “You stay where you are and keep breathing on the line.” She wants his breath like a metronome, proof the world is not only weights and edges.

She unzips the case on its side so the top hinge doesn’t brush the tread. A glassine packet catches the hitch-light from the ALS like fish skin; she lets it be and takes the tweezers, a swab, a small square of mylar, and a powder for touch transfer. The under-stair is a mouth with the tongue curled up. She keeps her shoulders outside the doorway and extends herself in, one knee on a cut carpet square she fished out of a dead office last week and keeps for cold floors. The rag is not immediately itself. It is a grayish ask at the back of the space, collapsible as breath. The red that woke the air is mostly on the stone and the edge of the wood where someone has used the stair as a lever. But the rag holds it in spots, matted, smeared. One end shows a print, not a handprint so much as a thumb-likeness, a pressure that gathered fibers into a fan. It is not a convenience rag; it is a whatever-was-close rag.

“Do not be more than you are,” she tells it, quieter than her radio voice. She lays the mylar under the leading edge without letting it touch. She does a dumb thing with her mouth—she holds her own breath—then stops doing that because it makes the world go away too quickly. “Owen?”

“I’m here.”

“Confirming rag, saturated, used in motion. The stain has spine spray,” she says, seeing the tiny arcs that break where the stair stringer interrupts them. “Height suggests knee-level to mid-thigh. That tracks with the scuff angles.” The scuffs talk: a body checking steps with shins or a hip, sliding, catching itself. The wrong shoe on the wrong foot would do it. Wet soles would do it. Blood would do it.

“Copy. You’re keeping your gloves clean?”

“Yes,” she says. The first glove is on her dominant hand, the zipper hand, the one that will touch everything that touches something. The other is flesh-bare except for a strip of paper bandage where a scrape turned mean two days ago. She looks at the rag and then at the skin. She does not move the bare hand closer. “Switching,” she says, and makes it true, the second glove coming on with a tug that stops at the webbing of her thumb. She rocks back just enough to reset her balance, then forward, chasing her own center across the cramped geometry. “I’ve got transfer on the wood. I’m going to lift a sample. Do not—” She hears the faintest grate behind the wall as something inside the house flexes. It is the house, it is always the house, but her spine takes the information for itself.

“Mara?” Owen says.

“Fine,” she says, too fast, then with more humiliating precision, “Fine. Lifting.”

She warms the swab under her tongue in her mind, not in her mouth—a habit, a trick of thinking that makes her hand gentler. The swab kisses the smear with the kind of respect someone might give a knuckle already split. She rolls, once, twice, no grinding, no scrub, and seals it in its coffin with the click of a cap that would be stupidly loud if she hadn’t heard worse. She labels it, her letters tidy, no flourishes. The name of the stair. The turn number. The tent numbers. The time.

“Would be nice if people bled where they could say sorry,” Owen says softly in her ear, and she breathes, because humor can be small and still be useful.

“People don’t usually lead with sorry,” she says, and places the mylar close enough that the rag can fall onto it without grazing the stone. The rag has dried at the edges and kept wet in the fold. Someone used it fast and then shoved it. The fibers raised into loops where fingers dragged. She photographs it where it is: the under-stair closet mouth; the grey pile along its edges; the scatter of grit; the little dome of a spider egg sac stuck to the underside of the second step like a pearl.

Click. Click. Click. She logs the numbers and watches each digit settle before she moves again. Then she angles the ALS a shade to make the wet gleam visible in the same frame as the scuffs. The photograph will do what it can.

“Tell me about the scuffs again?” Owen asks. He is filling silence or holding it open for her while her shoulders go stiff from not brushing anything.

“Right foot slides to the wall,” she says, giving him the words so the shape they make can stand near her. “Left foot stutters on the tread. Someone’s hand didn’t go to the rail. The rail is clean. They didn’t trust it or didn’t have it.”

“Or their hands were busy,” Owen offers, not pushing, just putting the thought on a plate at the table’s edge.

“Or they were busy,” she admits. “I’ve got a smear on the underside of the third riser, knee height, like a knock. And the rag. The rag is not a fresh launder.” There is paint on it, or old grease, the kind that comes from wiping a hinge once too often without solvent. It has been in a pocket that also had pocket things in it: grit, a short blond hair, a thread long enough to be useful snipped anyway.

She slides the mylar under again, close, closer, not touching. The tweezers have serrated tips, but she takes the corner she can see a hem, pinched soft so she doesn’t cut. The rag is heavier than it ought to be, heavy like shame, heavy like wet wool. She eases it onto the clear sheet and feels the drag through the tweezers into the tendons of her hand, a long animal trying not to be moved. It makes a small noise, skin off glass. The smell comes up, saturates the alcove, asserts itself over mildew and brass. She’s ready for it and she is not.

“Do not sneeze,” Owen says. It is absurd advice and perfect.

“I am made of discipline,” she says, which is a lie anyone would forgive, and curls the mylar to invite gravity to carry the rag toward the waiting cavity of the bag. The bag’s mouth gapes appropriately; its zip track is a clear solution. She does not let the rag drag. She does not let the corners kiss the bag lip. She does not let the more-wet place come near the plastic. Everything is a pause and a release, a pause and a release, a machine built for care.

The rag slides. The bag breathes with it, opening a little with the drop of weight, then closing with a whisper. She seals it without rushing, the slider making the soft sound of something intended to lock. The label takes ink cleanly. She prints: Location. Item. Collector. Time. Date. The squared corners of her letters make them look like they could stack.

Her collar curls warm at her throat when she speaks low. “Item secured.”

“Yes,” Owen murmurs in her ear, and it is the warmth of coffee and a human heading this way eventually. “Good.”

She eases herself back, back, until her shoulders clear the jamb and her knees let her stand. The house creaks in the walls like an old man negotiating a jacket. She photographs the void without its rag, the absence now properly recorded. The scuffs do not look less now that she has a bag in her hand. The ALS throws a reflected swath up to the plaster and makes the flaking tops of old white paint glow like the bellies of fish. She looks to the edges: no cast-off beyond what she has marked, no drip trail on the opposite wall. There is a single island of rust at the baseboard that is not blood, and a dead moth which is nothing except what it is. She logs. She breathes.

“Mara?” Owen again, the name like a hand over a coat, pressure without grasping.

“I’m up against it,” she replies. “Scuffs match the blood. Whoever used this rag used it quick and kept moving. I don’t have a stop. I’ve got a slide, a catch, and a move.” She says it even, but there’s a pulse in it, an admission that this doesn’t end neat at a cupboard.

“Copy.” A beat—no more than his swallow. “Got you.”

She keeps one glove on because she knows her own habits and the temptation to tuck hair behind ear or rub at the bridge of her nose if she goes bare. The other she peels, catching the cuff with the pinched fingers of the still-gloved hand, rolling the skin free. The air skims her knuckles and she feels how cold the stone has made her. She runs the back of that clean hand along the unbroken paint at shoulder height at the last turn—not touching the marks, just checking the room, making contact with something that hasn’t been asked to witness anything yet. The paint is cool and smooth; it holds no story for her.

“Detail shots logged,” she says, because saying it tidies the moment. “Chain recorded. Moving up.”

“Copy,” he says, and gives her something he thinks she might take, gentle as slipping a coaster under a sweating glass: “I’ve still got that thermos. They gave it to me because everyone knows a harp is thirsty.”

She huffs sound that could be a laugh if she applied heat. “Up one,” she says, placing her foot so the tread finds her, not the other way around. The ALS bumps the side of her thigh and keeps time. “Up two. Up three.”

“Copy,” Owen says every time, an echo set by choice and not by stone.

On the landing the flags pretend to be sky and get away with it. She sets the case down with the same care she used to pick the rag up. She can feel through the bag the rag’s weight trying to be story, and she refuses to let it tell her more than it should. She seals the evidence pouch inside a second pouch and presses the seam. She notes the item, the bag number, the stair sheet photo identifiers. She prints the time. Her letters are square and small. The house breathes. Somewhere down below a drop keeps its interval. She says into the collar a simple fact, no embroidery.

“Site confirmed. Evidence secure.”

“Good,” says Owen, and the word lands warmer than the stair has any wish to be. “Come up. I’ve got the sump’s music explained, and the lid off.”

She allows her mouth to make a smile that won’t bleed into her hands. Then she pockets it, fingers smoothing the label edge flat, and bends to the log. The stone at her back is a steady kind of cold; she lets it have the part of her that needs it, and keeps the rest for the work waiting at the top of the hall.


==================== SCENE 50 ====================
The attic landing was the house’s last held breath. The stair gave up pretending to be anything but a spine, the risers narrowing until her boots had to think about it. The flags that had pretended to be sky on the lower landing were copies here, faded and damp at their edges from air that found any gap and used it.

The squall had teeth on the roof—skitter, gnash, a long flute-note as wind threaded itself through slate seams. The corridor bent once and ended in a door that was practical the way knives are. It wore a padlock like a brooch and had a hasp so old it had rounded corners simply from time doing its work. Someone had hung a loop of bell wire on a nail beside it, as if the door might need something to listen to.

Iris waited with the keys in a blue lanyard. The lanyard was a bit of cheer that didn’t make it all the way to her face. Her hair was twisted up and already losing the argument to humidity; a few strands had escaped to stick against her jaw. She had on a cardigan that had seen paint, an old smear at the cuff that read green in the headlamp but might have been anything. She kept turning the ring of keys in her palm while pretending she wasn’t.

“Hi,” Iris said. It lived in a small space in her mouth and didn’t try to be bigger. “You’re frozen.”

“I’m working,” Mara said. “Frozen is extra.” She lifted her phone and took a shot of the door from the end of the hall, then stepped in, squaring it, then got closer and framed the latch, the padlock body, the screws on the hasp, the grain of paint around them. Each sound her phone made was an answer to a question she hadn’t asked yet.

“I didn’t call Julian,” Iris said quickly, as if that might help with some other ledger. “It’s just me. And you. He’s—he’s sleeping, or pretending to.”

“Good,” Mara said. “Fewer variables.”

The wind shoved. The door did a little dance on its hinges, the lock thumping soft. Dust rose in a thin, visible ribbon along the top rail where the headlamp picked it out.

“Before we do anything,” Mara said, “we’re going to establish a chain. Say your name. Say that you have the key to this door. Say where you got it and where it’s been.”

Iris made a face that was almost a smile and didn’t get there. “You mean…like…on camera.”

“On camera,” Mara said, and flipped the phone to video. She held it so the tiny red eye could see Iris, then the door, then Iris again.

Iris looked directly into it like it could tell her how her hair was. “Iris Hallow,” she said. “House manager.” Her voice found the title and climbed into it. “I have the key to the attic door. Nora gave it to me when she…when she left last week. It’s been on the hook in the pantry. Top hook. I took it down twenty minutes ago. No one else has had it in hand since.”

Mara said, “Date and time.” Iris supplied them. The storm clapped at the roof and the bell wire made a soft, startled note.

“Why did the door stay locked,” Mara said, keeping her tone neutral, keeping it easy, keeping it on rails, “since Nora left.”

Iris glanced at the door as if it could prompt her. “The attic is full of—” She rotated a hand as if stirring air would turn into nouns. “—old things. Papers. The darkroom. And mice sometimes. And the—” Her fingers twitched. “—and the raccoons. We don’t go up here unless—”

“That’s not a why,” Mara said. She leaned in with the lens and took stills of the padlock again: cut marks, none; rust around the body; the loop closed. Her gloved finger tapped the coat of paint around the hasp, not touching, just indicating. “Why.”

Iris pressed the puff of hair at her ear flat, remembered herself, put her hand down quickly. “Julian said to leave it closed until he could…sort permissions. And—” She swallowed. “—I also didn’t want anyone wandering. The storm. The stair. The leak up there. And I needed to…finish something. Before anyone else saw.”

“What something,” Mara said.

Iris’s eyes slid to the left, at nothing. The lie hunt happened in that little space to the side of a gaze—Mara watched it with the same interest she brought to hinge screws.

“I messed up.” Iris picked a key off the ring and put it back. Picked another, studied it like it might be a mirror. “The paint. From the Widow’s Walk door. I—I left the jar open. I was trying to get a read for the tour group because they like—the glow thing, and I left the jar open, and then everything had a…bloom. The handprints. I thought it was cute, and then it wasn’t, and then it was everywhere the kids were grabbing the railing and—so I shut everything down up here so I could…control the mess. It’s in the darkroom sink. I’m sorry.”

Mara let the answer have the space it asked for. The squall filled the rest; banging, then a fine simmer. She kept the phone up, not to intimidate, but because later somebody would ask her if she’d written this down and she wanted to say yes and mean it.

“The luminous paint,” Mara said. “Brand.”

“It’s called Ghost Line,” Iris said, relief happening fast, as if answering a specific made her good at all of it. “From a night-fishing catalog. It’s non-toxic. It’s very cheerful about that on the label.”

“You left the jar open,” Mara said.

“I did,” Iris said. “And then the house wore it like jewelry. I cleaned what I could. I didn’t want anyone else tracking it through.”

Mara nodded once. The truth had a temperature. This one was warmer than the landing. She put the phone down just long enough to take two steps, shooting every pivot point—the hinge plates, the frame, the way the paint pulled slightly where the door had swelled. Then she put the phone back up.

“I need to photograph the key going in,” she said. “Then I’ll shoot the threshold from both sides. You will open it, but you won’t let the door swing all the way until I say. You’ll state on camera that you’re opening it with my witness. You’ll hand me anything that comes out of that room before it crosses this line. Clear?”

Iris nodded. “Clear,” she said. She held the key like it might buzz and started to put it in.

“Wait,” Mara said. She bent, shot the gap between door and floor, the little nubbins of paint that had dropped and adhered to the shoe molding. She could smell dust that was softer than normal dust. Cotton-candy attic. She took a whiff again: salt, old paper, something sweet-metallic that might be developer or might be the idea of developer.

“Okay,” Mara said.

Iris breathed and guided the key to the keyway. The cut edges of the teeth whispered against brass. She turned. The lock’s shackle released with a sound like a lid unsealing. She took the loop out, set the padlock in her cardigan pocket, then held the hasp back with two fingers and looked at Mara.

“On camera,” Mara said, or maybe just felt Iris needed a bridge.

“Iris Hallow,” Iris said, pointedly facing the lens. “Opening the attic with Mara as witness.”

The hasp dropped. The door’s weight admitted itself. It came open two inches, then three, then paused because Iris had set her hip against it. Dust made a thread in the headlamp beam. Air came out that had been stored upstairs in jars with everything else. The roof made a long peel of sound like cloth tearing in another room.

Mara crouched again, shot the threshold—paint scuff, no fresh grind. The bottom of the door wore a burr of wood from someplace hungry in winter. She stood. “Slow.”

Iris let the door have more. The wedge of black grew and then became not-black when the headlamp cut it up: rafters like ribs; twine on nails; a garment bag half-silvered with age and dust; the bump-shoulder shape of a trunk that had once traveled and then retired. A string switch hung and didn’t show a bulb. The squall sent a small sift of grit through a seam in the roof. It made a sound like sugar hitting paper.

Mara stepped to the line of the jamb and shot the room as people shot sky—segments to be stitched later, over and over, letting it overlap. She kept the angle wide enough to catch the floor boards where they began on the other side. Any track would tell her if it wanted to.

Iris held her breath for as long as people can, then let it out and made herself breathe again. “I kept some things here because there’s no room in the safe that doesn’t have eyes,” she said. “And because these are cold. Nobody cares about negatives.”

“What negatives,” Mara said, still taking the room apart with light.

“Vivian’s,” Iris said. “From the portrait sittings. And the board supper. And the—” She searched. “—summer series she did with the staff. She liked to shoot us when we weren’t formal. They’re not in the archive because Julian—because it was easier if they weren’t in the archive. Nora told me to get them safe and not make a parade.”

“Why didn’t you hand them to Rourke when the kitchen sealed,” Mara said, quiet and not angry.

“I thought if I collected them clean and kept them out of the drip I’d bring them down in the morning with coffee and not a scene,” Iris said, voice small, eyes on the line of floor where Upstairs became Upstairs. “And also I didn’t want Julian to decide they were his. He takes things that look like they belong to the house and privately annexes them under ‘legal.’ I wanted—” She flapped a hand. “To still have my job. To hand them to someone who was going to write, not someone who was going to bury.”

Mara nodded again. The truth held. “You have them now.”

“I put them in a bakery bag because the sleeves slid,” Iris said, sudden, the way small, shameful practicalities arrive. “It’s clean. It’s new. I’ll show you.”

“Do it,” Mara said. “But do it slow. Hands high. If anything else comes with, name it.”

Iris slipped sideways through the gap she’d made and vanished into the dim as if the room took her in like one of its things. The headlamp caught her cardigan and turned it into a moving moth. She clicked a small flashlight and kept it pointed at the ground, careful like a person who had had to swear she would be.

“Don’t touch the string,” Mara said, noticing Iris’s head tilt toward it.

“I won’t,” Iris said. “The bulb’s dead anyway. And the chain to the second door—there’s another door, it goes to the eaves—hangs where you can—um—” She stopped talking. The sound of her shoes changed. It became hollow and then more hollow and then solid again. “There’s a board that’s loose,” she called, “not rotten, just not fixed.”

“I hear it,” Mara said. She was filming the frame and her own gloved hand, so there would be no question of her crossing a line she hadn’t. Her mouth was nothing but patience and paperwork.

A rustle of paper. The tiny clicky sound of negative sleeves being counted by touch. Iris moved back into the wedge of light with a brown paper bag held high, the bakery logo smiling absurdly bright in the beam, a scalloped top folded twice. She held it like a holy thing. She didn’t cross the threshold.

“Say it,” Mara said.

“Iris Hallow,” Iris said. She looked steadier now, the acting getting easier with practice. “Handing over a bag of negatives. Sourced from the attic darkroom cabinet. Contents are sleeves labeled by Vivian Hallow’s hand and date-stamped by lab stickers. No substitutions. No additions. I sealed the bag earlier with painter’s tape because—because it was what I had.”

“Painter’s tape,” Mara repeated. “Color.”

“Blue,” Iris said. “The good one. Doesn’t leave gum.”

“Hold it so I can shoot the seal,” Mara said. Iris did. The tape sat crooked across the fold like a child’s bandaid. There were initials on it: IH, thick from a dull Sharpie. The date looked right. The bag had no stains. The storm had not made it damp.

Mara took stills: the tape; the bag; the hands; the cardigan cuff with that old smear, which under the headlamp made itself green again and disliked the attention. “Other items,” she said.

Iris shook her head. “There’s a reel too. I don’t—I didn’t take it yet. It’s on the shelf under the trays.”

“Don’t move for it,” Mara said. “One thing at a time.”

Iris nodded. “Right.”

“Step to the line,” Mara said.

Iris did, toe to jamb. She held the bag out. Mara met it with both hands, something close to reverent because people worked hard to name their bits of order. The bag had a little warmth left from Iris’s hands; it gave that up to the gloves without complaint. Mara set it against her chest long enough to shoot a photo of the transfer with her chin and the edge of the phone in the frame, then slid it into a clean evidence satchel she’d brought in the crook of her elbow.

“Bag number,” Mara said, reading it out and letting the phone hear it. “Transferred from Iris Hallow to Mara Lavigne, witnessed by video, location attic landing north. Time. Date.”

The house threw rain at the roof so it sounded like applause or sorrow depending on your ear. A drafting of salt touched the back of Mara’s neck.

“I want you to say again,” Mara said, eyes on Iris, “about the paint.”

Iris swallowed, but she didn’t look away. “I left the luminous paint open. I did it. I’m responsible for the marks. I didn’t do it to scare anybody. I did it because the tour wanted a trick and I didn’t think it through.”

“Thank you,” Mara said. She meant it in the most boring, most necessary way the phrase can be meant.

The bakery bag made a soft papery sound as it settled into its new rectangle shape inside the satchel. Mara pressed the seal, wrote across the seam: attic negatives, Vivian Hallow, recovered by IH, transferred under witness, ML.

“Now the door,” Mara said. “We enter together. You keep to my left. You don’t touch anything. If you think my caution is rude, you can complain to me after.”

“I won’t complain,” Iris said. “I don’t like it up here.”

“Then stay near me,” Mara said, and checked her light, and then the angle of her phone’s lens again. She kept it rolling.

They stepped in. The floor answered them with each board’s biography; some were country people, some were city, some had seen water and some had not. The smear of the darkroom was at the far end—plastic jugs, a tray of tongs, a stack of filters. The smell was what she’d expected: vinegar ideas, silver halide ghosts, the citrus of hand cleaner. The jar in the sink wore the pale green of water that has been thinking all day about glow. Its lid was on now; the ring wore a tightness you could trust. The label’s fish in black silhouette chased itself in circles. It made her remember child hands on rails and then let her stop.

“See,” Iris whispered, and then thought better of even that.

Mara lifted the phone and took the room all around again, every shelf, every inventory of junk that had outwaited other junk. The roof let go of another sieve of grit in the far corner; it landed with a sound like someone shaking a bag of sugar once and then setting it down. She shot the reel Iris had mentioned where it sat on the shelf below the trays, but she didn’t reach for it.

“You’re going to log that too,” Iris said, small.

“I am,” Mara said. “And then I’m going to hand it to the person it belongs to before anyone else decides it’s theirs.” She said it to the lens; she said it to Iris; she said it to the dust. It all heard her.

Iris exhaled. The door behind them didn’t close because Mara had wedged it with a small rubber chock that lived in her pocket for precisely this work. The wind chose that moment to pick something off the roof outside and parade it along the shingles; the house shook the way an animal shakes off water. The bell wire on the landing sounded its tiny note, far and thin.

Mara took one last photo of the jar in the sink, the lid seated square. She took one of Iris’s hands, empty now, fingers held wide to show nothing hidden. Then she turned the phone back to Iris’s face, to fix it to what she’d said.

“Anything else,” Mara asked.

Iris wet her lips. “Just—if you put in your report that I told you the paint, can you also call it mine, not the house’s.”

“I’ll put what you said,” Mara said. “And what I saw.”

Iris nodded. She looked taller by an inch and also more tired by a pound.

“Good,” Mara said, and meant the geometry of it. She reached to her collar and clicked the audio tag for the entry closed. The phone kept seeing. The storm kept speaking to the roof. The dust came down in singles instead of in crowds now, patient enough to become part of the next thing.


==================== SCENE 51 ====================
The attic workbench ran the length of the gable like a pier—old butcher‑block scavenged from some earlier kitchen, dark with varnish, grooved from years of chisels and clamps. Someone had nailed coffee tins to the rafters; the labels were soft with age and packed with brad nails, screw eyes, bits of string. A window no bigger than a book was sunk into the wall above the bench and glazed over with salt. The storm made the pane wobble in its putty; a thin draft nosed along the wood and made the paper tags on the clamps lift and settle like slow fish gills.

Mara set her kit down and made the workbench into a station: clean tarp unrolled, two evidence bags crisp and clear, logbook like a talisman. She wiped a square of wood with a sterile cloth anyway, out of habit, out of respect. Iris hovered by the eaves, shifting her weight from foot to foot so the old boards took turns telling on her.

“Minutes,” Iris said, pinching her thumb and forefinger together. “It only took minutes for me to get from downstairs to here. I didn’t touch anything else.”

Mara held out her hand. “Let me hold what you brought before we decide what you touched.”

Iris winced, reached into her hoodie pocket like it was a burrow, and brought out the glassine sleeves. Five. The packets were edged in the soft dust of long drawers, each one bowed from a life of being thumbed and set down and thumbed again. The topmost had handwriting across it, pale pencil in a tidy, old‑fashioned block: 3: EAST HALL LAMP / 10 SEC. Beneath that, more notes crowded themselves: f/8, stand, test strip.

“Where did you get these, specifically,” Mara said. She didn’t take them yet. “I want the path.”

“The steel drawer with the index cards. Top left of the workbench. It binds.” Iris gestured, and her hand shook a little. “I used my sleeve to pull it. I didn’t—my hands were clean. I wasn’t painting, I was just—”

Mara raised an eyebrow. “You were just what.”

“Checking if any of Nora’s grandfather’s plates had been recopied onto film. He said once—never mind. The negatives were with the more recent prints. I thought it would be faster to give them to you than have you go through my whole fight with the drawer.”

Mara clicked her pen once, a metronome. She lifted her phone and took a still of Iris’s hand holding the sleeves, then another as Iris passed them over. The phone’s light gleamed along the silver of the staples.

Mara slid the stack onto the wiped square and fanned the sleeves with two fingers, careful not to share skin with anything. The images inside were faint as dried rain. She could see a stair balustrade in one, a curve of newel post; in another, the kitchen door with the torn “KEEP COLD” sticker still trying to convince history. The fifth negative had a window, a shape like two faces or two lanterns—she did not guess. She dated the logbook and wrote: 16-02. ATTIC WB. NEGS (5). SOURCE: IRIS W—. CUSTODY: M.

“Say out loud what you told me downstairs,” Mara said. “About the glow paint.”

Iris leaned back against a rack of dowels and then peeled herself off it like she’d touched a thing meant for demonstrations. “I left the jar open,” she said. “Last night. After the handprint thing. It wasn’t—” She pressed her fingers to her forehead, left five points of clean in the dust there. “I wasn’t trying to add to the show. I was making sure what we saw matched what I’d used. I put the lid on later. Too later. If you want the time, it was—” She checked her phone. “It was one forty‑nine when I texted Theo. So before that. Half an hour before that. Maybe more.”

Mara nodded, unhelpful on purpose. “The jar in the sink. Lid on tight when I saw it. What about the brush.”

“In the tray. Wrapped in foil.” Iris’s mouth tightened around the act of remembering. “The tray was on the far shelf. The UV wand was under a rag. You already logged all that. I’m not reversing time. I’m saying the lid wasn’t on for a while. I didn’t think air would do anything. It’s not volatile. It’s a binder with strontium aluminate. It just dries if you leave it open. Right?”

“What it does chemically isn’t my problem,” Mara said, and then softened the line of it with her voice. “What it does to my scene is.”

“It isn’t your scene,” Iris said, small but quick, and flushed after. “I mean—I know. I know.” She folded her arms and tucked her hands into her armpits. “Do you think I contaminated it.”

“‘Contamination’ is a word I use when what I’m trying to understand gets new variables in it,” Mara said, sliding each negative into its own fresh bag. She wrote the sleeve notes on the bag with a blunt point. “You’re a variable.”

“God, that’s fair and awful,” Iris said. “It was my paint to begin with.”

“Right.” Mara glanced up. “You left it open. So: potential transfer onto air, hands, sleeves, cuffs, brushes, the gloss of anything set next to it. Glow paints are clingy. If I wave a light over the landing and the banister lights up because you breathed glitter into the air, that’s one thing. If the kitchen door smeared because somebody saw the jar open and wanted to improve the drama, that’s another.”

“You think somebody else came up here after me,” Iris said. Her voice had an edge built from other arguments in other rooms.

“I’m not thinking forward,” Mara said. “I’m saying how it reads. In my report, it reads that the jar was open without supervision during the period between the handprints and my arrival. It reads that you had access, that you admit access, and that you closed it yourself at some point before we walked in.”

“I told you because you asked me for anything else. I told you because I’m not—” She broke off. “Do you want me to sign something.”

“I want your words,” Mara said, uncapping her marker. She labeled the last bag, then photographed the label. “Say all of it. Exactly. Once. No adjectives.”

Iris pushed her hair back, left a clean stripe over her ear. “I opened the jar of luminous paint last night in this room. I used it on the widow’s‑walk door. I did not paint any other surfaces. I did not bring the jar downstairs. I did not pour it into anything else. I left the jar open on the sink for—” She bit her lip, looked at the window like the putty might help count. “For more than ten minutes. For less than an hour. I closed it. I put the UV lamp where you found it.”

Mara logged each sentence as a bullet, dates and times left blank for later corroboration. She put a parenthetical: unquantifiable aerosol/eventual transfer possible. She underscored possible.

“Better?” Iris said. She was watching Mara’s pen, not her face.

“Clearer,” Mara said. She took a fresh bag for the empty sleeves Iris had carried them in and slid those in too. “Inspectors like clear. Judges like clearer.”

“Judges,” Iris repeated, as if tasting a lemon she had picked thinking it was a pear. “Okay.”

Outside, the wind hauled itself down the length of roof and shouldered the gable hard; the window rattled like a coin in a jar. A screw rolled somewhere under the bench and tapped against a jamb, insistently, like a small animal asking to be let out.

“What are these,” Iris said suddenly, nodding to the negatives. “I mean, I know what they look like, but I don’t—were they Vivian’s. They must be.”

“Not now,” Mara said. “Not until they’re someone else’s problem. My job is to get them from a shelf to a table to a bag to a lock. The seeing can come later, if it needs to.”

Iris pressed her thumbs together until they blanched. “I really didn’t mean to screw anything up. I’ve been caretaking. I thought—” She cut herself off and changed keys. “If the paint lit anything else, it’s because it was on my sleeves. Or because you can’t keep people from being curious. Not because I wanted a haunted house.”

Mara looked at her for a long beat, then set the stack of bagged negatives on a clean corner and sealed a larger master envelope over them with a numbered strip. She held the strip to the phone camera until it pinged and tied the number to her logbook. She held the envelope up so Iris could see the intact adhesive, the crease laying flat, the sticker that said tamper evident like a schoolmarm. Then she set it in the hard case.

“I’m tagging this workbench and the sink as potential contamination sites,” she said. “I’m logging the admission about the paint’s state. I’m logging your route.”

“Do we have to put ‘admission’ in it,” Iris asked. The word made her shoulders come up around her neck.

“Yes,” Mara said. “Because that’s what it is. It’s also cooperative and timely. Both can stand to be true at once.”

Iris nodded without looking convinced. “Do you want me to wash anything.”

“If you start washing now I’ll have to write down that you washed,” Mara said. “Stand where you are. Breathe at the window.”

“I’m breathing,” Iris said, testing the glass with her breath. A faint cloud bloomed and went away. “You’re so calm.”

“I’m busy,” Mara said. “Calm is the same hat.” She clicked the audio tag open again. “Time: sixteen twenty‑five. Attic workbench. Five negatives entered into custody from drawer top left, described by Iris W. as the locus. Secondary admission: luminous paint jar left open unsupervised for undetermined period. Potential for environmental contamination noted. UV, brushes, trays logged earlier. All items remain in situ except the negatives and Iris’s sleeves.” She looked at Iris’s sleeves. “Which will, if you love me, stay far away from anything I haven’t touched yet.”

“I do not love you,” Iris said, glumly, and then ruined it with a small, unwilling smile. “But I’ll stay.”

“Good,” Mara said, and sealed the audio.

They stood in the body of the storm for a minute—the rattling window, the hammer talk of the roof, the house distributing itself a fraction at a time to its four corners and back again. The attic smelled like a room that had chosen sawdust as its religion. The coffee tins hummed faintly in the draft.

“Anything else,” Mara said, without looking up from her notes.

Iris stared at her shoes. “I didn’t stage anything,” she said, and the sentence hung there defensively, an umbrella opened indoors.

Mara wrote: Denies staging. She didn’t add a period. She didn’t need to. She marked a box on her form with a dark X: scene flagged: potential contamination. Then she snapped the latch on the hard case and set it at her feet like a dog that would sit exactly where told and not jump.


==================== SCENE 52 ====================
The office off the main hall had swollen in the night. The door dragged at the threshold and the file cabinet drawers went out and in with a damp sigh. Someone had run a dehumidifier and set it on an old map box; it thummed steadily and blew warm air that smelled faintly of mildew and lemon oil.

Maureen arrived with her arms full and her cardigan buttoned wrong. She stacked binders and manila folders next to the blotter, then set a plastic tote on the floor with a practiced crouch that acknowledged her knees. Every corner of every binder was flagged with a different color of tape; her handwriting marched along the spines in a precise italic: SHIFTS—Q4; MAINT—MECH; MAINT—ELECT; PAYCODE SUPP; ARCH REF.

“Morning,” she said, as if announcing the weather. “We held what we could out of the damp. Some of the older paper pulled like skin. Don’t manhandle it.”

Mara, already at the desk, angled the task lamp and made space. Rourke stood by the window with a legal pad. Nora took the straight chair with both feet planted, the heel of one shoe tapping and stopping and tapping again. The staff rep—Paula, badge clipped to a waterproof jacket—hovered in the doorway, eyes quick, a coffee ring already etched into the document calendar as if by instinct.

“Say what we’re looking at,” Rourke said.

Maureen clicked a pen and pointed. “Staff shift logs for the last three months, including nursing, adjuncts, overnight watch, kitchen, grounds. Maintenance reports for mechanical and electrical—generators, pumps, call-bell panels, vents. Cross-referenced to work orders.” She tapped the thin binder labeled ARCH REF. “Historical cross-index I mentioned. There’s a prior link that has to sit with these. You can accept it or not, but it’s part of the staff context.”

Paula drew herself up half an inch. “We have privacy expectations in personnel. Health, family, religious—whatever that ‘historical’ is, it’s not for discipline.”

“Good morning to you too,” Maureen said, and laid out a stack of clean forms like cutlery. “Chain-of-custody covers context if the context affects whether a book is the book it says it is.”

Mara lifted her phone. “Audio’s rolling. Time nine oh three. Office off main hall, door tagged O-3. Present: Maureen, Paula from staff council, Rourke, Nora, and me. I’m photographing the exterior of each binder before opening. I’m assigning evidence numbers LC-171 through LC-179 to this set.”

“You’re not calling them evidence,” Paula said.

“I am,” Mara said. “It doesn’t make them indictments. It makes them reproducible.”

Maureen slid the SHIFTS—Q4 binder under the lamp. The laminate had bubbled along one edge; Mara touched the bubble with a gloved finger and took three photos. When she cracked the rings, the smell of wet paper came up like a breath held overnight.

The pages were ruled, lines faintly greening at the edges from water creep. Names, initials, check boxes that read CHECK-IN, CHECK-OUT, ROUNDS. In the margins, someone had drawn tiny clocks. Some lines had a slash through the end time with a notation: STAYED—WEATHER.

“Copy set here,” Maureen said, indicating a neat stack of black-and-white duplicates. “Originals there. The tabs are months. We’re in J and F.”

“You’ve redacted?” Paula asked, pointing at black rectangles over phone numbers.

“Yes,” Maureen said. “Contact details only. Not shifts.”

Rourke leaned in. “Your initials on the redactions?”

Maureen uncapped a stamper and thumped MAU-RED—17 on the corner. “Stamped and dated.”

Mara paged to the last week. “Storm peak was yesterday. I’m moving backward.”

They worked through the columns. Voices ran like an efficient call-and-response.

“Kitchen a.m. crew checked in at five thirty,” Mara said.

“Generator test at five,” Maureen said, flipping the MAINT—MECH binder to a corresponding tab. “Logged by Owen.”

“Does that check?” Rourke asked.

Maureen slid a carbonless work order under the lamp: GEN TEST—GREEN. SIGN: O.H. The signature floated like a tired fish.

Paula watched everything, hands tucked into the jacket sleeves. “Don’t forget the transport was canceled. That’s why the evening floats doubled.”

Nobody forgot. Mara kept reading, adding time codes out loud, her fingernail tapping. When she reached the overnight column, her nail stopped.

“Twenty-three hundred, twenty-three fifteen,” she said. “Bed check, east wing. Initials K.T.”

Maureen provided: “Kara.”

“Zero zero thirty,” Mara said. “West wing, med round. Initials N.P.”

“Nora,” Maureen said.

Nora’s heel stopped tapping. “I was on, yes.”

Mara turned the page. There was a ripple: the paper under her hand wasn’t there and then was again. The numbering in the corner jumped from 117 to 120.

“Hold,” Mara said. “Pages 118 and 119.”

Maureen’s eyes did not slow. She tugged the rings gently, made the paper fan. “They weren’t there when I retrieved this. I noted it on the intake log.” She produced a yellow slip: SHIFTS Q4—pages 118–119 missing; ring impressions present.

Mara eased the gap apart. The ring impressions were clean and bright in the damp—two little moons where paper had once been. Mara photographed the gap, the impressions, the jump in numbers. “Do we have a copy set for the missing pages.”

“Not in our repository,” Maureen said. “It’s a hand-entered original. The copier set runs weekly. The last copy pull was page 113.”

Paula’s voice sharpened. “Then this is curated. Somebody pulled those.”

“Or they fell out,” Nora said.

“They didn’t fall out,” Maureen said. “You can see the tear pattern hasn’t disturbed the tab alignment. That’s a lift, not a wash.”

Rourke made a small, controlled line with his pencil. “Time window those pages would have covered.”

Maureen flipped the tab back to the index. “The page count matches seventy-two hours around the king tide. Midnight slots around call-bell spikes.”

Mara didn’t look up. “I’ll mark that as gap one. Missing two pages covering approximately midnight to two. Paula, do you object to those being called missing.”

Paula’s mouth stayed firm. “Call them missing. Don’t imply motive.”

“Noted,” Mara said, and wrote: GAP—TEMP—NO MOTIVE.

She closed the shift binder slowly and opened MAINT—MECH. The paper was heavier, stamped with a contractor’s logo. Work orders, repair notes, initialed checklists. Owen’s name appeared, then Theo’s initials on drone charging outlets that didn’t belong in a maintenance book, then a line that had been rewritten.

“Here,” Maureen said, tapping a stapled pack. “Pump house, west trench. Cistern line purge and hose exchange.”

Mara brought it closer. The original entry read: PURGE POSTPONED—WEATHER. The P in POSTPONED had been pressed hard enough to emboss the sheet beneath. Below it, in a different pen, another line: PURGE COMPLETED—ASSIST: T.T.—03:10. A set of initials signed beside it: O.H.

Rourke leaned in. “Who added the second line.”

“Owen’s initials,” Maureen said.

Paula shook her head. “If he finished the purge, it’s good. He was everywhere that night.”

“When did he initial,” Mara asked.

Maureen reached into the tote and came up with a small spiral pad tied with string. “Owen’s notebook. He writes raw and transcribes. He handed this to me last night, reluctantly. You’ll see a three-oh-five entry for ‘hose swap with T., purge flush, cleared air from line.’ Then a note to ‘update main.’”

Mara paged. The graphite was blunt and dark, the words running together. Three-oh-five, purge flush. She photographed the page, then looked back at the maintenance log’s clean correction.

“The handwriting doesn’t match,” she said.

Maureen’s voice flattened. “We used a transcription protocol for clarity. Allen put the legible note in at nine this morning from Owen’s pad.”

“Allen,” Paula said. “Allen who’s at home in bed right now. No. That’s not neutral. That’s curation. You don’t rewrite the night to make it pretty.”

Maureen didn’t flinch. “We don’t let a court try to decipher Owen’s handwriting and claim ambiguity where there isn’t any.”

Mara lifted the page and angled it to the window. The original POSTPONED and the added COMPLETED fought each other faintly in the wet light. “I can accept a typed supplement if it keeps the original intact. This has both. We’re photographing both. But I’m marking the addition as a post hoc entry. That’s gap two.”

“Gap,” Paula repeated. “Or addition.”

“Gap in provenance,” Mara said. “Addition in content.”

Rourke rubbed the bridge of his nose. “Word it so it doesn’t read like you’re deciding anything.”

“I’m not deciding anything,” Mara said, and read it into the phone. “Maintenance report has a delayed entry added after the fact by staff member Allen, based on Owen’s notebook. The original line remains. Gap in immediate contemporaneity flagged.”

“Good,” Maureen said crisply. “Can we move to ARCH REF.”

Paula exhaled through her nose. “If you must.”

Maureen opened the thin binder and slid a protective sleeve out with the care of someone removing a wing from a box. Inside was a photocopy of a ledger index and a small Polaroid of a missing page—only not the page itself, just its outline and the stub edge where thread once ran.

“This was in the Essex Historical Room,” she said. “Parish register, baptisms for ’89. The index references folio ninety-four: ‘Child of caretaker—see donor notation—A.’ The folio is absent. It’s been absent since ’97. The payroll ledgers we pulled from the greenhouse—” she reached to the PAYCODE SUPP binder and opened to a page of numbers and surnames “—show a stipend added that same month: caretaker supplement, off-book, authorized by A.W.”

Nora’s shoe stopped tapping altogether. Her hands folded into themselves on her lap.

“Spell it out,” Rourke said.

“Alistair,” Maureen said. “Authorized a stipend for the caretaker. The index notes the baptism page directed the clerk to the donor notation under ‘A.’ The page is gone. The staff logs we just looked at refer to ‘child in residence’ on two separate overnight watch lines in that same year, initials N.P. and H.L. So the staff log recognizes a child in the caretaker’s quarters. The parish register, which would have named the father in the donor line, points to ‘A’ and is missing.”

Paula put a hand out flat toward the papers like she could calm them. “You don’t say the name. You keep it at initials.”

“I’m reading the initials on the index,” Maureen said. “You do with it what your conscience requires. The point is that the staff logs and the payroll ledgers were keeping parallel truths.”

Nora sat straighter, but her face didn’t change. She spoke once, low. “You have it in your hand and you’re still speaking like it’s a puzzle.”

Maureen looked at her. “We’re speaking like it’s a record. That’s all we get to do in here.”

Mara took photos of the index, the absent folio note, the payroll line. She spoke into the audio: “Link noted between staff logs referencing child in residence and payroll supplement authorized by A.W., coinciding with missing parish register folio per index. Originals of parish book not in our custody.”

Paula’s voice came tight. “We do not turn this room into a hearing. Our people logged their shifts and kept their heads down through storm and death. You want to mark your gaps, mark them. Don’t implode the place on a rumor in a dead book.”

“Nobody’s imploding anything,” Rourke said, moving his pencil to a clean line. “We’re accepting documents and marking what we can’t vouch for. That’s the job. It’s not judgment.”

He looked to Mara. “Chain.”

Mara set out the tamper-evident envelopes. She labeled them with neat, square numbers: LC-171 through LC-179. She slid the binders into separate sleeves, the notebooks into another. She took a strip and sealed the flap with a practiced push of her thumb, then held it to her phone until it pinged. The dehumidifier hummed on, indifferent.

“Each envelope is logged,” she said. “We’re signing for them. Custodian: Maureen. Transfer: Mara Levin. Witness: R. Rourke. Paula, you can witness if you want.”

Paula hesitated, then took the pen. “Witness to the transfer,” she said. “Not the content.”

“That’s what ‘witness’ means here,” Mara said.

Nora didn’t move. When the pen came near her, she shook her head once. “You don’t need me on that.”

“Noted,” Mara said.

Maureen signed, pen making a faint scratch in the humid air. “You’re accepting the set.”

“We’re accepting the set,” Mara said. “With two gaps highlighted on the record: missing pages 118–119 in the shift log covering midnight to two, and a delayed maintenance entry added this morning based on a notebook. The arch reference goes in as context, link described, not concluded.”

Rourke tore the pink copy from his pad and slid it across to Maureen. “You’ll get the chain receipt. We’re photographing it in situ before it leaves the building.”

He turned to Paula. “Any objection to that.”

“You’ll do it anyway,” Paula said, but her voice had softened by a degree. “Do it carefully.”

Mara rose, gathered two of the sealed envelopes, and set them end to end on the blotter like bricks. She checked the seals with her thumb. The office smelled like paper and rain and old varnish. The dehumidifier clicked and resumed.

“Time nine forty-eight,” she said to the phone. “Log set accepted. Two gaps highlighted. Context logged. Transfer complete.”

“Fine,” Maureen said, smoothing the edge of the blotter with her palm. “Next set after coffee. Or tea, if anyone’s still pretending to be civilized.”

Nobody laughed. They stood a moment in the brittle quiet, their breaths small and private, and then Mara picked up the envelopes and the room moved again.


==================== SCENE 53 ====================
The office door sighed shut on Paula and Rourke. Their footsteps faded down the hall, a diminishing metronome under the dehumidifier’s steady breath. The three of them were left with the hum, the rain ticking on the sash, and the neat row of tamper seals glinting like a caution.

Maureen didn’t go for coffee. She dragged another binder from the stack and flipped it open to a cloth-tabbed section, her manner brisk as if speed alone could cauterize the room.

“We’re not waiting to be civilized,” she said. “We’ll finish the linkage while the time stamps are straight in our heads.”

Mara set her phone on the blotter, camera already open. “Rolling.”

Nora stayed where she was, arms folded, posture making a fortress of her ribs. “What linkage.”

Maureen angled the binder so both of them could see. “Staff logs—kitchen, maintenance, night desk. Three weeks straddling Vivian’s fall and the cistern. You marked your gaps, Mara—midnight to two, missing. The page torn clean. Right.”

Mara nodded. “Right.”

Maureen flipped, found a dog-eared divider with a thin sheet protector behind it. Inside, a copy of a ledger page that wasn’t quite the same paper as the first set: a different register, older, the columns wider. Her fingertip tapped a line halfway down.

“This is the parish supplement,” she said. “Essex Historical Room gets their discard scans. Baptisms, confirmations, marriages. Fun reading if you like the nineteenth century to smell like mildew and petty disputes about pew rents. But they scan newer donations when the parishes declutter. Last month, St. Lucia sent over their 2001 to 2010 book. We asked for it because it had the quarry names. And I noticed this—”

Nora held up a hand. “We’ve been around this. You said a page was missing near the staff log dates and you went mystical about a baptism.”

“I went municipal about it,” Maureen said, not looking up. “Here. Page numbers don’t matter in the parish book because they’re not indexed. What matters is the way Father Renard annotated godparents. It was his habit to initial prominent names in the margins. I pulled the index card he made back when he still used those. It cites one ‘A.’ on a winter baptism for the caretaker’s household. ‘A.’ means Alistair, because Father Renard couldn’t resist bragging about his ‘celebrity godfathers.’ He used to tell the ladies’ circle he had philanthropists in his font. You remember, Nora. He bored you with it at the raffle.”

“I don’t remember his gossip,” Nora said, but the words came out thin.

Maureen slid a second sheet out of the protector: a photocopy of an index card in crabbed longhand, Father Renard’s cramped vowels marching toward the edge. “Caretaker’s child, male. Godparent: A. The year lines up. And the surname—” she tapped the scrawled name, “—matches Owen’s. Theo’s.”

Mara leaned in, tucking a stray curl behind her ear. “You’re saying Alistair stood as godfather to Theo.”

“I’m saying he was recorded as such,” Maureen said. “Now we step sideways. Payroll ledger, which you recovered from the greenhouse bench, page fifty-six. You already tagged it LC-174. Open it.”

Mara pulled the sealed envelope closer, checked the tamper strip, and sliced it open with a thumbnail. She withdrew the heavy book and let it fall open to Maureen’s ribbon. Across a grid of names and dates, a column of notes ran in tight pencil.

“There.” Maureen had a pen in hand but didn’t touch the paper. She hovered it above a line three months after the baptism date. “‘Special stipend—youth technical grant introduced by AGF, see Nora for policy; recipient: T.H.; disbursement via caretaker.’ The ‘AGF’ stands for Alistair Grey Foundation. Your initials in the margin, Nora. The house ran the stipend through payroll because everyone likes tax-deductible philanthropy when it pays their people.”

Nora’s stare didn’t flicker. “The foundation supports apprenticeships. If Alistair gave a small grant to the caretaker’s kid to keep him in tech programs and off the grounds at night, that’s what you want. That’s a straight line. It’s not a conspiracy daisy chain.”

“Not a daisy chain,” Maureen said. “A braid. Staff log, page missing midnight to two, night of Vivian’s collapse. Maintenance notebook shows a pump check that didn’t exist until this morning when Owen felt like remembering it, and he backfilled it. And this—” She pulled one more sheet from the folder and put it down like a trump card: a narrow strip of thermal paper, the kind a cheap receipt printer spits, browned at one edge. “Visitors’ fob log from the service entry. I dug it out of the drop safe in the mail room. Only prints for people with coded fobs. The system was installed when Julian couldn’t be bothered with the front door.”

Mara tilted the strip, reading. Times and codes marched down in bleached characters. “One dot one three A.M.—guest fob A/gray. That’s Alistair.”

“Staff registered him after hours at the service door.” Maureen tapped the gap in the staff log. “The page that would have countersigned his presence for liability is the one that’s gone. And the only annotation on the page before the tear? ‘Keep the boy out of circulation when A. is in,’ initialed V. Vivian’s hand. You can see she still had the good blue pen then. She switched pens in the next day’s entries when the ink cartridge died.”

Nora let out a breath that might have been a laugh if there had been comedy anywhere in the room. “You’re knitting. He was godfather once, he signed a midnight visit on a night the page is missing, someone wrote a note about keeping kids out of sight around donors. That is standard. You shield staff family from awkwardness, you don’t parade them. You have no line between that and…whatever you’re sniffing.”

“I’m not sniffing,” Maureen said. “I’m reading. Baptism with Alistair’s initial; foundation money to Theo; donor entry at one thirteen A.M.; missing staff log precisely where it should note who was on floor, who was in corridor, who moved with Alistair. You do standard, you preserve those pages, not polish them away.”

“You don’t know the page was ‘polished away’,” Nora snapped. She stepped forward and the chair legs behind her scraped. “You’ve built a moral conclusion on the shape of a tear. Missing page does not imply intent. It’s an inference dressed up as implication.”

Mara lifted her phone, took a slow photo of the receipt strip next to the staff ledger, and said softly, “She marked it as a working link, Nora. Not a conviction.”

“You don’t get to launder it with language,” Nora said. Her hands were still folded but the knuckles were white now, tendons standing up. “You and your chain-of-custody opera. You present three innocent connections and ask us to swallow a fourth as if it’s just the next bead. The boy’s father works here. The boy got help. The benefactor came and went late because he always did. The storm was making windows scream. Vivian liked her collations neat. You don’t use that to turn this place hostile to its own children.”

Something went through Maureen’s face—disappointment or tiredness or a private flick of contempt—but she kept her tone even. “Nobody is turning on children. We’re asking why the foundation beneficiary’s godfather came at one thirteen A.M., and the only record that should place him on the floor is the one that’s gone. That isn’t an innocent connection. That’s accountability.”

“It’s an accusation by arrangement,” Nora said, and then she swallowed hard. “Why are you doing this in here. Why not wait for Rourke. Why not call Owen in and let him answer you.”

“Because when a log page goes missing, you ask the person with their initials on the payroll margins why the donor used the back door,” Maureen said. “You handled the policy. You set up the stipend. You could tell us immediately whether Alistair’s visits had a regular cadence, who authorized after-hours entries, and why Vivian wrote that note. Instead you’re arguing grammar.”

“That note,” Nora said, tugging her gaze down to the blue-ink line as if it had just been conjured. “Vivian wrote a hundred notes a week. ‘Keep the boy out of circulation’ could mean anything. Theo liked to buzz his stupid drone around the hallways. He ran it into the fern and made me spill coffee on my shoes.”

“Then you’ll have no issue confirming that ‘A.’ didn’t like drones underfoot,” Maureen said.

Nora’s jaw worked. “Don’t be cute.”

Mara’s eyes moved from face to face, not interrupting, her finger steady on the screen as she took another photo. “The receipt strip. The payroll note. The parish index. We log them as a link. We don’t call it anything else.”

“Thank you,” Maureen said, and her thanks had an edge. She slid the parish copy back into its sleeve and squared the stack. “I made the connection days ago and sat on it because I thought maybe if a person saw it a certain way, they’d come forward without being nudged. But it’s been sitting, and then the page tears itself out on the very night that matters. We had a chance to fix the habit of vanishing pages when we took this place out of the Victorian era. We didn’t.”

Nora’s eyes came up fast. “Don’t put that rot on me.”

“You were operations,” Maureen said. “It is literally on you. You can’t deflect onto storms and social niceties every time a rich man comes through the kitchen door.”

Silence settled, a heavy blanket over the humming machine. Nora’s mouth opened, closed. When she spoke again, her voice was not tight but thin, the coolness ripped. “Alistair signed in at odd hours because Julian made him feel like he had to. He didn’t want to be a spectacle. He hated board-room theater. He—” She stopped, bit down, recalibrated. “I put my initials on that stipend because nobody else was going to if I didn’t. The boy didn’t get a fair shot. His dad’s shift was swallowing him. I did my job. That’s what this is.”

Maureen nodded once, slow, and if there was pity it was not soft. “Then help us do ours. Say, ‘yes, Alistair was godfather, yes, he stopped by, yes, I signed off.’ That doesn’t make you a criminal. It makes you accountable.”

Nora stared at the parish card as if the ink would rearrange itself to exonerate her from having touched any of it. She said nothing.

Mara slid the payroll book back into its envelope and sealed it. Her voice had none of Maureen’s bite; it was procedural. “I’m logging the link as a working lead. Staff log missing page covering one to two. Service entry at one thirteen by Alistair’s fob. Payroll note for Theo’s stipend via AGF with Nora’s initials. Parish index indicating Alistair as godfather to Theo. That’s the bundle.”

Nora lifted her chin. “Put in your bundle that none of those things prove anything beyond neighborly entanglement.”

“I’ll note your objection,” Mara said.

“Note it loudly,” Nora said, and then, quieter, not at Mara but at the room, “Note that we are not a courtroom. Note that we are a house. Note that this—this fishing expedition will break people who were just trying to keep the lights on.”

Maureen’s laugh was soft and bitter. “You’re asking for deflection in the register. You know what that is? A missing page.”

Nora’s hand snapped out, as if she might tear something herself, but she stopped it halfway and let it fall. The whiteness had crept from her knuckles up her wrists. Her gaze slid to the door, as if she expected relief to walk in. It didn’t. The rain counted on the glass. The dehumidifier exhaled.

Mara finished writing, the stylus dipping and lifting, her handwriting as exact as always. She looked up. “Nora. Can you answer a practical point.”

“What.”

“When Vivian wrote ‘keep the boy out of circulation,’ did she habitually call Theo ‘the boy’? Or did she use his name.”

Nora blinked once. Twice. “She…she called him ‘the boy’ when she didn’t want to use the name in a note anybody could read. She’d say names when she was in person.” Her mouth tightened as the admission left it. “You know how she was about privacy. It was performative.”

“Thank you,” Mara said, already entering it. “That helps. It’s a pattern.”

“It’s not a pattern,” Nora said, but the word lacked heat now, like steam bled off. She leaned her hip against the desk and steadied herself with a palm flat on the blotter. For a heartbeat she seemed to be deciding whether to sit and didn’t. She lifted her other hand and pressed the heel of it to her sternum, a small, private brace. “You’re going to make it into one, though. You always do.”

Maureen was watching her, eyes level, all indulgence burned off. “We’re going to make it into what the paper says it is,” she said. “And right now it says your donor was here after hours, your godson was kept out of sight, and someone plucked the page that would make the nights line up. That’s not nothing.”

Nora looked past both of them into the middle distance, a fixed, brittle stare as if the air had a point she could balance on. “It’s not nothing,” she said, and the concession sounded like it had been dragged out of her with a hook. “Fine. Mark your working lead. Do not call Owen in here like he’s on a dock with a flashlight in his face. Do not go to the boy with this. You understand me.”

“We’re not going to Theo,” Mara said. “We’re leaving kids out of it.”

Maureen didn’t nod and didn’t contradict. She closed the binder with precision and aligned its spine with the others. “We’ll see what the rest of the logs do when they arrive,” she said. “But the connection stands for now.”

Nora’s throat worked. She folded her arms again, the fortress rebuilt but less stable, the corners shifted. “Do what you’re going to do,” she said. “Just don’t pretend it’s neutral.”

“We’re not pretending anything,” Maureen said.

The dehumidifier clicked off. The room sounded suddenly cavernous without it. Rain, breath, the faint rasp of paper under Mara’s palm as she slid the envelope to the side. No one moved for a long moment. Then Maureen turned a fresh tab in the binder and said, in a voice that belonged to weather and records and other things that didn’t care about nerves, “Next.”


==================== SCENE 54 ====================
The office had that just-wiped smell, paper and lemon oil and the faint damp of the storm seeping through all the seals you thought were good. Maureen’s “Next” hung like a gavel stroke. She flicked two plastic tabs apart and slid out a photocopy with three dates highlighted. Mara watched the ink bloom under the plastic sleeve—old pen pressure that had dented the original page now showing like tide marks in the light.

“These are the nights,” Maureen said. She placed her finger by columns that had been filled by different hands. “Caretaker’s quarters: ‘Boy to infirmary, 2200, returned 0040.’ Security: ‘Donor at north door, 2215, escorted out 0035.’ And the ledger hand that tried to look like Vivian’s but wasn’t—the checkmark that shows a headcount re-run at one a.m. to normalize the totals.”
