Nora didn’t sit. She had that distant stare again, a picture hung a little crooked. “Stop saying ‘boy’ like you’re delivering a sermon,” she said. “You know his name.”

“We can use his name,” Maureen said evenly. “But the logs don’t. The logs go out of their way not to, and that’s the point.”

Rourke had been standing near the door with his shoulders taking up more space than the wood could handle. He shifted now and let the frame creak. “Alistair’s on those entries?”

“He’s on two of them,” Maureen said. Tap. Tap. “The third they just put ‘donor’ because they had already written him up twice in the same week and you can see the hesitation in the hand. The same hesitation you see when somebody goes to write a father’s name they’ve been told they can’t and then leaves it blank.”

“The baptism page,” Mara said. “The missing one.”

Maureen’s mouth tightened, not at Mara, but at the memory of it. “The missing page,” she corrected. “Which would have recorded the father if the mother permitted, and which is gone from the church register along with two baptismals on either side, to make it look like a clumsy bindery failure. Except the tear is clean and there’s no glue residue. And the staff log that night cross-references a trip to the sacristy for ‘record correction’ that doesn’t appear on the sacristy log. Fancy that.”

“Nora,” Rourke said, voice a shade softer than he’d used all week, like he was talking across a ditch he knew was deeper than it looked. “Did Vivian ever speak to you about Alistair taking an interest in the boy?”

“You don’t get to say it like that,” Nora said. She didn’t look at him. “Like there’s a bow on it.”

“I’m saying it like a cop,” Rourke said. “And because ‘donor’ is one hell of a euphemism for a man who is at your north door at eleven at night and back out at half past midnight on three separate dates when the caretaker’s child is moved.”

In the hallway, a scuff, a breath drawn in quickly. Julian’s shadow cut the door, narrow as a blade, the gloss of his shoe toe catching light. He’d been there long enough to know his way around the joints in the floor. Behind that, smaller, a stillness like someone on the balls of their feet. Theo, ears burning, a smear of wet cuff at his ankle from somewhere he’d stepped.

Nora cut a look to the hall and then away, as if she could unsee what the door had already told on. “We’re not having this within earshot,” she said. “We’re not doing this like theatre.”

“You stated we keep the boy out of it,” Mara said quietly. “We agreed. We can ask Owen only. We can—”

“Ask Owen what?” Nora snapped, the word cracking. “Ask him if he let a man with a foundation key come around the back of the house like he owned the night? Ask him if he carried his son down those tiles once he knew someone was looking? That man got raises. He got health care. He got his wife a job on holidays when she needed cash. That’s not grace, that’s leverage, and you know it.”

Maureen’s eyes cut to her. “You want to have that conversation? Because the logs will back that conversation all day. But they’ll also back this one.”

Julian shifted, a whisper of suit wool. He didn’t come in. He held the jamb like the wood had been made precisely for his grip. “It’s not a court,” he said, voice low. “If we’re apportioning lines, I’d prefer we remember that.”

“You’ll get your court,” Rourke said without looking back. “You’re going to be neck-deep in courts before you’re done with your filings, counselor.”

“We could stay in the present,” Nora said. “For once.”

Mara slid a paper forward. “The present is a line that connects these points,” she said. “And one point is that the donor’s car shows on the maintenance cam—right here.” She tapped a black-and-white capture, a trapezoid of grain that showed more night than car but a distinctive curved grille. “It’s not a pristine ID, but it’s consistent. And the other point is that the staff log calls the child ‘boy’ every time his movement coincides with that grille.”

“You have your points,” Nora said. “Congratulations.”

The sarcasm didn’t land. It evaporated on the way down. She was watching the door again, as if she could will the hall to be empty. She smoothed a hand down her sleeve like the fabric had put up a fight. “Where did you even get the cam footage? Julian has those locked.”

“Not all of them,” Rourke said. “Your system is old in places. People hide things in old systems. And sometimes they don’t hide them very well.”

Julian’s mouth thinned. He said nothing.

Maureen flipped to another set of photocopies, an intake form and three emails with subject lines that read like white gloves: donor visit; donor donation; donor concern. “He paid for the new generator that winter,” she said. “He paid cash for part of it to avoid procurement because procurement would have shown where the cash came from. He asked the pantry to add a certain brand of cereal to the shelf. He asked for staff lists not by name but by position, because names could be subpoenaed.”

“Stop,” Nora said. The word reached for volume and didn’t find it. “You’re loving this.”

Maureen lifted her head then, not angry so much as surprised. “I loved nothing about any of this,” she said. “I loved lunches where Vivian ordered oysters and told old stories about the coal trains and then we went back to a room where she had us file people like they were invoices. Don’t put joy on my face that isn’t there.”

The rain was a continuous curtain on the windows. It made the room feel like it had shrunk to the edges of the desk and then smaller, to the binder’s spine.

Rourke folded his arms. The leather whispered. “Nora,” he said, and the name came like a hand put out palm up. “We need context so we don’t run at the wrong thing. Was Alistair the father?”

The room made a sound that wasn’t a sound—just all the air agreeing to hold very still.

Nora’s mouth opened. Closed. For a beat her eyes did that thing she’d managed to stop them doing—water gathering and not falling. “It isn’t yours to ask,” she said at last. “It isn’t mine to answer.”

“That sounds like a yes,” Maureen said, and it was the first unkind sentence she’d allowed herself.

“It sounds like a line,” Nora said back. “It sounds like someone who understands that secrets belong to the people who have to live with them.”

Theo’s shoe squeaked once where he stood. Julian’s hand tightened on the jamb until his knuckles went white. He didn’t call Theo off. He didn’t move him. He didn’t do anything but look past Rourke at the paper that had all the teeth.

Mara could feel the temperature go up by degrees that had nothing to do with the air. She kept her voice down anyway. “We’re going to need to talk to Owen,” she said. “About access and about whether any staff felt pressured.”

“No,” Nora said. “You will not lean on Owen like that. If you so much as suggest he opened a door on purpose—”

“He did open a door,” Maureen said, sharper now, because the edge had shown and there wasn’t any point hiding it. “We have the keys signed out. We have him out of bed at 21:57. We have his initials. Your initials are next to his because you waived the hour rule and you wrote ‘exception’ and you didn’t document why.”

“Because Vivian told me to,” Nora shot back. “Because I was bad at saying no to the woman who raised me and made me and paid for my education and reminded me of every penny when she felt like it. Because the house was falling in. Because the generator was dying. Because a donor at your north door at eleven p.m. when your lights are out and your staff is stretched is not something you throw rocks at.”

“Because he was a father,” Maureen said, quieter but no softer.

Nora’s focus snapped to her. “You are my friend,” she said. “Do you remember that? Do you remember who signed your first contract? Who covered your dental when the board told you to wait six months? You’re sitting here with a binder like you don’t know we kept this place open with tape and promissory notes and favors.”

“I remember everything,” Maureen said. “Which is exactly why this smells like what it is.”

Rourke scrubbed a hand over his chin and then let the hand fall. “Okay,” he said into the static. “Everybody take a breath. We’re at the fork where we decide if we’re cooperating or if we’re lawyering up in separate rooms. I need a list of everyone with knowledge of these nights. I need to know if Vivian directed staff to move the child and if Alistair requested it. I need to know if money exchanged hands under the table for silence.”

“You’ll get what you get,” Julian said calmly from the door, his first clean line, silk over lead. “And you’ll get it through me.”

Rourke turned a fraction. “You taking point for the house or for Nora?”

Julian didn’t answer that, which was an answer.

“Theo,” Nora said suddenly, startling herself, and she crossed the room too fast. She hit the door and obscured the hall with her body. “You shouldn’t be here.”

He wasn’t looking at her. He was looking past her shoulder at the photocopy of the car’s grille, that little mouth of chrome in the dark. He had a way of making himself blank that was too practiced for someone his age, as if the trick had become a muscle.

“I’m not saying anything,” he said.

“Nobody asked you to,” Nora said. Her hand came up a little like she might touch him and didn’t. “Go down to the kitchen. Tell Owen we’re—tell him to stay in the office. Tell him not to come up.”

Rourke said, “He can stay.”

“He can go,” Nora said, without turning. “Please.”

Theo went. Mara heard him go because he was trying not to make noise and that meant every attempt not to became one. A fuller breath as he hit the corridor; the soft slap of wet sock inside shoe.

Nora stood in the doorway a second longer than she needed to, like she had to peel herself out of the hall with her shoulder. When she came back in, she was different by degrees. The line of her mouth was gone; she’d forgotten what it was for. She pushed the binder away with two fingers, very precise, as if it were a heated thing.

“This,” she said, imprinting each word like a stamp, “is not staff business. This is not a gossip line. This is family.”

“The staff are the ones who moved him,” Maureen said. “The staff are the ones who had to pretend they didn’t know what the man at the north door wanted. Family didn’t clock in at ten and lie to the sacristy about needing to pull a page.”

“Different staff,” Nora said, small, and now the heat was gone and in its place was something that hurt to look at. “Staff who are not in this room. Not you.”

Maureen blinked. The hit landed. And then she put the photocopies back under their tab like the papers had weight she had to lift.

Rourke’s voice had gone back to neutral, the place where he could stand if everybody else was aflame. “We’re breaking,” he said. “I’m not getting statements right now that’ll turn to stone and be worthless. Counselor, you’ll get me a list. Maureen, send me scans of the originals and notated copies with your source citations. Mara, hold your notes and don’t chase this past the kitchen. Do not, any of you, talk to press.”

“No one is calling press,” Nora said.

Julian’s mouth made a shape that might have been a smile if there had been any light in it. “They call us,” he said. “They always do.”

“Not today,” Rourke said. He opened the door wider, and the hallway swallowed some of the air. The voices that weren’t there started to feel like they were. Footsteps somewhere below. A cabinet shutting. The house clearing its throat.

Nora gathered herself into her jacket like she was using it for structure. “We’re done,” she said, and she didn’t mean just the meeting but she didn’t say any more of it. She fixed her gaze on a middle distance again—the strip of wall where Vivian had hung the watercolor of a harbor that never looked like this one.

Maureen didn’t look at her. “We’ll need the rest of the ledgers,” she said to whoever that sentence would stick to. “And the key ledger that shows exceptions.”

Julian cutting in: “You’ll get what is appropriate.”

“What is appropriate,” Maureen repeated, and her laugh had no sound.

Nora stepped around the desk with care, as if the floor had been laid differently than she remembered. She reached for the door, intercepted by Rourke’s shoulder for an instant, a polite block, and then not. They didn’t touch. She moved past Julian as if he were an umbrella stand.

In the hall, the sound opened, rambled. Rain. The dull beat of the dehumidifier starting again down the corridor. From the kitchen, a clatter, Owen’s voice low, and Theo not answering, the shape of silence you learn when you’re young.

Mara watched Nora’s back for one heartbeat longer than she should have, and then watched the way Nora turned at the corner without looking, like she could do this whole house with her eyes closed. If she spoke, the words did not come. She turned toward Maureen instead, and Maureen had her palm flat on the binder like she needed the press of it to keep the table from tilting.

“Send me the scans,” Rourke said again, to nobody and everybody, and then, to Julian, “Don’t sit on this. I’ll push through a warrant if I have to.”

Julian didn’t answer. He watched the empty doorway where Nora had gone as if it still had her in it.

The room, finally, couldn’t hold the rest. The meeting broke not at a word but at a temperature drop. Chairs shifted back under no one. Paper edges sat up and looked sharp. Maureen gathered her tabs, Mara capped her pen as if the click could make the ink less permanent, and Rourke left his card on top of the binder where anyone could see it and nobody wanted to.

In the hall, the line drew itself without anyone having to say it: Julian at one end with law and family, Maureen at the other with records and the weight of names, Rourke in the middle where lines cross, and Mara in the doorway, one foot on each tile like they led to two different houses. The rain did not stop. Nora had already made the turn and didn’t turn back.


==================== SCENE 55 ====================
The med room always felt like a room inside a room—no windows, all angles. Stainless counter, laminate cupboards with hinges that bit, a lockbox bolted to the wall. Fluorescents hummed the way a person hums when they’re trying not to talk. The little dorm fridge ticked in the corner, red light steady above a strip of masking tape: Temp log—Do not unplug.

Mara hung her damp jacket over the back of the spare stool and let the cold in the air settle her. Early afternoon and the house was sopping at the edges. Here, the smell was antiseptic cut with orange peels someone had left in the trash.

The night nurse was already inside, shoulders sloped, scrub top printed with moons that had seen a wash too many. She had a key ring like a janitor, and a timer clipped to her collar that counted up instead of down, as if today was a marathon she’d already finished. Her badge just said NIGHT, the ink bubbled from a spill. When Mara held out a hand, the nurse squinted, then took it, careful with her grip.

“Tessa,” she said. “But I get called whatever matches the shift.”

“Tessa,” Mara repeated.

Maureen arrived with a tote that said County on the side in underlined blue. She didn’t take a stool. She set the tote down and lifted out a three-inch binder the color of oatmeal, the kind of office color that says we mean paperwork here. Tabs stuck out like tongues: MARs, Narc, PRN, Refill, Waste, Exceptions.

“I pulled the med record backups before lunch,” Maureen said, laying the binder open to a month of grids. “Paper and electronic printout. Pharmacy refill sheets, a copy of the CII inventory, and the bed-check log for nights.” She didn’t look up when she spoke; she ran a finger down the neatest column like it would keep it neat.

Tessa worked the lockbox with a key that clicked twice before it turned. “Count at change of shift was clean,” she said. “But I’m not putting my license on anything until we do this together.”

Mara nodded. “We’ll do count-back, med by med. I want dispense times, initials, and any waste documented. Then I want the pill trays for last night, and bed checks. If there’s missing doses or double doses, we’ll find it on the paper before the math.”

“And the fridge,” Maureen added, pencil waiting. “Ativan, morphine concentrate, insulin lot numbers. If the power blipped again, I want the temps.”

“It blipped,” Tessa said, with a humorless curve to her mouth. “My pen did more work than the Pyxis.”

There was no automated Pyxis here, but Tessa said it like a joke nurses tell each other. She pulled out the first blister card: oxycodone 5 mg, 28-count, resident initials in Sharpie and the pharmacy label at a sag in the corner. She slapped it down on the counter like a playing card, then another: hydromorphone 2 mg, thirty. Lorazepam 0.5 mg, bubble pack. A bottle of morphine concentrate with a split-cap, volume mark drawn in Sharpie two days ago. A stack of PRN sheets rubber-banded and curling at the edges.

Mara gloved up. She had learned to move pill trays the way you move pages in a valuable book: touch only the corner, slide, don’t lift more than you need. She flattened a second notebook, her own, and wrote the first row: Oxycodone 5, Card A, Resident V.A., Pharmacy fill date, expected: 14.

“Who’s V.A.?” she asked.

“Vivian,” Maureen said, already an answer. Her pen stayed at the top of the page.

Mara kept her face neutral. “Okay. Card A. Expected fourteen remaining as of 0700, per this,” she said, tapping the narc book. “Counts signed by Tessa and—”

“—Bri,” Tessa said. “Day nurse. You can call her Bri, too. Nobody calls her Brianna except HR.”

Mara lifted the card in its clear sleeve and counted bubbles against the grid. Two empty that were not marked; one bubble pierced and half-full with a chalk smear.

“This one wasn’t popped clean,” she said.

“I know,” Tessa said, already reaching for a pair of tweezers. She plucked the half tablet out and held it like it might jump. “Bri likes to crush in applesauce. The foil tore. She left it there until she had time to waste it. Except then the fire doors did their thing and she was stuck on the other side for forty minutes.”

“Did she chart waste?”

Tessa slid over the PRN sheets. “That’s the thing. She charted the dose, not the waste, because she thought she’d waste it with me. Then the family meeting. Then—” She shut her mouth on the next word, moved on. “It’s still here. We can waste it now, witnessed.”

“After we count,” Mara said. She noted it: one partial, pending waste. She added the used doses to the column. “Vivian’s medication record: scheduled oxy five at 0900 and 2100. Any PRNs overnight?”

“Melatonin,” Tessa said. “Not counted in narc, but there.” She pointed to a lower shelf with bulk bottles and a row of plastic medicine cups like shot glasses.

Maureen turned pages and found Vivian’s MAR, the medication administration record, with square boxes that wanted to be filled with checks. “Here,” she said. “Oxy 2100 initialed B.R.” She read her own note aloud as she drew it: Night PRNs: none. Bed-checks: 2300, 0100, 0300, 0500—present, breathing unlabored, turned at schedule. “If we’re trying to make a timeline,” she said, “we stick these to the wall.”

“We stick them to the counter,” Tessa said, slapping the bed-check log down next to the narc book.

Mara moved to hydromorphone next. “Who gets this?”

“Caroline. Post-op hip,” Tessa said. “Different wing.” She watched Mara count anyway, each column lined up with each number in the inventory—thirty to start, minus two documented, twenty-eight in the card, and two signed out for wasted in the last week with double initials.

“I’m going to read the initials,” Mara said. “You tell me if the signatures match. Tessa plus Bri. Tessa plus Owen—”

“Owen? No,” Tessa said, a quick protest. “He doesn’t cosign meds. He fixed the pump in the hall and got trapped in here twice, that’s his name on a maintenance ticket, not a narcotic waste.”

Maureen leaned in. “She’s right. That’s a maintenance signature. The narc waste line would be below. Flip the page.”

Mara flipped. The waste line was there: Tessa plus K.K. She looked up.

“Kai,” Tessa said. “Agency nurse. Night before last. We argued about whether a half tab of Dilaudid is worth saving when the blisters crack. I won,” she added, without triumph.

Mara corrected the O to a K. “Okay. Hydromorphone reconciles.”

They made a small production of moving the morphine bottle. It had the kind of cap that sticks if you’re impatient. Tessa showed Mara the meniscus mark in Sharpie from the last count: 18.5 mL. There were little lines where someone had tried to eyeball it level.

“Concentrate is fifteen milligrams a mL,” Tessa said, reflexively teaching. “We do drops for hospice. Vivian wasn’t on it.”

Mara held the bottle at eye level. Liquid clung to the glass like honey. She set it down and checked the log. “Last administered: Mrs. N.G., 0400,” she read. “Ten drops. Witnessed.”

Maureen’s pencil wrote N.G. in a margin. “And N.G. is Nora’s grandmother? No.”

“Different wing,” Tessa said. “Nadeen. Not our floor. I pulled this for the count because it lives here, not because it touches their charts.”

“Fridge,” Mara said.

The insulin and concentrates were cold and well-behaved—lot numbers, dates, initials for the temp checks. The power log was punctilious: little green dots under a column that read within range. At 0207 there’d been a blip, two degrees up, recovered by 0215, and both Tessa and Kai had initialed the incident report stapled to the clipboard.

“We did a manual check on the door,” Tessa said, tapping the gasket like it was a pet. “Still sealed. No need to pitch stock.”

They moved through lorazepam next—PRN for agitation, two cards, one assigned to Vivian’s neighbor, one to a patient whose initials matched nothing they’d said yet. One dose missing, properly initialed. The narc book had the wrong date on one line—07/11 instead of 11/07—and Mara paused.

“Is that the tenth or the eleventh?” she asked, pointing.

“It’s the eleventh,” Tessa said, already annoyed at herself. “American format. I worked in Dublin for a year. My hand forgets where the month goes when I’m tired.”

Maureen put her pencil down, then picked it up again. “We’ll correct it in red, put a note. It’s clerical, not diversion.” She drew a square, wrote DATE TRANSPOSITION, and initialed it. “Clarity.”

Tessa breathed through her nose like she’d been bracing for something else. “Okay.”

Mara turned the page on the narc count to the oxy card again. “Back to Vivian’s,” she said. “If 2100 was given and initialed, 0900 was due today. Who gave the nine?”

“No one,” Tessa said. “She was nonresponsive at rounds. Vitals aren’t mine to release,” she added, reflexively, then saw Maureen, and saw the binder, and relaxed a fraction. “But she was gone before breakfast.”

Mara nodded. “Then per the record, no 0900. No PRN after 2100. Half tab in the blister unadministered. We’ll waste this now. That leaves fourteen intact in the card, two gone per schedule, one partial wasted, one partial given at 2100, accounted.”

“Given at 2100, five milligrams,” Maureen recited. “Bed checks at 2300, 0100, 0300, 0500. If we assume the 2100 dose peak and tail, we’re within guideline intervals. No undocumented PRN in the night. No double dosing.”

“Let’s waste,” Tessa said, eager to do something with her hands.

They did it by the book, even here where the book was a plastic sheet protector and a pen with a chewed end. Tessa placed the chalky half in a medicine cup, added water from the sink until it went milky, stirred with a tongue depressor until nothing held shape, and poured the slurry into the cat litter tub under the counter. Mara watched the swirl disappear and then signed the waste line. Tessa signed below. The cat litter smelled like a hardware store five years after the fact.

“Pill trays?” Mara said next.

Tessa dragged out the trays like drawer organizers, each slot labeled S M T W T F S, breakfast-lunch-dinner-bedtime. Vivian’s tray had neat white circles in breakfast for beta blocker, one lavender tab at bedtime, multivitamin at lunch. A whole row sat untouched under today, because today hadn’t gotten its shot yet.

“Who filled yesterday?” Mara asked.

“Me and Bri. She did evenings, I did mornings. We cross-checked.” Tessa scrunched her brow at Tuesday night; a tab was turned sideways like a fish trying to slip a net. She straightened it with a fingertip.

“Dispense logs,” Maureen said, and she pulled the spiral out of the binder like she’d been saving it. “This is where you go wrong if you go wrong.”

The dispense log was written in three hands—Bri’s rounder, Tessa’s tight print, and someone else’s loopy script that turned B into a butterfly. Maureen traced the times. “2012: Oxy five to V.A., B.R. initial. 2100: Bed check notes concurrent. 2135: Vivian called for water, per aide note. 2300: Slept.”

“There,” Mara said. “This tick mark. Under 0200.”

Tessa leaned. “That’s not a med,” she said. “That’s a comet. Bri doodles when she’s trying not to fall asleep.”

Maureen looked from the mark to the bed-check. “Aide note at 0100 says turned; 0200, no note; 0300, turned. The tick mark sits in the blank. It’s nothing.”

“It’s noise,” Mara said. She drew a line through it in her own notes and wrote no medication entry at 0200. “I want the aide sheet.”

Tessa went to the nursing station counter outside and came back with a clipboard and a biscuit tin that had never seen biscuits. The station was a rectangle with a pass-through window and a dead computer screen grinning blackly. A call light blinked in red down the hall for a room not theirs; someone else would get it. The biscuit tin held pens and bandage scissors, a tape measurer, a roll of stickers that said WITNESSED.

“The aide sheet,” Tessa said, flipping through. “Here—night aide, Ros. She wrote in pencil that tore through the paper. See? 2300 present. 0100 turned left side. 0300 turned right. 0500, found no distress.”

Maureen traced each time with a finger she kept very flat. “No medication-related entries. No PRN.” She lifted her head and met Mara’s eyes. “If there was an overdose, it isn’t coming from here.”

“Keep going anyway,” Mara said. “I’d like to rule out everything we can touch.”

They moved through the rest in a rhythm that got less prickly as the numbers kept behaving. Diazepam cards matched the book, the book matched the MAR, and the MAR matched the human memory of who got what when. The only snags were human: Bri’s 11/07 vs 07/11, the doodle that looked like a comet, the half pill waiting to be wasted. Each snag got a box around it and a note, then slid to the side.

At the nursing station, they spread the trays and logs like a quilt. Maureen penciled a thin line that ran through the dates and times, an axis for the whole mess.

“Clarity,” she said again, to the pencil line.

“Noise,” Tessa said, to the doodle, and flicked it with a nail.

Mara took off one glove and dialed the fridge timer reset with her bare hand so she could feel the cheap plastic click. “We can document that the narc count and dispense logs reconcile as of today, fourteen hundred. No missing doses. No double entries. The only discrepancies are clerical and corrected. Vivian’s last oxycodone was at 2100, witnessed by Bri and confirmed by the MAR. There’s no charting to indicate a PRN after that.”

Maureen closed the binder and set her palm on top of it like she was flattening a map. “And with bed checks as charted,” she said, “you have no window where an extra dose could have been given without notes. Aide present at all scheduled turns. Quiet night by the book.”

Tessa’s shoulders let go that fraction they’d been holding. “My license would like to sit down now,” she said. She found a stool with her foot and sat on half of it. “You’re not going to write me up for the date?”

“No,” Maureen said, and the word was simple.

Mara looked once more at the half-popped blister pocket where chalk dust clung to foil. She wrote the last line in her notebook and underlined the words once, not twice: Counts reconcile; no overdose window indicated.

In the hall, the dehumidifier clicked over to a new pitch. The call light down the wing stopped blinking. The room held, the numbers held, and for a moment the fluorescents hummed like they had a melody.


==================== SCENE 56 ====================
They reconvened at the library table that had turned into a control tower. Pill trays were stacked in neat, accusatory tiers beside the catalogue drawers; the safe was closed now, but the ledgers and MAR binders lay open under a brass reading lamp that gave everything a museum tint. Someone had dragged a blue extension cord over the Persian runner to feed the dying desktop scanner; it throbbed a warm plastic smell into the damp air. Through the tall windows, water beaded on the panes and made the yews outside go fuzzy, as if the hedge had opinions and was keeping them private.

Maureen wiped a clear pane on her readers with the edge of her sleeve and clicked the ballpoint she liked because it made a clean, thin line. Mara plugged her phone into the extension cord, dialed the number on the pharmacy label, and put the call on speaker. The tiny green diode on her portable recorder blinked patiently beside it, the way a crab might blink from under a rock.

“Good Harbor Community Pharmacy,” a recorded voice said. “If this is a refill request, press one. For immunizations—”

Mara hit zero. The storm made the connection huff and then settle.

“Pharmacy,” a woman said, efficient. “This is Luanne.”

“Hi, Luanne, this is Mara Vane at Harbor Light,” Mara said. “We’re auditing controlled substance refills and timestamp integrity for one patient. We have labels in front of us. I’d like to verify fill times, lots, and whether any early releases were permitted. I can fax consent if you need it.”

“We have current authorizations on file for Harbor Light auditing,” Luanne said. “Name and date of birth?”

Mara read them off. The way the pharmacist repeated each letter back, crisp and uninflected, made the storm hush in the edges. She heard keys like small, insistent rain.

“Okay,” Luanne said. “What are you holding?”

Maureen angled the oxycodone bottle into the lamplight. “Oxycodone immediate release, five milligrams, forty count—original quantity sixty. Dispensed nine days ago. Label says 11/12 09:47.”

“And we have in-house administration through 11/14 at twenty-one hundred,” Mara added, tapping the MAR. “We want external timestamps and courier sign-off to match our internal logs.”

“Pulling the profile,” Luanne said. “Give me a sec.”

Mara capped her pen and looked at Maureen. They were both listening to the pharmacy line fill up with a printer whir and a drawer thunk. In the hallway, someone wheeled a linen cart past the library doors. The smell of bleach folded in and then was gone.

“Found it,” Luanne said. “Oxycodone five milligram tabs, sixty count, DAW zero, NDC ending eight-zero-seven-two. Filled November twelfth at oh-nine forty-five and twenty-two seconds. Barcode printed at forty-six. Bagged with DME sticker for controlled protocol at fifty-one. Courier scanned out at ten-oh-six. We have a signature ‘M. Keane’ received at ten thirty-one at the Harbor Light service entrance. That’s you?”

Maureen nodded to herself. “Yes. Keane. That’s me.” She scribbled the times in a column. “Our internal narc count entry shows receipt at ten thirty-two. We logged a partial acceptance because the stool softeners were backordered, but we signed for everything in the controlled envelope.”

“Good,” Luanne said. “We have the controlled envelope listed as one item. No discrepancies at point-of-handover. I can fax the courier log if you need the actual squiggle.”

“Read it to me,” Mara said.

“Printed name, M-A-U-R-E-E-N K-E-A-N-E, last four of her employee badge, one-eight-four-nine, time stamp ten-thirty-one, no notes. Courier initials J.L.”

Mara drew a box around it. “Internal matches courier. No gap.”

“Next,” Maureen said, easing the diazepam card forward. “Diazepam two milligrams, blister card, thirty count. Label says 11/08 14:12.”

“Diazepam,” Luanne said. “Okay—filled November eighth at fourteen-oh-six and nineteen seconds. Printed at fourteen-ten. Courier scanned at fourteen twenty-one. Delivered fourteen thirty-seven with signature ‘B. Connolly.’”

Maureen made a face like she could see Bri’s chaotic bun. “That tracks. She took that run while I was stuck on the phone with case management.”

“Our internal log shows receipt at fourteen thirty-nine,” Mara said, flipping earlier pages. “Drug card lot number—Maureen?”

Maureen read it. “Lot two-two-four B twelve.”

“That’s what I have,” Luanne said. “Two-two-four B twelve, exp June twenty-six. We didn’t substitute vendor that day.”

Mara underlined long enough to leave a groove. “Any early-fill requests or overrides for either since the first of the month?”

“No,” Luanne said. “Oxycodone is set at minimum seven days between sixty counts. The prescriber put a ‘do not auto-refill’ flag. Last early-fill request on this profile was August, denied. Diazepam has no early fill noted. Next eligible refill date for oxycodone would be—” more keys “—November nineteenth at zero-eight-hundred if there were prn losses documented and a police report. Otherwise, normal would be the twentieth or twenty-first depending on use. We are showing next scheduled fill as hold; no request in the queue.”

Maureen tilted the blister pack, watching chalk dust seize to the foil. “We charted every dose through last night. Counts reconcile as of fourteen hundred today. If anything were leaving, it would have to be through the pharmacy side.”

Luanne made a small noise that might have been a laugh without the laugh in it. “Not with me,” she said. “Our controlled log is clean. We balance at every shift and again at close. You’ll make me go get the tote numbers if you keep that up.”

“Please do,” Mara said. She wasn’t joking. “Also, morphine solution—there’s an old order discontinued last month. I want to close it out in the external system, make sure no laundered refills crept in.”

“I like the way you say ‘laundered,’” Luanne said dryly. “Morphine oral solution ten milligram per five milliliter, four-ounce bottle. Last dispensed October twenty-first at eleven-oh-two, discontinued November first at nine-fifteen per prescriber message. No refills since. Bottle status in our system is ‘closed, expected returned.’ Did you return the remainder?”

Maureen flipped a pink carbonless form and then a second. “Yes,” she said. “Picked up by courier November second, signed by me at nine fifty-nine. They had a new kid, the quiet one. Initials F.T.”

“Confirmed,” Luanne said. “Returned to stock log scanned in at ten twenty-three with FT. Contents accounted for, waste logged. That’s closed.”

Mara checked a line on her own notebook. The rhythm of it—the ask, the check, the confirm—had a caliper satisfaction. “Any out-of-window activity on the e-script account last week? I’m thinking prescriber time stamps. We’ve had trouble with charting catch-up that makes the order look like it came later than it did.”

“Let me pull e-prescribe session history,” Luanne said. “Give me a second to click through a thing that hates me.”

While the line went quiet except for a smudge of typing, Mara leaned on one elbow and looked at the banner above the fireplace: a faded nautical flag under glass, its red chevrons bleaching toward ghost pink. The old house wanted to take every line and paper and turn it archival. She took her glove off again and pressed the corner of the pharmacist’s label, feeling the tooth of the thermal print as if that texture were a witness.

“Okay,” Luanne said, coming back. “Prescriber session for the oxycodone order: November twelfth, entered at zero-nine-oh-one from a device tagged ‘MD-OFFICE-2’, signed at zero-nine-oh-four, transmitted successfully at zero-nine-oh-five, confirmed receipt by our system at zero-nine-oh-five and twenty-six seconds. There’s a clinical note attached: ‘Pain escalations overnight, keep at five milligrams q6h scheduled, no PRN.’ That matches the instruction we printed. There are no other e-scripts for controlled meds for this patient between the eighth and today.”

Maureen tapped her pencil exactly once for each time code. “Our MAR entry for that new schedule was made at nine fifteen,” she said. “Initialed by me and Tessa. We announced the change at morning report.”

“And bed checks as charted show no gaps,” Mara said, almost to herself, but it went on the recording. “Okay. Next: senna, colace—non-controlled, but let me be boring.”

“Please do,” Luanne said. “Senna ninety count dispensed eleven/twelve at ten-oh-eight with your controlled. Docusate backorder resolved and dispensed eleven/thirteen at nine twenty with ‘B. Connolly’ signature. We had an eight-hour weather delay on that one.”

Maureen made a sympathetic noise that belonged to another, easier audit. “We left note on the MAR and started prune juice. It was a day.”

“While we have you, can you verify that no pharmacy staff substituted a different manufacturer for the oxycodone mid-cycle?” Mara asked. “We’re looking at tablet markings to confirm continuity; I want to be sure external documentation reflects that.”

“Let me see,” Luanne said. A pause where she was clearly navigating a dropdown she hated. “No manufacturer changes during this fill. Last switch from Qualitest to Mallinckrodt was in September when our wholesaler sent us a new NDC. This bottle is Mallinckrodt, white round, M over 05 imprint. That’s what we filled, that’s what your label supports.”

Maureen plucked a tablet from the blister pocket hovering above waste and held it in the lamplight. “M 05,” she said. “Matches.”

“Okay,” Mara said. “Luanne, one more bucket: reconciliation. We are running daily narc counts against the controlled log. I want to match your receipt and dispense ledger to ours for the month. I can send photos if you want, but if you can read me the dates and times for controlled deliveries to Harbor Light since the first—just the time stamps and courier initials—I’ll mark them against our narc intake sign-offs.”

“You like to work for your fun,” Luanne said, but there was no complaint in it. “Hang on.” Keys, a double-click that sounded annoyed. “Here we go. November first: hydromorphone for room twelve, delivered at ten forty-two, courier initial B.K., signed by—doesn’t matter for your patient, but I’ll read them anyway.”

“Read them,” Mara said. She liked listening to things line up.

“November third: diazepam two milligram, delivered fourteen eighteen, J.L. November sixth: fentanyl patches, not your patient, delivered eleven-oh-seven, F.T. November eighth: diazepam two milligram, we discussed, fourteen thirty-seven, B. Connolly. November twelfth: oxycodone five milligram, ten thirty-one, M. Keane. November fourteenth: no controlled deliveries to Harbor Light. November fifteenth: hydromorphone again, thirteen oh-four. November sixteenth: none. Today: none.”

Maureen’s pencil ticked in time. When Luanne finished, she drew a low line under the column the way she closed out a day on a restaurant ticket. “Our narc acceptance sheets reflect those exact dates, times plus or minus two minutes depending on how chatty the courier was. Initials match. No ghosts.”

“Anything in your system to indicate a partial or corrected label that would put two bottles in the same bag by mistake?” Mara asked. “We’re ruling out every dull scenario before we start inventing exciting ones.”

“No double-bagging on controlled envelopes,” Luanne said. “We lock each one with a serial zip and scan the code at pack, hand-off, and receipt. If I do that wrong, DEA comes live in my glove compartment. Believe me, I don’t do it wrong.”

Mara smiled at the table. “I believe you. Last thing: time zone errors. Our in-house clocks hiccuped with the storm, and the fridge timer drifted fifteen minutes. Do your labels print from a time server or a local machine? No daylight saving weirdness?”

“Time server,” Luanne said. “Clock synced off NIST. We had a blip the first Sunday but it resolved by Monday. The twelfth is clean.”

“Thank you,” Mara said. “You’ve been patient. If you can fax the courier signatures and the e-prescribe sheet, we’ll append them to the audit. Number is on file for the library machine.”

“I have it,” Luanne said. “Give me five minutes and listen for the dinosaur.”

They hung there for a breath with the silence of a line that has gone true. Mara clicked off the speaker. The phone went back to being a rectangle instead of a voice.

“Tessa will want to kiss that woman’s feet,” Maureen said, half to herself, stacking the blister cards by size. She made a neat cathedral of them and then undid it, as if superstition and order could coexist on the same table. “Not that we can fix the cistern with any of this.”

“We’re not fixing the cistern,” Mara said. “We’re fixing what the cistern didn’t do.” She checked the recorder light, then turned it off and set it on top of the nurse notes the way you press a seal on wax. “Refill times match. Courier signatures match. Manufacturer consistent. No early fills. No substitution. External records corroborate internal logs. That’s one clean lane.”

Maureen nodded, a small, precise movement. “I’ll scan and upload the narc acceptance sheets and the witness waste forms for the half tablets. I’ll include the prn page with ‘none’ circled so largely it screams.”

The fax machine on the sideboard—a relic beside a hill of town reports and a ceramic lobster—woke with a yowl like it had been stabbed. The first page shuddered out with Luanne’s header stripe and the courier squiggles, their loops shameless and uniform. Maureen walked over and lifted the paper as it came, smoothing it down with her palm so the ink wouldn’t smudge. The machine spat a second, then a third.

“E-prescribe session sheet,” she read. “Look at that—seconds. Even the seconds.” She laid them beside the MAR as if two birds might look at each other and agree.

Mara took her pen and, on a fresh page, wrote the same thing she had written at the nursing station, but with an added clause. Counts reconcile; no overdose window indicated; external pharmacy data corroborates internal schedule and receipt. She underlined it once.

Maureen was reading the courier signature like it might reveal character. “J.L. is Joseph. He took a header on the ramp last winter and still brings us peppermint bark in December.”

“We’ll put him on the staff holiday list,” Mara said. She stood, the chair legs scraping stone, and collected what they had into two stacks: one for evidence, one for comfort. The evidence stack went into a plastic envelope and clicked shut. The comfort stack—tea bags, a wrapped granola bar someone had set aside—stayed. The library smell of cloth and old paste held steady.

When they carried the envelope to the safe, Maureen balanced the faxed pages on top and squared the corners with both palms. “No medication sabotage,” she said, as if tasting the phrase for grit and finding none. “No pharmacy irregularities. What’s left is what’s left.”

“What’s left is enough,” Mara said, because the numbers had behaved. The lamp threw a clean circle they had brought into being by sheer insistence. She checked the time and wrote it in the margin, because it was what the day had trained her hand to do.

From somewhere down the corridor, a kettle clicked to a stop. The house sighed its slow, damp sigh. The dehumidifier shifted up a key. Behind them, the fax machine swallowed one more page of Luanne’s proof and released it into their hands, white as a blank verdict. They filed it without ceremony and closed the safe. The click of the lock sounded satisfied, nothing more.


==================== SCENE 57 ====================
The resident wing at night behaved like a barometer. Everything was measured: breath counts, door clicks, the way the air gathered and let go down the long corridor lined with closed doors and slotted nightlights. Someone had taped felt to the rolling cart’s edges so it wouldn’t squeak. Its little bottles—lotion, sanitizer, a packet of barrier cream, a stack of folded wipes—rode in quiet formation. A clipboard lay across the top like a well-fed cat.

Bree pushed the cart, a compact woman with a cardigan knotted at her waist. Santi walked beside her with a key ring that chimed once in a cautious way when his thigh touched it. Their rubber soles made no sound. The hall’s window looked toward the black ridge of the lawn and the sea beyond, but the glass only held their reflections: three figures moving carefully through a house that remembered salt.

“Start at L,” Bree whispered, touching the bed-check grid with her pen. “We go L through R, like always.”

Mara nodded. “I’m not here to interrupt routine. I need your routine.” She’d tucked Maureen’s faxed sheets away; this was a new ledger. The bed-check sheet was ruled for half-hours: 2000, 2030, 2100, and so on through six in the morning. Beside each time were boxes for initials, a column for comments as narrow as a confession line.

They stopped at the first door. Bree tapped twice with a knuckle. Santi opened only as far as the magnet allowed, and Bree bent to watch for chest rise. She counted silently, lips moving in fives, then signed the box: BB 2000. Santi checked the call-button light next to the door.

“Green means reset,” Santi breathed. “If it goes amber, someone hit it and we didn’t answer.” He glanced at Mara. “It never goes amber without a line in the log.”

Mara held her small recorder down at her side where it wouldn’t glint and listened to it take their voices. “I have system pulls for the call bells and the door contacts and the pressure mats. We’ll walk it against memory.”

They moved on. At L‑3, a radio murmured a baseball game from someplace where the season hadn’t ended. Bree reached in, clicked it down a notch, and wrote BB 2000. The corridor had an echo like a cave’s and a scent like old paste and eucalyptus. There was a framed print of a schooner nailed between the fire extinguisher and the thermostat, and someone had looped a string of plastic sea glass around the extinguisher’s neck like a lei. The green of it was ghostly.

“Stop me if my questions throw you,” Mara said as they went.

Bree waved a hand: go on.

“At exactly midnight,” Mara said, “the motion log in this wing shows no movement in Room N‑4. The bed pressure mat shows an occupied state from 2208 to 0441. The door contact shows closed from 2143 to 0513. Does that match what you recall?”

“N‑4 is Ms. Dempsey,” Santi whispered. “She sleeps like the dead unless the Sox lose, and then she throws the sheet off and threatens the radio with a spoon. Last night she was a stone.”

Bree nodded. “She was a stone. I wrote BB 0000. We didn’t open her door again until our two a.m.”

Mara drew a short line on her index card: N‑4 calm, corroborated.

They hit M‑1 and Bree leaned in to make a note: cough syrup offered, refused. “He hates the cherry,” she said when she came out. “Writes letters to the manufacturer in his head.”

“In the log,” Mara said, “there’s a motion burst at 0017 in M‑1. Seven seconds. Door contact stays closed, no bed exit. That would be you?”

“Me,” Bree said. “Peeked and adjusted his blanket. He curls himself down to a stripe.”

Santi’s key ring bumped and chimed again. He stilled it with his palm. “The sensors read us as ghosts when we just lean,” he said to Mara without apology. “Sometimes the mat picks up our elbows.”

“They don’t lie about the big things,” Bree added. “Open doors, actual out-of-bed. They lie small.”

Bree initialed boxes: BB 2030, BB 2100. At N‑1, she paused, glanced at Santi, and lifted a baby blanket off a pair of sensible shoes that had fallen over. “Ms. Gold artifacts,” she said, setting them upright. “She keeps everything paired.”

On the clipboard, the “Comments” column under 0000 held three cramped notes, each of them clinically precise, almost tender for all their constraint: ambulating to restroom, steady, returned; repositioned to side; sleeping.

Mara walked with them, listening to the small machines the house had been retrofitted with. Every door had a magnetic strip where the old brass slide had worn a scar into the wood. The strips were pitted where salt had nibbled them, but the LED lights burned, faithful and cheap. At the end of the corridor, the door to the outside had a contact sensor the size of a matchbox, and beside it, a palm-sized annunciator that chirped if anyone opened the door after ten. It had a sticker on it that said DON’T COVER WITH TAPE in a teacherly hand.

“Midnight,” Mara repeated once they reached N‑7. “I need you both to walk me minute by minute, because your initials say you did. What did you see? What did you smell? Who was awake? Any anomalies? You told Maureen earlier that you heard a chime.”

Bree scratched a check next to N‑7 and tucked a stray hair behind her ear. “The chime wasn’t midnight. Closer to eleven. The laundry door didn’t latch and the wind flirted with it. It chirps twice—like this.” She mimed a bright, soft tone. “Then Santi stuck his shoulder against it and it thought better of being a mouth.”

“I logged it,” Santi said. “We have a place for ‘incidental door alarm.’” He pulled the second page on the clipboard up and revealed their own in-house log of small stupidities: LAUN 2258 SECURE, S.G.; PATIO CONTACT TEST 2300 OK, BB.

“Your LAUN 2258 matches the door alarm log,” Mara said, pulling a folded printout from her pocket. “Laundry door contact open 22:58:04, close 22:58:23. The exterior door shows nothing between 21:47 and 05:31.”

“Because we don’t touch it,” Bree said. “We don’t like the wind grinning in.”

They stopped at two more doors, the order uncanny in its devotion. Bree’s pen adjusted its angle to the paper as the corridor lamp warmed to itself. Santi’s shadow shook a little on the carpet as the dehumidifier at the far end changed key.

“At 0012,” Mara said, “the motion log shows a high-activity block at R‑3. That’s a two-minute scatter that looks like light turned on, person up, person back down.”

“Mr. Crowley,” Santi said. “Night bathroom nerves. He wakes and doesn’t trust the dark to hold him.”

Bree smiled. “I was there with the light because his lamp switch is mean. We stood there and counted tiles to the bathroom. I could do it in my sleep.”

“You initialed?” Mara asked.

“BB 0000 and then BB 0030 with SC,” Bree said, tapping the squares. “Light assist. Back to bed.”

“Logs show two entries that match,” Mara said, making her own check. “No other cross traffic. No door opens to the med room. No alarm on the med cart sensor.”

Santi blew air through his cheeks, the quietest expression of relief imaginable. “We told you,” he said. “Once we lock the cart we don’t even look at it. I feel like it glares.”

They reached the med cart niche and Bree’s hand went to the keypad out of habit, then hovered. She didn’t touch it. The keypad’s screen was asleep, the backlight a polite dimness. There was a little tamper sensor above it that would squeal if anyone yanked the cart. It had a wrinkle of duct tape near the top that looked like it had been sanded smooth.

“Cart logs,” Mara said, touching the paper in her folder. “Last open 21:17 by 6723, which is Bree. Close 21:23. Next open 05:52 by 5510, Santi. No access between. No alarms.”

“Bless it,” Bree murmured. “We called both those to Maureen. She made us say our PINs out loud and then scolded us for saying our PINs out loud.”

They moved on. At R‑5, a soft cough came from within and the shape shifted under the blankets. Bree stood, counted to twenty, and let the door fall back against its magnet. Her initials landed square in the box like a carpenter’s nail.

“Talk me through the end of the corridor,” Mara said. “The outside hall camera picked you up at 23:34 heading toward the linen closet. That’s this camera here.” She pointed to the dark bubble in the ceiling.

“We ran pillowcases,” Santi said. “Ms. Gold artifacts had a spill.” He flipped the page to their linen log: PC x 2, 23:36, BB/SC.

“Camera sees us at 23:56 going back,” Bree said, remembering. “We’d talked about the Sox at the nurses’ station. I gave you the score and you said it was a curse and a blessing.”

“I said both because I didn’t want to choose,” Santi said, with an apologetic grin. “We initialed again 0000 in order. We always go door to door.”

Mara read from her own sheet, the system’s motions, its rationally timed heartbeats. “From 23:58 to 00:06, there’s movement logged at R‑3 and R‑6, none at R‑4. Ms. Dempsey doesn’t stir. Mr. Crowley does. R‑6 shows a brief door open; you stepped in. The rest of the hall shows no transit. The med room stays dark. The alarm annunciators read idle. The stairwell door contacts read closed.”

Santi nodded with every sentence like a metronome. “We were a tunnel of Boston in the middle, nothing at either end. That’s how it felt.”

They arrived at the end of the corridor where the resident wing met the older part of the house. The threshold was two millimeters higher than you expected. Everyone learned it once, hard. Bree’s cart had a half-moon dent on the underside from a first week that hadn’t forgiven her.

“This door,” Mara said, tapping the plate. “Ten p.m. locks. If it opens, the annunciator chirps. We have nothing after ten.”

“We hate the chirp,” Bree admitted. “We’ve all dreamed it sits on our chests.”

Mara stood with her back to the plate and looked both ways down the corridor. It was a tidy thing, symmetry imposed on stubborn bones. Her watch ticked once on her wrist, a noise so small it sounded like a thought.

“Let’s circle back to Mr. Crowley’s lamp,” she said as they retraced their path and Bree initialed 2030, 2100 for the last two rooms in line. “You turned it on at 0012. You turned it off at 0014.”

“On at twelve, off at fourteen,” Bree said. “He doesn’t like the switch because it bites. I use the wall.”

“The log says on at 00:12:31, off at 00:14:20,” Mara read. “He doesn’t get out of bed, the mat doesn’t trip, the door stays in. That’s consistent with a sight check and a wall switch.”

“Exactly,” Santi said softly. There was a pleasure in being read correctly.

“And at 03:29,” Mara said, “the hall motion shows activity near M‑1. The mat shows occupied to unoccupied for three seconds and back. I’m not matching that to anything on your sheet.”

Bree frowned a little, then nodded. “That was me leaning in. He kicks his foot out and it slides toward the mat edge. If the mat curls, it reads what it shouldn’t.” She rubbed her thumb over the top of the pen. “I didn’t mark it because it wasn’t a check time. I was fussing. You can mark it if you like. It was nothing. If it had been something, I’d have written it and put my name so big it woke the page.”

Mara wrote: 0329 M‑1: elbow read; staff leaning. The motion log would never know what an elbow was, but she liked giving it a word.

At R‑2, Bree lifted a water glass to check the meniscus, added a finger of cold from a little carafe, and set it back on a coaster that read SAIL AWAY in jaunty letters. She initialed, BB 2130.

“When you’re done at midnight,” Mara said, “you return to the station or you go straight to the kitchen for ice?”

“Station first. We put the clipboard back so if we’re hit by a bus the floor still looks like someone is minding it,” Bree said. Her voice was not humorous; it was simply the truth’s shape. “Then ice. Then check the charts for the early meds.”

“Drive your finger along the path you took,” Mara said.

Bree made the path with her finger in the air: door to door, to the nurses’ station, the pivot, back toward the linen room. Santi mapped his own path beside hers. They overlapped so precisely it looked rehearsed.

Mara glanced down at the device logs one more time. In the low light, it looked like a cardiogram. There was no spike where there shouldn’t be, no gap. The hall’s motion close to midnight was a ribbon with two small, expected ripples. Ms. Dempsey slept. Mr. Crowley had his lamp. The exterior door stayed closed. The med room stayed asleep. The cart never squealed. The bed mats hummed along in their binary way and said what they had to say: in, out, in.

They reached the nurses’ station at the end of the wing. It was a shallow bay with two swivel chairs and a cork board laminated under a plastic sheet. A silver bell sat on the counter and had been silenced forever with a square of felt. There was a list of birthdays pinned at the top and a comic strip about a cat who loved paperwork.

Bree slid the clipboard into the rack, spine outward. Santi put the pen beside the phone cradle so it wouldn’t leave a smear on the desk’s edge. The air from the dehumidifier rose in a gentle column and made the corners of the posted notices flutter, a little shot of movement that made the paper feel alive.

“Anything you feel in your bones that doesn’t have a column?” Mara asked.

Bree looked down the corridor. “Everyone was where they were supposed to be. Even Ms. Dempsey’s spoon. Santi would tell you if he’d heard more than two chirps because he counts his heartbeats against it.”

Santi laughed once, without sound. “She’s not lying. I keep time with anything.”

Mara placed the system log printouts on the counter and lined their edges, then used her palm to flatten the curl where the paper had learned humidity. She wrote across the top of a new page in the errand-script the day had trained her into: Bed-check reconciles with device logs. No unaccounted door opens after 2200. Motion consistent with scheduled rounds. Med cart secure 2117 to 0552. No anomalies at 0000. She underlined each phrase once, separate little lines that made a fence.

“Do you want the motion download for your file?” she asked them.

Bree shook her head. “We want to know if you believe us.”

“I do,” Mara said. “The devices agree with you. That’s the point of them. They make your work into a shape I can hold.”

Santi pushed his key ring deep into his pocket so it wouldn’t chime and wake anyone and held up his hands like a man showing he had nothing more to carry. “Then we’ll go on to two a.m. like we do.”

They set off again. The corridor took them into itself. Bree’s cart rolled soft and obedient through the seam where old floor met newer. The house breathed through its vents; somewhere, a pipe ticked once as if it approved. In the far room, Mr. Crowley’s lamp was dark. At M‑1, a foot stayed in from the mat’s edge. At N‑4, Ms. Dempsey slept her stone sleep.

Mara stood for a last moment at the nurses’ station and watched the little green LEDs hold their vigil. The logs were an even field. She wrote the time in the corner of her page and slid it under the clip with the rest. The clock above the cork board clicked over to the half hour with an almost polite reluctance. Bree’s initials waited for their next square; Santi’s for his. The night, measured, kept its shape. The line from pharmacy to bedside held without a gap, and she let it.


==================== SCENE 58 ====================
Pre‑dawn made the glass of the funicular house a color between pewter and bruised plum, the salt crust turning the panes milky. The surf below worked like a slow machine; every few breaths, a heavier stroke boomed up the cut in the cliff and turned the air damp against the teeth.

Rourke rubbed his sleeve across a square of glass and peered in. “Nobody home,” he said. “Except whatever you’re hoping is under that floor.”

Mara took the short lanyard from inside her jacket and found the brass tooth with the stamped F. The key was warmer than the air; her thumb had learned it. She put it into the old Yale cylinder under the latch and felt the grit of time give. When she twisted, the whole door replied through its frame in a low, compacted groan, and a ribbon of wet wind barged in around them, carrying iodine and kelp.

“Hold it a second,” Rourke said, putting his hand to the top where the hinge stuck. “Don’t wake the cliff.”

“Nothing sleeps,” she said, but she eased it, shoulder to the jamb, until the catch surrendered quietly.

Inside: a cubicle of plank and bolted steel, the cart cradled at the top of its track like an animal with its legs pulled under. The console crouched to the left with its trio of levers and a deadman pedal capped by a rubber disk scarred with heel marks. Someone had left a coil of rope on a hook, a belay harness with a faded manufacturer tag, a tin of marine grease with a quarter inch of dust. The stenciled warning—AUTHORIZED OPERATORS ONLY—had been touched up with a paint pen. Beneath that, four screws showed a different shine than their neighbors.

Rourke flicked his light on and steadied the beam low to spare their eyes. The light caught a rag of tide table pinned behind the switch panel and the twitch of a spider’s line over the inspection pit. The track dropped away through the viewing slot in a perfect incline, black into the fog eddies.

Mara went to the console. The keyhole there was twin to the door’s, but this one took a smaller profile. She let her fingers walk to it without thinking and found the match. The cylinder turned cleanly. A relay under the panel made a small cluck, intimate as a throat clearing.

“Wait,” Rourke said. “Before you run anything with power.”

“I’m not running it,” she said, and tapped the panel. “I’m opening it.”

She slid her blade under the lip of the faceplate. The screws were recent; they gave up with the easy half‑turn of things that had been asked to perform more than expected. When she lifted the panel away, the smell was warm dust edged with ozone.

Rourke whistled once, through his teeth. “For a house that swears it’s a museum piece.”

The original wiring was cloth‑wrapped and carefully laced, its colors gone to tea and mud. Over it, someone had laid modern vinyl jacket. The new bundles were zip‑tied along the old harness and then, where a factory conduit led to the motor, a stranger decision: the modern line peeled off, drilled through the back of the console casing in a neat hole that had not existed in any design Mara had seen. The burr on the metal still held a faint flash of silver where the drill had been generous.

She pulled a glove on and traced gently. The new cable ran through the hole, across the wall’s inside surface, and down into the gap beside the inspection pit. In the pit, it ducked into a half‑inch conduit painted to match the frame. The conduit did not match any drawing she’d been shown by Owen when they’d locked this place down in the last blow. It headed away from the motor—in the opposite direction of anything a sane mechanic would need here—then caught a ninety and disappeared under the short stair into the storage shelf.

“There,” Mara said.

Rourke angled his light. “And there’s our favorite little black snake.”

The short stair was nothing more than two treads up to a shelf where lifebelts and a coil of cable lay. Mara pushed the belts aside. The shelf wasn’t wood; it was a trap, hinged along the back. The paint line on the hinge was fresh and thin, its brush marks shallow and fast. Someone had been proud of making a thing look like itself.

“Give me a second,” Mara said, and slipped the blade into the seam. The shelf lifted without complaint. Underneath: a narrow landing and the beginning of a descent that the public never used. The service way hugged the cliff inside the building’s wall, vertical to start, then angling into a tight switchback. Rusted ladder rungs were sunk into poured concrete, then modernized halfway down with a steel stringer and steps cut from grating.

The conduit marched beside it, clipped neatly at intervals, always just where a hand would want to rest and not quite low enough to be seen without ducking.

Rourke crouched, breathed the air from below, and made a face. “Clean solder, last month at worst. And that’s not grease. That’s lithium.”

“Makes sense by the ounces,” Mara said. She looked out through the viewing slot. The ocean had crept up the shore to the line where rock began. In places along the bottom of the cliff, the foam already licked at the base of the boathouse pilings like a cat’s tongue. The clock on the console—mechanical, not digital—stood at 4:37, its tick tardy and honest.

“How long until the high?” she asked.

“Twenty‑five,” Rourke said, checking his watch. “Maybe thirty if the wind holds. The path’s going to get hair on its teeth when the cross‑set starts.”

“We don’t have thirty if anything down there’s still dry.” She dropped to the landing and tested a rung with her boot. The metal flexed the way sound flexes: a little, then steady. “You coming?”

“You want me to say no and feel like the adult? Because I can do that,” Rourke said. He scanned the little house, then the track. “We don’t take the cart. We lock the cart in place and we tag the breaker.”

“It’s tagged,” she said, and reached up to pull the key from the console. She set it in her chest pocket and zipped the pocket. “We go by the service stairs. It gets us at the foundation and around the door.”

“And the tide gets us at the ankles if we dally,” he said. “All right. Ground rules. Steps are wet, you call it. We don’t argue on stairs. If that conduit goes hot or hums, we don’t touch it.”

“It’s low‑voltage,” she said.

“Low‑voltage still bites,” he said. “And I am not writing up ‘died of optimism’ on a coastal report.”

The wind pressed a cold palm against the back of Mara’s neck. She took out the slim camera and shot three frames: the opened panel, the new hole, the conduit’s first bend. She angled for the serial plate of the console and caught in the corner of the frame a smear of black on the hinge screw, the kind left when a glove gripped too tight.

“Chain,” Rourke said, holding out a small bag. She dropped the screws into it one by one, the sound tiny as candy. He wrote the time and location on the label, neat, sideways on his thigh. The surf underlined the seconds with insistence.

“Tell me when you’re good,” Mara said.

“I was good at the door,” he said. “I’m just trying to keep us in one piece.”

They went down into the throat of the place. The air narrowed, salt and metal and the old damp that houses grow in their bones. Mara’s light slid along the grating and turned the screws glittering. Below them, the conduit's shadow paired with itself. They descended past a square cutout where the inner wall met the cliff face—a window into rock smeared with old tar, a waveprint of damp. Rourke’s footfall made a hollow shift then held: the stringer here had been replaced, newer steel beneath older bolts. The paint was matte, the way a careful hand tries to hide something the eye would slip on.

“Hold,” Rourke said softly.

Mara stopped with her boot poised above the next tread.

Rourke leaned in and put two fingers to the conduit as if taking a pulse. He didn’t touch; he hovered. “No heat,” he said. “No hum.”

“Someone used it recently,” she said. “But it’s passive now.”

They rounded the corner where the staircase parted the wall and the inside of the funicular house to spill into a side tunnel the width of a door and the height of patience. At the far end of that tunnel, a square of gray light. The boathouse’s under‑deck, ribs of timber over surge water. The light had the color of a coin you find in the street.

Mara crouched to pass. Her jacket scuffed timber; the salt polished everything to a slight slick. Rourke held the light on her boots, then on the next patch of floor, always a hand ahead of where need would fall. The conduit held to the right‑hand wall, then vanished into a drilled hole through the boathouse’s concrete plinth, not the original pour—Mara could see the halo where the patch had been troweled clean.

“What do you bet that dumps into a junction box where nobody thinks to look,” Rourke said.

“I don’t bet,” Mara said. “I open things.”

They came to the last step. The tunnel’s mouth gave onto a narrow ledge under the boathouse, no more than a foot of concrete between wall and water. At the far end of the ledge, a half‑height door set into the masonry, painted the same dull gray as the foundation. Someone had dragged a crab pot against it as camouflage, then thought better and left the pot askew, seaweed dried across it like hair. The door had no knob, only two finger holes cut into it, rings of bare wood showing where use had burned the paint away.

Mara set her palm to the door and felt for the rhythm of the sea. The next wave reached, sucked, fell back. The reach that followed threw spray no higher than her shin. The tide clocked itself in careful, plodding language.

“Window is here,” she said.

“Window is tight,” Rourke said.

She pulled. The door wanted to stick. Rourke shouldered in with her and they gave it a double pull that was more like persuasion than force. It moved, and the smell behind it was newer—plastic, tape, the faint acid ghost of batteries.

Rourke leaned around her. “Bridge of common parts. We have ourselves an access path.”

“Confirmed,” Mara said, and her breath vented white in the low light. The tide breathed back at her feet. The conduit ran on through the little space and down into the boathouse under‑floor.

Rourke looked at the water measuring its next inch and then at her. “We go now or we don’t go until afternoon.”

“We go,” she said.

He nodded once. “Don’t argue on stairs. You first.”

She stepped onto the ledge, put one hand to the wall, and slipped sideways through the door’s throat toward the boathouse guts, the pressure of time at her back like a hand doing her a favor by not shaking.


==================== SCENE 59 ====================
They came up inside the boathouse like divers surfacing into a cavern. The under‑floor was a crawlspace that yawned wider as they sloped up into the main volume: a cathedral of ribs and planks and tarred smells, the center slip gaping black to the harbor where water pulsed and withdrew with the steadiness of a jaw. The wind had a voice in here, but a different one—wood talking to itself, rope answering in little twangs. Rourke’s beam took a second to figure out what mattered. Then it struck a rectangle high on the north wall and threw a spiderweb of shadows.

“Hold,” Mara said. “Back off and give it a sweep.”

He went slow. The wall wasn’t a wall. It was a stage.

Two short‑throw projectors were bolted to a joist, their housings mummified in clear plastic wrap and duct tape, each one angled to bounce off mirrors that hung like clipped wings. The mirrors weren’t glass. They were lightweight—the cheap acrylic kind—hung on ring bolts and balanced with fishing weights so a finger would send them seesawing. The projectors’ power lines ran to a laminated board studded with DC converters and slick blue packs the size of bricks. The packs were hand‑labeled in silver paint pen: T.G. BOATHOUSE A, T.G. SOUTH PATH, T.G. WALK—SPARE. Someone had drawn lightning bolts next to the initials, the way a kid would tag his own gear. Zip ties and cargo netting kept the guts in place, but someone had also built in breakaways: slip‑knots you could yank in a hurry.

Mara lifted her phone and shot from three angles, the first pass wide, the second through her thumbs and forefingers to frame the key nodes. “Theo’s hands,” she said, and angled Rourke’s light closer. “His label style. We have his battery network.”

“Time stamp it,” Rourke said. He knelt and traced the power flow with the tip of his pen, not quite touching. His voice kept to that clipped calm he used in kitchens and morgues. “Chain of custody begins with observation. Don’t move anything we can document still. If the tide buys us thirty minutes we can tag the whole rig.”

Water slapped the pilings, a clean backhand. The sound came up through their boots.

“We don’t have thirty,” Mara said. She was already across the catwalk to a second cluster: a pair of hidden speakers sunk flush into the inside of a piling, screened with louvered slats painted the same flat salt‑gray as the rest, the kind a person’s eye would skip. A black cable disappeared behind a fake knot board hung to look like décor. She lifted the board with two fingers. Behind it, a small amp sat wrapped in neoprene, the kind they sheath cameras with. More silver pen: T.G. DOCKCALL 1. A clipped phrase on blue masking tape—CHILD, LIGHT WIND—was stuck beside the dial.

Rourke swore under his breath. “He recorded a library for the wind. That’s intentionality.”

“Hold a light,” Mara said, not looking away. She shot the speaker cavity, the way the screws had been counter‑sunk and painted; shot the masking tape; shot the patch panel at the amp. She let the phone lag a second on the settings so you could read where the little pots had been set. “You hear that? It’s pushing from somewhere else too.”

Rourke swung the beam to starboard. Along the ceiling rafters: a run of monofilament and nylon cord, some in blaze orange, some in dyed black, fed through eyelets and little brass pulleys. A tug on one near the wall set a mirror yawing, which sent the projector’s test grid sliding. Another tug made a shabby sailcloth square unfurl on the far side of the slip, where no one would expect a screen. Sandbags kept its bottom edge live against a roller, so the sail could be heaved up and down with one pull. Beside the pulley, a chalked set of notations: W1, W2, W3. Someone had used a carpenter’s pencil on the beam.

“Walk me through it,” Rourke said.

“Projectors throw to mirrors. Mirrors throw to screen or to the water—look, that angle? That’s your ghost on the surface.” Mara tugged a line gently and watched the mirror tilt. The water picked up a rectangle of light and shook it like jelly. “Speakers give you a location cue. The sail is your emergent image. Pull W2 and she—whatever she is—appears. Pull W3 and she’s drowned. All on battery. No grid.”

“Document, then arrest,” Rourke said. He squinted at the battery board again. “I need serials. I need capacity labels. I need a continuous shot such that counsel cannot argue a gap.”

From the slip: a hollow clunk. The tide reached the under‑slats, shouldered, took back an inch. The boards above the waterline were damp to the first finger joint.

“Or,” Mara said, “we haul first. This all sits six inches lower than the dock. The tide will flood here before we see it out there. We save what we can, we document on deck.”

“You know what happens when we move its position,” Rourke said. He didn’t raise his voice. He didn’t have to. “I get a courtroom exhibit that says ‘these officers rearranged a contrivance they then claim shows intent.’”

“We get a pile of salt‑fried plastic that once was a contrivance,” Mara shot back. She took one step onto the cross‑grating. Her boot slid; she caught a beam with her palm and felt wet. It tasted like copper when she sucked the sting; damn splinters. “We can fix position with marks. We can laser distance. We can chalk footprints and then pull.”

“I am chalk and bag,” Rourke said. He unrolled his kit with practiced speed: evidence flags—tiny neon numbered tents—wax pencils, a small reel tape measure, a Tyvek tag book. He dropped to a knee at the edge of the slip and pulled a carpenter’s crayon and made a bracket on the plank under the battery board: R1. He shot the crayon next to his hand in the frame, then the same mark with the battery board above it, then a slow pan to the projectors and their hung mirrors, giving the geometry context. “Flag one: battery array. Flag two: projector mount. Flag three: mirror A. Flag four: mirror B. Flag five: pulley panel. Read me labels as I point.”

“Battery array: T.G. BOATHOUSE A,” Mara said, snapping. She liked the way the words came clean under pressure, like bone under a knife. “Voltage written is twelve, capacity forty‑eight amp hours. DC converter reads brand PAXI, eighteen volt out, two amps. Wire gauge is fourteen. Tie wraps are Home Depot orange. The case is a Pelican knockoff, likely from the same supplier as the amp cover.”

The water bumped the sail. It belly‑danced against the roller. The screen shivered; the mirror went jittery again.

Rourke tossed her the roll of Tyvek tags. “Initial with the time. Don’t seal. We mark and shoot; we don’t bag until after. I need an overview after every new flag.”

“Copy.” She looped a tag through a battery pack handle without lifting the pack. MARA 19.06.12/04:17. She whispered as she wrote, a long‑practiced little chant that made time stay honest in her head. “Tagging the amp. Tagging the speaker cavity. Tagging the knot board.”

Rourke laid a tape measure from the pelican case to the edge of the beam. “Twenty‑eight inches. Write it on the chalk mark.”

She did. The chalk squeaked. The tide made a sound like a throat clearing.

“Rourke?” she said, looking to the slip.

He looked too. The center channel had gone from black to slate. It was coming fast. They had minutes.

He flicked his choice between them like a coin and caught it without show. “Rapid documentation,” he said. “No narratives. No debate, no artistry. Wide, tags in frame, serials, connections, distances. Then we pull mirrored surfaces and power sources first. Speakers last. We secure in the library on plastic sheeting. You get receipts and an evidence log. I get photos transferred to the secure drive from your phone before anyone breathes on it.”

“Copy,” Mara said. “Go.”

They moved. The boathouse answered.

She did the wide: whole wall, floor, ceiling, tide, their boots—everything that made a geometry. She used her hand as scale beside the chalk marks. She took the projector mount, the bolt heads, the washers. She found the disguised extension behind a false vergeboard; it had seaside grime applied with a paintbrush to make it live there. She listed it as extension one. Rourke called numbers and she echoed them back: “Flag six. Flag seven. Flag eight.” He reached, but only to point; he didn’t lay a finger on what he didn’t have to. When it came to serials he bent with the phone and zoomed so the little engraved digits shone.

“Pack marked T.G. WALK—SPARE,” she said. “Serial 77B451. Pack marked T.G. SOUTH PATH, serial 77B472. These came in a lot.”

“Document the paint pen,” Rourke said. “Stroke style, letter forms.”

“You want his handwriting expert to cry later?” Mara asked.

“I want his handwriting expert to testify,” he said. “Next.”

She leaned to shoot the pulley cluster. The chalked codes up there were rough but consistent. Theo had catalogued his own ghost.

“W1, W2, W3,” she said, shooting. “Add smaller notations—X with circle. That must be home position.”

“We’ll test after,” Rourke said. “Don’t pull now.”

A wave shouldered in and licked the bottom rung of the ladder. The sound changed in that instant from wood to throat to water on metal.

“Time check: four‑twenty‑one,” Mara said.

“Make it quick,” Rourke said. He pulled a handful of evidence flags and stuck them like prayer markers: one at the speaker cavity, one at the line pulley, one on the sail roller. He wrote the numbers on the Tyvek with his thumbnail as desk. “We have nine, ten, eleven. That’s enough for one wall.”

Mara hooked two fingers in the monofilament to steady it for a macro shot of the knot. The knot was a bowline with a safety; Theo wasn’t dumb. The safety was a tiny zip tie cut with flush cutters so there was no burr. She shot the cut. The phone stuttered with damp and adrenaline grease on her fingers and she wiped it on her jacket and kept going.

Rourke looked at the water over her shoulder. He didn’t say anything. He didn’t need to. The sail bumped her thigh now.

“We’re good,” Mara said. She stepped back. “We have enough to establish. We pull live elements.”

“Mirrors first,” Rourke agreed. “You unclip, I tag.”
