She stood on the rim of the slip and reached above her head. The mirror’s ring bolt was finger‑tight, no wrench marks. Smart: designed for speed. She eased the bolt, kept the mirror in plane so it didn’t catch wind, and let Rourke slip a Tyvek through the ring: ROURKE 19.06.12/04:23, FLAG THREE REMOVED. He stuck the tag to itself with a piece of blue tape and wrote mirror A in block letters.

“Do not drop,” he said, not because he thought she would but because a thing was safer when it had a sentence around it.

“I’m not married to that outcome,” she said. She stepped back and handed it to him. He set it against the wall away from the water with a wedge of scrap wood to keep it upright.

Mirror B fought her—its bolt was salt‑seized. She braced, but gently. The wave came up and said hurry. She got her multi‑tool out and used the pliers just enough to break the crust and then her fingers again. She didn’t want tool marks that someone could later say were hers, not his.

“Mark that you used pliers,” Rourke said without looking.

She nodded and said to the phone’s video, “Pliers used to crack corrosion, light pressure, no marring.” The mirror came loose. She exhaled without spending the air, just enough to say thanks without making it religious.

They went to the battery board. The water lapped over her boot toe and kept it for a heartbeat before letting it go. It made her skin think of teeth. Rourke laid a trash bag on the deck and threw another over his shoulders like a cape.

“I’ll hold the board,” he said. “You cut zip ties, never the wires. Record each cut.”

She ran her nail under a tie, identified the head, and snipped through with the small scissors on her multi‑tool. “One. Two. Three.” Each snip had a photo with the scissors tip still in frame. The ties curled up, bright and useless. The battery array sighed like it had been waiting to fall. Rourke slid his hands in under the Pelican mimic and lifted with his legs.

“Tag reads R1 with photo,” he said, breath even. “I have it. I am moving it to the dry zone.”

She backed for him, flashlight at his feet, and kept recording. The tide said yes and tried to come at once, a blind dog wanting in. The board that had been dry at the start looked like a tongue now, slick and ready to taste anything you dropped.

“Speakers,” she said. “We leave in place until decks are clear.”

“Projectors,” Rourke said. “One—two—”

“Hold,” she said. She shot the mounts again before unscrewing—one quarter turn each so the heads were still nested in their cups for the photo, then coming clean into her hand with no scrape. “Marking that I will not rotate beyond initial positions prior to removal. See witness.” She swung the phone to Rourke’s face. He gave it the same flat yes he gave a warrant.

“Bag the screws,” he said. He opened a small envelope, held it with the corner triangles splayed, and she let each screw ping in with the specificity of coins. He wrote the frame number on the envelope with his thumbnail as desk again.

They stacked the projectors by the mirrors. The water clapped its appreciation and came up another inch.

“Rope pulls,” Mara said. “We cut the lines at the tails after documenting their knot structures. We leave the pulleys up and cut ties so each fall line retains its position relative to the beam. If anything survives after we’re done, I’ll buy it a drink.”

“Make your cuts low,” Rourke said. “Leave the beam clean.”

She shot each knot, traced it with her finger in the air for the camera—a ghost of a knot made of gesture—and snipped below. She kept the tails and rolled them into the envelope with their flags. She wrote tail lengths on the Tyvek tags because somewhere, sometime, someone would ask and she meant to have the answer.

The wind gusted. Out on the harbor a buoy bell struck twice and then found its pattern again. The boathouse took the gust, shrugged, and let it go. The big door shuddered in its track.

They paused. Not for drama. For the count of their own breaths.

“Checklist,” Rourke said. “Mirrors A and B removed and tagged. Projectors one and two removed and tagged. Battery board removed and tagged. Amp unbagged, photographed, retagged, still in situ. Speakers in situ, tagged. Pulley lines cut, tails retained. Sail roller flagged, in situ. Labels photographed.”

“We have Theo’s initials on power packs,” Mara said. “We have his hand. We have the whole event environment documented before removal.”

“Then we pull the rest,” Rourke said. He looked at the water like it was a schedule, not a threat. “After we move this to a high table.”

She nodded. Her teeth had started to chatter, not with cold but with a body’s idea of how to release adrenaline. She made them stop by saying the next thing. “I’ll ferry to the library. Plastic sheeting is rolled under the card catalog.”

“I will keep a camera on me the entire trip,” he said. “We do not break custodial chain by stepping off without visual continuity. You will narrate, and when we get there we will photograph the staging area itself before we set anything down.”

“Copy,” she said. Her hand landed on the battery pack with T.G. SOUTH PATH and stayed there a heartbeat. The paint pen had left tiny ridges. She lifted it and the weight surprised her. Not heavy—just dense. Evidence had its own gravity.

They turned and began the awkward dance of extraction: Mara moving backward with the pack, Rourke carrying the mirror like a fragile barnacle, both of them keeping the tags turned out. The tide grumbled under the boards, but it didn’t reach further than their ankles, not yet. The opening they’d come through looked smaller now with gear in their hands. Rourke braced it with his foot, kept his shoulder in the door, and let her pass.

“After shots come first,” he said as she slid by. “Then we tug the speakers, then the amp. We will come back for the sail last.”

“After shots come first,” Mara repeated. The boathouse air tasted like pennies and kelp. Her phone buzzed as it wrote a backup to the cloud she’d set, and she felt the little lift inside like the moment you clip a specimen slide and know it will not leave your hand.

“Let’s move,” Rourke said.

They moved.


==================== SCENE 60 ====================

The dock lurched; the word operator snapped out of her. They both looked down. Water punched the gaps between planks and stayed there, sloshing across her boot toes. The bay had been a bared set of teeth all afternoon; now it clamped.

“Back,” Rourke said. His voice didn’t lift but it shortened. “We don’t commit to the crossing. High table first.”

She pivoted and glanced through the ribbed mouth of the boathouse. Inside: the speaker pair perched under the rafters like black gulls; the sail roller strapped and flagged; the amp—squat and ugly—still on its milk crate; the pulley tails coiled and tagged; the second mirror leaning on a sawhorse. Their neat little universe, labeled and rational.

A slamming noise from deeper under the dock, the kind of hollow drum that says something big has come unmoored and found a new friend to hit: the tide.

Mara took one step toward the doorway and the tide took another into the house. It poured over the threshold like a crowd that had waited too long. Cold brine rolled around her shins, then her knees, bringing with it eelgrass and an ancient smell of copper and mud. The tag on the battery pack went dark.

Rourke planted the mirror against the door jamb and reached for her elbow with his free hand. “We retreat. We raise. Now.”

“We lose the amp,” she shouted. Her voice felt ridiculous in the bass of the water. “It’ll short.”

“It’s already in the drink. Go.”

She edged sideways until her hip met the high worktable they’d cleared for staging. She dragged the battery up and set it there. Rourke snapped a photo and then another, quick little gunshots of light. The table creaked but held.

Inside, a new sound crawled into the room: a long zipper being pulled very fast. The sail roller. The strap they’d run through its buckle had shifted. The roller sagged, then twitched, then spun as the surge got a hand on the free canvas. Gauze unrolled from it, ghost-white sheets whipping like jellyfish bells, slapping the water and then slipping under. Their neat flag—a red strip of Tyvek—tore loose and spun away like a fish.

Mara waded for it.

“Mara,” Rourke said.

“The sail is the rig,” she threw over her shoulder. “If we can—”

“The rig is documented,” he said, and he was up next to her now, one hand on her collar, not dragging, not yanking, but there, a line in the water. “You will not be the reason we call for a medevac.”

She stopped because he said it like an order he’d given before and because the cold had found the hinge of her spine. She put a hand flat on the side of the sail roller, splinters like teeth under her palm, and pushed. It didn’t move. The current had it, a thing with a fist.

The amp toppled. The milk crate went sideways and rode its crossbar like a man surfing badly for one, two feet, then the whole block swallowed and rolled. The amp rose, exhaled a bubbling gulp, and sank again, cables squirming out of its ports like eels.

“Document,” Rourke said again, already stepping back for the mirror. He got one shot—Mara saw the strobe light the room like heat lightning—before the mirror took a slap from a piece of timber and skated. He swore, reached, caught its frame with his forearm, hissed when the edge bit him through his jacket. He levered it upright and slid it up the door jamb like lifting a painting off a nail.

“What do you need right now?” he said, voice even, eyes not. Blood ran down his wrist to the cuff of his glove, too bright, too cheerful in all that green.

“The smalls,” she said immediately. “Label packets, the remote. That pelican case.”

“The case is on the sawhorse,” he said. “The remote was on the amp.”

“Right,” she said, and didn’t look at the amp again.

They moved like the novice version of a practiced team: him planting things on the high table, then photographing them in situ, then as soon as she set them down, in a new in situ; her harvesting anything within arm’s reach that wasn’t screwed to the wall. Cables went around her shoulders like scarves. The bag of label strips went under her chin. A roll of gaffer tape banged her sternum like a bell.

A wave puffed the big door inward and shoved it along its track a few inches. It stuck, then jumped, then kept going as the water found its leverage. The gap widened. New water spilled in over old.

“Block,” Rourke said. He planted his shoulder and hip against the door, boots slewing. “Jamb, anything. That two-by.”

Mara caught the two-by more by sound than by sight—the clatter as it pinwheeled off the saw table. She waded into its path and let it hit her thighs and then let her thighs bounce it back so she could get both hands around it. She brought it to Rourke and jammed it up under the lower track where he pointed. The door groaned, flexed, then set against it like a stubborn horse finally at a hitching post.

“Good,” he said. “You good?”

“Cold,” she said. “Angry.”

“Copy,” he said. “Maintain. Mirror two.”

She clocked the second mirror still on its sawhorse, gilded edge flecked with salt, hose water from some other century drying in patches across it. She didn’t know how or why Theo had chosen these mirrors—old, too ornate, too tender for this—but she knew they were part of the trick, and that if she could get the lensing and the angles onto a schematic later, she could show anyone how a ghost walks out of the air.

“Tag’s on the left,” she said. “Photographing.” She held her phone over it, thumbed the shutter. The sound felt obscene and ordinary.

The sail roller unthreaded from its brackets and swung. For a moment it was a pendulum, obliging, rhythmic, almost calm. Then the mounting screws tore clean out of their pilot holes with a shriek and the whole cylinder swung wide and cracked against the beam. The gauze snapped like a flag. The roller dropped into the flood with a decisive thud and vanished toward the open bay door under the dock like a whale going down.

“Gone,” she said uselessly.

“Documented,” he said. He wasn’t lying to comfort her; he was saying the difference between evidence and a story you tell later.

She laughed once, a bark. A plank ricocheted off the doorframe and caught her knee. She cursed in a way that would have made their mother slap her, if she’d had a mother who slapped. She put a hand on the sawhorse and the leg sank an inch as the silt under it liquefied. Everything in the room was deciding what it wanted to be—a thing or a float.

“Batteries,” Rourke said. He had the mirror under his arm, ridiculous and perfect, gilt catching dull light. “How many with initials.”

“Two in hand,” she said, tapping the pack on the high table. She pointed with her chin to the crate at the threshold. “Two more in the crate. One in the far corner. The rest were blank.”

“Prioritize marked,” he said. “Then pull one blank for working demonstration. Then we quit.”

“Copy,” she said, because arguing with him when he used the word quit like that would be a stupid use of breath.

They made a line. She ferried, he lifted, he shot, she narrated: “Battery pack one—white paint pen T.G. SOUTH PATH—placed on staging. Battery pack two—white paint pen T.G. NORTH PATH—placed on staging.” The far-corner pack had tried to make a run for it and lodged under the lip of an old dinghy hull, webs of spider and kelp tying it into a new family. She crawled for it over the slippery floorboards, hand over hand using the cross-braces like a ladder, the water riding up her arms to her shoulders and then sliding back down again as the room lifted with the surge.

Rourke’s voice came from above her like a rope. “In frame. Keep your face up.”

She did. She got a palm on the edge of the dinghy and a palm on the plastic casing, felt the cold of it bite like glass. She twisted it, it jammed, she worked the tag loose first because she had learned—today, exactly today—to do that before she did anything else. The tag tore, and she said “Tag torn; photo of tear,” and then she bit the tag in her teeth to keep it from going under and wrestled the pack free.

By the time she stood with it, the water was chest-high on her and the boxier shapes—amp, crate, God knew what—were bumping against her thighs and then ghosting away. Something with a handle smacked her knuckles and she swore again and Rourke laughed once, too, a flat sound, just because hearing her swear like that and not hearing her say ow seemed like air coming back into the room.

They hauled the third pack onto the high table. He shot it. She set the torn tag beside it and held her wet glove off to the side so the water wouldn’t pool into frame. She felt like a butcher trying to keep blood off a wedding cake.

“Enough,” Rourke said in a voice that didn’t entertain alternatives.

“Blank battery,” she said.

“Enough,” he repeated, and the door flexed behind him so hard the wood grain flared like muscle. “We cannot swim with this table. Move.”

She didn’t argue. She grabbed the pelican case and slung the strap across her body. He took the mirror and his phone and moved like someone who had carried fragile things through narrow rooms his whole career. They shoved the door along its hacked wedge, let the gap widen to let them out, then stepped onto the dock that had changed from a walkway to an animal.

The dock rose under her feet, then dropped like an elevator in a bad building. The rope along the edge sawed in its cleats. The wind felt personal. A buoy bell out in the chop gave a single warped note that sounded like a lie compared to the real music happening under the boards.

“Library,” he said. “You first. I’m on your shoulder.”

She started down the dock and then stopped. The crate with the non-initialed batteries had popped free and bobbed like a stubborn suitcase in the boathouse doorway. A wave pushed it into the jamb and it slid backward again.

She looked at Rourke. His jaw was set. He shook his head once.

“We have what names it,” he said. “We do not drown for a better exhibit.”

The anger came again, clean and bright, and she let it stand in her chest with everything else. She thread her fingers into the rope and moved. The dock made a series of decisions and she went along with them and did not fall. Behind her, Rourke called out each step into his camera—not poetry, not even explanation, just facts: “Departing boathouse with three marked battery packs, one pelican case, one mirror, label packs. Amp in water. Sail roller lost. Speakers… ”

They both turned their heads without meaning to. The speakers held on longer than she would have bet. Then a knot gave up somewhere, and one black box tumbled off its nest and smacked the water hard enough to send up a slap of spray like applause. The second swung, hesitated, and then went with it, twin coffins, neat in their fall.

“Speakers in water,” Rourke said.

“Speakers in water,” she said, and kept moving.

They made the lip where dock met boardwalk. The boardwalk had the decency to be fixed to pilings bigger than her torso. It felt like land even though it wasn’t. She shoved through the gate they’d sandbagged earlier and shouldered down the gravel path past the funicular house, the pelican case thudding her spine, the mirror scraping bark off a cedar. They cut into the library, a room that had become a fortress only because they had declared it one, and set the saved things down on a plastic sheeting that gleamed like new ice.

It was quieter in the library. The storm still sang around the windows, but the room had a habit of keeping secrets, and it kept theirs for the moment. She peeled her gloves off and shook her fingers, trying to feel, trying to get ink back into them. Rourke’s bleeding had stopped; the cut looked clean, the kind that means you got lucky.

He took a last shot of the table, then panned to her face. “State it,” he said.

She looked at the saved pile: three batteries with Theo’s initials. The mirror, salted and ridiculous and telling. The pelican case, which she opened and found full of cable adapters, a spare SD card in a jeweler’s bag, and a folded map with THEO written in the corner in that same careful paint-pen hand. She held the bag up and he photographed it. She said the thing.

“We lost the amp, the sail roller, and both speakers to the surge,” she said. “We retained three power packs labeled by Theo, one mirror used in the visual rig, the control case with SD and adapters, labels, documentation. We have enough to tie this to him.”

“Say your full name for the record,” he said, and she did. He said his, and he killed the video with his thumb.

She heard herself breathing again, rough in the hush. She leaned against the card catalog and felt the old wood soft under the plastic sheeting, damp creeping because damp creeps. The anger had nowhere new to go and settled into rails along her ribs.

Rourke nudged the mirror upright against the table leg and checked the edges for chips. “You did not go after the sail,” he said finally. It was as close as he would come to praise.

“I wanted to break the ocean with my hands,” she said.

“You chose not to,” he said. He met her eyes. “Which is why you’re here to be angry with me.”

She huffed a breath that would have been a laugh if she had more oxygen. “We have enough,” she said again, not to reassure him and not to convince herself, but because it was true and that was the only kind of sentence that held in weather like this.

He nodded once. “We have enough.” He tapped the paint-pen initials with the back of his knuckle, soft. “And enough is good evidence.”

Behind the glass, the storm saw its own face in the mirror and didn’t like it. The library windows rattled. The buoy bell rang two notes and then found a third, irregular, wrong. The boathouse and dock kept their own music, out of sight now, crashing and muted. They stood in the middle of the library floor with salt drying on their pants and recorded, without ceremony, that control had met water and water, as it always does, had not cared.


==================== SCENE 61 ====================
The library’s reading room had been reduced again to a room with a table and a door that clicked shut. Plastic sheeting still lapped the wainscoting where the squall found every seam. The makeshift interview light was a clamp lamp with a daylight bulb, hot on its stem, making a bright circle in which the objects of the hour sat: the pelican case open like a jaw; the mirror propped against a leg in a corner where it could not cut anyone; three brick batteries with THEO stenciled in paint pen; a roll of gaffer tape; a quart bag labeled SD—SPARE and the little jewelry envelope inside; and a sheaf of printouts in a binder clip that made a thwack when it was moved.

Rourke had dragged a second table into service and pressed it against the first to make a longer, more official surface. He had laid a clean painter’s drop over both and numbered the corners with Sharpie the way he always did: one, two, three, four. He had set the recorder on two. He had taken his jacket off because it was still shedding salt. He wore a black polo that had lost its collar shape and a silver badge that didn’t glint so much as sit there like a fact.

Mara had not changed her clothes. She had rung them out and hung them over a library cart in the hallway and then put them back on. Salt grit whispered when she folded her elbows on the edge of the table. The smell of wet paper was better than rot, worse than anything else.

Theo came in with his hood up and took it off when he saw there was nowhere soft to sit. He didn’t drip; he steamed. His hair matted in little V shapes on his forehead. He looked at the lamp, at the recorder, at the batteries as if they were an audience. You could feel him adding columns up in his head—what do they have, what can I say, where is the edge—and exchanging one fear for another as he did it.

“Sit,” Rourke said. He didn’t say please. He didn’t say the chair’s damp, sorry. He didn’t say anything but sit.

Theo sat. He set his hands palms down. He looked at his own knuckles like they might volunteer something.

“For the record,” Rourke said, and hit the red square. “Late morning. Temporary room, library. Present: Sergeant Rourke. Consultant Mara Linden. Interviewee: Theo Hennessey.” He slid a signed consent form across. “State your name and whether you understand you’re being recorded.”

Theo’s eyes went to Mara, not the paper. She didn’t move. He signed his name where the X wanted it, the careful hand that had made the paint-pen letters on the batteries.

“Theo Hennessey,” he said. “I understand.” His voice did the thing voices do in small rooms: it came back at him, thinner.

Rourke clipped the consent to the flight-log stack. “We’ll start with the easy items. Identify these.” He tapped a knuckle, the way he’d tapped before, a soft drumbeat: the pelican case, the mirror, the batteries. “Are these yours?”

Theo looked down for a performance beat too long. He took a breath so measured it had to be deliberate. “They’re gear for the salvage rig,” he said. He kept his focus on the hard line of the table edge. “We’ve been trying to get eyes on the shoreline. That’s everybody’s gear.”

“Your initials,” Mara said. She didn’t lean. She kept her voice level, because the steadier thing always makes the splinters show. “On three power packs. On the inside flap of the case. On the fold of this map. Same white paint pen, same open-tail H. You wrote THEO three times, and on the map you added your full name in the corner like you were saving a seat.”

Theo’s jaw flickered. “You asked me to label things.” He glanced at Rourke as if the sergeant might sponsor that story. “Chain of custody, right? That’s what you keep saying.”

“Good,” Rourke said. “Chain of custody. Say it again, because you’ll hear it a lot today.” He lifted the binder clip’s belly and fanned the printouts without letting them slide into salt. “We pulled your flight logs from your controller and synced with the app. Time stamps. GPS tracks. Battery cycles. I had Owen identify the waypoints along the south bluff and over the boathouse. These are your logs.”

Theo’s mouth tipped toward a smile that wasn’t. “Everybody’s app logs the same way.”

“Scan code tied to your controller serial,” Mara said. She slid the top page across just enough for him to see the device ID. “The one registered under yahtzee.theo at a black dot mail. The one you used to upload the child voice clip on the ninth.”

He looked up so fast the chair squealed. “No,” he said. “No, that wasn’t—”

“The track over the boundary fence,” Mara said, and put a fingertip on the thin line on the paper. “The hover at six meters while the audio plays. Then the quick arc to the cove and back to the maintenance bay. Six minutes, thirty seconds. The camera was on, Theo.” She put the jewelry envelope down next to his hand and let it sit there. “You know what was on the card.”

He tried silence like it was a door he might shoulder through. When that didn’t open, he adopted exasperation. “People were already on the fence. They came because of Cass. Everybody knows that. I ran the drone to see where they were, where they might go if the tide pushed—”

“You played a track that said Help me,” Mara said. “A child’s voice. In a storm. To move people to a fence you weren’t going to open. You did that.”

He spread his hands, showing pale crescents of damp under each nail. “It was ambient,” he said. “We have ambient files. It’s not like—”

“Ambient,” Rourke repeated, flat. He set the stack aside and pulled the bagged SD from the pelican case, held it at eye level between thumb and forefinger. “On this card is a folder marked ambi_rye. In that folder is a subfolder marked KidsDay. File name helpme_take3.wav. Creation date is the afternoon Cass was in here cutting B-roll audio. You ripped her voice track out and pitched it up. You layered the water from the east stairwell and a bell and you looped it. You named it helpme_take3 because the first two takes clipped and you wanted it clean.”

Theo’s cheeks went the color of a healed bruise. “That’s not illegal,” he said, and the sentence had too many edges on it. “It’s a beach. It’s air. You can play whatever you—”

“It’s not a question of illegal yet,” Rourke said. “It’s a question of what you did and whether you’re going to keep pretending you didn’t.”

The room had a sound the recorder wouldn’t catch: the fabric of the drop cloth shifting under the table’s weight and the breath of three people scaling itself down. The storm nosed the mullions and made the panes complain. A drip found metal and counted.

Mara tapped the mirror’s taped corner. “Let’s talk about the cistern,” she said. “Because the drown that wasn’t, and the hose, and the cut on the temple that wasn’t from a fall. You set a scene, Theo.”

He laughed, short, like biting an olive and hitting pit. “I’m not—this is ridiculous. I don’t go down there. Ask Owen. It’s his realm. Pumps and slime and whatever else he lets grow in that pit. I don’t—”

“You were in the kitchen at two twelve,” Mara said. “You keyed the maintenance bay at one fifty-seven. Your phone pinged both because cell towers don’t care who’s doing errands. Your drone battery four,” she slid the leftmost brick forward with one finger, “ran a short test flight across the north gully at two-oh-five and then sat on the prep table next to the mop sink until three. The kitchen cam would show that if someone hadn’t unplugged the NVR.”

Theo’s head went sideways like a gull looking for handouts. “The power was out.”

“The NVR has a battery,” Mara said. “Which you know because you put the battery in last month.” She didn’t smile. “You signed the work order we had to give to the insurance guy, Theo. Your full name. Open-tail H.”

Rourke let that sit a beat. Then he reached under the table and set down a clear bag with a strip of gaffer tape inside it, black and salt-sanded. “Kitchen tape,” he said. “Edges match the roll in your case. We cut a sample. That’s what this other bag is.” He held up a smaller one. “We will send it to the lab and a man in a lab coat will have feelings about adhesives. In the meantime, I am very interested in why the tape on the cistern hatch has fibers from a blue neoprene cuff.”

Theo’s eyes did a loop—mirror, table, door, nowhere—and landed on Mara’s cuffs. Hers were black. He swallowed. “It’s a kitchen,” he said, stubborn small. “Everyone tapes things. The whole place is taped together.”

“You staged the body,” Mara said. Not a question.

He lifted his hands, dropped them, set them, made little adjustments as if he were aligning something only he could see. Then he blew air out through his nose and stopped. “It wasn’t—it wasn’t like that,” he said. He looked at the space in front of Rourke’s badge instead of his eyes. “You’re making it sound like I had a plan. There wasn’t a plan.”

“What was there,” Rourke said.

Theo’s mouth went wet and he wiped it with the back of his hand like a kid. “There was a mess,” he said. “There was everything happening at once, and Julian saying we had to control optics, we had to—” He shut that down, too late. He tried a different door. “You seen what this place makes out of rumor. You heard how fast a bell turns into a ghost.” His voice went up on ghost as if the word still held magic. “It was already spinning. I just—I thought if people were looking at the wrong thing for a little while, we could—”

“Put a body in a cistern,” Mara said. “Run a hose to fake the drown marks. Bleed water onto the floor because you forgot the drain slope, and call it tide. Let the house do the lying for you.”

Theo looked at her and something mean in him woke up, grateful to have a job. “You like the lie,” he said. “You live for the reveal. You wanted it to be the picture behind the wall. You wanted—”

“I wanted the person in the cistern not to be a person,” she said, and the sentence came out without any of the extra he expected. “But they were.”

He had nothing for that. He looked down into his own empty hands and the silvering callus on his ring finger where a pair of scissors had lived for a summer.

Rourke slid the flight logs back until they touched the batteries. “Walk me through it,” he said. “Words. Not moods. Not optics.”

Theo inhaled like a diver. “Okay,” he said. “Okay. The drone audio—fine. I pulled the track. I layered it. I ran it when Cass was boosting posts because we needed a visual. It worked. People came to the fence and took videos and that meant somebody would call it in and we could say look, look, there’s interest, the foundation will—” He knuckled his eye. “I shouldn’t have used her file. I shouldn’t have done a kid. It was messy. I—”

“The cistern,” Rourke said.

“After,” Theo said. “After we found—after Owen opened it up and she was in there and there was a minute where nobody knew what they were looking at, and then there were five minutes with everybody talking at once.” He wiped his hand down his face again, but it didn’t change anything. “Julian said freeze it. He said don’t let anybody move anything. He said I was not to touch. I didn’t, not then. I swear. We shut the kitchen. You did that,” he said to Mara, like an offering. “You taped it. And then later, when the tide came up again and the floor started taking water, I went in to check the drains and the hatch had shifted and I saw the—” He let the sentence escape. “Everything felt wrong. It looked wrong. The way she was—this is going to sound stupid, but it looked weird, like a magic trick mid-act. So I—”

“So you finished the trick,” Mara said.

He nodded. Small. Miserable. Relieved to use the metaphor. “I made it read,” he said. “I put the hose where people would expect a hose. I pulled the hand a little so it would show. I taped the hatch so it would look like someone had taped it before, when they were—so it would have a tape story. I did not put her there,” he said, launching the sentence like a lifeboat. “I did not—”

“You moved a dead woman to match the idea in your head,” Rourke said. “And you did it with your cuffs shedding fibers and your gloves wet, and now the timing is a mess and the photographs are a mess and you’ve bought yourself into all of it.”

Theo’s shoulders came up and stayed, like he could put his head back into his body that way. “I thought you would know,” he said to Mara, hoarse. “I thought you’d look and say this is story, not crime. I thought you’d see that.”

“I did,” she said. “But it didn’t erase the crime.”

He nodded again, a dog accepting a leash.

Rourke picked up one of the batteries and switched it in his hand so the initials faced Theo. “Who labeled these.”

“I did,” Theo said.

“Who programmed the audio flight.”

“I did.”

“Who set the cistern to read as drown.”

“I did,” he said, quiet.

Rourke clicked the battery back on the table in the spot it had been on the painter’s drop, like a puzzle piece put back into its outline. He reached into his pocket and came out with the neat bundle that looked like a bracelet until it didn’t. “You’re going to stand,” he said. “You’re going to turn and put your hands on the table where I can see them.”

Theo didn’t do the theater thing. He didn’t shout about process or rights or how he would talk later. He stood and put his palms on the painter’s drop and left two handprints in damp that darkened and then lightened again as air moved. He bowed his head. He let his shoulders move in and out once, twice. When the cuffs went on, they made the simple sound they always made—metal on bone, ratchet on tooth—and Theo said Ow, not like a protest, just like pain.

Rourke read it to him. The words were not ceremonial here, not marble. They were practical and complete. When he was done, he tapped the recorder off, bagged the SD card like he was settling it to sleep, and looked at Mara. “You want a minute,” he said, but it wasn’t a question.

She looked at Theo. “You did not put her there,” she said, without softening. “You worked very hard to tell me a story you thought I would like more than the truth. I like the truth,” she said. “It stays put.”

Theo lifted his head like he might say something back, something sharp or clever that would make him feel like himself again. Nothing came. He looked at the mirror against the table leg, saw a flash of his own cuffed hands in it, and then looked away. He nodded once. “Yeah,” he said. “Okay.”

Rourke opened the door. The hallway was a strip of cooler air and the sound of the storm, still testing every window in the building. They walked out into it: the sergeant steady, the man in cuffs blinking, the woman behind them stacking the evidence in the order she wanted it later, in the order that would make sense. The library clock, the one that had stopped twice that season and started up again when someone thumped the case, said twelve eighteen. The light was a color you couldn’t name. The room felt bigger with one less person in it, and louder with the recorder off. The handprints on the painter’s drop dried to nothing. The batteries sat in their labeled corner, white letters right where they were supposed to be.


==================== SCENE 62 ====================

They did not go far—just across the hallway to the long table Mara had been using as an intake station since the squall began. The lamp there hummed steadily, a reliable rectangle of light amid the storm’s dull percussion on the old windows. Rourke angled Theo into the chair closest to the corner—wall at his back, no exit behind him. He clicked the cuffs’ double locks with his thumbnail so they wouldn’t ratchet tighter if Theo shifted.

“Sit.” Rourke’s tone left no air in it.

Theo sat. The chair squeaked in a tired, resigned way, like it had listened to apologies before.

Mara pulled a fresh chain-of-custody log from the stack and wrote the case number at the top, the neat block printing that had marched in rows all day: incident, date, location. She slid on clean nitrile and glanced at the table where she’d laid out the gear earlier: the Pelican case with the punctured foam, three gimbal arms, two microSD wallets, a spool of invisible fishing line, a compact Bluetooth amp, a dented cone speaker still wet at one seam from salt air.

Rourke took out his phone, tethered the county radio app to the house Wi‑Fi that Owen had bullied back to life, and pressed the transmit square. “Unit Seven to Desk.”

The delay was three crackles and the storm’s breath. “Desk,” a woman answered, bored but competent.

“Field arrest, one male. Name Theo Atkinson. Charges to open: criminal trespass, fraud under color—yes, the entertainment one— and interference with law enforcement. Requesting temporary hold for booking, property impound with technical items. Weather’s a factor.”

“Copy. Booking packet in your inbox. You want transport, you’ll wait.”

“Understood.”

He set the phone down, then pulled the little aluminum clipboard he used for field bookings, the one with a scored surface from years of ballpoint impressions. “Full name,” he said, even though he’d just transmitted it.

Theo looked up, blinked like he’d had sand thrown at him. “Theo Graham Atkinson.”

“Date of birth.”

He gave it. Rourke wrote, pausing only to glance at the cuffs to make sure they weren’t marking skin.

“Address.”

Theo gave a PO box and then, under look, the physical address that matched the contractor parking ticket from two months back. He kept his voice flat, dehydrated of charm.

“Occupation.”

Theo opened his mouth as if to say something elaborate—creative technologist, experiential designer—but what came out was “Freelance.”

“Good,” Rourke said without warmth. He slid the booking form toward Mara to photograph for redundancy and went to work on the property table. He dropped a Faraday sleeve on the wood and nodded at the laptop.

Mara unspooled a length of antistatic bubble, lifted the laptop by its edges, shut it without waking it, and eased it into the black bag. She read the serial aloud. Rourke repeated it for the recorder and for the log.

“Pelican case, drone and accessories,” Mara said, turning the foam to the light. “Serial number on the frame is etched—QF-7132, matching the lading on the box he shipped last month. Three flight batteries marked with white paint, A, B, D.”

“Where’s C,” Rourke asked, as if he were inquiring after a cousin.

Theo’s eyes flicked. “Dead. Haven’t used it in weeks.”

Mara wrote: Battery C missing. She didn’t look at Theo. She keyed off the smell of hot plastic from the amp and took its make and model down, then the cone speaker, then the amp’s power bank. She layered evidence tags like bandages.

Rourke took a card out of his wallet. It had numbers the state made him recite exactly when he opened certain charges. He read them like he was reading a grocery list: the statute for criminal trespass in the first degree, the one for fraud and misrepresentation inducing another to act—a statute that had been written for old swindles, repurposed for people who staged hauntings and sold tickets—and the local ordinance on obstruction and interference during an active death investigation.

“Okay,” Theo said, weary rather than combative. “Can we talk about scope.”

“We are,” Rourke said. “Do not talk unless you’re answering a direct question. Request counsel if you want to. Otherwise, answer the question asked.”

Theo considered that, then nodded. “Question.”

“What was your route onto the boathouse roof on the nineteenth. Ladder, dock, swim.”

“Dock. Low tide. There’s a seam in the planking by the second piling. You can put weight there, it doesn’t groan.”

“That seam is county maintenance,” Rourke said, pen neither speeded nor slowed. “You affixed the cone speaker when.”

“Night before—two nights? It all blends.” He grimaced. “Two nights. Late. I anchored to the gutter bracket and ran line to the weathervane.”

“For the child audio,” Mara said, same steadiness, as if confirming an item on a purchase list.

“Yeah.”

“Source.” Rourke didn’t look up.

Theo’s cuff chain slipped against the chair rung when he lifted his shoulders. “Library recordings. The laugh from the oral history cassette. It was—you know—authentic.”

Mara wrote: Vivian’s microcassette previously seized by Julian; not this. “Which cassette,” she said.

“Not the one he took. The old open‑shelf one. Tinny as hell. I mixed, EQ’d, looped. It sounds more like a hallway when you spread it.”

“And you uploaded that file where,” Rourke asked.

Theo hesitated long enough that the storm filled in, the windows rattling, a loose sash knocking somewhere in the stairwell. “Cloud drive. Cass pulled it from there. He told me timing. He did the caption push.”

Rourke’s pen made a square note with a line under it. “Payment from Cass Norris to you.”

Theo shook his head immediately. “It wasn’t like that.”

“Was there payment.”

“Gear loans. Access. He said exposure. The usual sugar for idiots.”

“Did you receive money,” Rourke said, not changing any syllable.

Theo’s jaw worked. “Fifty for gas. That’s it.”

“Fifty dollars,” Rourke repeated. “Noted.”

Mara shifted the amp and got the damp to stop wicking. She could see the way the rig had been wired to cheat the room’s shape—tone to draw the ear toward the water. She labeled the spool of line and bagged the remote.

Rourke turned a page. “Explain the cistern.”

Theo swallowed. “I didn’t put him there.” He dragged the words out like they weighed something. “I lowered the hose after. Owen talks loud. You can hear everything he says on the landing if you’re not an idiot. I heard ‘pump’ and I knew they’d put it together. I wanted it to look like a panic, not a plan. So I—” He snapped his fingers once, an old habit that seemed to surprise him. “—messed the picture.”

“Motivation,” Rourke said.

Theo stared at a stapler. He had the look of a man rummaging through a drawer for the cleanest shirt. “Thrill,” he said finally. “Control.” He looked at Mara. “Leverage, if you want to be poetic about it. Not money. I’m not a ghoul.”

“Leverage over whom,” Rourke said.

“The house,” Theo said, like it was a person that had slighted him. “All of you. You behave differently when you think something is watching.”

“You’re saying you interfered with evidence because it gave you a feeling.” Rourke wasn’t making it into a moral. He was pinning it to the cork.

“You’re making it sound stupid.”

“It is.” Rourke’s voice didn’t rise. “Did you access the call bells.”

Theo shook his head. “No. That was—” He stopped, chewed the inside of his cheek, realized that answering a question not asked was a hole. “No,” he said again.

Mara clicked photos as she went, the numbered tags like small flags in a field. She slid two microSD cards into separate envelopes, sealed and signed the tape, and wrote: pulled from Pelican side pocket. She bagged a pocket flashlight with UV diodes, then paused, turned it in her palm. “Yours,” she said to Theo.

“Yeah. But the paint wasn’t mine,” he said quickly, understanding where the thought went. “I just—look, I just made old noises look like new people. That’s all. The rest was there.”

Rourke ran the brass tip of his pen down the impound form, then read back the list. “One laptop. Two microSD wallets containing—count it.” He pushed them across to Mara.

“Eight cards total,” she said. “Four with sharpie circles. Two with dates. Two blank.”

“Three flight batteries A, B, D. One projector, handheld, with ceiling mount. One Bluetooth amplifier. One cone speaker. One spool monofilament. One gimbal head. One set spare props. One notebook.” He held up the notebook. “We’ll have words about this people love to keep notebooks business.”

“You can’t read that,” Theo said quickly. “Client notes.”

Rourke looked at him for a long second, then at the cover. It had a greasy handprint that had smeared dust into a pattern. “Search warrant covers it,” he said. “And you can argue later.” He put it with the rest.

“Phone,” Rourke said.

“In my pocket.”

“Left or right.”

“Right.”

Rourke reached with care, keeping Theo’s shoulder against the chair, pulled the phone out with the same indifferent professionalism he would have used to remove a thorn, and slid it into a Faraday sleeve. He wrote down the IMEI from the settings page without opening any app. “You decline to provide passcode at this time.”

“I—Yeah,” Theo said, the cheap bravery in it sounding like a teenager slamming a door.

“Fine.”

A gust shouldered the windows and one gave back a wood‑framed thump. Somewhere upstairs, a dropped length of chain tapped a radiator like a metronome. The building was full of the sort of noises that made untrained people decide a house was haunted. The trained people at the table wrote numbers that would not change in the morning.

“Last question for this round,” Rourke said. “Did you at any time enter the kitchen during the period the scene was sealed.”

Theo touched his tongue to the corner of his mouth. “No.”

“And the greenhouse,” Mara said.

“Yes. Before. Roof was still on,” he said. “I shot coverage. It looked cinematic.”

Mara put a dot beside the greenhouse entry on her own list. Not an argument. A pin.

Rourke stood, slid the booking form into a plastic sleeve of its own and clipped it shut. “You’re under arrest for criminal trespass, fraud, and interference. You have been advised. You are not to speak to anyone in this house except me or counsel. You are not to communicate with Mr. Norris or anyone else about dissemination of any audio or video. If you attempt to, that turns into a different set of problems. Understood.”

Theo nodded once. “Can I say one thing.”

“No,” Rourke said. Then, after a beat, because he wasn’t theatrical either: “Say it.”

Theo looked at Mara and tried for a version of a smile that used to work. “For what it’s worth, I liked your report notes,” he said. “They’re clean.”

Mara capped the marker. “They have to be,” she said. “People count on them staying that way.”

Rourke touched the cuffs, making sure the chain wasn’t biting skin, and then nudged Theo’s shoulder, a direction more than a shove. “Up.”

They stood. The room stepped back from them. Mara slid the impound boxes toward the end of the table, lids taped, labels facing like disciplined faces in a class photo. She added the last tag to the amp’s cord and wrote the final line in her custody log: equipment impounded, serials recorded, seals applied.

Rourke keyed his phone. “Unit Seven to Desk. One booked on scene, awaiting transport when you can float a boat or a car. Property secured, impound manifest uploaded. Copy.”

“Copy, Seven. Put him in the study. We’ll roust the van when the causeway stops throwing.”

“Received.”

He guided Theo back toward the hallway, past the library clock that had decided to stay faithful for once, and into the narrow study they’d converted with a chair bolted to a ring in the floor for exactly this kind of weather. He clipped the cuffs to the ring, checked the double lock again, and stepped back.

“You want water,” he asked, not as grace, just as routine.

Theo nodded. Rourke handed him a bottle, cracked, straw bent toward him. He took three pulls and set it by his ankle.

“Counsel,” Rourke said.

“I’ll call when I get a phone.”

“You’ll get one downtown.” Rourke closed the study door until the latch caught. He wrote the time on the whiteboard outside in block pen: 12:34, subject secured.

Back at the table, Mara slid the Faraday sleeves into a hard case and clicked the latches shut. She wiped the sharpie marker across her palm on a scrap of paper towel, then threw it away. She affixed her initials across three seals, Rourke’s across two more, and photographed each intersection so no one could say later they were different.

“Done,” she said.

Rourke nodded, the satisfaction not triumph but alignment. “Done,” he agreed. He set the impound case beside the drone Pelican and the line spool, the little mountain of someone’s cleverness now a stack of tagged evidence that would sit still.

The storm banged its empty pots. The house held together. Order, for the moment, did what it was supposed to do.


==================== SCENE 63 ====================
The study was a tight lung in the big house, a room with its own weather. The storm put its fists on the walls, a sustained pressure like someone leaning in to listen. The light was old‑gold through salt‑blinded panes, thinned to a mean ribbon by the taped Xs. The chair with the floor ring sat between the desk and the shuttered window; Theo was clipped to it, knees askew, water bottle tipped against his ankle like a buoy without moorings. The old desk wore a blotter cut from a sheet of neoprene to serve as an evidence mat, the corners held down with blue painter’s tape. On it: a scuffed microcassette, the size of a postage stamp, naked for once, its white paper label smudged at the edges with someone’s thumb oil. Beside it, an elderly dictaphone with a green power light holding steady like a pilot’s eye.

Rourke closed the door and the latch said yes. He wrote the time beneath the earlier notation—12:42, personnel present—and clipped the marker back to the board’s frame.

Mara was already at the desk, elbows tucked in, the smack of wet on her jacket where she’d leaned into a doorframe earlier drying to salt. She turned the dictaphone toward the room, checking the slot, the jack, the built‑in speaker, tissue from a pocket twirled around a toothpick to clean the head. Her hands were careful, like you are when you hate that you have to touch something that matters.

Nora hovered one step off the rug, her toes on wood, heel on wool, as if a tiny legal boundary ran there. Hair pinned up in a rush that the storm had started to undo, she kept her hand on the doorjamb, steadying herself or the house—unclear. The collar of her raincoat was still beaded, a string of dull pearls where the water hadn’t decided yet to let go.

Julian came in last, no knock. He brought the remains of sunshine with him, or pretended to. Coat impeccable, cuffs dry, the only person who could look delivered by a private breeze in a house that smelled like rope and iron. His eyes went straight to the desk and stuck.

“We are not,” he said, a step before his mouth reached the rest of the room, “playing anything.”

“We’re documenting,” Mara said. She kept her voice even. “That’s a different verb.”

Theo snorted, small and derisive, the sound of someone not invited to this part of the conversation but unable to stop himself from attending. Rourke looked at him and Theo stared back through his lashes, then angled his jaw toward the window. The water bottle made a hollow plastic click as it settled.

Julian’s gaze landed on Rourke’s uniform—on the badge, the holster, the sanctioned bulk—and then came back to the cassette. “That,” he said, “is attorney–client material. Under my custody. Seized from me under protest while I was performing my duties. Any reproduction, any listening, any further handling without counsel present is a violation.”

“You’ve said that,” Rourke replied. “Logged, in your words, at eleven sixteen and again at twelve oh‑five. This room has ears, counselor. It remembers.”

Nora flinched at counselor. Her knuckles went white against the jamb. “Julian,” she said, and there was a small rip in it, his name. “If that’s Vivian’s voice—”

“It is privileged, Nora.” All assurance. He didn’t look at her as he said it.

“It’s evidence,” Mara said. “Potential will evidence. Your privilege claims don’t glue themselves to everything within arm’s reach.”

“It’s my property,” Julian snapped. The syllables sharpened, corners out. “You took it. I have receipts.”

“You generated a memo to yourself and took a photograph of your hand holding the tape,” Mara said. “That’s not a receipt. That’s performance art.”

The boat‑hull groan of the house moving on its own bones went through them all. The study breathed and settled. Rourke stepped forward; the floor complained once under his weight, then quit.

“We’re going to listen to it,” he said, and he wasn’t baiting anyone, just laying track. “We’ll log who hears it. We’ll document chain. If there’s privileged material we’ll treat it accordingly. But it doesn’t leave this room unless I sign it out in a bag that has three seals and two sets of initials.”

Julian’s smile had no edges of its own; he had to draw them on. “You’re very proud of your stickers.”

“Stickers keep people out of jail,” Rourke said. “Sometimes they put them in. Either way: they hold.”

Mara bent to the dictaphone, the cassette not yet in her hand. She was waiting for the wave of protest to crash and spend itself so she could do the piece of work she was good at. Nora took one half‑step toward the desk without meaning to, and stopped herself like she’d grabbed a hot pan.

Julian’s glove of courtesy slipped. He sprang, quick as a fish. His hand cut the air in a clean, practiced reach—no tell, no warning—and went for the microcassette like it owed him money.

Mara flinched back instinctively. The cassette clicked once against the neoprene and spun to the edge, the little reels inside showing their teeth. Julian’s fingers brushed it. He would have had it if not for the dictaphone’s power cord, which snagged the cuff of his jacket; the machine jerked and skidded, an ugly sound of plastic on plastic.

Rourke’s hand was already there. He didn’t slam Julian, just caught his wrist a half‑inch before possession, a hard, sure pinch that stopped the tendons from doing what they wanted. It was not a fight, exactly; it was the removal of one outcome from a menu.

“Stop,” Rourke said, a word with more weight than it looked like it should carry.

Julian’s other hand came up. Rourke’s grip tightened and the tendons lost their ambition. The cassette did a small wild dance and popped off the mat—Mara’s palm flashed, and she trapped it with a smack, pinning it to the neoprene with the flat of her hand like a mother stopping a spoon from falling off a high chair tray.

Theo laughed then, a single bark, involuntary and without pleasure.

“Jesus,” Nora said, both hands to her chest now as if she could press the air back into her. “Jesus, what are you doing?”

Julian tried to take his wrist back. Rourke didn’t give it up. For a heartbeat the room was filled with small noises: the hiss of weather around the windowframe, the dry wheeze of the dictaphone’s eject spring, Theo’s breath catching. The brittle sound of linen fibers in Julian’s cuff complaining.

“Counselor,” Rourke said, quieter. “You grab at evidence again and I put cuffs on you in front of your client and your friends. I’ll call the van and sit you in it until the tide gives us a road.”

“Your client,” Theo said under his breath, head cocked. “That’s funny.”

Julian stopped pulling. The stillness had its own violence. He looked at Mara’s hand on the tape. Then at Nora, who wouldn’t meet his eyes now.

“You do not have a warrant for this,” he said, but the line was smaller than it was supposed to be.

“This is a consent search,” Mara said, not moving her hand. “By the trust’s acting officer.”

“Nora is not—”

“I am,” Nora said, her voice half‑away from her throat but finding its footing. She stepped onto the rug, onto the same piece of ground as the rest of them. “I am acting. And I consent.”

Julian blinked, like that fact had been directly in front of him for hours and he had chosen a different piece of sky to look at. The practiced outrage guttered, then flared. “This is not how any of this—”

“It is today,” Rourke said. He released Julian’s wrist only when Julian’s fingers had backed off open, slow, the performance of compliance completed. “Step away from the desk.”

Julian’s jaw worked. He took a step. He smoothed his cuff with two fingers and looked down at the wrinkle like it was the real injury.

Mara didn’t announce the movement, didn’t make a ceremony of it. She lifted her palm and turned the microcassette over into her other hand, thumb on the clear side, careful not to smear the label. She held it up so the room saw what it was, what it still was, and slid it into a fresh antistatic bag. The bag sighed as it opened its mouth. She pushed the cassette down and smoothed the zip, then pressed the seal, heat of her thumb warming the adhesive like a benediction for a thing she did not bless.

“Time,” she said.

Rourke read it off the board. “Thirteen oh‑one.”

Mara pulled a seal from the roll and wrote the time, date, location. She wrote her initials, then held it so Rourke could add his. He made the R clean and the slash through the seven like a tiny fence. She fixed the seal across the bag’s closure, then—overkill, always, because that was the point—added a second at a perpendicular angle.

The room adjusted to the new fact: the tape was in a bag, and the bag was not for anyone’s pocket without a fight.

Julian’s face did a thing then—something inward, like stone settling under water. The flint in it went out. He let his shoulders drop and the breath that came with it was grateful and awful both.

“It doesn’t matter,” he said, and his voice chose flatness over heat, a different tool. “It doesn’t matter, there’s nothing on it you can rely on.”

“Then you won’t mind us listening,” Mara said, untroubled, which only made him look at her harder.

“You said it was privileged,” Nora said. “Why would it be privileged if it—”

“Because it was mine to hold,” Julian snapped, then broke off, the heat sneaking back in. His mouth compressed as if he could force the words back where they’d come from. “Because if she said anything, she didn’t mean to. She was—she was not… You are all making this worse than it had to be.”

“You’re the one who grabbed,” Theo murmured.

“Julian,” Nora said, and the rip in his name widened. “What did you do?”

He looked at her then. Looked at her in a way he had been careful not to for days. Whatever calculation had been running in him slowed, ticked, and paused. Outside, something metal banged in the wind, then banged again, then stopped. The house listened with them.

Julian pressed the pads of his fingers to his eyelids, just for a second, as if he could make the pain of light fix something else. When he let his hands fall, his mouth had made a decision his posture had not yet caught up with.

“She was going to blow the structure apart,” he said. “You have no idea—no idea—what would have happened if she’d filed what she said she was filing. The board would have split. The creditors—” He cut a hand through the air, meaning ruin, meaning mess, meaning the expensive fiction of stability torn to its studs. “It was never meant to be a public document. It was her thinking aloud.”

“What did you do,” Nora asked again, and there was no softness in it this time.

Julian looked at the bag in Mara’s hand like it was an animal that might bite. He exhaled. The fight went out of his shoulders in a visible wave; what replaced it wasn’t contrition so much as the relief of the inevitable.

“I took the pages,” he said, each word measured so the floor wouldn’t tilt. “The ones she… the draft she had. The one with the messy blue ink and the corrections,” he added, as if inventory would make it sound less like a confession. “I took the pages and I tore them. I burned the torn pieces in the kitchen sink. I ran water over the ash so it wouldn’t float. I put the plug back in the drain so no one would see. I made sure the smoke alarm didn’t trip. I opened the window a crack and sprayed citrus cleaner so the room would smell like something else. I did that. Are we done?”

Mara stood very still. Theo’s head had tipped down, his smile gone, as if this part wasn’t funny even to him. Rourke didn’t move. He had the posture of a man who has been given, finally, the answer to a question he has carried in his pocket for a while and is reluctant to touch.

Nora made a small sound, not a sob—she was past that—but something quieter and harder, the noise a hinge makes when it admits it’s been rusting all along. Her hand went to the chair back nearest her and closed on it, the wood taking the grip.

“Why,” she asked. Not the rhetorical why. The one you ask when there is only one answer you’ll accept and you already know it.

Julian looked at her and then away, because you can say a thing easier to a windowpane than to a face.

“Because the trusts,” he said. “Because the way we set them up—because the shell companies hold the encumbered properties, and if she—if she diverted assets—” He stopped, teeth on his bottom lip to keep nouns from turning into confessions. “I kept them intact. That’s what you do, Nora. You keep the thing intact.”

“Even if she didn’t want it that way,” Mara said.

“She didn’t know what she wanted that week,” Julian shot back, tired now, done with being elegant. “She wanted to hurt people and then she wanted to be loved for it. She wanted to punish everyone, and then she wanted clean hands.”

Rourke took the bag from Mara without drama and slid it into a hard plastic evidence sleeve. He clicked the clasp shut. The sound was small and final.

“We’re not done,” he said, because they weren’t. He wrote the tape onto the log on the desk, block letters neat, time, description, location, initials. Then he moved the hard case already labeled with other devices, made room for the new thing, nested it in foam like a baby bird. He shut the lid. He flipped the catches. He put another strip of seal across them, and wrote the time again.

Julian watched every movement. Rage didn’t come back; something else did—emptiness, maybe. Something like that.

From the chair, Theo cleared his throat. “You should have used the grill,” he said, conversational on purpose. “Sink fires leave soot lines on the porcelain. Even if you scrub.”

Julian’s eyes flared, then dulled. He sat down in the spare chair by the wall like his legs had made the decision first.

Nora looked as if she’d been told someone had stolen a season from the year and no one was getting it back. She took her hand off the chair and put it on the desk instead, close to the empty place where the cassette had been, then pulled it back quick as if the neoprene were hot.

Mara straightened the dictaphone, centered it on the mat again, mostly so her hands would be busy. She looked at Julian without the triumph he might have been braced for.

“Say it clean,” she said. “So I can put it in the report.”

Julian’s smile came and went without landing. “I destroyed pages of a draft,” he said, like a deposition. “I tore and burned them to prevent her from filing a change that would have destabilized the trusts.”

“Thank you,” Mara said. She wrote, her pen even. She didn’t look up.

Outside, the wind banged a shutter and then let it go. Inside, Rourke nudged the evidence case with his boot until it was under the desk, under his knee. He put his hand on top of it, casual as you’d rest a palm on a dog’s head. The study, that tight lung, exhaled once.

“Okay,” Rourke said. “We’re going to take statements. After that, if the causeway gives us charity, we’ll go downtown. Until then, nobody touches that case but me.”

“Understood,” Julian said, and for the first time all afternoon, he meant it.


==================== SCENE 64 ====================
The study had the storm’s breath in it—salt damp caught in the rug, a faint trill in the windowpanes. Rourke cleared the blotter, laid out a legal pad and a slim recorder he produced from his inside pocket. He set it between the green lamp and the inkwell, pressed a red button, and announced the time, the date, the location. His voice was plain as a ledger.

“Voluntary statement,” he said. “No one’s cuffed. You’re free to stop at any time, Mr. Harrow.”

Julian rubbed his thumb and forefinger together once, as if feeling for ash that wasn’t there. “I understand,” he said.

Mara angled her chair so she faced him and the recorder both. Her notes from earlier were squared in front of her, edges aligned with the mat, a habit that had nothing to do with neatness and everything to do with making a boundary on the desk they were sharing.

Rourke kept his palm on the sealed evidence case under the desk. He wasn’t looking at Julian, he was looking at the lamp cord, at the fact of it, at its plug in the wall as if the room could be unplugged and carried away if need be. Then he raised his eyes. “State your name for the record.”

“Julian Albright Harrow.”

“Spell it.”

He did.

“Okay,” Rourke said. “Tell me what you did with the will pages.”

Julian’s first breath was theatrical by reflex, the second was just air. “She was going to file a codicil,” he said. “Vivian. She wanted to roll certain holdings into a charitable remainder trust, dissolve two of the legacy vehicles, and redirect distributions to Nora.” He looked at Mara as he said her name, not pleading, only acknowledging where the gravity in the room had shifted. “Those vehicles—what you’re calling shell trusts—feed other structures. I kept them intact.”

“Names,” Mara said.

“Inland Pelican,” Julian said. “Sable Reef. Calder Road. Harbor Light Operating was the real nostril for the money, but those three were the lungs.”

“And you did what?”

“I removed the pages referencing them from a draft will. I tore them out. I burned them. I did so to prevent her from filing a change I believed would destabilize obligations already in motion.”

“Whose obligations?” Mara asked.

“Mine,” he said. “Investors who don’t like their names on paper. People we pay who wouldn’t get paid if the flow hiccuped. But largely mine.”

Rourke clicked his pen, not writing yet. “Walk me through. Time, place. Start at the beginning.”

“Two nights ago,” Julian said. “After dinner. The generator was sulking but still on the line. Vivian told me she’d recorded a note for you all—” his mouth ticked, then steadied—“and that she had final language ready for her counsel to file once we got a clean window on the causeway. She showed me the draft. There were annotations in the margins, her hand. She still used the dictaphone to talk herself through edits. I asked for time to ‘run numbers,’ a phrase that works on people who think money is a discipline rather than an appetite. She left the draft in the library safe because she likes the old theater of it.”

“Liked,” Mara said. It wasn’t a correction, only a pin in the fabric.

“Liked,” he said. “I took the duplicate key from her dressing table. I’m not proud. I went to the safe, removed the draft. In the study I used the sink.”

“The porcelain sink to your left,” Rourke said, glancing at it. A thin crescent of soot clung under the lip where the drain’s metal met the firing.

“Yes.”

“How many pages,” Mara asked.

“Four, torn from the middle section that dealt with schedule B and schedule D references. The codicil language was attached with a clip. I burned that too.”

“Did you read it.”

“Yes.”

“Summarize it.”

“Replace me as co‑trustee with Nora. Dissolve Inland Pelican and Sable Reef over twelve months and roll assets into a charitable remainder trust for health and historical preservation in Essex County. Sunset Calder Road into a donor‑advised fund unnamed in the draft but clearly intended for Nora’s discretion. Revoke the right of first refusal on the quarry option.” The last clause came out like he’d bitten it.

Mara’s pen moved. “How long did you let the pages burn.”

“Until they were ash. The edges curl slower than you think. I had to re‑light one corner. I used a box of matches in the top drawer,” he said, eyeing the drawer without moving.

“What did you do with the ash.”

“Scooped it with an index card into the wastebasket. I ran the tap to sluice the remainder. There was a scum on the trap after.”

“What did you do with the wastebasket liner.”

“Tied it and took it to the greenhouse burn barrel. There was already refuse from the roof debris. I turned it over with the poker. No one saw me.”

Mara’s voice stayed even. “That was when the greenhouse roof had failed.”

“It was already hemorrhaging,” he said. “I wore gloves.”

Rourke’s pen scratched finally. “What gloves.”

“Blue nitrile from the pantry. A box labeled Food Prep. Size medium. I threw them out in the same bag.”

“Anyone else know,” Mara asked.

“No,” Julian said, and then, after a beat, “Theo suspected. You all did, eventually.”

“Focus on then,” Rourke said. “Anyone else know then.”

“No.”

Mara’s eyes moved to the shelf behind him. There was a paperweight, a slice of agate with a fossilized fern inside it, leaves etched white from 300 million years of pressure. She didn’t look long. “Why did you think you could get away with it.”

“Because I’ve gotten away with things that mattered more,” Julian said, and his mouth tilted a fraction, not as humor. “Because storms wash prints. Because lawyers make doubt. Because in this house if you keep your voice level and your hands still, people mistake you for the adult in the room.”

Rourke let the quiet sit until it found its edges. Outside, the wind hunted along the clapboards and moved on.

“Say the words,” Rourke said. “For the record.”

Julian nodded once, a man stepping into the exact outline of his crime. “I destroyed portions of a testamentary instrument,” he said. “I tore and burned will pages to prevent their effect on existing trusts.”

“Knowingly.”

“Yes.”

“Voluntarily.”

“Yes.”

“Without permission.”

“Obviously.”

Mara didn’t flinch. “Who drafted the shell trusts.”

“Wardell & Pratt for the initial frames. Maintenance moved to a boutique in Boston when the optics went bad—Halden Street Advisors. They act like a family office but they’re a laundromat with mahogany paneling.”

“Beneficiaries on paper,” she said.

“Cousins and a foundation named for a seaside bird. The distributions land elsewhere. None of this is a revelation to anyone who looks long enough.”

Rourke flipped a page on his pad. His handwriting was blocky, not the neat print he used on evidence seals. “Potential charges I’m noting for the file,” he said, as if Julian had asked for a rundown: “Destruction of a testamentary document, tampering with evidence, obstruction. If any of those trusts touched public money or fraud, there’s a wire angle. I’m not your counsel. You’re not under arrest right now. You should know what I’m hearing.”

Julian’s chin lifted a fraction, then fell back to neutral. “Understood.”

“Who has the original will,” Mara asked.

“Vivian’s counsel in town. The draft matched the last filed will with the exceptions I burned.”

“Where is the microcassette Ms. Hale used to dictate the codicil.”

Julian glanced, involuntary, at the case under Rourke’s knee. “I had it,” he said. “I tried to keep it. I did not succeed.”

Rourke looked at him until he looked away. Then he checked the recorder’s red eye, steady as a heartbeat. “Statement is clean so far,” he said to the machine. To Julian: “Anything you want to add about why.”

“My father taught me money was a tide and we were the ones with the moon in our pocket,” Julian said. “That’s the kind of thing that sounds wise until you are ankle‑deep in black water in your own house, trying to decide whether to save paper or people.” He exhaled. “I chose paper. That is what I did.”

Mara’s pen stopped. She capped it. “Did you ever tell Vivian you opposed the change.”

“Yes.”

“How did she respond.”

“She said I was confusing prudence with entitlement. She said legacies are not bins you empty into your children. She said she was tired.”

“Did you argue.”

“No,” he said. “I said we’d look at it after the storm.”

Rourke tapped the recorder once with his knuckle, a soft cue. “We’re going to wrap this pass and I’m going to read it back,” he said. “Then you sign.”

Julian’s mouth was dry. “Fine.”

Rourke rewound, played the statement back in that metallic room‑tone that makes everyone sound like an actor learning to be ordinary. He slid the pad across. Julian read, took the pen, and signed with a signature that looked practiced even when his hand held a tremor.

Rourke dated it, initialed each page. He slotted the signed sheets into a clear envelope, peeled the seal, and smoothed it with the heel of his hand. He made a second copy and handed it to Julian. “Keep that,” he said. “Don’t lose it.”

Julian looked at the copy like it might bite, then folded it once and set it on the desk without touching the ink.

“Questions for me,” Rourke said.

“No,” Julian said.

Mara lifted her gaze to him finally. “One more,” she said. “If she had filed it, if the trusts had been unwound and the money routed where she said—what would you have done.”

“Adapted,” he said. “The word people like me use when we mean ‘found another way.’”

Mara nodded, as if she’d expected that exact shape of honesty and nothing underscored it. She gathered her notes, slid them into the folder. “We’re done.”

Rourke clicked the recorder off. The red light went dark. He put it in his pocket. He checked the evidence case seal again with two fingers, an old habit that had nothing ritual in it, only care. The room felt heavier for a moment, then not.

Julian stood, then sat again because standing implied leaving and there was nowhere to go. He put both hands on his knees and looked at neither of them.

“We’ll be noting potential charges in the file,” Rourke said. “You’ll be contacted.”

Julian’s laugh was one unlit sound. “I’m already contacted.”

Mara rose. She pushed her chair in so it didn’t scrape, set it flush with the desk like that mattered. “Thank you for answering,” she said. It came out simple, the way you say thank you to a man who just told you something that costs him.

“For what it’s worth,” Julian said, “I didn’t do it because I hate her. I did it because I’m a coward who liked the shape of my life.”

“Noted,” Rourke said.

The wind shoved at the shutter and the shutter shoved back. The study held. They stood in the sound of it until the sound was just weather and the rest was paper.


==================== SCENE 65 ====================
Evening pressed against the library’s mullioned windows, the panes waking and sleeping with the wind. Lamps made small islands on the long table where the catalog cards were stacked in tidy bricks, Mara’s evidence bags lined in a grid beside them. The cot where Vivian had died had been folded and leaned against a shelf; its imprint still dented the rug. Rourke set a small gray tape recorder in the center, the kind with a window the size of a postage stamp and the kind of buttons that never forgave a sloppy thumb.

Nora stood with her back to the map case, arms folded tight as if to keep from touching the furniture. Maureen had come straight from the historical room, her cardigan wet at the shoulders, a ledger wrapped in brown paper clamped protectively to her. Julian sat at the end of the table nearest the door, his chair angled to make leaving easier. He didn’t leave.

On the far end, a tablet lay faceup, Cass’s number pinned open. The connection light showed a thin green. “Still here,” Cass’s voice said, hollowed by distance. “Hearing you. The storm took one node but we’re fine.”

“Everyone ready,” Rourke said, not a question. He held up a sealed evidence sleeve. Inside, a microcassette glinted, label handwritten in a neat, old-fashioned hand: LIB/DICT—V—27B.

Maureen nodded toward the sleeve. “That’s the set labeled for library dictation. Vivian used a new one each quarter. I keep the index card for every box.”

“Let’s start with chain,” Rourke said.

Mara flipped open her notebook and read without looking down. “Recovered: library desk—dictation drawer—beneath stationery tray. Sealed by me at twenty-one fourteen, Chapter 12 night, with Officer Rourke present. Seizure interrupted Chapter 13 by Mr. Vale” —she didn’t look at Julian— “claiming legal custody. He removed the cassette in my presence, I marked the empty case and signed the removal. Tonight, nineteen forty-two, Mr. Vale returned the cassette to Officer Rourke.” She tapped the seal with her pen. “Seal is new, but the internal label matches my notes.”

Rourke held the sleeve up to the lamp, turned it. “Internal leader’s uncut. No splices visible. We’ll confirm with a forensic scope later.” He looked to Cass. “You’ll hear me start the transfer. You record your screen on your end. Time-stamp running?”

Cass’s keys clicked faintly. “Stamped and screen-capped. I’ll hash the file as soon as I’ve got it.”

Julian leaned forward, forearms on his knees. “We’re pretending the custody gap isn’t material?”

“No,” Rourke said. “We’re documenting it. If there’s an issue with authenticity, you’ll state it on the record, and it will be tested. You’ll have your counsel later. This is content verification.”

Nora’s voice came from the maps. “Play it.”

Maureen slid the brown paper free of her ledger and opened it to a page ribboned with tabs. “And after,” she said, “I can match the date against my schedule book. She was particular. Supper at six; coffee; dictation at six-forty-five. I made a note about a cough that week.”

Rourke broke the seal with a small, precise motion, slid the cassette out, turned it over to check for cracks, then set it in the cradle and closed the lid. He pressed play.

The recorder’s motor began with a hum like a held breath. Static, a soft clack, room tone—the library announcing itself as a low box. Then Vivian’s voice, unmistakable, the grit and schooling in it and the fatigue she’d been wearing in the last month: “Library dictation, evening, after supper. The date is… Monday, November the eighth.”

Maureen’s finger landed on a box in her ledger. “I have Monday the eighth—dictation, six-forty-eight to seven-fifteen,” she murmured.

Vivian again, paper moving near the microphone. “Addendum to my last will and testament. Codicil, short form. My previous dispositive provisions stand with the following revision.” The sound of a sip. “If the trust instruments fail or are voided by action of this house or manmade interference”—a dry laugh, then breath—“I designate Nora Shale—Nora—to receive my residuary estate, free and clear of liens, and to administer restoration of Gull’s Stair in my name.”

The room stilled around the tape. The wind made a wide ribbon of noise outside, but the table held the center.

On the tablet, Cass exhaled. “Recording. Check. I’m getting a clean wave. No empty frequencies. No rack hiss.”

Julian glanced at him. “And that proves—what? That someone recorded a voice.”

“Keep listening,” Mara said, not unkind.

The tape picked up Vivian’s pencil, a scratch and a rap. “Nora, if you’re present, don’t interrupt. Maureen, note that I’m speaking into the library dictation unit because the main office machine is full and I’m tired. You can transcribe in the morning. Put the codicil with the old papers. Title it plainly. Rourke can have a copy. Date it.”

Maureen lifted her chin at her own name, thumb tapping the edge of the ledger. “She said that to me. Word for word. I wrote ‘tired’ more than once that week,” she said, almost to herself.

Vivian continued. “This naming is not contingent on Julian Vale’s sobriety, marriage, or penance. He knows why. If he has a quarrel, he can have it with me when I’m dust. Alistair is excluded as ever.” A pause. “That’s the substance. The rest is boilerplate you can stitch from the exemplar. If this is the last tape I make, fine.”

There was the sound of the library clock, far back in the room, beginning to toll seven. One, two, three—each bell carrying the same minute decay. Cass spoke softly. “Clock matches. Seven strikes. The ’hum’ I’m listening to—the desk lamp ballast at sixty hertz—stays constant through the bells. That’s one continuous take.”

Julian’s jaw moved. “Or you layered it.”

“On a microcassette?” Mara said. “Do you have a micro splicer? I don’t.”
