Rourke rewound three seconds and played the chime again. He stopped the tape. “We’ll have the lab check the leader and the tension for edits. For now, we’re establishing content.” He looked to Maureen. “Walk us through your side.”

Maureen slid one ledger aside and brought out the smaller schedule book, its corners furred from a purse. “Vivian dictated nightly if she had strength. My entry reads: ‘V—dict. codicil—name Nora—note Julian exclude.’ That’s my shorthand. I transcribed the following afternoon in the records room—Essex Historical—because the library table had deliveries. I made a carbon, filed the draft in the manor folder I keep for legal instruments. Vivian intended to review, sign, and notarize, but she got pulled into the greenhouse roof emergency.” She lifted her gaze at Nora. “She told me at the stairs: if I don’t sign, the tape stands as my instruction. She said, ‘Maureen, I’ve said it. You write it plain.’”

Nora’s jaw softened. She let one arm drop, then put it back.

Rourke nodded. “Do you have the transcribed draft?”

Maureen reached into her brown paper package and drew out a manila folder, also sealed with tape. “I do. I didn’t release it when Julian asked for everything,” she said, not apologizing. “Because Vivian told me to give it to Rourke.”

Julian shifted. “You kept copies in a public archive?”

“In a locked staff drawer in a room no one visits for anything but tide tables,” she said. “And I logged the withdrawal.” She turned the schedule book toward him. “Would you like to see.”

He didn’t take it.

Rourke broke the tape on the manila, slid the single sheet free. It was formatted in the tight, careful way of a person used to forms: heading, date, the phrase “I, Vivian Ann Calder,” and three neat paragraphs. He set it beside the recorder and read aloud. It matched the tape almost word for word. He slid it to Mara. She scanned it, found her place, then passed it to Nora.

Nora took it with both hands, as if the paper had a temperature. She read fast, then again slower. “This is her voice,” she said, quietly. “In the words. The way she pinches.”

Julian looked at the recorder’s window, at the brown ribbon turning. “You’re all very sure for people in a room with a cassette player,” he said, but his voice didn’t land anywhere.

Cass cleared his throat through the tablet. “I’m matching the hiss signature to a baseline from the previous week’s dictation Mara sent me—different cassette, same unit. The mechanical warble is identical at six minutes. That’s not proof in court, but it’s… a fingerprint of the machine.”

Rourke pressed stop. The tape thunked. He ejected the cassette, showed it to the camera on the tablet, then slid it into a new sleeve, labeled and initialed the seam. “Content established,” he said. “We have intent from Vivian and an instruction naming Nora. Maureen’s transcription corroborates contemporaneous acknowledgment. The clock chimes confirm the stated time. The chain-of-custody gap is noted and will be addressed. For the purposes of inventory and immediate decisions, this codicil is operative.”

Julian opened his hands, then closed them. “On what authority.”

“On hers,” Nora said. She didn’t raise her voice. She set the paper down where she could touch it again. “And on the fact she told Maureen to do this and Maureen did.”

Maureen pushed her glasses up. “Vivian would have signed if not for the roof. She said the tape is her sign. I am a boring person, Julian. I put things where they go. This goes here.”

Mara, unusual for her, spoke with no adornment. “We’re not doing theater. You tore and burned the will. She anticipated you. She put this where we would find it.”

Julian’s mouth tightened. “I admitted my part. I’ll admit another: I didn’t take that cassette when I seized the safe because Maureen was watching and because it looked like a grocery list. It didn’t occur to me she would dictate the stakes.”

Rourke sealed the manila with the transcribed sheet into its own evidence envelope and wrote along the edge: CODICIL DRAFT—TRANSCRIBED—MAUREEN. He set both envelopes side by side, their seams bright.

Cass’s voice softened. “I’m uploading the digitized file to three redundant storage points. One is offsite. I’ll send you the hashes, Rourke. You can write them on the evidence label.”

“Do it,” Rourke said. He took the notepad Mara slid to him and wrote the alphanumeric strings as Cass read them, then initialed each. He handed the pen to Maureen. She initialed. He offered it to Nora. She hesitated, then put V.A.C. in the corner, realized, and wrote her own name instead, small and precise: N. Shale. She didn’t look at Julian. No one insisted that he sign.

Rourke stacked the envelopes and set a heavier book on them—a county law code from 1967 someone had never returned. “All right,” he said. “Ms. Shale is the named beneficiary under the codicil in the event the trusts fail or are voided. The draft and the dictation align. Maureen’s records place the dictation at the stated date and time. This will go into the file and into the court with the rest.”

The library clock, having told its piece earlier, said nothing now. The room accepted the new order without ceremony. Paper lay where it had to. The storm did what storms did.

Nora finally reached out and rested her fingertips on the envelope as if confirming addressee and sender. “Thank you,” she said to no one in particular and to everyone. “Let’s go on.”


==================== SCENE 66 ====================
The rain found a new seam somewhere above the stacks and ticked into a bucket Maureen had slid beneath the leak hours ago. The law code still sat squarely on the two evidence envelopes, dull red cloth against duller oak. The room had the calm of a place that had survived shouting and decided to be useful again.

“Let’s go on,” Nora had said. She didn’t move her fingertips from the envelope.

Maureen, who had not stood since the cassette came out, eased her stool closer to the library desk. She pulled a narrow green ledger from the pile she’d been guarding with her elbow. “We’ll need the logs. The ones Counsel told me to keep off the main desk in case Julian decided to tidy.”

Mara reached for the ledger. “Give it here. We’ll put it next to—no, not under—the code book. I like being able to see everything breathing.” She lifted the county code a fraction to make a neat lane and set the green book to the right, spine square, page ribbon tucked.

Nora let the envelope go and sat opposite them, as if there’d been assigned seats all along. “Call her,” she said.

Maureen didn’t ask who. She pressed two buttons on the phone she’d plugged into the old linen-covered line earlier. It crackled, then steadied. “Counsel? We have you on speaker in the library.”

“I’m here,” a woman’s voice said, crisp, not unkind. “Who’s in the room?”

“Mara,” Mara said. “Maureen.”

“Nora,” Nora said.

“Good,” Counsel said. “No one else?”

“No,” Maureen said. “Rourke took the officers to the mudroom to keep them from tracking brine into the Persian. He’ll get my head if I let them ruin that rug.”

“Then we can speak plainly.” The voice filled the library without raising itself. “You found the dictation and the transcribed draft. You’ve sealed both. Chain of custody was recorded?”

“Rourke wrote hashes from the digital upload onto the evidence label,” Mara said. “Maureen and Nora initialed the codicil envelope. I took photographs of the process with timestamps. The recorder is in Maureen’s desk, and nobody’s touched it since.”

“Good. Maureen, pull your logs. I want your entry for the date Vivian dictated and any witness or visitor entries around that time. Also your call-book, if you kept one.”

“I keep two,” Maureen said, offended at the idea she might keep only one. She slid out another, this one wider, with cloth corners. “This is the staff log with my notes. The smaller one is reception. And this—” She patted the green ledger—“is my maintenance and work order book, because the roof was leaking that day and I had to note which buckets had which floors.”

“Open them,” Counsel said. “Mara, you read. Nora, you listen. Do not multitask. I need to hear your voice if something is unclear.”

Maureen flipped with quick, precise fingers. “Here,” she said, tapping. “October third, three-twenty p.m. ‘Ms. Vivian in library, dictating to me. Door ajar. House quiet. Cass not on site. Julian out. Nora in greenhouse.’ Then—” She turned the page—“maintenance: ‘Roof leak corridor B, buckets three and six, placed. Vacuum breaker for cistern pump making noise. Turned off.’ And reception: ‘Theo called to say drone workshop rescheduled; Ms. V. declined.’”

Nora leaned forward, her brow pulling. “I was in the greenhouse that afternoon. I remember the way the heat stayed and the light fell sideways. She asked me to leave her with Maureen.” It wasn’t said to convince anyone else. It sounded like she was retrieving a book by smell and weight and knew where it belonged.

Counsel’s voice softened a notch. “That aligns with what Vivian told me. She told me to instruct Maureen how to transcribe and to sign the transcribed draft in Vivian’s usual place if possible. The roof and the storm that week prevented an in-person witness session. The audio was to substitute. Maureen, do you have your own signature as transcriber on the printed sheet?”

“Yes. Lower left. Date and time. My printed name. I stamped it with the library’s date stamp too, for my own peace of mind.”

Mara shot her a look. “You stamped the codicil?”

“I stamped the transcription,” Maureen said. “I wasn’t going to stamp the envelope. I am not a notary. I am tidy.”

“Good,” Counsel said. “Now the witness logs.”

“That’s the staff log,” Maureen said. “I also have the bell pulls.” She reached—another book, narrower, with hand-drawn columns. “I drew this up when the electrician reconnected the call-bell system. Every time the library cord was tugged, the indicator in the hall pinged, and I wrote it down.” She turned it around so Mara could see. “On October third, four-oh-two, the library bell rang once. That is when Ms. Vivian said she was finished. I went in. She said, ‘Put it where you put the payroll ledgers. If anyone opens the greenhouse and rain comes in, I want you to be able to reach it.’ Then she sent me to the linen closet to get the archival sleeves.”

“Payroll ledgers,” Counsel repeated. “And you did that.”

“I did,” Maureen said, pleased to be asked.

Mara’s finger followed the bell record. “You logged subsequent pings?”

“Yes,” Maureen said. “That evening, late, when the storm hit, it rang again, but that turned out to be the wind tugging the loose cord in the passage. I tightened it the next day.”

Counsel let the room breathe in the beat that followed. “Here is what I’m going to do. With your permission, Nora, I will file today an emergency petition in Essex Probate to admit Vivian’s codicil as a testamentary instrument supplementing the last valid will. We will attach the audio, the transcription, Maureen’s affidavit as transcriber and custodian, and the logs as contemporaneous records. We will request an immediate order maintaining the status quo and appointing you as special administratrix pending full hearing, with full authority to secure assets and engage auditors. Mara’s photographs and Rourke’s chain-of-custody notes will be exhibits. We will also file a motion for sanctions for spoliation against Julian regarding his admitted destruction of portions of the will. When the court sees both the sabotage and Vivian’s work-around, it will be clear.”

Nora wet her lower lip without realizing it. “Say it plainly.”

“You are the named beneficiary under the codicil if the trusts are void or fail,” Counsel said. “Given what we’ve already documented, I am confident the court will recognize your position and grant you authority to act. That means you will be the one directing the inventory, securing the microcassette, deciding who stays in the house. Not Julian.”

Maureen set both hands flat on the desk as if ready to steady it. “I have the template for an affidavit,” she said. “You sent it last month with the note that it was ‘in the event we needed the boring truth.’ I can fill it out now.”

“Do,” Counsel said. “Include the date, time, your description of Vivian’s capacity—”

“She was perfectly lucid,” Maureen said. “She made one joke about how my typing made her sound less bossy. Then she dictated the exact phrasing, slowly, and made me repeat ‘Nora Evelyn Shale’ back to her three times. She said, ‘Spell it for posterity.’ I spelled it. She was not slurring. She was not short of breath. She drank her tea.”

“Put that in,” Counsel said. “Mara, you will draft a chain-of-custody affidavit for yourself. Include the safe opening with Nora’s keys and the sequence of evidence handling today.”

“I’ll have it typed and printed in ten minutes,” Mara said. “Maureen, you still have the manual typewriter?”

Maureen slid open a drawer and pulled out a black case like a violinist’s. “Of course.”

Nora looked at the case, then at the envelope, then back at the phone. Her voice did not waver. “What do you need from me?”

“Two things,” Counsel said. “First, a signed verification that you consent to me filing on your behalf and a statement accepting nomination as special administratrix. Second, a short declaration that the voice on the tape is Vivian’s and that the initials on the transcribed draft are your own, along with the note that you were present in the house that day though not in the room, and that Maureen told you afterward what Vivian had said to expect in the event the trusts collapsed. Keep it factual. We are not building drama. The strength is in the boring details.”

Nora nodded to nobody. “I can do that.”

Mara’s eyes had gone narrow the way they did when she saw how to lay out a route through dense underbrush. “We should also make a clean copy of the logs with page numbers, photograph each page, and put the originals back in the safe with the evidence.”

Maureen’s pencil was already twirling. “I can do the copies now. The machine is warmed. I turn it on every morning because somebody will need a paper, even in the flood.”

“Do that,” Counsel said. “While you work, I’ll dictate the captions for the exhibits. Are you ready?”

Mara pulled a legal pad close. “Ready.”

“Exhibit A is the audio file of Vivian’s dictation, dated October third, timestamped as per Maureen’s recorder and verified by Cass’s upload log. Exhibit B is the transcription with Maureen’s transcriber signature and library date stamp. Exhibit C is Maureen’s staff log entries for the dates covering October first through fifth; please photograph the entire double-page spread for context, then the detail. Exhibit D is the bell pull log page for October third and fourth with notation regarding wind. Exhibit E is the maintenance/work order entry re roof leaks and cistern noises; this establishes the context of the interrupted signing. Exhibit F is Mara’s chain-of-custody photographs, including the envelopes, the writing of hashes, and the placement under the law code. Exhibit G is Nora’s declaration identifying Vivian’s voice and her own initials.”

“Got it,” Mara said, her pen working.

“Nora,” Counsel said, “once you sign, we’ll scan and submit. The court clerk knows me. I can get this in the queue this afternoon. We’ll request a same-day status conference by teleconference if the judge is in chambers. If not, first thing in the morning. Either way, do not let anyone move those envelopes except Rourke into secure storage. And change no locks until the order is in hand; I don’t want a procedural annoyance to distract us.”

“Understood,” Nora said. She sounded like she had learned how to stand with the weight in the hips that lawyers and dancers used when they stood all day.

Maureen rose, the old stool legs scuffing the floor. “I’ll bring the copier here; the hallway is damp, and the cord won’t reach anyway.” She disappeared and reappeared with a squat gray rectangle that looked defiant in its obsolescence. She plugged it into the socket by the fireplace, where the surge protector already had too many mouths.

Mara gathered the logs as if shepherding small animals. “I’ll handle the photographs,” she said. “We should put a ruler along the page edges for scale.”

“Do it,” Counsel said. There was a rustle as she took a sip of something on her end. “Nora, say aloud: do you accept nomination as special administratrix and are you willing to serve?”

Nora didn’t look at the others. She looked at the envelope just long enough to put the words in line. “I accept nomination as special administratrix, and I am willing to serve.”

“Thank you,” Counsel said. “That’s clear. Maureen, when you finish, sign each page of your affidavit. Initial the corners of the photos. We will be a wall of initials. Mara, your affidavit should include the inventory station you set up and the steps you took three minutes after you found the codicil to secure it. We are doing a litigator’s happiest thing: making it impossible for anyone to say later that the important thing wandered.”

The copier hummed, the rain ticked, the phone purred. The rhythm settled.

Maureen fed the first page through and glanced up. “I have also the visitor sign-in from the week prior. It shows Julian came on the second to ‘review financials,’ which is when he had me fetch him tea and then he went into the library alone. I wrote ‘unsupervised’ in the margin because I’m not a fool. Would you like that?”

“Yes,” Counsel said, not missing a beat. “That will be Exhibit H. It makes the narrative plain: he had access; he admitted destruction; Vivian took a parallel path with someone she trusted; and we have paper and magnetic tape to underpin it.”

Mara laid the staff log flat and took the first shot. The click sounded like a small decision being made correctly.

Nora held out her hand for the declaration form. Maureen, with a stately motion, slid a blank sheet across and placed a pen on top as if she were laying a sprig of rosemary on a loaf. “Write,” she said, without drama, as if writing was simply a thing they did when the air needed less noise.

Nora wrote. Her handwriting was small and precise, the way she’d written her name on the evidence envelope. After a moment, she stopped, looked at the phone, and said, “Thank you.”

“You’re welcome,” Counsel said. “One last thing. When you file as special administratrix, you will need to post notice. We will prepare a proposed order waiving bond, given the documentation and the need to move. I will also draft a preservation notice to all parties and the bank, freezing any movement in and out of the trusts pending hearing. That buys us time, which is our most expensive resource on a day like this.”

Maureen’s copier spit out a warm sheet. She stacked it on the left, squared the corners against the desk’s edge, looked satisfied. “You say ‘bond’ and I think ‘rope,’” she said. “But I know you mean the other.”

“I do,” Counsel said. “And I’ll bring the rope if anyone wanders too close to the Persian. Send me scans as you finish. I’ll be at my desk until this is filed. If the power goes, text me photographs. We work with what we have.”

“We have enough,” Mara said.

“We do,” Counsel agreed. “Nora, when this is over, you will be in your proper place. For now, finish the packets. Call me the moment Rourke returns; I’ll need his badge number on the chain-of-custody cover sheet.”

“Understood,” Nora said again.

The copier hummed, the camera clicked, the rain ticked. Maureen’s pencil moved like a metronome on a desk calendar as she checked off columns—log copied, page signed, photo taken, exhibit lettered. Mara typed with the speed of someone who refused to let facts be late. Nora signed where there were blanks and did not press too hard, as if mindful that paper conserved pressure as well as ink.

Counsel listened and, occasionally, said, “Good,” in a tone that kept the room moving.

When the first packet was complete, Maureen lifted it, a tidy stack with a binder clip that could have pinched a finger if anyone tried to pull a page loose. She set it to the left of the law code, not under it, but close enough that the weight of one seemed to guard the other.

“There,” she said. “It goes here.”


==================== SCENE 67 ====================
The morning felt wrung-out. The light came pale and metallic off the water, as if the ocean had been hammered flat and left there to glint. Mara’s boots made a sound like wet bread on the sodden path as she followed Rourke up toward the overlook. The wind moved in long, tired breaths. Gulls rode the lift along the cliff with the arrogance of birds that had slept well.

Rourke had his field jacket zipped to his throat. Salt had dried in a frost along the collar. He didn’t look back as he climbed; he had the forward lean of someone who didn’t trust the ground not to slide out from under his feet.

“Watch your left,” he said. “The shoulder’s gone soft.”

Mara tested the edge with the tip of her boot. It gave, a mushy surrender, and she shifted back. “Smells like a broken cellar.”

“Clay and rot,” Rourke said.

They reached the wooden rail at the overlook—a rail that had been two rails yesterday and now was one stuttering line. The second had been ripped free and sat thirty feet down on a ledge, speared into a tangle of thrift and blackberry like a dropped ladder. Beyond it, the cliff path simply didn’t exist.

Where there had been a cut in the rock and a thread of stairs clinging to the face, there was a pale vertical—fresh raw stone, damp, veined with seams like old scars exposed. The drop was a clean, obscene absence, as if a bite had been taken out of the headland. The sea worked the new edge with a crash-and-suck rhythm, chewing the dark mud into streaks that fell like ash-laden waterfalls.

Mara put her hand on what remained of the post. It wobbled, untrustworthy as a loose tooth. “That was fast.”

“It always is,” Rourke said. He scanned down and to the south, where the path used to switchback to the boathouse landing and the lower service trail. There was a smear of darker soil where the path should have turned. Nothing else. “Storm took the whole run. Stairs, brackets, underlayment, the lot.”

From the bottom of the bluff, a board surfaced in a swell, rolled, and vanished. A rope end flicked and slapped like a tail.

“This was our clean access to the lower grounds,” Mara said. “We still need to document the bluff face below the ballroom and the collapse points by the cistern outflow.”

“Not from here you don’t,” Rourke said. He took a half step to the right to sight down the new face. His mouth made a flat line. “And not today from anywhere.”

They stood for a moment with the soft geography of disaster between them. Spray snapped up occasionally, carried on gusts that hit their cheeks and dried already-dry places. A length of conduit poked from the broken cliff like a bone. A gull landed on it, then thought better and lifted away.

“Funicular?” Mara asked.

“Rail’s intact. Platform’s not, and the supports on the north cribbing are undermined,” Rourke said. “I had a look before I called you up. You touch that release and it’s a coffin. If anyone says otherwise they can ride it themselves.” He lifted his radio and keyed. “Dispatch, Unit Seven. Standing at the cliff overlook. Confirming collapse of the coastal path from marker eleven through fifteen. Request county engineer and public works barricades. Immediate closure.”

A burst of static, then a voice too practical to be surprised. “Copy, Seven. Engineer ETA ninety minutes. You want Road Crew 2 or 3?”

“Two. They know this hill. And get me Solid Waste for debris down below. We’ve got treated wood in the wash.”

“Copy.”

Mara crouched. A ragged skid of wet dirt still held impressions of small triangular treads—the last set of steps before the ground stopped being ground. She took two photos. She measured the distance from the blistered post to the edge, wrote it down: twenty-seven inches. She measured the depth of a crack running parallel to the cliff, the start of a failure that had not yet finished failing. Less than a hand deep. More than enough.

“I can work from the top,” she said. “Long lens. Sketch. We don’t need to stand on it.”

Rourke snorted. “You don’t stand on anything. You breathe on it and it’ll go.”

He walked the line where the warning tape would go and decided against walking it. “We’ll set closure from the old flagstone to the torpedo roses by the turn,” he said. “They’re rooted in good dirt. The rest of it’s soup.”

Mara lifted the camera and shot down, past the fresh wound to the rocks and the churn. She found the boathouse roofline through the fog-haze and snapped that, too. The roof was mottled from the storm—seaweed lace and a black plastic bag plastered flat. “We still have the bottom door,” she said, more to frame the options than to argue. “We can come in by water, if we have to.”

Rourke didn’t answer immediately. He watched a wave hit the new face and recoil, throwing up a white veil that dropped in ragged tassels. “County won’t let us put a skiff under this until that face stops shedding,” he said at last. “You see those lines? That’s all moving. It doesn’t care about your schedule.”

Mara capped the lens and stood, knees popping. “The schedule cares about me,” she said. “I need the lower path to finish the outflow documentation and photograph the lower rail bolts. I can’t in good faith say the staging couldn’t have been run from the shore approach without putting my feet where theirs were.”

“Then we don’t say it until it’s safe,” Rourke said.

“Safe costs,” Mara said.

“Everything does.” His tone wasn’t unkind. It also wasn’t movable. “I’m not putting a body on that slope to shave a day off the report. We’ll get it from the water when the engineer signs off. Barge in if we have to.”

“Barge is ten thousand with crew,” Mara said. “More if we need a crane arm and a man-basket. And we do.”

He looked at her, then past her toward the house where the eaves still dripped like a clock. “Then it’s ten and more,” he said. “You want cheap, you pick a different cliff.”

A gull laughed like a hinge. Mara pulled out her field book. The paper had wolfed a little from the damp and wanted to curl. Her pencil left a strong line anyway.

Cliff path collapsed from marker 11 through 15; complete loss of switchback to lower landing. Funicular compromised; do not operate. Recommend full closure. Alternative access by water only; barge or lift needed. Costs high. Timelines extend.

She added, in smaller print, on the right-hand edge: Photographic limitation from above; angle insufficient to confirm bolt shearing vs. bracket failure. Need close inspection.

Rourke unrolled a spool of yellow tape and looped it around the surviving posts and a hawthorn trunk, tugging until it made a bright, cautionary hem. He tied a knot that would not slip, even if someone tried.

“You think they’ll climb it anyway?” Mara asked.

“They’ll try,” he said. “They always think they loved it more than gravity.”

“Then we staple signs to their love.”

“I brought signs,” he said, and he had—laminated rectangles with the bland language of municipal care: Path Closed. Hazard. Do Not Enter. He attached one to the wobble-post and paused as it knocked against the wood in a small, upset rhythm. He moved it to the hawthorn. “This one stays.”

A truck engine sounded from the road above—Public Works, early. Another followed immediately with the rattle of pipes in a bed. The crew climbed out, two men and a woman with the windburned faces of people who work under any sky. One of them tipped two fingers to his cap.

“Morning, Sergeant,” the woman said to Rourke. “She give you a bite?”

“Took the whole arm,” he said. “You’re going to want three sawhorses and two barricade stands here. Then run tape from the roses to the oak. Keep pedestrians on the inland side of the drive. The edges are lying.”

“Copy that,” the woman said. She looked past him and let out a low whistle. “That’s a clean one.”

“Fresh,” Rourke said.

Mara stepped back while they laid the wooden legs and unfolded the stands. The sawhorses went together with a kindness of old habits. The bright barricade boards made the place look like a toy village ready for an event no one wanted to attend.

Rourke took his radio again. “Seven to County Engineer. When you land, meet me at the overlook. We’ll need your call before we authorize any shoreline work.”

“Copy,” came a voice, thin from altitude. “Ten out.”

Mara looked down the drop again, the new face already stained by the ocean’s breathing. She could feel the pressure of the story in her chest like weather. The checklist of what had to happen expanded. Photographs. Diagram. Engineer sign-off. Vendor quotes. Notices. She wrote as if the pencil could build its own bridge.

“We’ll need to notify the insurer,” she said. “And the Board. This triggers their emergency plan.”

“They wrote it for fire, not for a cliff deciding it’s tired,” Rourke said.

“Plans are written for a day with less wind,” Mara said. She tucked the pencil behind her ear. “I’ll file a site hazard report now and flag the access issue. I’ll ask Counsel to add it to the preservation order so nobody sets foot down there without her seeing a certificate and a tie-off plan.”

“Do that,” he said. He hammered a stake with the heel of his hand and didn’t flinch when the stake hit a stone and refused to go deeper. He moved it three inches, tried again, and made it bite. “I’ll write the closure notice and send it to County. And I’ll call Harbor Patrol to remind them to chase off any bright ideas.”

Mara watched the Public Works crew run tape, their faces contained, their motions practiced. One of them, the younger man, laid a sandbag at the base of a stand, patted it, and stepped away as if it were a sleeping dog. The storm had taught them all to handle weight like that.

“Could we rappel it?” she asked at last, because her job was to annoy the limit until it showed where it actually was.

Rourke shook his head. “We could, but we won’t. The soil is a cake in the oven now. You stick a knife in to test it and you ruin the whole pan. You wait.”

“Waiting is the most expensive thing we do,” Mara said.

“Yes,” he said. “And cheaper than a helicopter for a rescue we decide we regret.”

She nodded. She looked at the path that was no path and felt the day turn a notch heavier. “I’ll get three quotes on barge and crane,” she said. “Two locals, one out of Gloucester. I’ll add the cost estimate to the report and prepare a change order for the Board. I’ll note the delays in Exhibit E.”

“Put my name on it,” Rourke said. “Sergeant Rourke, safety closure. No one can accuse you of making the cliff fall to fatten a bill if it’s on my letterhead.”

“I don’t usually get accused of creative geology,” Mara said.

“You do now. Everyone’s broke after a storm,” he said. “They start doing math with their fear.”

He walked the inland edge once more, eyeing cracks like a man checking a dog’s temper. He put his boot heel on one fissure and listened. Nothing. He stepped away anyway.

The engineer arrived with a clipboard and a resolute mustache. He peered, measured, and said the words that would be repeated in emails until they became the day’s weather: “Closed. Indefinite. No approach from above. No shore access within forty feet of the face.”

“That includes work crews?” Rourke asked.

“That includes God,” the engineer said. “You want to come in by water, you do it on a day flatter than this and you stand so far out you need a telescope to see what you’re doing. I’ll send it in writing in an hour.”

“Send it to me, Counsel, Public Works, Harbor Patrol, and the Board liaison,” Mara said. “And include the language on slope stabilization. They’ll ask.”

“They can ask.” The engineer drew a square around the area on his map page with a red pen. “You’re going to lose more of it when the sun comes. This isn’t done.”

Rourke lifted a hand. “We have what we need. Thank you.”

The engineer nodded, took a photo, and left his warning voice behind with the tape.

Mara bounced the pencil against her chin once and pushed it back into motion. She put a square around her own note: Area closed; alternative access by water only; expensive; schedule impact severe. She could see the Board reading it, their faces arranging themselves into a shape she knew. She could see the calls—who pays, who waits, who’s to blame, as if blame could mortar the dirt back onto the cliff.

Rourke tied the last ribbon of tape and stepped back. The overlook looked different when fenced—not just smaller, but farther removed, a place you could only visit with your eyes. The sound of the ocean filled the vacancy where footsteps used to be.

“You’re going back to the library after this?” he asked.

“Yes,” Mara said. “I’ll send Counsel the hazard report and the cost projections. I’ll update the evidence log to reflect loss of access routes. I’ll note the impact on the lower grounds survey and the timeline. I’ll add photographs.”

“Good,” he said. He looked at her boots, at the mud up to the laces. “Change your shoes. Don’t track that into Maureen’s room.”

“She’d staple me to the carpet.”

“She would.”

They stood a second longer because not looking felt disrespectful, like leaving a conversation before it finished. The sea obligingly lifted, struck, and spoke for the cliff.

Rourke took his gloves out of his pocket and pulled them on. “All right,” he said to the air. “That’s that.”

Mara slung her bag higher on her shoulder. “Area closed,” she said. She didn’t have to add the rest. They both could count.

They walked back up the path past the new barricades, which made a small, serious parade in the morning light. The tape snapped a little in the breeze and then lay back down, yellow against the damp green like a line drawn by a steady hand.


==================== SCENE 68 ====================

“Got your engineer’s attachment,” Cass said. She tapped the trackpad, dragged a PDF into a folder flagged REDLINE, and winced as her phone vibrated, then again. “Christ. They found the statement.”

“Which statement.”

“The one where I say the board member told us about the cover-up at Harbor Light.” She held the phone up like she was showing a captured animal. The screen was a barbed wire of messages. “Two from the liaison—‘This city does not authorize you to impugn’—one from PR—‘We urge you to contextualize’—and a voicemail I’m not playing until I’m in a church with a lawyer.”

Mara checked the time. Noon twelve. “Plug your phone in. We need your time stamps,” she said. “And don’t delete anything.”

“I’m not deleting, I’m curating,” Cass said, and shoveled back her hair. She clipped her recorder to the edge of the table and met Mara’s look. “Fine. Archiving. It all goes to the landing page.”

Mara cracked her knuckles, an unpretty sound in an old room. “Let’s do it.”

She dictated to herself as much as to the cursor. “Section one. Safety and access liabilities.” She typed as she spoke, the words landing flat. “One: Cliff path between the overlook and the south steps has failed. Edge retreat estimated at nine to fourteen feet. Barrier installed. Area closed.”

Cass took a photo of the yellow tape and overlaid coordinates. “Map inset?”

“Second paragraph,” Mara said. “Alternative access by water only in conditions flatter than today’s, with Harbor Patrol escort recommended. Engineer’s language: prohibitive risk without slope stabilization; costs nontrivial; schedule impact severe.”

“Want me to soften ‘prohibitive’ for the Board?” Cass asked, looking at her coffee like it had a script in it.

“No,” Mara said. “Next: Funicular locked out. King tide inundation risk confirmed by Harbor Patrol. Power cut at the top station. The car shows evidence of tampering removed. Rourke has the padlock key.”

Her screen pooled text. She itemized: failing pumps and fuel from Owen’s walkthrough; greenhouse roof failure; lower grounds unsearchable until debris cleared; loose masonry hazards at the stone stair where she’d seen the blood rag. “Note: Sawdust and debris in the servants’ corridor still tracking onto main stair; slip risk. Add signage. Cost for professional cleanup, estimate attached.”

Cass’s phone danced again and she thumbed it silent without looking. “We’re going to get ‘optics’ in all caps,” she said. “You know that, right?”

“Optics don’t fix stairs,” Mara said. She pulled up an image of a broken newel post, rotated it until the crack caught the light just right, and dropped it in with a caption: structural compromise, repair required, do not lean. “Section two. Evidentiary losses and constraints.”

Cass sat up, the journalist in her hearing the change in the room. “Go.”

“Water intrusion at the boathouse destroyed approximately half of the ghost-rig equipment,” Mara said. “See Appendix D photographs. Note: wiring and battery remnants recovered; Theo’s admission recorded; Harbor Patrol log attached. Observation: Loss does not impede proof of staging; complement with recovered components and Theo’s statement.”

“Good,” Cass said, tap-tapping the keys. She zapped her recorder on and read the file name. “Theo_confession.wav linked.”

“Lower grounds survey stalled due to access failure,” Mara continued. “Evidence search for additional UV pigment caches postponed. The widow’s-walk door paint transfer already documented; see Appendix F. The luminous paint can was recovered earlier and is in storage; chain-of-custody intact.”

A male voice poured out of the doorway like hot tar. “We’re ‘postponing’ a lot, aren’t we,” the Board liaison said.

Mara didn’t look up immediately. She finished the line she was on: “Kitchen sealed per Rourke. Cistern reopen date pending.” Then she looked.

He was damp at the shoulders and too clean at the shoes, a man who had stood under a portico long enough to be seen standing under a portico. The Board badge hung like a monocle from his pocket. He didn’t come all the way in; it was the library after all, and even the Board didn’t like to track mess over its wool rugs.

“Mara,” he said, with the cordiality one gives to a subpoena. “We need to talk about how you’re presenting ‘liabilities.’ That word has a chilling effect.”

“On whom,” Mara said, and clicked the next cell.

“Donors,” he said, as if the word had a moral handle. “Stakeholders. Our constituents.”

“Do you want the words ‘unbudgeted required repairs and mitigations’ instead,” Mara asked without tone. “Because I can type that. It means the exact same thing.”

He altered his stance, discovered the fans, and frowned at the invisible movement of air. “We have a fundraising email ready,” he said. “It reads better if the report says ‘temporary closures’.”

Cass snorted into her coffee, then coughed to make it sound like a cold. “Temporary like ‘the cliff will put itself back.’”

He flicked a look at her, then in that polite Board way flicked the rest of her out of his field. “You also cannot assert that ‘cover-up’ at Harbor Light as if it’s a finding.”

“I didn’t,” Mara said. “I attributed the statement to a board member. It’s in quotation marks. Also, it’s in my taped notes. Also, you should talk less loudly inside a library.”

The liaison’s smile was a slice. “Counsel asked me to convey that the city would appreciate—”

“I’m copying Counsel,” Mara said. “They can appreciate directly.”

Cass whispered without moving her lips, “God, marry me,” and shot the engineer’s red-squared map into the document.

“Section three,” Mara said, turning the volume down on the liaison by not seeing him. “Findings to date.” Her fingers moved quick and unpretty. “A: Vivian’s death—overdose ruled out by pill tray audit, pharmacy refills, and bed-check notes; clean timeline established. See Appendix B for audit sheets and photographic sequence.”

“Color me a problem,” the liaison said, leaning on nothing. “Do you have to use ‘ruled out’ if there’s any risk of… legal hedging?”

“There’s always risk,” Mara said. “I’m not hedging the chain-of-fact. B: Staged drowning confirmed by lividity photographs, cistern hose evidence, and Theo’s admission. Kitchen sealed by Rourke; chain-of-custody numbers index attached.”

Cass dragged in the photos: the purplish bloom of livor mortis where it shouldn’t be, the hose coiled with the wrong intent. She pinched and zoomed, face set. “Appendix E,” she said, and added metadata in a column—time, date, camera type.

“C: Hidden wall void behind the sealed ballroom portrait identified,” Mara said. “Contents inventoried. See Appendix H for list and photographs; negative sleeves recovered in the attic—see Iris’s statement—are logged under separate envelope. D: Payroll ledgers recovered from the greenhouse lead box—Appendix G; confirm irregular disbursements.”

“E: Active call-bell system and hidden AV cabling through vents confirm human staging of ‘summons’,” Cass said, as if reading the script ahead of Mara. “Appendix C schematic drafted. Do you want the close-up of the junction box with the tape you circled?”

“Yes,” Mara said. “F: Microcassette seized by Julian under claim of ‘legal custody’ is acknowledged lost to our immediate review. Document as evidentiary loss pending order. Note: Dictated codicil played by Rourke—Vivian naming Nora—and confirmed by Maureen’s records. Appendix I. G: Funicular key used to access boathouse rig, as noted previously.”

The liaison cleared his throat in a way meant to sound official and came off as needy. “Mara, I’m asking for proportionality. This all reads like a prosecution memo.”

“It’s an incident report,” Mara said. “The adjective is ‘accurate,’ not ‘soothing.’”

Cass kept half an eye on her phone. “PR is calling it ‘alarmist and prejudicial’ in their group chat,” she said conversationally. “Also, someone just wrote ‘we regret Ms. Talbot’s misunderstandings.’ Should I regret my misunderstandings back at them in six-point italic?”

“Don’t answer,” Mara said. “Archive. Section four. Cost and schedule impact.” She put a new header in, black on gray, clinical as a scalpel. “Slope stabilization engineering consult—line item. Greenhouse remediation—line item. Masonry repair—line item. Professional cleanup—line item. Security augmentation for lower grounds pending alternative access, given increased trespass attempts drawn by social media exposure.”

The liaison fixed on that. “Yes. Exactly. Could you at least attribute those attempts to media incitement? We have donors asking if ‘the reporter’ created this problem.”

Cass laughed once, the kind that would stain if it spilled. “I ‘created’ the wind, did I.”

Mara didn’t look up. “The trespassers came because there’s a cliff and the story of a dead heiress,” she said. “They would have come without your emails, without hers. But sure. Add: multiple attempts to breach boundary fence correlated with unrelated online chatter. Harborside patrol increased. See Cass’s uploads for audio relevant to earlier events, already in evidence.”

He opened his mouth, then closed it like a soft-case violin.

Mara got the last pieces in, her tone a straightedge. “Action items: 1) Immediate signage and barricade extension; 2) Contracts for stabilization consult; 3) Controlled re-entry plan via water with Harbor Patrol input; 4) Reissue internal memo clarifying chain-of-custody protocols—no private seizures; 5) Set schedule for continuation of search when safe access restored.”

Her cursor blinked. She added a line. “Language: non-negotiable, clear. Subject lines: explicit. People read subject lines.”

Cass’s phone buzzed hard enough that it traveled forward on its own. She palmed it before it leapt the gap. “Liaison to PR to Counsel,” she read. “They want an edit on the word ‘staged.’ They want ‘suggestive of staging.’”

“No,” Mara said.

“You’re getting a reputation,” the liaison said softly, as if it were a carved insult. “You make it hard.”

“My job is to make it clear,” Mara said. “Clarity is hard.”

He looked at her then the photos like he might be allergic to them. “Can we at least move ‘liabilities’ to the appendices.”

“No,” Mara said again. “I want it where you’ll see it.”

For a moment the only voice was the fan. Then the mail ding sounded as Cass’s laptop caught a gust of wireless and a hundred complaints found their way into their chairs.

“Print the appendices list,” Mara said as if they weren’t being shouted at by pixels. “We’ll wet-sign and scan.”

Cass obeyed, grateful to have a muscle task. The printer started with a stubborn sigh and fed out sheets that smelled like toners at war. She stapled crisp paper with precise violence.

The liaison’s phone rang; he let it ring through, crafting the affront carefully. “We’ll be issuing a statement,” he said.

“Good,” Mara said. “We’ll attach yours under ‘response.’ It keeps the record together.”

He chose not to hear that and turned instead to Cass with that jolly pity men sometimes use when they mean to be punitive. “You’ve put yourself in a difficult position, Ms. Talbot. It would be wise to disavow your more inflammatory phrasing.”

Cass met him with a gaze the camera couldn’t improve. “It would be wise to stop saying ‘wise’ like a nun with a ruler,” she said. “I recorded you. I recorded your friend. I have dates, times, and the kind of handwriting they teach in law school. You want me to disavow it, file a motion.”

“You’ll find the Board is less forgiving than—”

“Than what,” Mara said, and the flatness of it was harder than a shout.

The liaison’s smile got thinner. “Than storms,” he said, trying poetry and failing. “Send it.” He nodded at the report as if it was his to nod at and turned his neat shoes out of the room, leaving the fans to say what the Board did not: you can’t stop a lot of things with tone.

They let him go without gifting him their faces. Cass exhaled like she was done holding her breath for strangers.

“Do we redact donor names on the greenhouse repair estimate,” she asked.

“Just the home addresses,” Mara said. “Names stay. Money is a person when it belongs to a person.”

Cass stapled the appendices in order like she was building a case. She slid over the signature page. “Sign here, initial here, date here.” Very solemn, very clerk.

Mara signed in a hand that looked like it had learned on a dock: fast, legible, without ornament. “Attach the photos: cliff line, barricades, boathouse rig remnants, lividity, hose, payroll ledgers, wall void, AV cable schematic, pill tray audit sheets, funicular lock.”

Cass ticked them off with a pen, the list becoming real with each tick. She inserted the engineer’s red-squared map. She added the Harbor Patrol’s note about boats. She flicked in her own minutes: “Board Liaison present 12:06–12:19, requested euphemisms; request denied.”

“Appendix J, committee correspondence,” Mara said. “Just the excerpts. No commentary.”

“God forbid commentary,” Cass said, and smiled sideways. “Look at us. We’re the school of no adjectives.”

The scanner ate the signature page with a satisfying swallow. The outgoing email window sat there pocket-sized and eternal. To: Counsel, Public Works, Harbor Patrol, Board liaison, Rourke. Subject: Chapter 23 Incident Report—Access Failure, Evidentiary Impacts, Findings, and Required Actions.

Mara attached the PDFs, then the photos, then the audio links. The progress bar moved like a determined inchworm. She added one line in the body: “Submitted per protocol; appendices attached; photographs embedded; audio linked. Please acknowledge receipt.”

“Want to add ‘please do not call it ‘preliminary’ if you plan to sit on it,’” Cass asked.

“No,” Mara said. “They’ll call it what they call it. We’ll file what we file.”

“Mood: carved granite,” Cass said, and lifted her phone to document the last moment because that’s what she did. “Do the click.”

Mara clicked Send.

The room did not change; the light did not bless them; the fans did not clap. But the tiny chime of outgoing was a bell anyway.

Cass’s laptop chimed back almost immediately—Counsel: received. Harbor Patrol: received. Public Works: received with thanks. The Board liaison’s reply landed last, heavier without being longer. Received. Reviewing.

Cass raised her coffee in a toast the color of failing courage. “To weasel words not winning today.”

“To clear words being enough,” Mara said.

A box of glossy four-by-sixes spit itself out of the printer: the cliff’s new line like a surgery scar, the yellow tape doing its small, brave job; the hose coiled; the negative sleeves titled like fish in a net. Cass collected them and slotted them into the appendices sleeve with a practiced hand.

There was a beat where a hundred miles of weather spoke outside. In here, the library kept its own temperature.

“Do you think they’ll—” Cass started, and then shook her head like the thought was a fly. “Never mind. That’s not our job.”

“No,” Mara said. “Our job is the record.”

Cass pulled on her headphones and resumed her archiving, the soft thud of the keys a metronome. Mara logged the submission in the ledger in her cleanest block letters: Report transmitted 12:31; Appendices A–J included; photographs appended; audio links embedded; signatures scanned.

She capped her pen, and the click sounded like a door closing correctly.


==================== SCENE 69 ====================
The office had been a sunporch once—someone’s idea of gracious—now walled in with beadboard and a door that stuck on swollen afternoons. The radiator clanked like a drunk metronome. A taped-up plastic drop cloth trembled over the window where the wind had found a seam. On the desk: a municipal-blue phone with a coiled cord, a stack of invoices fanned like bad tarot, three empty paper cups the color of despair. Against the wall: a corkboard of deadlines stabbing through with pushpins.

Cass had claimed the rolling chair with one blown wheel and taught her body the angle. She had her laptop open to a spreadsheet of horrors in alternating shades of yellow and gray: line items turned into loss.

Nora came in with a file box hugged against her ribs like a shield. She set it down, lifted the lid, and exposed manila folders labeled in her housekeeping print: Insurance—Active, Insurance—Lapsed, Payroll, Fuel, Contingency (ha). Her mouth was a straight seam.

“You look like you’re opening a parlor trick,” Cass said without humor. “Watch me pull a solvent budget out of a hat.”

Nora pulled a legal pad instead. “You’re not on camera,” she said. “You can skip the patter.”

The phone lit before the second hand of the office clock had a chance to take its jerk-forward step. BOARD LIAISON, the caller ID said, with the cheerful anonymity of a fire alarm.

Cass flipped it to speaker and put her finger beside the volume button. “Cass Kitteridge, Nora Leary on the line.”

Static sighed, and then the liaison’s voice arrived, precise and crisp like an ice cube hitting a glass. “We read the report,” she said. “Thank you for the speed of submission. Now we need to discuss the optics. And the costs.”

“The costs first,” Nora said.

“Oh, excellent,” the liaison said. “Let’s do everything at once. The editorial board at the Chronicle has already requested comment on whether your organization incited trespass during a weather emergency. They have audio.”

“That audio’s already in evidence,” Cass said. “And it’s contextualized. Our message leads with safety advisories, then the timeline, then corrective steps. I’ve drafted the statement.”

“You’re not sending anything without sign-off,” the liaison said. “You go off-script, and we all get burned.”

“What script?” Cass asked. She turned her laptop so the screen glanced Nora’s shoulder. “We’re improvising in a building with a leak.”

Nora kept her eyes on her legal pad. She’d drawn a box and written Fuel/Food/Payroll in it. “Line item totals,” she said. “We’ve got eighty-two in the operating account, sixty committed to payables within ten days, two hundred and forty in restricted funds, which are restricted for a reason. We can’t move them.”

“When will insurance weigh in?” the liaison asked.

Nora tapped the folder labeled Insurance—Active. “Initial contact made. Adjuster says five business days. They’ll want photographs, appraisals, the usual. They’ll dispute storm versus deferred maintenance. They’ll dispute everything.”

“Deferred maintenance is not a concept we market,” the liaison said sharply. “The message is ‘extraordinary event.’ The message is ‘public trust.’ Right now, the message is whatever you already let loose on your channels.”

Cass steepled her hands and then flattened them again. “We pulled the offshore audio entirely. We’re down to a holding statement: ‘We are cooperating with authorities, our priority is safety, structures are closed, updates will be posted on official channels.’ Calm, clear, and factual.”

“You are not in a position to set calm,” the liaison said. “You weaponized curiosity, Ms. Kitteridge. You were quoted luring audience to the fence line in a storm. Donors have screenshots.”

“Donors can have nuance as well,” Cass said. “That quote is three weeks old and out of context.”

“Context doesn’t trend,” the liaison said.

The radiator popped. The clock did its jerk-forward. Somewhere down the hall, someone laughed once and then remembered themselves.

“And the cliff,” Nora said. “Public Works says the path is gone. We need geotechnical bids for stabilization and shoring, if shoring is even viable. We need emergency fencing. We need signage. We need money.”

“Bids,” the liaison said, like the word tasted metallic. “Three comparables per scope. Your thresholds require Board approval if any single contract exceeds fifty.”

“Everything exceeds fifty,” Nora said.

“Then the Board will convene an emergency finance committee,” the liaison said. “In the meantime, you will freeze nonessential spending.”

“Define nonessential,” Cass said.

“Everything that doesn’t keep people from dying or suing,” the liaison said. “Tours are suspended. Docent hours cut to zero. The winter gala is canceled. Media buys canceled. Offsite programs postponed. Restore-for-the-holidays is paused. Payroll will be examined for trims.”

Cass set her jaw. “You cut messaging, rumor fills the space. Then rumor writes our budget for us.”

“You don’t have a budget to defend if you don’t have a building that stands,” the liaison said.

Nora flipped to a fresh page. Her pen was surgical. “Emergency procurement,” she said. “We can solicit preliminary numbers by close of business. I’ll draft the RFP language. Geotech assessment. Temporary barrier. Debris removal. Electrical inspection for subpanel exposure.”

“Add environmental testing,” Cass said, eyes flicking to the word cistern on one of her own tabs. “Water intrusion, mold.”

Nora wrote Environmental testing underlined. “Add legal,” she said. “We’ll need counsel for donor notifications, for probate complexities, for contract review, for liability. I’m not doing this drop by drop. We set aside a reserve.”

“You don’t have a reserve,” the liaison said.

“Then we declare one,” Nora said. “We flag cuts to make room.” She opened a fresh folder labeled Cuts—Potential, uncapped. “Gala is gone. Holiday marketing is gone. Social media budget is minimal. Volunteers suspended, which costs us goodwill but saves on coordination hours. Collections climate-control stays, obviously. Fuel rationed to critical systems. We’ll consolidate offices to one wing to cut heat. Staff prop cards get pulled. Endowment draw—”

“That requires the full Board,” the liaison said. “And an investment policy statement rationale.”

“Put it on your calendar,” Nora said.

Cass looked up at the plastic rattling against the window. The afternoon light was a weak bruise. “If you take us dark online,” she said, quieter now, “the story becomes whatever they want. And it will be about the dead. About how we—how I—” She stopped herself, not out of guilt; out of discipline.

“We aren’t going dark,” Nora said without looking up. “We’re going thin. One channel. No comments. Daily update at four o’clock with facts only. I’ll approve it.”

“So you’re going to do my job now?” the liaison asked.

“No,” Nora said. “I’m going to be the person who signs the checks that let you keep yours.”

There was a pause long enough for the radiator to sigh again.

“Alright,” the liaison said finally. “Parameters: One controlled channel. No images that could be construed as sensational. No speculation. Every post is cleared by counsel. You will provide me the drafts by three-thirty. Ms. Leary, you will circulate the cut list with annotations by end of day. And you will begin the bid process for remediation immediately.”

“Already on it,” Nora said. “We’ll hit three firms: Hathaway Geo, Shoreline Stabilization, and Barnett Marine. They’ve got emergency services. We’ll ask for site assessments within forty-eight hours, proposals within seventy-two. We’ll attach our photos and the topo.”

“Spoiler alert,” Cass muttered. “Costs: catastrophic.”

Nora drew a line under Bids and wrote: Prelim numbers by Friday; site access clearance; safety briefings; insurance notified. She put an asterisk beside Insurance and added: Photographic packet to adjuster; appraisals; inventory.

The liaison breathed like someone smoothing out a napkin. “Donors,” she said. “We will need to reassure them that the bequest is being responsibly managed.”

“The bequest is a house that ate through its savings and then the cliff,” Nora said. Her voice wasn’t raised. It was too cold for that. “What they get is a balance sheet.”

“What they want is a narrative,” the liaison said. “And right now, the narrative is that you’ve inherited a scandal as well as a structure.”

“That’s accurate,” Nora said. “We’ll carry both.”

Cass leaned forward. “Draft donor letter: ‘Due to structural damage and ongoing investigations, we are reallocating resources to immediate safety and remediation. Programs are suspended until further notice. Your prior gifts are acknowledged and protected under restricted-use agreements. We will provide transparent accounting.’”

“Send it to me by six,” the liaison said. “And take your name off any public-facing statement for the next week.”

Cass didn’t flinch. “Reroute the hate mail.”

“Consider this your verbal reprimand,” the liaison said. “There may be formal consequences.”

“Consider this my acceptance,” Cass said. Her hand was steady where it pressed to the desk. “Do you want me to brief the city desk or do you want silence?”

“I want to minimize exposure,” the liaison said. “You’ll provide a written Q&A. We’ll designate a spokesperson from the Board.”

Cass nodded once and typed: Q&A—Board voice. “Copy.”

Nora capped her pen, stripped off a Post-it, and stuck it to the top of her pad. It read: Call counsel; set meeting; reserve fund; notify staff; schedule with adjuster; draft RFP. Beside it, she wrote: Probate counsel, tort counsel, insurance counsel—three names and a timeline. Her handwriting did not tilt.

“Anything else?” she asked.

“Yes,” the liaison said. “Are we at risk of additional... surprises?”

Nora flipped to the payroll ledger and the fuel log. “We’re at risk of invoices coming due,” she said. “We’re at risk of pipes freezing if the temperature drops and we cheap out on heat. We’re at risk of a press cycle that doesn’t care about nuance. We’re at risk of donors who prefer ghost stories to line items. The remedy is simple and ugly: we live inside the numbers.”

“Not exactly inspirational,” the liaison said.

“Not my department,” Nora said.

A beat, and the liaison made a small sound that could have been assent or exhaustion. “Very well. I’ll set the finance committee for tomorrow afternoon. Send me the cut list and the bid RFP by five. Ms. Kitteridge, no off-the-record with anyone. Do you understand me?”

“Perfectly,” Cass said. “We’re the school of no adjectives.”

The line clicked. The phone returned to its steady, indifferent hum.

For a second neither of them moved. The plastic at the window lifted and settled like a slow-breathing creature.

Cass sat back and pinched the bridge of her nose. “You going to fire me?” she asked, as if she were asking about coffee.

“I don’t have time to fire anyone,” Nora said. “Do your job. Smaller. Colder.”

“That I can do.” Cass reached for her keyboard and began to type with discipline, not speed. Holding Statement v4. Donor Letter v1. Q&A_BoardVoice. Her cursor marched.

Nora opened her laptop and created a new folder named: Remediation_Bids. She drafted the scope like she was doing something clean: Site stabilization; soil anchoring; toe protection; debris; safety fencing; access limitations; coordination with Public Works; proof of insurance; references. She copied the address block from a grant that would not happen now, and repurposed it without apology.

She dialed counsel first. “We need three sets,” she said when the paralegal answered. “Probate, contract, tort. Yes, today.”

Next she called Hathaway Geo and left a message with the voice of someone ordering landscape gravel. “We need emergency assessment on a coastal cliff failure. Walkthrough tomorrow if you can. Site is taped and secured. We have topo and photographic documentation.”

She texted Rourke a list for site access. She added a note to herself: Staff meeting at three—deliver cuts, brace for reaction, answer questions, no promises. She underlined no promises. She was not going to say it soft.

Cass hit send on the holding statement and slid her chair to the corkboard to pin a printout at eye level under a thumbtack shaped like a lighthouse: 4 p.m. Update—Facts only.

“Do you want me to apologize?” she asked the room.

Nora stacked the Insurance—Active and the Insurance—Lapsed in new order: the past under the present. “No,” she said. “We’re past apology. We’re at execution.”

“Good word,” Cass murmured, but there was no pleasure in it.

The office clock did another single, definitive jump. Trucks droned somewhere on the road with the flat sound of after-weather. Outside, the wind had run out of vocabulary; it raked and then stopped.

Nora pulled her hair back into a rough knot and picked up her pen again. Numbers behaved if you disciplined them. People less so. The list would be the ritual and the shield.

She wrote: Freeze discretionary. Cancel gala. Notify vendors. Suspend tours. Draft furlough letters. Confirm unemployment resources. Staff Q&A. Budget to zero minus known obligations. Create legal file, digital and paper. She paused and added a final line, plain as a street sign: Accept liability; manage exposure.

“Okay,” she said to Cass, and to the plastic, and to the rattling radiator and the obstinate clock. “Let’s make this uglier before we make it cleaner.”

Cass nodded and hit send again. The laptop made its small bell tone. In this room at least, bells still meant action and not ceremony.
